# 冲虚至德真经四解

*The Fourfold Exegesis of the Perfect Scripture of the Dao of Primal Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue*

**Title:** *The Fourfold Exegesis of the Perfect Scripture of the Dao of Primal Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue*
Compiled by Gao Shouyuan of the Jin dynasty, completed circa the *yiyou* year of the Dading era (1185). Twenty fascicles.

**Source Text:** *Zhengtong Daoist Canon*, *Dongshen* section, *Yujue* category.

**Liezi**
Liezi, surnamed Lie, personal name Yukou, was a native of Zheng. He dwelt in the Zheng gardens for forty years, yet no one recognized him. Initially, he studied under Master Huqiu, later became a disciple of the Old Master Shang, and befriended Master Bogao. Having mastered the Way of these two masters, after nine years he could ride the wind and travel. His disciple Yan Hui asked: "Is the purpose of seeking the Dao for wealth?" Liezi replied: "Jie and Zhou prized profit and scorned the Dao, hence their downfall."

His work comprises eight pian. Liezi was a man of the Dao, and Zhuangzi frequently praised him. Today, the Liezi Temple in Bianliang, Zhengzhou, and Putian stands on the site of his former retreat. During the Tang dynasty, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected Person of Primal Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue, and his work was titled *The Perfect Scripture of the Dao of Primal Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue*.

Sima Qian, in his account of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, placed them before the Six Classics, thereby demonstrating his reverence for the arts of the Dao. Why, then, did he omit *Liezi*? Liu Xiang later collated and edited the text, which elucidates inner and outer truths, verifies life and death, and harmonizes self and other—largely in accord with Master Meng of Zhuang. Even the passages on "not knowing whether I ride the wind or the wind rides me," or "Zhou becoming a butterfly, the butterfly becoming Zhou," seem as though spoken from a single mouth.

Yet among later commentators and transmitters, few engaged with *Liezi*. In the Jin dynasty, there was Zhang Zhan; in the Tang, Lu Chongxuan. Compared to the *Nanhua* [Zhuangzi], Zhang Zhan is like Guo Xiang, and Lu Chongxuan like Cheng Xuanying.

By the Zhenghe era of the Song dynasty, there were exegeses, and Fan Zhixu (style name Qian Shu) of the Left Auxiliary also produced a commentary. At that time, the empire established Daoist learning, with teaching and nurturing methods equal to those of the Three Halls’ *jinshi*. The Confucian minister Wang Li memorialized: "The two works *Zhuangzi* and *Liezi* are the wings of the Old Master [Laozi], just as Yan [Hui] and Meng [Zi] are to Confucius. Their subtle words and wondrous principles enlighten later generations, restoring the resplendent Way of the Yellow Emperor—a merit no less than that of Yan and Meng. It is fitting that the court order officials to deliberate on how to honor and esteem them." The court assented, and thus the text gained widespread circulation.

In Pingyang, the reclusive scholar Gao Shouyuan, styled Shanchang, collected two exegeses, combining them with the works of Zhang [Zhan] and Lu [Chongxuan] into a single volume. This truly enriches the learner, enabling one to knock at the gate of mystery and explore the threshold of the sages, attaining vastness while exhausting subtlety—how could this not be right? I have often reflected that the art of exegetical glossing has ever been difficult. Lu’s preface states: "A sage appears but once in a thousand years, and even then, they stand shoulder to shoulder; enlightenment dawns across ten thousand generations, yet it differs not from dawn to dusk." One cannot but sigh in lament.

In the third lunar month of the *jiyou* year of the Dading era, written by Mao Hui, Retired Gentleman for Meritorious Service, former Vice Prefect of Qinzhou, Cloud Rider Cavalier, and recipient of the *Feiyu* Fish Bag.

The newly compiled book is hereby finalized, comprising eight chapters.

The Protector of the Left Directorate of Waterways and Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, Minister Xiang, respectfully reports: The *Liezi* in the imperial archives consists of five chapters. Your minister Xiang, along with Minister Can, Magistrate of Changshe, collated three chapters from the Grand Temple archives, four from the Imperial Historiographer’s archives, six transcribed by your minister Xiang, and two by Minister Can—twenty chapters in total from both internal and external sources. After collation, twelve duplicate chapters were removed, leaving eight chapters definitively established. The imperial archives contain more, while external sources have fewer; passages are disordered and scattered across chapters, with frequent character errors—such as *jin* (exhaust) miswritten as *jin* (advance), or *xian* (worthy) as *xing* (form). In the newly compiled book, collation was conducted based on the imperial archives’ established text, all transcribed onto bamboo slips for fair copying.

*Liezi* was a native of Zheng and a contemporary of Duke Mu of Zheng, a man of the Dao. His learning derived from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, and he was numbered among the Daoists. The Daoists grasp the essential and hold to the root, embracing clarity, emptiness, and non-action. In cultivating the self and engaging with others, they esteem non-contention, aligning with the Six Classics. However, the chapters *King Mu* and *Tang’s Questions* are extravagant and fantastical, not the words of a noble person. As for the *Power and Fate* chapter, it wholly espouses Yangzi’s doctrine of predestined fate, valuing only unrestrained abandon—two doctrines at odds, unlike the work of a single school. Yet each has its insights, and there is much worth observing.

During the reign of Emperor Xiaojing, the arts of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi flourished, and this book circulated widely. Later, it was lost and scattered among the people, with no transmitters. Moreover, it contains many parables, akin to those of Zhuang Zhou. Thus, the Grand Historian Sima Qian did not compose a separate biography for him, merely recording him in brief. Your minister Xiang, at the risk of death, respectfully submits the catalog of the *Liezi* collated by the Protector of the Left Directorate of Waterways and Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, Minister Xiang.

Presented on the *renyin* day of the eighth month in the third year of the Yongshi era.

Zhang Zhan of the Jin dynasty’s commentary and preface.

I have heard from my late father that my grandfather, along with Liu Zhengyu and Fu Yinggen, were all nephews of the Wang family, and in their youth, they studied under their maternal uncle, Wang Shizhou. Shizhou’s elder cousin, Wang Zhengzong, and his younger cousin, Wang Fusi, were both fond of collecting books. My grandfather and the others obtained nearly ten thousand scrolls from the family library of Wang Zhongxuan. The Fu family, too, had long been a scholarly household, and the three youths, in their childhood, vied to transcribe rare books. When they grew older, they encountered the chaos of the Yongjia era and fled south with Yinggen to escape the turmoil. Each carriage bore as much as its strength allowed, but with bandits and raiders growing ever more rampant and the road ahead still long, I said to Fu: "Now we cannot preserve all that we carry. Let us instead sort through what is most rare in the world, each safeguarding and recording what we must, so that nothing is lost." Thereupon, Yinggen took only the collected works of his ancestor Fu Xuan and his father Fu Xian. Among the books my grandfather had transcribed was the *Liezi* in eight scrolls. By the time they reached the south, only a few remained. Of the *Liezi*, only the scrolls *Yang Zhu*, *Explaining the Omen*, and the *Catalogue* survived. Later, during the upheaval, Zhengyu, who had become Inspector of Yangzhou, had already crossed the river and possessed four scrolls in his household. Soon after, I obtained six scrolls from the home of Fusi’s son-in-law, Zhao Jizi. Comparing and collating what was present and absent, the text was at last made complete. The general purport of the book is to clarify that all beings take ultimate emptiness as their fundamental principle, and that the myriad things are verified by their eventual dissolution. Spiritual wisdom is preserved in quiescent constancy and wholeness.

Reflections that cling to things bring about the loss of life, while wakefulness and transformation are as insubstantial as dreams—whether great or small, they are not confined to a single realm; success or failure does not depend on wisdom or effort. In cultivating the self, the highest value lies in unrestrained spontaneity. Follow one’s nature, and wherever one goes is fitting; one may tread upon water or fire. Forget all concerns, and nothing, however hidden, remains unilluminated. Such is the essence of this work.

Yet what it elucidates often intersects with Buddhist scriptures, while its fundamental return aligns with Laozi and Zhuangzi. Its diction and allusions bear a marked resemblance to the *Zhuangzi*. Zhuangzi, Shen Dao, Han Fei, Shizi, and the *Huainanzi* all point toward the same end, frequently citing its words. Thus, I have annotated it accordingly.

**Preface and Discussion by Lu Chongxuan, Tang Court Gentleman for Miscellaneous Affairs**

Liu Xiang said: Liezi was a man of Zheng who lived contemporaneously with Duke Mu of Zheng. He was, in all likelihood, a man of the Dao. His learning originated with the Yellow Emperor and Laozi and was called the Daoist school. The Daoist school grasps the essential and holds to the root, embracing clarity, emptiness, and non-action. In governing the self and engaging with things, it esteems non-contention and aligns with the Six Classics. However, the chapters *King Mu* and *Tang’s Questions* are extravagant and fantastical, not the words of a noble person. As for the *Power and Fate* chapter, it entirely defers to destiny, while the *Yangzi* chapter values only unrestrained freedom. These two doctrines contradict one another and do not resemble the work of a single school. Nevertheless, each has its insights, and there is much worth observing. Moreover, it abounds in parables, much like the *Zhuangzi*. For this reason, Sima Qian, the Grand Historian, did not write a separate biography for him.

Zhang Zhan’s preface states: The general purport of the book is to clarify that all beings take ultimate emptiness as their fundamental principle, and that the myriad things are verified by their eventual dissolution. Spiritual wisdom is preserved in quiescent constancy and wholeness. Reflections that cling to things bring about the loss of life; wakefulness and transformation are as insubstantial as dreams—whether great or small, they are not confined to a single realm; success or failure does not depend on wisdom or effort. In cultivating the self, the highest value lies in unrestrained spontaneity. Follow one’s nature, and wherever one goes is fitting; one may tread upon water or fire. Forget all concerns, and nothing, however hidden, remains unilluminated. Such is its essence. Yet what it elucidates often intersects with Buddhist scriptures, while its fundamental return aligns with Laozi and Zhuangzi.

I, Chongxuan, consider the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi to have expounded the Dao for ages, yet no one in later generations has truly understood them. All attempt to discern their true essence through the distinctions of emotion and intellect, but though their explanations may be many, they all lead to incomprehension. Though their expositions may be numerous, they merely elaborate upon the dregs, invariably pointing toward the great Dao’s profound remoteness within the vast emptiness. The essence of the Dao is subtle and wondrous, transcending all speech. To expound upon it is to obscure its true nature, leaving only empty words; the ultimate principle of emptiness and nothingness is preserved in speech alone, yet people fail to realize that the Dao arises from within the self. They borrow words to explain it, forgetting that one must grasp the meaning and abandon the words, seek realization beyond language, and yet they remain entangled in the discriminating thoughts of birth and extinction, moved by the vulgar heart of emotional activity. They pride themselves on the flowery adornments of the Dao, seeking fame at the cost of substance.

Our August Emperor of Kaiyuan, Sagely in Culture, Divine in Martial Prowess, knows that the Dao is the root of life and that the highest virtue transcends words. He has broadly summoned scholars from the four directions and inquired widely of the myriad regions, hoping to find one who has grasped its profound principles, that he might succor all living beings. I, a humble servant of limited understanding, have by chance come to admire this Dao. Having twice received the sagely decree, I have re-examined its subtle words. Respectfully tracing the text of the *Liezi*, I have annotated its essential teachings, though my wisdom is meager. This is not an attempt to provide a guide; if by chance I might silently align with the subtle and formless, like the dark pearl grasped by the one without conscious intent, it would be my greatest wish—though I dare not hope for it.

**Discussion:**
What, then, is life? It is the meeting of spirit and form.

What, then, is death? It is the parting of spirit and form. Form knows birth and death, but spirit knows neither death nor birth—thus Laozi says, "The valley spirit does not die," and "That which dies yet does not perish is longevity." Yet this birth and death pertain only to form; as for the function of spirit, there is no birth or death at all. Spirit is fundamentally empty and mysterious, and those who align with the true constitute its nature. Form is fundamentally substantial and obstructive, and those who are stained by it constitute its emotions. The perfected person forgets emotion and returns to nature, thus drawing near the Dao; the ordinary person, deluded, clings to nature and pursues emotion, thus losing the true. Therefore, to impair the limbs and dull the senses is the constant nature of those who follow the Dao; to crave life and fear death is the common sentiment of all things. Do not cling to love and thereby harm life, nor seek fame and thereby cast away the treasure. Thus *Zhuangzi* says: "In doing good, do not draw near to fame; in doing evil, do not draw near to punishment." "Follow the axis of the Dao as your constant," for by this one may nurture life and fulfill one's years. Yet later generations, believing that the absence of striving for fame allows the heart to indulge in evil, have strayed far from this truth. Why is this so?

When others laugh, one laughs; when others wail, one wails; what others fear, one cannot but fear—how then could one do evil? Thus we know the spirit is the master of life, while the form repays the spirit’s merit. The spirit possesses the power to aid beings, and the form receives the reward of honor and reverence; the spirit exerts the function of harming beings, and the reward takes the form of baseness and degradation. Therefore, the spirit’s movement is inexhaustible, while the form has its span of long or short; when the reward is exhausted, death ensues, and when merit is manifest, new life arises. It is like the pure and upright ascending to glory, while the greedy and cruel descend in demotion. Speaking in terms of rank, if the form does not change, the station differs; speaking in terms of the spirit, if the spirit remains unaltered, the form transforms. The perfected person fully comprehends this Way and thus possesses and treasures the true. The true spirit has no form, and the mind’s wisdom is its function—this function may be defiled or pure. The distinction between the common and the sage lies here: those submerged in defilement become common, while those abiding in purity become one with the Dao. The Dao has no form or substance; one need only depart from emotion—how could it be sought in the depths of obscurity or discerned beyond the realm of the indistinct? Thus *Laozi* says: "My Way is most easy to know, most easy to practice, yet none can know it, none can practice it. Why is this so?" Later generations merely regard the form as life, not knowing the spirit is the master of life; they regard qi as death, not knowing the spirit is the root of qi. Attachment to form gives rise to emotion, and delusion about the spirit causes one to lose the Dao. Bound to what confuses the root, drowned in what destroys the return, they forget the way back. The sage laments their stubborn clinging and sighs that those who know the Dao are not easily encountered. Thus it is said: "A worthy in a thousand *li* is like a neighbor; one who knows the Way in ten thousand generations is no different from one in the morning or evening"—how deeply to be pitied! Is it not so?

Should one take this discourse to heart, relinquishing emotions and intellect to return to the root, diminishing again and again until acting through non-action, then upon reading the book of *Liezi*, one may have already grasped more than half of its profound meaning.

*Preface to the Zhenghe Commentary*
The Dao flows through the myriad things, yet things are confined to a single corner. The people of the world see things but not the Dao; the sage sees that nothing in things is not the Dao. Thus truth and falsehood stand apart, dream and waking are distinguished, being and non-being are discerned, and past and present diverge. Those who attain do not rely on wisdom; those who lose are not due to folly—yet the disparity between success and failure arises from the mismatch of effort and fate. Those who act for themselves abandon benevolence; those who act for others abandon righteousness—and the words of Yang Zhu and Mo Di are ridiculed by masters of the great Way. Yet *Liezi* transcends truth and falsehood, unites being and non-being in one principle, and leaves gain and loss, success and failure to their natural course. He embraces self-interest and universal love alike, achieving great harmony, while deeply pitying the delusion of the people. They see profit and forget their true nature—like thieves, like those who snatch gold—deluded without return, rushing headlong without a backward glance. Thus he composed eight chapters to reveal the wondrous workings of the spirit, which moves freely beyond all bounds, and the ever-victorious Dao that holds fast to the rear and guards softness in the realm of non-contention. His teachings are vast and unbridled, drawing on external discourses and attributing words to the Yellow Emperor and Confucius. Yet in essence, they all trace back to the intent of the *Daodejing*. However, when we examine his words, probe his meaning, and investigate his attainments—seeing how he was entranced by spirit-mediums, prostrated himself before Bo Hun Wu Ren’s archery—we find that his progress in the Dao culminated only in riding the wind home. Thus he remains far from Zhuang Zhou.

*Zhuangzi* says: "Liezi rode the wind and traveled, yet still relied on something." Alas! To be swift without haste, to arrive without moving—only the utmost spirit of the world, the true embodiment of the Old Master, achieves this. In the intervals of my myriad duties, having already studied the *Five Thousand Characters* and composed annotations for them, and having also commented on the *Zhuangzi*’s *Inner Chapters*, I could not leave the book of *Liezi* without a proper account. Thus I offer these explanations based on what I have heard, awaiting the understanding of future sages.

*Preface written on the first day of the intercalary ninth month of the Wuxu year of the Zhenghe era.*

*Preface by Fan Zuo Cheng, Left Assistant Minister, Wu Shizhong*
What the world values is the written word, yet writing is but speech. What is valued in speech is meaning, and meaning has its source. Those who grasp the meaning may forget the words. Those who fail to grasp the meaning not only misvalue what should be valued but also stand in disorder, collectively rejecting it—how can they be worthy of discussing the great Way?

*Liezi* was a man of virtue from the state of Zheng. Observing his teachings and discourses, one sees his elucidation of the principles of nature and destiny, and his salvific, medicinal words for the world—surpassing all other masters. Beyond words and meaning, he generally took the Primal Origin as his foundation. In his choice of phrases and analogies, he galloped alongside *Zhuangzi*. Yet vulgar scholars and worldly teachers, constrained by names and things, unable to transcend their rigid views, sought only within narrow bounds and thus rejected him as beyond sagacity and wisdom. Even Sima Qian, who especially revered Daoist learning, did not include him in his *Records*; Liu Xiang, erudite and well-versed, collator of books, dismissed the *King Mu* and *Tang’s Questions* chapters as absurd and fantastical, unworthy of a gentleman, and thus rejected and condemned them. Was this not because they failed to grasp the source of his meaning and lost sight of what should be valued?

I have observed the *Zhenghe Commentary* and seen how, in later ages, it exalts the Daoist teachings, aiming to guide the people of the world back to the feelings of their nature and destiny and restore the Great Ancestry—what a profound bestowal! Thus, it commanded scholars of the imperial academy to study the Daoist scriptures alongside their regular texts. Under the hands of great masters, the books of Laozi and Zhuangzi shone like the sun and stars. Those who read and were transformed found their models and could no longer engage in idle debate.

As for the book of *Liezi*, Zhang Zhan once provided a commentary, but its errors and inconsistencies were severe. Not only did it fail to grasp the method of exposition, but it also missed the essence of the text, which has long troubled scholars. Thus, those who truly grasp the meaning of this book, though they may wish to forget words, how could they?

Left Assistant Minister Fan, Master Taichu, in the midst of harmonizing governance, personally provided annotations, expounding the meaning as if it sprang from *Liezi*’s own mind. His explanations suffice to dispel the obstructions of learners. Subtle words and wondrous Dao, after thousands of years, are suddenly made clear—like parting clouds and mist to behold the blue sky. Those who read his book will grasp its meaning in an instant, without even leaving their seats. Thus, its contribution to our sovereign’s way of transforming the world is of boundless benefit. Therefore, I have overseen its carving to broaden its transmission, and respectfully place this preface at the beginning of the volume.

*Written on the full moon of the first autumn month of the first year of the Xuanhe era.*

*The Four Commentaries on the Perfect Book of Profound Virtue, Volume One*
Commentaries by Zhang Zhan of the Jin, Lu Chongxuan (Vice Director of Communications) of the Tang,
*Zhenghe Commentary* of the Song, and Fan Zhixu (Left Assistant Minister) of the Song.
Compiled by He Guang Sanren, Gao Shouyuan.

Zhang observes: How can great and small be at odds, or long and short differ in nature? Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad beings numerous, all that falls within the realm of living things and pertains to the domain of activity and function is governed by the natural signs of life, death, and transformation. Only that which is utterly still and supremely empty, concentrated in oneness and unchanging, lies beyond the cycles of yin and yang or the shifts of the four seasons.

Xu comments: All moving beings take life as their master, yet they cling to life without understanding the principle of life begetting life. That which undergoes generation and transformation is the realm of form; that which begets life is formless. Form belongs to things; the formless is called spirit. It may be employed like yin and yang, yet in its true nature, it eludes the grasp of yin and yang. Thus, the *Book of Changes* states: "That which yin and yang cannot fathom is called spirit." Is this not the great numinous portent at the heart of heaven and earth? Hence, it is called *Heavenly Portents*.

The *Zhenghe Commentary* explains: Things undergo generation and transformation, but the Dao knows no past or present. Only those who embody the Dao can remain unchanging while responding to things, without harboring intentions. Thus, *Heavenly Portents* begins with generation and transformation and concludes with Guoshi’s act of thievery.

Fan remarks: Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things numerous, all flow into the realm of life and death, all fall into the mechanism of emergence and withdrawal. They follow one another in cycles of beginning and end, succeed one another in transformations, and in life and death, long life and early demise, gain and loss, fulfillment and deficiency—none escapes the natural signs. Those who embody the Dao transcend the world of things, rising above the domain where the ten thousand forms flow and turn, merging with the unchanging source of singular nature. Day and night cannot enslave them, yin and yang cannot mold them, and thus they master things without being mastered by things.

The *Liezi* places the character *zi* (Master) above the surname, suggesting that the opening chapter may have been recorded by a disciple. He dwelled in the gardens of Zheng for forty years, yet no one recognized him. Not mingling in form with things, nor engaging in speech with them, they remained unaware of his utmost virtue, as if he were unknown. Lords, ministers, and great officials regarded him as they would the common folk. Not that he isolated himself from things, but his words bore no judgments, his actions left no traces—thus, things could not comprehend him.

The *Zhenghe Commentary* states: The ancients who excelled as scholars were subtle, wondrous, and profoundly penetrating—so deep as to be unfathomable.

Fan says: The ancients who excelled as scholars were subtle, wondrous, and profoundly penetrating—so deep as to be unfathomable. Thus, they embraced their nature and held fast to spirit, wandering among the common world, flowing with things and moving with their currents. How could they ever adorn their wisdom to astonish the foolish or strive to set themselves apart from the world and its customs?

When the state suffered famine and dearth, he prepared to depart for Wei. To leave one’s home is called "marrying out."

Xu notes: "Insufficiency" refers to famine. "Marrying out" means to go forth.

A disciple said: Master, you depart without a set time for return. Dare we make a request?

Lu explains: *Ye* (谒) means "to request."

What teaching will you leave us?

The Master had not heard the words of Huqiu Zilin?

Huqiu Zilin was Liezi’s teacher.

Liezi laughed and said: What did Master Hu say? The four seasons proceed, the hundred things are born—do they require words?

Fan remarks: The "pot" (*hu*) is empty yet indestructible; the "mound" (*qiu*) is firm and unmoving. *Zilin* (Master Lin) embodies the mother of the Dao, ruling all under heaven. The Dao cannot be questioned, and questions receive no answer. Those who embody the Dao silently comprehend it, needing no words. To speak much is to stray far from the Dao.

Nevertheless, the Master once spoke to Bohun Maoren, and I overheard it. Let me try to tell you.

Bohun was Liezi’s friend, a fellow student under Huzi. That he did not say he received teaching directly from Huzi reflects Liezi’s humility.

The *Zhenghe Commentary* states: The Dao cannot be spoken; to speak of it is to miss it. Then what did Huzi say? Only when unavoidable did he speak, and so I heard and now tell you.

Fan says: The Dao cannot be spoken; to speak of it is to miss it. Yet in the end, he could not avoid words. This is because his "wordless words" were never truly spoken. In this case, he spoke only to convey the broad outlines for you.

Bohun Maoren embodied the Dao and became a thing, perpetually preserving his radiance and concealing his brilliance, leaving nothing to be seen. Some called him "the man of no-thing." From the perspective of one who stands apart from the world and accompanies heaven, this is what Huqiu Zilin meant when he spoke to him. The Dao cannot be heard, nor can it be told. Thus, when he heard it, he said, "I overheard it"; when he told it, he said, "Let me try to tell you."

His words were: There is life that does not live, transformation that does not transform. This inert form of ours is born by things yet does not birth itself; it exists in change and alteration, transforming things yet does not transform itself.

Lu says: Not born by things, not transformed by things, it can thus give life to all living things and transform all transformations. That which does not live can give life to life; that which does not live is the origin of all living things. That which does not transform can transform transformations; that which does not transform is the master of all transformations. That which lives cannot but live; that which transforms cannot but transform. The living do not live by their own power, nor do the transforming transform by their own power—they simply cannot but live, cannot but transform.

Lu says: All that has life must die. Those transformed by things are ever-shifting; how can they be without life or death, without transformation or change? Thus, they are ever-living, ever-transforming. All that falls within the realm of movement cannot be without it for even a moment. Ever-living, ever-transforming—they are never without life, never without transformation. Life and transformation follow one another; existence and annihilation, presence and absence, alternate without end.

Fan says: The spirit’s mechanism and the mother of qi rise and fall, enter and exit. From the wriggling of insects to the fluttering of winged creatures, all dwell within the realm of generation and transformation. Only those who are not things yet master things can give life without being bound by life, transform without being bound by transformation.

That which is generated by generation depends on this to be generated and cannot but be generated—thus it is ever-generating, without any moment when it is not generating. That which is transformed by transformation depends on this to transform and cannot but transform—thus it is ever-transforming, without any moment when it is not transforming. How could one tread upon form and yet transcend the realm of generation and transformation? To be neither generated nor transformed is to merge with the profound sameness of the Dao/Way—this is called the Perfected Person.

Yin and yang, the four seasons—yin and yang, the four seasons, the things of change and transformation—all belong to the realm of the generated, following this shaping and revolving. The four seasons shift without ceasing; the myriad things transform without rest.

Fan says: All that are shifted by yin and yang, turning with the seasons, are things of form. Moment by moment they shift and transform, their life and death endless—thus they are ever-generating, ever-transforming. The ungenerated is called "solitary." How could the generation of the ungenerated be verified in reality? It is suspected of being obscurely one, without beginning or end.

Fan says: The spirit has no fixed comparison, hence it is called "solitary." Laozi says: "Solitary, it stands alone and does not change." "Suspected" means not daring to speak decisively to clarify what is profoundly wondrous. The untransformed moves to and fro—its boundary cannot be exhausted. Alternation without gap, the succession of forms and qi—its Dao/Way does not end. Suspected of being solitary, its Dao/Way cannot be fathomed. How could one know whether it is fathomable or not? One simply suspects that it stands alone and does not change, circulating without end.

Fan says: The changes of the four seasons cannot be exhausted; the functioning of the spirit and its transformations cannot be fathomed.

*Zhenghe* states: Generation arises from nonbeing to become being; transformation proceeds from being to become nonbeing. That which is generated and transformed is a thing; that which is neither generated nor transformed is the Dao/Way. Things are bound by number, thus the generated cannot but generate, the transformed cannot but transform. The Dao/Way moves through things, thus it is ever-generating, ever-transforming, without any moment when it is not generating, without any moment when it is not transforming. Standing alone above the myriad things, thus the ungenerated is suspected of being solitary, responding without exhaustion. Thus the untransformed moves to and fro—its boundary cannot be exhausted. For none know its beginning or end. Suspected of being solitary, its Dao/Way cannot be fathomed. For it cannot be fully investigated. No thing can pair with it—how could one truly know its reason? One can only suspect it.

Fan says: Yin and yang illuminate, cover, and govern one another; the four seasons replace, generate, and destroy one another. Who directs this? Who maintains its order? Is there a mechanism that ceaselessly operates? Or does it revolve without being able to stop itself? Yin and yang do not depart from qi; the four seasons do not escape from number—thus they cannot transcend the realm of generation and transformation. The true essence of the Dao/Way stands alone and does not change. Because it does not generate itself, it can endure forever. The wondrous functioning of the Dao/Way circulates without end. That which daily transforms with things is the one that does not transform. To transform things without being transformed is to command the transformation of things while alone preserving its origin. Thus, the boundary of no-boundary, the beginning and end returning to no beginning—who knows where it ends? To generate things without being generated is to exist before heaven and earth yet not be long-lasting. Thus, the image of no-thing—none can pair with it—who knows where it ends?

*The Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: "The valley spirit does not die." This ancient book once existed, but now it is no longer extant. The valley is empty yet houses being—just as Zhuangzi speaks of the "ring’s center." Utterly empty of things, thus it is called the valley spirit; fundamentally without generation, thus it is said not to die. This is called the Mysterious Female.

Laozi has a chapter on this. Wang Bi’s commentary says: "Without form or shadow, without opposition or resistance, abiding in lowliness without moving, guarding stillness without decline. The valley completes it yet its form is not seen—this is the ultimate thing. Abiding in lowliness and unable to be named, thus it is called the Mysterious Female. The gate of the Mysterious Female is called the root of heaven and earth. Vague yet existing, its use is inexhaustible."

Wang Bi says: "Gate" refers to that through which the Mysterious Female operates. Tracing it to its origin, it shares the same body as the Great Ultimate—thus it is called the root of heaven and earth. If one wishes to say it exists, its form is not seen; if one wishes to say it is gone, the myriad things are generated by it—thus it is said to be "vague yet existing." No thing is not completed by it, yet it does not labor—thus it is called "without movement."

Fan says: The valley is empty, and qi resides within it; the form is empty, and the spirit dwells within it. "Mysterious" means wondrous and without form; "female" means responding without fixed direction. From birth to death, nothing does not depend on it—thus it is called the "gate." The root of all that has form—thus it is called the "root." Seen yet not visible, used without exhaustion—thus it is said to be "as if existing."

Fan says: The Yellow Emperor and Laozi both embodied the spirit and illuminated the Dao/Way. The Dao/Way is one alone—how could their words differ? Thus, the teachings of the valley spirit and the Mysterious Female appear in Laozi, while Liezi attributes them to *The Book of the Yellow Emperor*. The valley’s function has no appearance; the spirit’s essence has no fixed direction—it is that from which the myriad things receive their mandate. "Mysterious" is the color of heaven; "female" is the kind of earth—it is that from which the myriad things receive their forms. Though their names differ, in reality they are one thing. Heaven and earth are the above and below of the myriad things, yet the gate of the Mysterious Female is that through which even heaven and earth enter and exit. From its own root, from its own origin, existing since antiquity—it endures like the transmission of fire, yet its end is unknown. To generate generation without being generated, to transform transformation without being transformed, moving yet ever emerging—what labor is there in this? Thus, that which generates things is not generated; that which transforms things is not transformed.

*Zhuangzi* also contains these words. Xiang Xiu’s commentary says: "My generation is not generated by me—thus generation generates itself."

Does that which generates generation possess any thing? Thus, it does not generate. My transformation is not transformed by any thing—thus transformation transforms itself. Does that which transforms transformation possess any thing? It possesses no thing, thus it does not transform. If that which generates things were itself generated, and that which transforms things were itself transformed, then it would be transformed along with things—how would it differ from them? Only by illuminating the ungenerated and untransformed can one become the root of generation and transformation.

Lu says: This spirit is the master of generation, able to generate and transform things, yet no thing can generate or transform it. It generates itself, transforms itself, forms itself, colors itself, knows itself, empowers itself, diminishes itself, and ceases itself—all of its own accord. How could there be a master to preside over it? To speak of generation, transformation, form, color, knowledge, power, diminution, or cessation is mistaken. If there were a mind intent on generation, transformation, form, or color, how could it govern heaven and earth and contain the myriad things, overseeing all living beings without omission?

Lu says: The spirit’s solitary movement is not compelled by any thing. If it were bound by emotion and stagnation, becoming like the generation and transformation of things, this would not be the Dao.

*Zhenghe* says: The movement of yin and yang, the progression of the four seasons, the principles of the myriad things—suddenly they exist, suddenly they vanish. Form is real, color is manifest, knowledge and strategy arise, power is exerted, waxing and waning follow one another, ending only to begin again. Though nothing moves without change, and no time passes without shift, all abide in the Dao, yet their ceaseless turning is of their own accord.

Fan says: The things of the world are born from being, and being is born from non-being—thus no thing fails to be generated. Hidden transforms into manifest, manifest transforms into hidden—thus no thing fails to be transformed. Only the ungenerated and untransformed can serve as the source of generation and transformation. Thus, filling the space between heaven and earth, the living grow of themselves, the transforming succeed of themselves. Form divides from the Great Beginning, color emerges from the Great Simplicity. Knowledge varies in greatness, power in strength; some diminish and diminish, some cease and cease—all of their own accord. Who is it that acts upon them? Once there is intent in the mind, this is called not-Dao.

Master Liezi says: Of old, the sages relied on yin and yang to unify heaven and earth. Heaven and earth refer to the totality of forms; yin and yang clarify their measures and governing principles.

Lu says: All things with form have that by which they are generated and moved. The greatest of these is heaven and earth; that which moves heaven and earth is yin and yang. Yin and yang are the transformations of qi, without substance or form. Heaven and earth rely on them to manifest life and death. Yin and yang are easily distinguished, but the spirit’s discernment is difficult to fathom. Borrowing one to illustrate the other serves as an analogy. Only then can one know that the spirit governs form, and that being does not give rise to being.

Fan says: To "unify things" means to bind them in relation; that which is unified permeates them. Heaven moves above, earth rests below, and the sage stands between them. How could this suffice to unify them? Yet here lies the Dao: above, it reaches heaven; below, it coils through earth. It completes, assists, encompasses, and shapes—nothing lies beyond it. Thus, by relying on yin and yang to unify them, heaven and earth, vast as they are, will not exceed the bounds of one’s measure.

Yangzi says: Exalting heaven, embracing earth, distinguishing groups, pairing things, ensuring they do not lose their unity—nothing is better than the ruler. That which has form is born from the formless. To call it "generated" is not to say it is not non-being; to call it "non-being" is not to say it does not generate. Thus, the non-mutual generation of being and non-being is a principle that stands as such. Then from what does being arise? Suddenly, it generates itself. Suddenly, it generates itself, yet knows not how it generates; not knowing how it generates, generation is fundamentally identical to non-being. Fundamentally identical to non-being, yet not non-being—this clarifies how the formed takes form of itself, without the formless shaping it. Then how do heaven and earth come to be? If heaven and earth have no source from which to be generated, they generate naturally.

Lu says: Heaven and earth are the greatest of forms. Yin and yang are not spirit or discernment. If that which has form is born from the formless, do heaven and earth possess spirit, discernment, mind, or nature? If they are formless, from what do they arise? This hypothetical question will clarify that the myriad things possess generation.

Fan says: Heaven and earth are the smallest things in the void, the greatest within being. Thus, they are confined within form alongside the myriad things. Tracing their origin, there must be that which existed before heaven and earth—what the *Book of Changes* calls the Supreme Ultimate.

Zhuangzi says: The luminous is born from the obscure; order is born from the formless. Thus, there is the Great Simplicity, the Great Beginning, the Great Inception, and the Great Simplicity. This clarifies how things progress from the subtle to the manifest, how transformations follow one another in succession.

Fan says: It has no body, no number, and is obscured by qi. Before form and substance separated, it was called the Great Simplicity. As for the Great Beginning, it had already begun to manifest in qi; as for the Great Inception, it had already divided into form; as for the Great Simplicity, it had already become manifest in substance. How could there be no origin to trace? The knife is what tailors the garment, yet when the knife is first used, there is no garment—this is merely the garment’s beginning. Thus, at the beginning of qi, it is named the Great Beginning. Only after the beginning is there an inception. The woman receives inception and gives birth; the terrace is begun and completed. That which gives birth is on the left, that which completes is on the right. Thus, at the beginning of form, it is named the Great Inception. Before it receives dye, it is unadulterated. Once dyed, it remains pure and does not contend with things. Thus, at the beginning of substance, it is named the Great Simplicity. These four progress from the subtle to the manifest, having already departed from non-being. Thus, they are spoken of in terms of being. The Great Simplicity has not yet revealed qi.

The term *yi* (易) signifies that which is inexhaustible and unobstructed. When it congeals in stillness within the realm of the Great Void, what can be perceived? This is akin to the *Taiji* of the *Yi*’s *Xici* or the "undifferentiated unity" of the Old Master. Fan states: There is yang qi, there is yin qi, and there is the harmonizing qi—all arise without movement. Before the Great *Yi*, qi has not yet appeared, let alone form and substance.

The Great Beginning (*Taichu*) is the inception of qi. Before yin and yang are distinguished, this is what the following phrase calls "undifferentiated unity." Fan states: In the Great Beginning, there is being and non-being, yet no name exists; it dwells in the indistinct and nebulous, transforming to give rise to qi. Thus, the Great Beginning is the inception of qi.

The Great Inception (*Taishi*) is the inception of form. Once yin and yang are distinguished, the myriad things flow into form. Fan states: The *Yi* says, "Qian knows the Great Inception." That which has an inception also has what precedes inception. What has an inception is called the Great Inception. Only when there is no inception of inception does the formation of all forms depend upon this as their origin.

The Great Simplicity (*Taisu*) is the inception of substance. Substance is nature. Once a thing exists, it possesses square or round, hard or soft, still or restless, sinking or floating—each with its own nature. Fan states: With qi and form, substance is complete; the manifestation of all colors arises from this.

When qi, form, and substance are present yet not yet separated, this passage discusses only qi, form, and substance, no longer mentioning the Great *Yi*. The Great *Yi* is the primordial origin of these three, which will be clarified in later passages. Fan states: The primordial qi of the *Taiji* contains the three as one. Thus, when qi, form, and substance are present yet not separated, it is called "undifferentiated unity." This is what the *Laozi* refers to as "the undifferentiated." Though appearances, images, sounds, and colors differ in ten thousand ways, all are contained within this. Hence, it is called "undifferentiated unity." Undifferentiated unity means the myriad things are intermingled yet not separated.

Though it is a single, undivided qi, the Way of the Three Powers (Heaven, Earth, and Humanity) is already latent within it. *Lun* is an auxiliary particle. It cannot be seen, cannot be heard, cannot be grasped—hence, it is called *yi*. *Yi* has no form or boundary. (The following character is unclear.) The *Laozi* says, "That which cannot be seen is called *xi*." Here, it is called *yi*, another name for *xi* in its simplicity.

The meaning of the Great *Yi* is thus: it serves as the primordial lord of the myriad transformations, obscurely one and unchanging. Fan states: Within undifferentiated unity, the three cannot be scrutinized. That which is colored is manifest, yet the source of color has never appeared—thus, it cannot be seen. That which is sounded is audible, yet the source of sound has never arisen—thus, it cannot be heard. That which is formed is tangible, yet the source of form has never existed—thus, it cannot be grasped. Such is this: I know not its name, so I style it *yi*.

*Yi* transforms into One. The so-called *yi* is profound and indistinct, beyond transformation. Qi relies upon it to transform, so it is provisionally named "transformation." One transforms into seven, seven transforms into nine. Nine transformations are the ultimate; the ultimate is the limit. The transformation of one into seven and nine does not follow sequential numbers but fully encompasses the yang numbers to summarize their convergence. It then transforms back into One. One is the inception of form’s transformation. Once it enters the realm of form, principle and number unfold from one to nine. When the nine numbers are exhausted, it returns to One. Returning to One is a reversion to the inception of form’s transformation, illustrating that transformation and change are ceaseless and boundless.

Fan states: The great image has no form—who can distinguish high from low? Descending into numerical reckoning, transformation arises from this. Thus, *yi* transforms into One—this is what is meant by "the Dao gives birth to One." The arising of One has what precedes form. Though it enters into numbers, it remains close to the Dao. Yet once it is called One, can it remain without transformation? Once it is named One, can it avoid transformation? Thus, seven and nine arise from the division and transformation of One, ensuring its inexhaustibility. Seven is the number of young yang; nine is the number of old yang. When numbers reach their limit, they are exhausted. Thus, nine transformations are the ultimate. When exhausted, they transform; when transformed, they flow. Thus, nine returns to One. One is the inception of form’s transformation. Beginning and end revert in an endless cycle, like a ring without seam. From this point onward, even the most skillful reckoning cannot calculate it.

That which is clear and light ascends to become Heaven; that which is turbid and heavy descends to become Earth. What are Heaven and Earth? They are merely the self-distinction of emptiness and fullness, clarity and turbidity. This entire chapter is from the *Qian Zaodu* of the *Zhou Yi*.

Fan states: Once undifferentiated unity is distinguished, the Three Powers commence their division. Heaven arches above, Earth sprawls below, and humanity dwells dazedly in between. Heaven is but accumulated qi—clear and light, belonging to yang. Earth is but accumulated mass—turbid and heavy, belonging to yin. Humanity receives the mean of Heaven and Earth to live, thus bearing yin and embracing yang, harmonizing with the harmonizing qi. The harmonizing qi gives rise to humanity. Thus, Heaven and Earth contain essence, and the myriad things transform and are born.

Extending this reasoning, yin and yang qi intermingle and harmonize; when qi harmonizes, humanity is born. Once humanity is born, it has something upon which to rely and stand. Lu states: One, three, five, seven, nine—these are the numbers of yang. When they reach their limit, they return to One, circulating endlessly. The *Yi* says: "That which is rooted in Heaven inclines upward; that which is rooted in Earth inclines downward." That which inclines downward is the category of grasses and trees; that which inclines upward is the category of sentient beings. Thus, animals possess spirit, while plants lack consciousness.

Those without consciousness are transformed by qi; those with spirit are moved by consciousness. Thus, from *Taiyi* and *Dahuo* to the primordial chaos of *Hunlun*, this speaks of the gradual transformation of qi. Within it, the most refined and pure is called "spirit"; those whose spirit and qi are subtle become the worthy and the sagely, while those whose spirit and qi are turbid become the common and the foolish. Extending to all living things, their distinctions are manifold.

*Zhenghe* states: Yin and yang are the greatest of qi; Heaven and Earth are the greatest of forms. When qi transforms and takes form, then there are yin and yang, and thereafter Heaven and Earth. The Dao/Way is their universal principle; the sage is the steward of the Dao/Way. This is how the sage governs Heaven and Earth through yin and yang.

The *Yi* speaks of the Supreme Polarity, which gives rise to the Two Modes. This is what *Zhuangzi* refers to when saying the Dao/Way exists prior to the Supreme Polarity. Thus, *Taiyi* is the state before qi is manifest, dwelling in the realm of boundless obscurity. When it transforms and qi arises, this is *Dahuo*, the beginning of qi. When qi transforms and takes form, this is *Taishi*, the beginning of form. When form differentiates and substance emerges, this is *Taisu*, the beginning of substance. When qi, form, and substance are complete yet not yet separated, the full embodiment of the Dao/Way resides therein. Thus, it is said: *Hunlun* is what Laozi refers to as "the thing that is undifferentiated and complete."

With nothing to employ its clarity, it cannot be seen; with nothing to apply its hearing, it cannot be heard.

With nothing to gather its strength, it cannot be grasped. These three—seeing, hearing, and grasping—cannot be pursued through inquiry; thus, they merge into one. Yet once called "one," how could there be no discourse on it? This is why it is forcibly named *Yi* (Change).

*Yi* has no fixed form or boundary, for it has no body. When *Yi* is compared to yang, it is the origin of the One. Thus, it transforms into the One. Numbers arise from the One, so it transforms into seven—coiled yet not extended. Seven transforms into nine—intertwined and undergoing change. When numbers reach their limit at nine, they revert to the One. The One is the beginning of all transformations of form, and heaven, earth, and humanity all derive their existence from it. Thus, it is said: the pure and light ascends to become heaven; the turbid and heavy descends to become earth; the harmonious and balanced becomes humanity.

Essence is the One. The One gives rise to the Two, the Two to the Three, and the Three to the myriad things. Thus, heaven and earth contain essence, and the myriad things are transformed and born.

Master Fan says: When yin and yang concentrate their essence, they become heaven and earth; when their essence scatters, they become the myriad things. Heaven and earth are the above and below of the myriad things. Things and heaven-earth have no inherent priority; when the sequence of the Great Dao is made clear, there is heaven and earth, and then the myriad things are born. Thus, the *Yijing* says: "Heaven and earth interact, and the myriad things are transformed and born."

Master Liezi says: Heaven and earth have no complete functions, the sage has no complete abilities, and the myriad things have no complete uses. "Complete" here means "exhaustive." Master Fan says: The great wholeness of the Dao is divided between above and below—this is how the positions of heaven and earth are established. That which fulfills the capacities of heaven and earth is the sage; that which fills the space between heaven and earth is the myriad things. Their functions of covering and supporting, their complementary abilities, and their diverse uses have never not relied upon one another—how could any possess the great wholeness in full?

Thus, heaven’s duty is to give life and cover; earth’s duty is to shape and bear; the sage’s duty is to teach and transform; and things’ duty is to fulfill what is fitting. "Duty" means "to preside over." Each life has its nature, and each nature has what is fitting for it. Master Fan says: Those with duties should heed their superiors.

Therefore, when the Three Powers are established in their positions and the myriad things are scattered in their diversity, all have their constant duties. Yet when one ascends beyond form, observes heaven and earth, and looks down upon the myriad things without sharing the sage’s concerns, what cannot be encompassed by sound has no part in it. Indeed, what duty could it possibly receive?

Thus, heaven has its shortcomings, earth has its strengths, the sage has its limitations, and things have their penetrations. That which is suited to one direction will be obstructed when applied elsewhere. Wang Bi says: Form must have its divisions, and sound must have its categories. For example, warmth cannot be cool, and the *gong* note cannot be the *shang* note.

Master Fan says: When the Three Powers are complete and the myriad things are divided, their uses have never not relied upon one another. Thus, what has shortcomings has strengths, and what has penetrations has limitations. Why? That which gives life and covers cannot shape and bear; that which shapes and bears cannot teach and transform; that which teaches and transforms cannot violate what is fitting. Compliance leads to penetration. What is fittingly determined does not exceed its position. All have their inherent allotments, which cannot be reversed.

Master Fan says: Heaven arches high and is strong and vigorous, covering all without fail, yet it cannot necessarily shape and bear. Earth is heavy and still, sustaining all without fail, yet it cannot necessarily teach and transform. The sage stands between them, observing above and below, participating with heaven and earth. Teaching is established by the self, and transformation is enacted by the self—only thus can one assist in heaven and earth’s transforming and nourishing. Yet how could the function of teaching and transformation violate what is fitting for things? Things have no constant fittingness; what is fitting lies in following the moment. I simply comply with their nature without disturbing their established patterns, allowing the myriad things to be born, each attaining what is fitting for it.

Thus, teaching emerges from non-speech, and transformation is accomplished through non-governance. Not violating what is fitting for things—this is how one assists and harmonizes with what is fitting for heaven and earth. Therefore, the Dao of heaven and earth is either yin or yang; the sage’s teaching is either benevolence or righteousness; and what is fitting for the myriad things is either softness or hardness. These all follow what is fitting and cannot exceed their positions.

The square and the round, the still and the active—principles cannot be combined. Yet when examining form and addressing affairs, names and divisions cannot encroach upon one another. In the coherence of principle, the square and the round need not conflict. Thus, the virtues of the Two Modes, the Dao of the sage, covering and nourishing the multitude of beings, and permeating the myriad things with their blessings—all attain their purity, tranquility, and true essence. Then, diverse paths merge, movement and stillness become one, for the sage does not violate the natures of the myriad things, and the myriad things do not transgress the sage’s transformation.

Whatever is fixated on one direction is obstructed by the divisions of form. The operation of the Dao is always profoundly penetrating and without dependence. Master Lu says: That which is moved by qi can cover and bear; that which is moved by spirit can teach and transform. Thus, heaven and earth give life to the myriad things, and the sage follows their forms and employs them.

The *Zhenghe* says: Heaven is positioned above, earth below, and the sage between heaven and earth—all to bring about transformation. As transformations alternate and arise, the myriad things have their differing fittingness. How could heaven and earth, along with the sage, violate what is fitting for them?

Thus, the sage’s relation to heaven and earth: when distinguished, they are the Three Extremes; when interconnected, they are the Three Powers. That which gives life and covers cannot shape and bear; that which shapes and bears cannot teach and transform; that which teaches and transforms cannot determine what is fitting. What is fittingly determined does not exceed its position. This speaks of the divisions of duty.

Therefore, in terms of their distinctions: the sage’s Dao reaches up to heaven and coils down to earth, transforming and nourishing the myriad things, yet it cannot be represented by images. Then, above and below flow together without separation—how could there be the mutual manifestation of strengths and shortcomings, or the mutual divergence of penetration and obstruction?

Master Fan says: Heaven has yin and yang, and earth has yin and yang. Thus, in the Dao of heaven and earth, yin and yang must value their interaction.

Without transformation, there is no life; without righteousness, there is no completion. Thus, in the sage’s teaching, benevolence and righteousness must value their mutual fulfillment. Movement and stillness have their constancy, and hardness and softness are thereby determined. Therefore, in the fittingness of the myriad things, hardness and softness must value their mutual blending.

Yet heaven and earth embody the Dao, and so they possess the power to cover and bear. The myriad things depend on them for life, yet they never retain the Dao. The sage embodies the Dao and assumes the duty of teaching and transformation, so things depend on him for completion, yet he never harbors intention. Both simply follow what is fitting for things and do not exceed their positions.

Why should the roc and the quail mock each other for their size? Why should Kui and the centipede pity each other for their many or few legs? One does not bathe the swan to blacken the crow, nor spin the duck’s down to sever the crane’s legs—each abides by its natural state and is left to spontaneity.

Thus, there are those that are born, and those that give birth; there are those that have form, and those that give form; there are those that have sound, and those that give sound; there are those that have color, and those that give color; there are those that have flavor, and those that give flavor. Form, sound, color, and flavor all arise in an instant yet cannot generate themselves. That which cannot generate itself is the root of non-action. The root of non-action is not bound to a single image, nor tied to a single flavor, and so it can serve as the master of form and qi, the source from which all movement proceeds.

Lu says: The beginning of form is called life; that which gives life to this life is called formative spirit. That which forms its form, sounds its sound, colors its color, and flavors its flavor—all are the work of spirit, using non-being to govern being. What is born of life dies, yet that which gives life never ends. What is formed of form is substantial, yet that which gives form never exists. What is sounded of sound is heard, yet that which gives sound never emits. What is colored of color is manifest, yet that which gives color never appears. What is flavored of flavor is tasted, yet that which gives flavor never presents itself.

All that is confined to a single form follows the cycle of generation and decay, and so what is born must end, while that which gives life to things knows no change. These are all the duties of non-action. The utterly non-existent is thus able to serve as the ancestral master of the myriad transformations.

Lu says: What spirit employs must have a beginning and an end. Form, sound, color, and flavor are not self-differentiating. Therefore, the hidden operation is the supreme work of spirit—non-action yet nothing left undone.

*Zhenghe* states: The arising of form, sound, color, and flavor are all transformations of things, so the hidden departs as the manifest arrives. Yet that which returns—what gives life, what gives form, what gives sound, what gives color, what gives flavor—are all the subtleties of the Dao. Who can trace their origin? Who can grasp their end? The Dao is constantly non-active yet leaves nothing undone—this is what is meant.

Fan says: The solitary does not generate; what does not generate can generate life. Thus, form, sound, color, and flavor all arise in dependence. Yet within the great void, when things attain the principle of life, their forms naturally appear. The great mountain and the autumn down—from where do they derive their form? Only the great image without form can give form to forms. The myriad winds blow differently, yet sounds naturally respond. Thunder’s roar and the midge’s flight—from where do they arise? Only the great sound without sound can give sound to sounds.

After movement ceases, color arises, yet it cannot color itself. Ornamentation without color is the color-giver of the active. After things are formed, flavor arises, yet it cannot flavor itself. Blandness without flavor is the flavor-giver of the active. It gives form yet has no form itself, so like a mirror’s stillness, beauty and ugliness are fully revealed, yet the mirror has no form—how could it share existence with what it reflects? It gives sound yet has no sound itself, so like an echoing valley, good and evil all respond, yet the valley has no sound—how could it share emission with what it echoes?

What is colored of color is manifest, so if one probes its root, traces its end, follows its hues, reverses its numbers, and seeks its limit, though colors differ, that which gives color never appears. What is flavored of flavor is tasted, so if one responds to saltiness, acts upon sourness, transforms through bitterness, exhausts sweetness, and changes through pungency, though flavors differ, that which gives flavor never presents itself. Then can the subtlety of life-giving be bound to perish with what is born of life? If not for that which is non-active yet leaves nothing undone, who could assume this duty? Thus it is said: These are all the duties of non-action.

It can be yin, it can be yang; it can be soft, it can be hard; it can be short, it can be long; it can be round, it can be square; it can give life, it can bring death; it can be hot, it can be cool; it can float, it can sink; it can be gong, it can be shang; it can emerge, it can submerge; it can be dark, it can be yellow; it can be sweet, it can be bitter; it can be rank, it can be fragrant. It has no knowledge, no ability, yet there is nothing it does not know, nothing it cannot do. When knowledge is exhausted, there is no knowledge; when ability is perfected, there is no ability—thus there is nothing it does not know, nothing it cannot do.

He Yan’s *Discourse on the Dao* says: Being as being relies on non-being to arise; affairs without affairs are accomplished through non-being. The Dao acts without speech, is named without a name, is seen without form, is heard without sound—this is the Dao in its entirety. Thus it can illuminate sounds and emit qi and things, envelop form and spirit and manifest light and shadow. Through it, the dark becomes black, the plain becomes white, the carpenter’s square becomes square, the compass becomes round. Round and square attain form, yet this has no form; white and black attain names, yet this has no name.

Lu says: The *Laozi* states: "I do not know whose child it is—it seems to have been before the Lord of Heaven." This is spirit, prior to heaven and earth, spirit of spirits and lord of lords—none can know it, none can verify it.

If one can embody and realize this Dao, then within heaven and earth there is nothing one does not know and nothing one cannot do.

Fan says: That which is hidden without form and profound beyond measure is called *yin*; that which exquisitely crafts heaven’s work and illuminates the myriad things is called *yang*. To be able to embrace both *yin* and *yang* is to transcend what *yin* and *yang* themselves can fathom.

To establish the root through bending and adapt to the times through flexibility—this is called *softness*. To embody solidity as one’s nature and employ decisiveness as one’s function—this is called *firmness*. To be able to be both soft and firm is to transcend what softness and firmness themselves can define.

Length and shortness take shape in relation to one another, as do the foot and inch—yet the Dao can be both short and long. Roundness and squareness are refined through one another, as are the compass and square—yet the Dao can be both round and square.

To be able to give life and bring death is to stand beyond fate’s reckoning. To be able to bring heat and coolness is to stand beyond time’s confinement.

When things are in water, the sinking cannot float, and the floating cannot sink—yet that which can both sink and float is like sunlight upon the water.

When things produce sound, striking *gong* moves *gong*, and striking *shang* resonates *shang*—yet that which can be both *gong* and *shang* is like the spontaneous music of heaven’s breath.

That which emerges from mechanism swiftly enters mechanism; that which emerges from darkness swiftly enters darkness. Only that which does not turn with mechanism or darkness can thus emerge and submerge.

In heaven, the profound is small, yet it is the subtlety of the Dao; on earth, yellow is central, yet it is the radiance of color. Only that which is not confined by heaven and earth can thus be both profound and yellow.

To be able to be sweet and bitter is to rest in the blandness of flavorlessness; to be able to be pungent and fragrant is to abide in the indifference of odorlessness. Such is the Dao’s non-action that is yet non-non-action—thus it is without knowledge.

Encompassing the myriad things without omission, it is without not-knowing and without not-able; carving all forms without being crafty, it is without not-able.

*Zhenghe* says: To have some knowledge and some ability is but a partial aspect of the Dao, not its full and complete realization. Yet to be without knowledge and yet without not-knowing, to be without ability and yet without not-able—this is to be all-encompassing, all-pervading. What then could one not do?

*Yin* and *yang* are *qi*; softness and firmness are materials; shortness and length are forms; roundness and squareness are implements; life and death are fate; heat and coolness are seasons; floating and sinking are conditions; *gong* and *shang* are sounds; emergence and submergence are traces; profoundness and yellow are colors; sweetness and bitterness are flavors; pungency and fragrance are scents. All transformations and creations arise from these. Is not such a person complete?

The six penetrations* and four openings*, the small and the great, the refined and the coarse—all their movements are present everywhere, whither could one go and not find them at leisure?

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue in Four Explanations, Scroll One* ends here.
*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue in Four Explanations, Scroll Two: Heavenly Portents*

When Master Liezi journeyed to Wei, he took a meal by the roadside. His followers noticed a century-old skull, overgrown with brambles, and pointing to it, he turned to his disciple Baifeng, saying: "Only he and I know that we have never been born and never died. Both of us tread the path of transformation—thus I am born while he is dead. Yet if we push this to its ultimate realm, in principle there is neither birth nor death."

Lu* said: "In form there is birth and death, but in spirit there is neither death nor birth. I abide as spirit remains, while he departs as spirit departs. Though the skull and I differ in birth and death, if one awakens to the spirit, there has never been birth or death. Is this an excess of nurture? An excess of joy? When one encounters form, one cannot but nurture it; when one meets life, one cannot but rejoice. These are the errors of the deluded, not the true reality of principle."

Lu said: "Once one receives form, joy and nurture lose their proper measure, leading only to death."

*On the Seeds of Transformation:*
First, we inquire into the number of kinds of transformation—how many are there?—before clarifying them below.

The *yuan* becomes a quail; this is recorded in the *Mozi*. When it obtains water, it becomes a *geng*; at the border of water and earth, it becomes the clothing of frogs and toads. "Clothing" here means covering. Born in high and pure places—*lingtun* refers to lofty, clean ground—it becomes *lingxi*. This is transformation according to the place of birth.

The *lingxi* obtains *yuqi* and becomes *wuzu*. This is mutual generation through combination. The root of *wuzu* becomes a grub, and its leaves become butterflies. "Root" refers to the origin, "leaves" to the scattered parts—thus *wuzu* is the origin of the grub, and its dispersed parts transform into butterflies.

Butterflies, in turn, all transform—*xu* means "all," indicating that all things undergo change—and become insects, born beneath the stove. Their form resembles a cast-off skin, and their name is *yiduo*. This is transformation within a single form.

After a thousand days, *yiduo* dies and transforms into a bird called *qianyugu*. The foam of *qianyugu* becomes *simi*. "Foam" here refers to the essence that arises. *Simi* becomes *shixi yilu*, *shixi yilu* is born from *shixi huangxu*, *shixi huangxu* from *jiuyou*, *jiuyou* from *maorui*, and *maorui* from *fuxuan*. These are all cases of a single form dying and being reborn.

Sheep’s liver transforms into *digao*, horse’s blood into *zhuanlin*, and human blood into *wildfire*. These are all spontaneous transformations within a single form.

The sparrowhawk becomes a *zhan*, the *zhan* becomes a cuckoo, and the cuckoo in time reverts to a sparrowhawk. The swallow becomes a clam, the field mouse becomes a quail, the rotten melon becomes a fish, old leeks become amaranth, the aged ram becomes a *yuan* (a ram is a male sheep), and fish roe becomes insects. These are all natural transformations without external cause.

The beast of *Danyuan* conceives by itself and is called *lei*. *Dan* is pronounced *shi*. The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: "On Mount Danyuan there is a beast resembling a wildcat with fur, named *lei*, which reproduces as both male and female."

The birds of rivers and marshes conceive by gazing and are called *yi*. This is birth through mutual gaze. *Zhuangzi* says: "White *yi* birds gaze at one another, their pupils unmoving, and the wind transforms them."

The purely female is called *dayao*, and the purely male is called *zhifeng*. *Dayao* refers to creatures like turtles and soft-shelled turtles. *Zhi* means "small." These transform without male or female.

Having discussed the principles of insects and beasts, we now clarify that the Way of humans is likewise.

A longing scholar remains unwed yet becomes moved; a longing maiden remains husbandless yet conceives. The *Classic of the Great Wilderness* states: "There is a country of Thoughtful Seclusion, where longing scholars take no wives, and longing maidens take no husbands." Through the subtle resonance of essence and qi, they bear children without intercourse. This is akin to the white *yi* birds.

Hou Ji was born from a giant footprint. Records say: "Jiang Yuan, consort of Emperor Gaoxin, saw a giant footprint and, admiring it, trod upon it. As if moved by some principle, she conceived and gave birth to Hou Ji. Grown wise, he became an aide to Yao and was the ancestor of the Zhou."

Yi Yin was born from an empty mulberry tree. Records say: "Yi Yin’s mother lived by the Yi River. After conceiving, she dreamed a spirit told her: 'When mortar water flows out, flee eastward and do not look back.' The next day, she saw mortar water flowing out and told her neighbors. Fleeing ten *li* east, she looked back to see her village submerged. Her body transformed into an empty mulberry tree. A girl of the Youxin clan, gathering mulberries, found an infant within the hollow tree and named him Yi Yin, presenting him to her lord. Ordered to be raised by a cook, he grew wise and became Prime Minister to Tang of Yin."

Jue Zhao was born from moisture—this is birth through steam and dampness. Vinegar flies are born from wine—this is birth through sour qi. The sheep’s millet compares to the *bugun*—this is affinity between different kinds.

Old bamboo gives birth to *qingning*, born in forests and marshes. *Qingning* gives birth to *cheng*. From *geng* to *cheng*, all are living things—snakes, birds, insects, and beasts—showing their ceaseless transformations: some change through form, some die and are reborn, beginning and end following one another endlessly.

*Cheng* gives birth to horses, horses to humans, and humans in time enter the mechanism. All things emerge from the mechanism, all things return to the mechanism. How can the transformations of life and death be fathomed?

Born here, one may die there; dying there, one may be born here. Yet the master of form’s arising has never for a moment been absent. Thus the sage knows that life is not eternally preserved, death is not forever extinguished, and the transformations of a single qi take on ten thousand forms.

The ten thousand forms undergo ten thousand transformations, yet that which does not transform abides in the unchanging—this is called the *mechanism*. The mechanism is the origin of all beings, the source to which all movement traces back. Thus, from non-being it issues forth into being, from being it scatters back into non-being—nothing does not proceed from it.

Lu asks: Are there kinds among seeds? Or do they mutually give rise to one another? This question is posed to clarify that where the spirit goes, there is life; where the spirit departs, there is death. Form has no constant master, spirit no constant shape. The spirit has no fixed term, while form congeals—once it receives the substance of being, it is bound as if by the mechanism’s constraints. To live is to emerge; to die is to return.

In the *Zhenghe* edition of the *Book of Changes*, it is said: "Trace beginnings back to their end, and thus know the discourse on death and life." For where there is life, there must be death, yet those who die here may not fail to be born there. One who grasps this knows only the present and the other—never having been born, never having died. Now living, now dying; now dying, now living—thus one nurtures form and delights in life. What exists now is but what once passed away. Hence it is said: Is this nurturing a passing? Is this delight a passing? As the ten thousand things succeed one another in different forms, the transformations of life and death are beyond reckoning. Thus it is asked: How many kinds are there? What follows is but what the eyes and ears can perceive.

That frogs become quails speaks to the transformations of the ten thousand things between rivers and lands. *Xun*, the cast-off skin of frogs and toads, and *lingxi* are of one kind. Some, finding water or the border of water and earth, are born below; others, finding high ground, are born above—this shows how the ten thousand things transform according to the forms and qi they encounter. *Lingxi* that obtains dense shade becomes *wuzu*; thus it borrows another thing as its body. The root of *wuzu* becomes a grub, its leaves become butterflies—thus the same body scatters into different things. Butterflies, called *xu*, transform into insects, born beneath the stove, their form like a cast-off shell, their name *yiduo*. Here, winged creatures transform into wriggling ones. After a thousand days, *yiduo* transforms into a bird called *ganyugu*—here, burrowing creatures transform into forest-dwellers. Some shift through form, as when *simi* becomes *yilu*. Others arise from origins like the yellow *huangkuang* born from rotten *guanxuan*, or the transformations of *tianlu*, *zhuanzhen*, and wildfire. Some reverse through nature, as when the sparrow hawk becomes the goshawk, the goshawk the cuckoo, and the cuckoo reverts to the sparrow hawk. Swallows become clams, field mice become quails, rotting claws become fish, old leeks become amaranth, aged rams become apes, and fish eggs become insects—some follow their kind, others form kinds from what is unlike. The beast *danyuan* conceives itself and gives birth to its kind without external stimulus. The river marsh bird *yi* is born from gazing—without mating. The purely female is called *dayao*, the purely male *zhifeng*—thus among things, there are those that generate themselves through a single yin or yang.
