The longing scholar remains unwed yet stirs, the yearning maiden remains husbandless yet conceives—thus among humans, there are those who connect subtly beyond yin and yang. Even the birth of Hou Ji from a giant footprint, or Yi Yin from the hollow mulberry, though beyond inquiry, are not to be marveled at. *Juezhao* is born from dampness—transformed by qi; *xiji* is born from wine—transformed by flavor; *yangxi* pairs with *bugou*—transformed by habit; old bamboo gives rise to *qingning*—from the insentient arises the sentient; *qingning* gives birth to *cheng*—from the unknowing arises the knowing. The *Shizi* takes *cheng* as a kind of leopard; *cheng* gives birth to horses—thus like gives rise to like; horses give birth to humans—thus unlike gives rise to unlike. Yet the comings and goings of insects, the life and death of plants and trees, are transformations without end—how can one know the order of life and death? Only this: the ten thousand things have their origins in sprouting, their return in dissolution. The sage, knowing this, understands they are bound by the mechanism’s constraints and cannot cease.

Fan says: The Dao has no beginning or end, but things have life and death, smelted in the great transformation’s crucible. When qi gathers and forms arise, we forcibly call it life; when the mechanism of creation turns and something is left behind, qi scatters and form decays—we forcibly call it death. The gathering and scattering of qi are but the coming and going of floating clouds; the presence and absence of form are but the rising and vanishing of a bubble. The names of life and death stand in opposition: now dead, now alive, as a dream ends and waking comes; now alive, now dead, as waking ends and dreaming comes. Who knows the reason for this? Only those who trace beginnings back to their end know that one has never died, never been born—coming without source, going without destination, nearly entering the realm of neither death nor life. A skull a hundred years old is but the remnant of decayed bone—what does it know? Yet *Zhuangzi* records its words: "How could I abandon the joy of a king facing south to return to the toils of the human world?" Thus it is clear: it did not delight in life and loathed death. The benighted of the world cling to a single body and wish, fixated on the ten thousand transformations. Born, they transform into death and grieve; thus is this nurturing a passing? For they fear death. Dead, they transform into life and rejoice; thus is this delight a passing? For they delight in life. Yet what is there truly to fear in death, or to delight in in life? These are but the ten thousand transformations, endless and unbounded. Moreover, as the ten thousand things succeed one another, their names differ—quails, *xun*, the cast-off skin of frogs and toads, *lingxi*—these are one kind, yet some find water, some the border of water and earth, some high ground, and their births differ accordingly.

The mole cricket, the grub, the butterfly—they and the *lingxi* are also of one kind, yet some dwell in dense thickets, some on leaves, some on roots, and their transformations differ accordingly. The *ciruo*, the dried bones, the *simi*, the vinegar gnat—they and the *xu* are also of one kind, yet some thrive beneath the stove, some after a thousand days, some in their own foam, and their births differ accordingly.

The vinegar gnat is born of vinegar; the yellow *huang* feeds on vinegar, the yellow *huang* is born of the *jiuyou*, the *jiuyou* of the *maorui*, the *maorui* of the rotten *quan*—thus their kind is unknown, arising naturally. The sheep’s liver transforms into the *digao*, the horse’s blood into the *zhuanlin*, human blood into wildfire, the sparrow hawk into the *zhan*, the *zhan* into the cuckoo; the swallow into the clam, the field mouse into the quail, rotting claws into fish, old leeks into amaranth, aged rams into apes, fish eggs into insects—thus their kind is unknown, transforming naturally.

There are those conceived of themselves, like the beast *danyuan*; those born by gazing at one another, like the birds of rivers and marshes; the *dayao* kind, purely female without males; the *zhifeng* kind, purely male without females. By the longing of the scholar, one may conceive without a wife; by the longing of the maiden, one may conceive without a husband. As for the *juezhao* and the vinegar fly, they arise from some cause, while the *yangxi* without haste has no cause to compare. The *jiuzhu*, the *qingning*, the *cheng*—these too are born of horses and humans.

Observing thus, some things are born of emotion, some without emotion; some emotion gives rise to the emotionless, some the emotionless to the emotional. Some are born without cause, some without stimulus—ten thousand forms, ten thousand transformations, without end or limit. Could it be that there is a mechanism, an inevitability? When function emerges, it bursts forth vigorously—all that emerges does so from this mechanism. When function retreats, it flows smoothly away—all that retreats does so into this mechanism. Though myriad in difference, born and dying, none know their origin. Yet in the tension of the spiritual mechanism, the movement of the qi mechanism, there is assuredly one that governs, revolves, and controls.

Liezi first discussed the office of non-action, then followed with this—rightly so. The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* states: "When form moves, it does not generate form but shadow; when sound moves, it does not generate sound but echo." Where there is form, there must be shadow; where there is sound, there must be echo. These arise naturally, emerge together, and vanish together—how could there be a sequence of dependence or precedence?

Guo Xiang’s commentary on the *Zhuangzi* elaborates this in detail. Yet世’s speakers claim that when form moves, shadow follows, and when sound issues, echo responds. The sage takes this as a metaphor, clarifying that when things move, they lose their root; when still, they return to it—no longer entangled in the intricate interplay of influence and response. Without movement, the insubstantial does not generate the substantial. The existence of the substantial relies on the insubstantial to arise; to speak of arising is necessarily from the insubstantial, yet the insubstantial does not fail to generate the substantial. This function of circulation and penetration necessarily depends on the insubstantial. Thus the term "arising and moving" is established in accordance with circumstances.

Lu says: "What has form can generate, but cannot generate the formless—shadow and echo are examples. The spirit, though formless, when moved, generates the substantial—all things are examples." Fan says: "The shadow as shadow seems to depend on form, yet in truth depends on nothing. When form moves, shadow naturally follows—shadow does not seek form. The echo as echo seems to depend on sound, yet in truth depends on nothing. When sound moves, echo naturally responds—echo does not seek sound. The principle of being born from the insubstantial is like this."

Form must end. Do heaven and earth end? They end together with me. Measuring great and small, calculating long and short, they differ from me; yet in converging upon an end, the principle is no different. Lu says: "Though great and small differ, they alike come to an end." Fan says: "The end of things is like the end of the year; the end of numbers is like winter. Without things, without time—who knows where they end? Heaven and earth are the greatest of forms; once confined to form, though they may wish not to end, can they avoid it?"

Do they advance toward an end? One cannot know. "Advance" should be "cease." In this text, "cease" is often written as "advance." When things gather, they form; when they scatter, they end—this is the world’s so-called end and beginning. Yet for the gathered, the beginning is solid form, the end is dispersion; for the scattered, the beginning is emptiness, the end is solid form. Thus they alternately serve as each other’s end and beginning, yet in principle there is truly no end and no beginning.

Lu says: "‘Advance’ should be ‘cease’—suppose one asks whether heaven and earth have an end. Or does one not know? The answer follows. The Way ends at what has no beginning, ceases at what is not long-lasting. ‘Long-lasting’ should be ‘existent.’ Without beginning, it does not end; without existence, it does not cease." Fan says: "Without things, without time—who can speak of end and beginning? Without days, without years—who can speak of long and short? To say the Way ends—how could that be? It is simply unknown."

The "no beginning" it speaks of is what *Zhuangzi* calls "never yet having had a beginning." Can the Way truly be said to cease? It simply remains unknown. The "not long-lasting" it speaks of is what *Zhuangzi* calls "existing before heaven and earth yet not being long-lasting." Where there is birth, there is a return to non-birth; where there is form, there is a return to formlessness. The born revert to their end, the formed revert to emptiness—such is the natural principle.

Lu says: "All things with a beginning and an end originate from what has no beginning and return to what does not exist. If we speak from the perspective of *Tai Chu* (Great Beginning) and *Hun Dun* (Primal Chaos), this implies a beginning—how then could there be no end? How could there be no cessation? The 'non-born'—this non-born first had birth before reverting to death and extinction. It is not originally non-born; the originally non-born has never had birth or extinction from the outset. The 'formless'—this formless first had form before reverting to dispersion. It is not originally formless; the originally formless has never had aggregation or dispersion from the outset. That which gives birth to life does not itself undergo birth; that which gives form to things has no form. Thus it can give form to the myriad things while its own essence remains unchanged. To say that what is born and formed later returns to non-birth and formlessness is merely the cyclical alternation of existence and extinction—it is not the unchanging nature of beginning and end."

Lu says: "The 'non-birth' of spirit does not mean it was never born. The birth of the myriad things and the formation of all beings are all set in motion by spirit. Because it can give birth, once born, it embodies itself without birth or extinction—it is not that there is no form or birth, but that it is identical to the *qi* of the Great Void."

Fan says: "What is born by birth dies and returns to non-birth. What is formed by form becomes solid and returns to formlessness. The 'non-born' is not originally non-born but arises from having been born and then reverts to this state. How can this be what gives birth without itself being born? The 'formless' is not originally formless but arises from having had form and then reverts to this state. How can this be what gives form without itself having form? Birth is a principle that must end. Ending cannot but end, just as birth cannot but begin. The born does not give birth to itself yet is self-born—though born, it does not know how it is born. Not knowing how it is born, birth cannot be severed; not knowing how it dies, death cannot be resisted. To desire to preserve one’s life and exhaust one’s end is to be deluded by principle."

"Exhaustion" means annihilation.

Lu says: "Things that are born must have an end, just as the harmonious *qi* that sprouts plants and trees cannot but give birth. To wish for eternal life is to be deluded by the supreme principle."

Fan says: "Birth has its origin in sprouting, death has its return in dissolution. Beginning and end revolve without end, and none know where they cease—there is surely a principle governing this. To desire to fear their exhaustion is merely to cling without loss to the cycle of birth and death, unaware and unenlightened. Is this not delusion?

Spirit belongs to heaven’s portion; bones and flesh belong to earth’s portion. What belongs to heaven is clear and disperses; what belongs to earth is turbid and aggregates. When spirit departs from form, each returns to its true nature—heaven’s portion returns to heaven, earth’s portion returns to earth, each reverting to its origin. Thus it is called *gui* (ghost). *Gui* means "return"—returning to its true dwelling. The true dwelling is the realm of the Great Void."

Lu says: "When spirit and brightness depart from form, it is called death. Returning to the true dwelling is reverting to the Great Clarity. To take the Great Clarity as the true dwelling is to clarify that this form and bones are but empty illusion."

Fan says: "Essence is water; spirit is fire. Water and fire combine to generate earth. Thus when a person is born, essence gathers and spirit assembles, and the hundred bones, nine orifices, and six viscera are all thereby established. Spirit belongs to heaven’s portion because it moves without end and is thus clear and disperses. Bones and flesh belong to earth’s portion because they are constant and unchanging and are thus turbid and aggregate. When spirit disperses, each returns to its true nature—this is why it is called *gui*. To elaborate further: when a person is first born, spirit, *hun*, *po*, and *qi* are all complete before form is established. *Hun* is like clouds—it follows spirit without ceasing. *Po* is like whiteness—it governs things and has its limits. The sage uses *hun* to restrain *po*, so spirit does not become enslaved to form. The common person follows *po* with *hun*, so spirit is insufficient to command form. When spirit does not become enslaved to form, even in death there is no difference from life. When spirit is insufficient to command form, even in life there is no difference from death. The death of a worthy person becomes a ghost—though one may say their ghost lacks spirit, it is still distinct from those who sink into dark yin and transform into other beings. *Liezi*’s words, though not reaching the ultimate, provisionally speak of returning to the true dwelling, and thus call it *gui*."

The *Erya* says: "*Gui* means 'return.'" The Yellow Emperor said: "Spirit enters its gate, bones and flesh return to their root—what of me remains? What birth lacks form? What form lacks *qi*? What *qi* lacks numinosity? Thus the mind, wisdom, form, and bones are one body in the yin realm, a partial accumulation of one *qi*. When they depart from form and return to the root, they revert to their true dwelling, and I have no thing."

Lu says: "Ordinary people take form as the self, and through the self, emotions arise. Those with many emotions are deeply mired in love; those with few emotions have meager desires. Only the perfected person has no self and fully comprehends spirit. Ordinary people, not understanding, cling ever more tightly—when spirit returns to the true and form returns to earth, where then is the former self?"

*Zhenghe* says: "Stillness returns to nature; movement departs from the root—such is principle."

Form moves yet does not generate form—it generates shadow. Sound moves yet does not generate sound—it generates echo. Non-movement does not generate non-being—it generates being, and thus one strays far from the root. Non-being generates being; being must return to non-being. Hence it is said: form must end. Heaven and earth are born together with me; when they end, they end together with me. Who knows their limit? Thus to speak of an end is to advance into the unknown.

That which has an end has a beginning; that which endures has a fleeting moment—these pertain only to temporal things. Therefore, to have a beginning is to take the beginningless as the supreme Dao; to end at the root is to reach the ultimate. The beginningless is again the ultimate. That which endures takes the non-enduring as the supreme Dao, advancing toward the root; the non-enduring is again the ultimate.

Why is it that when there is life, it returns to non-life, and when there is form, it returns to formlessness? The non-living is not suddenly non-living; the formless is not originally formless—it is only from the perspective of those with life and form that this appears so. Life is principle’s inevitable end; what ends cannot but end, and what lives cannot but live. Yet to desire life’s eternal persistence, to take the end as a boundary—how can one then comprehend the boundlessness within the cycle? This is the delusion of those who fixate on numbers.

Life is heaven and earth’s entrusted harmony. Spirit is heaven’s portion—thus it is clear and disperses. Bones are earth’s portion—thus they are turbid and congeal. When spirit scatters and returns to its true nature, what remains of me? Yet this is spoken from the perspective of ordinary people.

Thus it is said: spirit enters its gate; bones return to their root—what then remains of me? But the sage, above, roams with the Creator; below, returns with those without end or beginning. Thus his form never decays, and I alone endure.

Fan says: The myriad things have their emergence, yet none see their gate; they have their birth, yet none see their root. Spirit entering its gate returns to whence it came; bones returning to their root revert to whence they were born. If this is so, then returning to the true dwelling, what remains of me? The sage gathers essence to concentrate spirit, employs spirit to govern form, and sustains spirit through form. Essence remains whole and undiminished; spirit is used yet inexhaustible; form lives yet does not decay. Closing the gate, no thing enters through the eyes; deepening the root, no thing can shake it. The unperishing appearance has existed since ancient times—this is the Way of long life and enduring vision. The ancients cultivated themselves for twelve hundred years, yet their forms never decayed, for they had advanced beyond this.

From birth to end, the great transformation has four phases. Between them, change occurs without a moment’s pause—these four are but a rough outline. Lu says: The infant has not yet given rise to right and wrong in the heart, thus virtue is profound and the will is single-minded. When desires and thoughts arise in abundance, those who attack them must be many. In old age, *qi* softens, drawing nearer to the Dao, and at life’s ultimate end, one rests. Infancy, youth, old age, death.

Fan says: Observing from the perspective of non-being, the true and constant abides in profound stillness, enduring since ancient times. Thus rivers rush ceaselessly, yet in truth they never flow. Observing from the perspective of being, the great transformation shifts imperceptibly—crossing arms, one has already lost it. Though Mount Song stands firm, it cannot avoid concealment. Once one enters the mechanism of yin and yang, traverses the path of creation and transformation, then what takes form is indeed real. No movement is without change; no moment is without shift. Borrowing light from a mirror, the present "I" is not the former "I"; halting a lamp before a jar, the flame is not the later flame. Grasping the tools of time, dwelling in the boundless, appearance, complexion, wisdom, and demeanor remain unchanged for a day—yet can one truly escape transformation? Thus from infancy to youth, from old age to death, the great transformation advances daily, with no interval to spare.

Only one who is seamless day and night can penetrate day and night, and knowing that ancient and modern do not replace one another, can participate in the ten thousand years. Reversing and returning through ends and beginnings, lifting heaven and earth to hasten toward stillness—this is transformation transforming yet not transformed by transformation.

In infancy, *qi* is single-minded and the will is unified—harmony at its utmost. No thing harms it; no virtue surpasses it. *Laozi* says: "One whose virtue is profound resembles an infant." Fan says: The child dwells without knowing what it does, moves without knowing where it goes. Thus its *qi* is single and unadulterated; its will is unified and unwavering. Inwardly, harmony is firmly established—nothing can harm it; sorrow and misfortune cannot enter; perverse *qi* cannot assail it. Thus its virtue is complete and its form unharmed. This is what *Laozi* calls "one whose virtue is profound."

In youth, blood and *qi* overflow; desires and thoughts arise in abundance. Things assail it; virtue thus declines. Standing in the arena of striving, things do not align with it. Fan says: This is what Confucius called "when blood and *qi* are just at their peak." In old age, desires and thoughts soften; the body prepares to rest, and no thing precedes it. Rest. Having no competitive heart, no thing contends with it. Though not yet equal to the infant’s wholeness, it surpasses youth. Fan says: This is what *Zhuangzi* called "letting me grow old in ease."

In death, one comes to rest, returning to the ultimate. Lu says: Drawing near to nature, one embodies the Dao; deluded by emotions, one loses the true. Thus one whose virtue is profound resembles an infant; weary yet not acting is still secondary. Compared to those who race and compete, the difference is vast. *Zhenghe* says: In infancy, *qi* is single-minded and the will is unified—harmony at its utmost. This is what *Laozi* called "one whose virtue is profound." In youth, blood and *qi* overflow; desires and thoughts arise in abundance. This is what *Zhuangzi* called "engaging with things in contention."

As one ages, blood and *qi* wane, desires and thoughts soften, and the body prepares for rest. When one returns to the true dwelling, one comes to rest, reverting to the ultimate. This is what *Zhuangzi* meant by: "The great clod bears me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death."

From infancy to death, all passes away day by day—thus it is called transformation. Fan says: This is what Zigong meant by "the noble person comes to rest." The four transformations of form are common to all life. The multitude undergo physical transformation, and their hearts transform with it; the sage undergoes external transformation, yet within remains unchanged.

Confucius, traveling on Mount Tai, saw Rong Qigi walking in the wilds of Cheng, clad in a deerskin robe with a rope belt, plucking his zither and singing. Confucius asked him: "Sir, what is the source of your joy?" He replied: "My joys are many. Heaven gives birth to the ten thousand things, and man is the most noble—yet I have been born a man: that is my first joy. Extending this reasoning, the spirit and *qi* of man differ not from those of other beings; only their paths diverge, hence their forms vary. Thus Rong Qigi deeply probed the causes of fortune and misfortune, clearly discerned the signs of the hidden and manifest, and so rejoiced in attaining human form and, moreover, the dignity of manhood—his words were no idle boast. Between man and woman, man is honored and woman is humble; thus to be born a man is esteemed. Having been born a man, that is my second joy. Before a person is born, whether man or woman is not yet fixed—thus I rejoice again in having attained a male body. Among men, some never see the sun and moon, never leave their swaddling clothes; yet I have lived to ninety years: that is my third joy. Poverty is the constant lot of the scholar; death is the end of all men. To dwell in constancy and meet one’s end—what cause for sorrow?" Confucius said: "Excellent! He is one who can console himself." Not entirely forgetting joy and sorrow, he was nonetheless adept at reasoning to console himself.

Lu says: When the great smelter casts metal, it follows the mold to take shape. Thus spirit serves as the mold, and the myriad forms are completed through it. Whether man or woman, long-lived or short-lived, yin and yang are already determined—why? Spirit directs its work, and form repays the labor. Since form cannot perfect itself, and spirit, though formless, already knew Qigi’s fate, his joy only began in old age. This is to awaken to the Dao late, his emotions still bound to form. The Master merely praised his self-consolation, not granting him deep attainment of the ultimate Way.

Lin Lei was nearly a hundred years old, yet no records of him survive—likely a recluse of old. In late spring, he donned a fur robe (for it was still cold), gleaned stray grains from the harvested fields, and sang as he walked. Confucius, traveling to Wei, saw him in the wilds and said to his disciples: "That old man may be worth speaking to. Go and inquire of him." Zigong volunteered and met him at the edge of a ridge. Facing him, he sighed and said: "Sir, do you have no regrets, singing and gleaning as you go?" Lin Lei did not pause in his steps or cease his song. Zigong pressed him repeatedly, until at last he looked up and replied: "What should I regret?" Zigong said: "In youth, you did not strive in conduct; in maturity, you did not compete with the times; in old age, you have no wife or children; death approaches—what joy do you find in gleaning and singing?" Lin Lei laughed and said: "The source of my joy is something all men possess, yet they turn it into sorrow. What I find joy in, all men share, but they cannot remain level in the face of events, so they know no moment’s gladness."

Lu says: The humane are not anxious, the wise are not fearful—they do not cling to form, for life’s lot follows them. Thus the noble person is not distressed by poverty and low station, nor restless for wealth and honor. Those who fail to grasp this turn it into sorrow—why do you find me strange? In youth, I did not strive in conduct; in maturity, I did not compete with the times—thus I have lived so long. Not striving in conduct, I left behind fame and reputation; not competing with the times, I had no profit or desire. With these two absent from my heart, I have attained a lifespan of a hundred years without seeking it.

Lu says: To pursue conduct beyond one’s lot, to compete for opportunities beyond fate—such striving harms life and shortens years. My joy comes from knowing Heaven’s decree and attaining this longevity. In old age, I have no wife or children; death approaches—thus I can be joyful like this. This is what it means to delight in Heaven and know fate, and so be without sorrow.

Lu says: Wife and children only weary life and trouble the heart—how can they extend a man’s years? Dwelling in constancy and awaiting one’s end, the heart knows no sorrow or distress—thus one can be joyful like this.

Zigong said: "Longevity is what men desire; death is what they detest. Why do you find joy in death?" Lin Lei said: "Death and life are but a going and a returning. Thus one who dies here—how do we know they are not born there? So I know they are not the same. How do I know that busily striving for life is not delusion? How do I know that my death now is not better than my past life?" Pursuing this meaning, existence and extinction, going and returning, are endless.

Lu says: Knowing that form undergoes change while spirit knows no death or life, that going and coming are like morning and evening—why then cling greedily to this and fear that?

Zigong, not comprehending his meaning, returned and told the Master. The Master said: "I knew he was worth speaking to—indeed, so it is. Yet what he has attained is not yet complete." Hearing Lin Lei’s words, Zigong had thought he had reached the ultimate, but the Master said he had not yet fully grasped it. To be complete is to be complete in all things, yet also to be complete in nothing—only then is principle wholly fulfilled. Now, when one opposes non-being to being, rejects that and takes this, one cannot but perceive a difference between inner and outer. Yet what remains incomplete is but a small part.

Should the myriad transformations return to the mysterious One, where self and other are both forgotten, principle naturally levels itself—yet in truth, nothing is cast aside. When inner darkness and outer wandering merge with the common crowd, how can there be any question of completeness or incompleteness?

Lu says: To die here and live there is the inevitable principle. What Lin Lei spoke of was but words of doubt and semblance—hence it is called incomplete.

In the *Zhenghe* edition, the *Book of Changes* states: "Rejoice in Heaven and know fate, thus free from care." Abiding in constancy to the end, death and life bring no change to oneself—this is the source of one’s joy. Cultivating the self, accepting poverty or success, knowing that going and coming are not real, and that I am not disturbed by change in the heart’s deliberations—what sorrow can there be? Yet to know joy and to know sorrow is not true joy. Confucius took the absence of joy as true joy. Rong Qiqi was truly able to ease himself, while Lin Lei had grasped it but not fully.

Fan says: The living are companions of death, and the dead are the beginning of life. In the ten thousand transformations, there is no end—only those who trace origins and return to endings truly understand the discourse on death and life. From the perspective of not delighting in life nor detesting death, those who greedily seek life may be called deluded. Yet how can one know it is delusion? From the toil of life and the rest of death, the dead today are surely better than the living of the past. Yet how can one know they are better than the living of the past?

Observing Lin Lei’s words, one sees he is truly worth speaking to. Yet when viewed by what he took as joy, he still could not avoid opposing non-being to being, taking this and rejecting that—thus the Master said he had grasped it but not fully.

Zigong, weary of learning, said to Confucius: "I wish to rest." The purpose of learning is to return to one’s original state, yet to grow weary of it is to damage the principle of nature. Confucius said: "In life, there is no rest." To toil the mind and employ the limbs—these are the affairs of the living. As *Zhuangzi* truly says: "Life is corvée labor."

Zigong said: "Then is there no place for me, Ci, to rest?" Confucius said: "There is. Look to the grave mounds—soaring, towering, swelling, and rising—then you will know where rest lies." Seeing the distinctness of their burial mounds, one knows rest has its place. As *Zhuangzi* says: "Death is rest."

Zigong said: "Great is death! The noble man rests in it; the petty man is laid low in it." Rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate, awaiting the end with composure—this is the noble man’s rest. Departing from sorrow and suffering, dying in oblivion—this is the petty man’s submission.

Confucius said: "Ci, you have understood. People all know the joy of life but not its suffering; they know the weariness of old age but not its ease; they know the dread of death but not its rest." As *Zhuangzi* says: "The great clod burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death."

Lu says: Life is the substance of activity and function—only in death can one truly rest. It is like the sun’s streaming light, where all things move; the noble man pursues fame, the petty man pursues profit—never resting.

"Zhenghe says: If one studies the Dao yet does not reach the understanding of death, what is the use of such study? Zigong grew weary of learning and wished to rest—this shows he did not comprehend the meaning of death. Thus the Master told him that in life there is no rest, gazing toward the grave: its brightness is like the sun’s radiance, its height like the ruler’s majesty, its mound vast as the earth, its vessel sealed—utterly cut off from the world. This is where rest lies. Yet while the common people call death a thing, the noble man, though dead, does not perish. Hence it is said: the noble man rests in it; the petty man is laid low in it.

The suffering of life is what is meant by 'labors me with life'; the ease of old age is what is meant by 'eases me with old age'; the rest of death is what is meant by 'rests me with death.' Fan says: *Laozi* states, 'In the pursuit of learning, one gains daily.' And again: 'Cut off learning, and there is no sorrow.' Zigong, weary of study and wishing to rest, had not yet advanced in daily accumulation—how then could he reduce and reduce again, achieve non-action yet accomplish all things, and attain that which is called 'cutting off learning'? Confucius told him that in life there is no rest, urging him to strive unceasingly until death.

*Zhuangzi* says: 'The great clod burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death.' Does the one who embodies the Dao have no part in the ease of old age or the rest of death? Is this spoken only for those burdened with form and labored by life? Thus Confucius answered Zigong in this way.

Yanzi said: 'Excellent! The ancients had death.' Life and death are the same in past and present, yet only the death of the ancients is praised—this makes clear that the ancients did not delight in life nor detest death. The virtuous man rests in death; the unvirtuous man is laid low. Cultivating the self and acting with caution, ever mindful and fearful—this is the concern of the virtuous. Indulging in desires without restraint, never satisfied—this is the suffering of the unvirtuous. Only in death do they find rest and submission.

Death is the culmination of virtue. 'Virtue' means 'attainment'; 'culmination' means 'return.' It speaks of each attaining its proper return. The ancients called the dead 'those who return.' If the dead are called 'those who return,' then the living are 'those who journey.' To journey without knowing return is to lose one’s home. If one person loses their home, the world condemns them; if the whole world loses its home, none know to condemn it. This is how the many and the few overthrow one another. Yanzi, a Confucian and Mohist in his teachings, valued form and life—yet he did not distinguish this in his words, merely borrowing what he cited.

Lu says: *Laozi* states, 'Returning to the root is called stillness; stillness is called recovering one’s mandate; recovering one’s mandate is called constancy; knowing constancy is called illumination.' Not knowing constancy, acting recklessly—this brings misfortune. The embodied spirit receives but a single term of retribution, deluded by its origin and clinging to existence, exhausting spirit and mind—weariness reaches its limit. Only in death does one return to the true, like casting off shackles and laying down a burden. Those who cling to life and fear death, attached to existence, do not know to return to the root—yet the world does not condemn them. The deluded are many.

There are those who leave their homeland, part from the six relations, abandon their family and livelihood, wander the four directions without returning—what manner of people are these? The world must call them reckless and dissolute. And there are those who cling to worldly worth, cling to worldly worth—one would expect them to value form and life, boast of their skills, cultivate reputation, and vaunt themselves in the world, yet they know not themselves—what manner of people are these? The world must call them wise and cunning. These two are both lost. Though their conduct differs in small ways, neither escapes drowning and ruin. Yet the world approves of one and not the other—only the sage knows whom to approve and whom to reject. Those who regard life and death as waking and dreaming are to be embraced; those who drown and forget their return are to be abandoned.

Lu says: To abandon the root and chase the branches, to exhaust spirit and mind—whether following emotions or seeking fame, whether pursuing desires or chasing profit—both are lost. What is weighty, what is slight? Yet the multitude calls one right, and the whole world follows; whatever they cling to, the whole world calls wrong."

Only those who embody the Dao know what to embrace and what to abandon. Thus *Zhuangzi* says: Zang and Gu, two shepherds, both lost their sheep. One was absorbed in gambling, the other in reading—though their conduct differed in good and ill, the loss of their sheep was the same. If one strays from the Dao, then wandering the world or pursuing study—how far apart are they?

*Zhenghe* says: Life and death are likewise submissive. To perfect one’s life is to perfect one’s death, hence the saying: "Excellent indeed!" The ancients had death, yet death without annihilation is called longevity. The humane attain longevity, while the inhumane perish with things—thus it is said: "The humane rest, the inhumane submit." There is a place of return.

Life is yang. Life is the radiance of virtue, and radiance arises from yang. Death is yin. Death is the return of virtue, and return arises from yin. Thus to take life as journey and death as homecoming is the meaning of yin and yang, movement and stillness. The reckless lose themselves, and the cunning lose themselves. Outwardly they reject this to grasp that—such is the blindness of the world. Only the sage knows what to embrace and what to abandon.

Fan says: When a person dies, it is called *gui* (ghost). *Gui* means "return"—to return to one’s true dwelling. Once grasping the finite span of life, entrusting oneself to an inescapable place, clashing and grinding against things, one’s conduct is like a galloping horse that cannot be reined in—is this not lamentable? To toil one’s whole life without seeing accomplishment, wearily laboring without knowing where to return—how can this not be pitiful? Such are those who wander astray without turning back; people call them "undying," but what benefit is there?

Consider one who leaves homeland, severs the six relations, abandons family and livelihood, and roams the four directions without returning—what manner of person is this? Merely a wanderer of creation, a traveler in the inn of yin and yang, wearily laboring without knowing return. Then there is one who clings to worldly worth, prides himself on conduct and ability, cultivates reputation, and strives to display himself in the world without self-knowledge—what manner of person is this? Merely one who adorns cleverness to startle the foolish, cultivates the self to expose others’ flaws, and peddles fame alone in the world. Though these two differ, in drowning and forgetting their return they are one. Yet the world approves of one and not the other—some deem themselves reckless, others deem themselves cunning. In essence, only the sage knows what to embrace and what to abandon.

*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Sijie*, Scroll Two, concludes.
*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Sijie*, Scroll Three.

Someone once asked Master Liezi, "Why do you value emptiness?"
Liezi replied, "Emptiness is beyond valuation. All valuations arise from preferring this over that, affirming oneself while negating others. But when both sides are forgotten and the myriad differences merge into oneness, this is called emptiness. If emptiness is truly empty, where can the names of noble and base arise?"

Fan said: "The valley responds because it is empty; the mirror reflects because it is empty; the flute and pipe receive sound because they are empty; the ear hears because it is empty; the eye sees because it is empty; the nose smells because it is empty. If there is solidity or fullness within, it obstructs these functions—emptiness is indeed worthy of esteem. Yet what is esteemed here is despised there, and the names of noble and base remain untranscended. If one is transformed by the Dao, why value emptiness?"

Master Liezi said: "It is not about the name. Things have their evident reality, not merely derived from names. Nothing compares to stillness, nothing compares to emptiness. Stillness and emptiness attain their proper place; grasping and giving lose their proper place. The principle of emptiness and stillness lies not beyond the mind’s deliberations or outside the bodily form. Seek and attain it, and it is one’s own nature. When the inner self is at peace, natural wholeness is realized. Thus, all things that remain whole do so through emptiness and stillness, thereby attaining their proper peace. Those that fail do so through restless seeking, thereby losing their proper place."

Lu said: "When asked why I value emptiness, I answer that there is nothing to value. What I cherish in emptiness is not for the sake of a name. Emptiness is where brightness arises and auspiciousness abides. Only in stillness and emptiness does one attain their proper place. If one indulges in grasping and giving, the spirit loses its peace, and then names, profits, right and wrong all surge forth in contention—how can one endure it? Thus, emptiness is not what *I* value."

Fan said: "Unchanging oneness is the perfection of stillness. Harmony without resistance is the perfection of emptiness. To steadfastly guard stillness and thoroughly attain emptiness—how could this concern the trivialities of fame and reputation? Within the unity of nature lies the true dwelling. Where there is a corpse, there is decay; where there is decay, there is attachment. Stillness is sufficient to respond to all movements; emptiness is sufficient to receive all realities. Stillness and emptiness do not contend with things—the true dwelling is within me, and residing in it brings peace. But if there is grasping, there is seeking; if there is giving, there is responding. The body and nature are driven astray, submerged in the myriad things. Gazing toward the old capital brings joy; pointing to the former home brings tears. Longing to return to one’s true nature yet finding no way in—is this not like an exile who has left their homeland? Though one may wish not to lose their proper place, can it be attained? This is the sorrow of those in the world who have lost their true home.

The *Book of Changes* takes the well as ‘dwelling in one’s proper place’ and *Gen* as ‘stopping in one’s proper place.’ The well is the source of nature; *Gen* is the stillness of nature. Only these do not lose their proper place. When affairs are broken and ruined, those who then perform rites of benevolence and righteousness cannot restore them. One should act before things arise and govern before disorder begins. When integrity is lost and vitality withers, then to drum up benevolence and righteousness in hopes of returning to the ultimate of nature and life—this is never attained."

*Huǐ* is pronounced *huǐ*.

Lu said: "What I speak of as emptiness is cultivated before disorder arises. If the true nature is broken and ruined, the heart-mind and spirit are muddled and confused. To then play with the teachings of benevolence and righteousness or stir the waves of human emotions is to be unable to recover."

*Zhenghe* states: "Where there is nobility, there is baseness; where there is name, there is reality. Emptiness is without these. *Laozi* says: 'Attain the utmost emptiness, guard stillness steadfastly.' Emptiness is sufficient to receive the myriad realities; stillness is sufficient to respond to the myriad movements. Thus it is said, 'Nothing compares to stillness, nothing compares to emptiness.' To regard emptiness and stillness as attaining one’s proper place means that the myriad movements and realities cannot obstruct them. To regard grasping and giving as losing one’s proper place means clinging to this while rejecting that.

When the great Dao is abandoned, there is benevolence and righteousness. When affairs are broken and ruined, and only then do people perform benevolence and righteousness—how can they return to the Dao?"

Fan said: "*Zhuangzi* says, 'If the Dao and virtue are not abandoned, why resort to benevolence and righteousness?' For when the Dao is lost, then virtue arises; when virtue is lost, then benevolence arises; when benevolence is lost, then righteousness arises. Benevolence establishes others; righteousness establishes oneself—yet they are far removed from the Dao. When affairs are already broken and ruined, then to hobble about in benevolence and stamp about in righteousness, seeking to console the hearts of the world—all under heaven rush to respond to benevolence and righteousness, yet none can return to their nature and recover their original state. This is why the utmost persons of old struck and discarded them—there was good reason for it."

Zhou Xiong said: "The turning of the cosmos never ceases; heaven and earth shift imperceptibly—who notices? This is the meaning of *Zhuangzi*’s ‘boat and valley.’ Confucius said, 'Day and night without pause—I pass away thus.' All things take transformation as their body, and the body follows transformation in its changes. Transformation does not pause even briefly—how can things remain unchanged? Thus, the form that lived before is not the form that lives now. In the space between lowering and raising one’s head, ten thousand changes have already occurred. Qi disperses, the form decays—not suddenly in a single day, yet the deluded grasp at vessels of inevitable change, entrust themselves to ceaseless movement, and claim they can escape transformation. Is this not lamentable?"

Fan said: "Yin and yang illuminate, cover, and govern one another; the four seasons replace, give birth to, and destroy one another. The teeming myriad kinds daily pass away in a single breath. In the space of no delay, they swiftly form and swiftly decay, rise and fall in alternation, flourish and decline in succession, suddenly appear and suddenly vanish. The treasures of valleys and marshes—what is now is not what was before. In the time it takes to cross arms, it has already become an old trace. The great transformation shifts imperceptibly, yet the deluded do not know. Without penetrating the Dao of day and night, who can awaken to it?"

Thus, what is diminished there is filled here; what is completed here is depleted there. This is what is meant by rivers running dry and valleys emptying, hills leveling and abysses filling. Loss and fullness, completion and depletion follow the world and follow death—yet this world may also be called life. Coming and going connect without interruption, with no gap to spare—who can perceive it? One who has just achieved completion is already depleted; one who has just attained life is already secretly dead.

Fan said: When hills level and abysses fill, what is diminished there is not necessarily unfilled here. When a tree is planted and grows, what is completed here is not without depletion there. To diminish oneself is to benefit; to complete oneself is to ruin. Life and death cycle through boundless transformation; coming and going revolve through ceaseless mechanism. Day and night proceed without pause, with no interval to comb through—unless one is profoundly enlightened to the beginning and end, who can perceive the reason for this?

For qi does not advance all at once, nor does form deplete all at once. One does not sense its completion, nor does one sense its depletion. All occurs in obscurity and transforms in secret, inherently beyond the reach of ears and eyes.

Fan said: What dissipates soon breathes its last, yet does not advance all at once. What fills soon empties its form, yet does not deplete all at once. In its advance, it daily creates what was not and delights in what is new. In its depletion, it daily reduces what was and diminishes what was completed. Though one may grasp and try to retain it, it departs from obscurity. As *Zhuangzi*’s metaphor of valleys and marshes says: "At midnight, a forceful one carries them away"—this clarifies the point.

It is like a person from birth to old age: appearance, complexion, wisdom, and demeanor change without a day’s pause. Skin, nails, and hair fall away with the world, not remaining as they were in infancy. Even the coarse aspects of form and appearance—new and old replacing each other—are unrecognizable, let alone what is subtler than this? The interval is imperceptible; one must wait until later to know.

Lu said: The mind’s subtle operation, yin and yang’s dynamic interplay—thus the body transforms, heaven and earth shift imperceptibly. Loss and gain, fullness and depletion—who can awaken to it? This is why the Dao is prized: to know the root and not grieve over loss.

*Zhenghe* states: The spirit is that which subtly governs the myriad things. If its subtlety is unknowable, then all that is bound to form, constrained by number, or confined between heaven and earth—where the revolving of the two qi never ceases and the coming and going of the myriad things never ends—cannot be grasped in its governing and unfolding. How then can one know it? Only the sage, penetrating to the source of things, awakens to this and merges with it. Those who wait until later to know are the last to awaken, if they awaken at all.

Fan said: Born between heaven and earth, once one receives one’s completed form, it does not perish until it is exhausted. The form transforms, and the mind transforms with it. Yet it rushes and gallops, unperceiving and unawakened—how can one not lose the heart of an infant? The ancients who embodied the Dao used spirit to guide form, transforming without being transformed by transformation. The four limbs and hundred parts would become dust and grime. Death and life are great indeed, yet one does not change with them. Though myriad transformations have no end, how can this weary the heart? Thus, one of profound virtue returns to the state of an infant, roaming above with the Creator and below with those who transcend life and death, equalizing beginning and end as friends.

A man of the state of Qi worried that heaven and earth would collapse and he would have no place to dwell, forsaking sleep and food. Another, worried about his worry, went to enlighten him. The first man’s worry was deluded, yet the second, though not deluded, worried about his delusion—refusing to worry about what he worried about—only deepened the confusion. How could they enlighten each other?

He said: Heaven is but accumulated qi. There is no place without qi. Like bending and stretching, breathing in and out, one moves and rests within heaven all day—why worry about collapse? The azure of heaven is not a solid substance. Then what is called heaven—is it merely distant and without limit? From the earth upward, all is heaven. Thus, looking up and down, breathing—one never departs from heaven.

The man said: If heaven is accumulated qi, should not the sun, moon, and stars fall? The enlightener said: The sun, moon, and stars are luminous within accumulated qi. Qi is all-pervading—even the vastness of heaven and earth rests securely within the realm of the Great Void, let alone what is lifted by qi. Even if they fell, they could cause no harm.

The man said: What of the earth’s ruin? The enlightener said: The earth is but accumulated clods, filling the four directions. There is no place without clods. Like stepping and treading, one moves and rests upon the earth all day—why worry about its ruin? The man released his concern and rejoiced greatly. (Note: *she* should be *shi* [释], meaning "release." The enlightener, too, released his concern and rejoiced greatly.)

These two—one worrying about inevitable ruin, the other rejoicing in inevitable preservation—did not know the true cause of worry and joy. Though they consoled and enlightened each other, they remained deeply deluded.

Lu said: Heaven is accumulated qi—where is there no qi? Earth is accumulated clods—where is there no clod? Clods hide nothing; qi collapses nowhere. The sun, moon, and stars are luminous within qi—why worry about collapse?

Fan said: What stands firm and rises is heaven in motion—is it merely accumulated qi, naturally clear? If not, it might tear apart. What sinks and settles is earth in stillness—is it merely accumulated clods, naturally at peace? If not, it might erupt. Kunlun’s vastness stands as an obstruction in the Great Void. To worry about its ruin is delusion; to worry about another’s worry is even greater delusion.

To be free from delusion is to aspire to the greatest freedom from delusion.

Changluzi heard this and laughed, saying: The rainbow, the clouds and mist, the wind and rain, the four seasons—these are the accumulations of qi that form heaven. The mountains and peaks, the rivers and seas, metal and stone, fire and wood—these are the accumulations of form that constitute earth. To know that qi accumulates and that clods accumulate—how can one then speak of indestructibility? Before the primordial chaos divided, heaven and earth were one qi, and the myriad things were one form. When they separated into heaven and earth and dispersed into the myriad things, this was merely a difference of convergence and divergence, of emptiness and fullness in form and qi. Now, heaven and earth are but a tiny thing within the void, the greatest entity within what has form. That they are difficult to exhaust, difficult to fathom—this is certainly so. That they are difficult to measure, difficult to comprehend—this is certainly so. To worry about their ruin is indeed far-fetched; to claim they will not be ruined is also incorrect. Heaven and earth cannot but be ruined, and when they meet their ruin, they will return to it. When that time comes, how can one not be anxious? This is to know that what has a beginning must have an end, that what has form must be destroyed, yet not to recognize the vicissitudes of fortune and the rise and fall of yin and yang, nor the emergence and disappearance of vessels and heaven and earth.

Lu said: Qi and clods accumulate to form heaven and earth—where there is accumulation and formation, how can there be no ruin? Only because their vastness makes their end difficult to witness. Yet if one encounters their time of ruin, how can one not be anxious?

Fan said: The classic states: How do we know that the demise of heaven and earth does not give rise to something greater than heaven and earth? Viewed from within the void, they are but a tiny thing. It also states: Heaven and earth are the greatest of forms. Viewed from within what has form, they are the most immense. Because their end is difficult to exhaust, they are difficult to fathom; because they are difficult to measure, they are difficult to comprehend. To worry about their ruin is indeed far-fetched; to claim they will not be ruined is also incorrect. Yet to say that heaven and earth cannot but be ruined, and that when their time of ruin comes one should not be anxious—this places Changluzi’s words squarely in the realm of the laughable.

Master Liezi heard this and laughed, saying: To say that heaven and earth will be ruined is mistaken; to say they will not be ruined is also mistaken. Whether they are ruined or not, I cannot know. Nevertheless, that is one perspective, and this is another. That perspective speaks of indestructibility; this one speaks of ruin. If they are not ruined, they endure together with humanity; if they are ruined, they perish together with humanity. Why rejoice or grieve over this? Thus, the living do not know death, and the dead do not know life; the coming does not know the going, and the going does not know the coming. Whether ruined or not, why should I concern myself with it? Not knowing life while alive is like not knowing death while dead. When something comes into being, none know its destruction; when it is destroyed, how can one know its formation? This is the visible proof of going and coming, the clear evidence of success and failure. I accept them all—my feelings hold no distinction between them. Where is there room for concern?

Lu said: Heaven and earth are the greatest of things; the physical body is the smallest of things. The great are but one thing, and the small are but one thing. All things must be ruined—what is the use of debating it? Moreover, in life one does not know death, and in death one does not know life; coming and going are not self-aware, and formation and ruin cannot be understood. If one cannot even comprehend what is close at hand, why expend the mind on heaven and earth and worry over debates beyond the realm of things?

Zhenghe said: From the perspective of vessels, there is formation and there is ruin; from the perspective of the Dao, there is neither formation nor ruin. Those confined to vessels say that because things have form and qi, they cannot but be ruined. Those who comprehend the Dao know that they neither collapse nor fall, and thus cannot be ruined. Only the enlightened understand this passage. This is why Master Liezi has no room for concern.

Fan said: Heaven and earth within the void are like a bubble in water. Water forms its own boundary, and the bubble becomes water; when it is ruined, it is still water. It forms and is ruined in an instant, ruined and forms in an instant. The worry of the man of Qi over its ruin is like mourning the death of a person. Those who worry about what others worry about can enlighten them—this is like mourning the mourning of others. Changluzi’s laughter is like mourning those who mourn the mourning of others. Advancing further, whether ruined or not, there is no room for concern—this is the ultimate discourse. Thus, the transformations of life and death, the mechanisms of coming and going, are not sufficient to be known. Whether ruined or not, why rejoice or grieve over it?

Shun asked Zheng: Can the Dao be possessed?

Shun wished to clarify that all beings are identical to nothingness, so he took the Dao as the starting point for his question.

Zheng replied: Your body is not your own—how can you possess the Dao?

Guo Xiang said: The body is not something you can possess; it simply exists of itself. If you do not even possess what you have, how much less can you possess what you do not have?

Lu said: You and I are merely designations for self and other in form; they do not refer to the spirit. The common people take their own bodies as "I" and others as "you." How can one hope to possess the Dao? Thus it is said: Your body is not your own—how can you possess the Dao?

Shun said: If my body is not my own, then who possesses it?

He pressed the question further, based on the possession of this body.

Zheng replied: It is the entrusted form of heaven and earth, a partial manifestation of the one qi. Life is not your possession—it is the entrusted harmony of heaven and earth. Only through the accumulation of harmony does life come to be. Fate is not your possession—it is the entrusted compliance of heaven and earth. Only through the accumulation of compliance do existence and perishing come to be.

Guo Xiang said: If the body were yours to possess, then beauty and ugliness, life and death, would be determined by you. Yet qi gathers and life arises, and you cannot prevent it.

When qi scatters, death comes, and you cannot halt it. Clearly, its gathering and dissolution arise of themselves—it is not yours to possess. Your descendants are not yours to possess—they are the entrusted shedding of heaven and earth, the cicada’s molt as qi gathers and disperses of itself. If they were yours to possess, then even the number of sons and daughters would be determined by you. Thus, in walking you know not where you go; in dwelling you know not what you hold; in eating you know not why. All emerges from the self-so, not from knowing or doing.

Heaven and earth are the supreme yang—qi itself. How then could they be possessed? Heaven and earth are but the greatest of all entrusted gatherings. Now, in walking, dwelling, eating, and resting, all are set in motion by the supreme yang—how could you know the reason? Supreme yang is like unyielding solidity, yet it is not the ultimate principle of unyielding solidity. When turned toward the extreme of emptiness and harmony, there is no form, no life, no death, no end. Then to what would fate and life be entrusted in compliance? To what would descendants be entrusted in shedding? Where would walking and dwelling find their mooring? On what would eating and drinking depend?

Lu said: Since the numinous clarity of the Way is unknown, one borrows heaven and earth to speak of it. Heaven governs the numinous function; earth governs forms and things. That which enters into being is the entrusted form. That which embodies harmony is the life of nature. That which responds and functions is the entrusted compliance. That which nurtures emotions is the entrusted shedding. Now, in your walking, stopping, eating, and resting, you know only the movement of the supreme yang, yet remain unaware of the true lord of numinous clarity. How then could you possess the Dao?

Someone asked: Yu and Shun were sages—how could they not know the Dao?

The answer: When borrowing guest and host to dispel confusion, how could one remain in silent mystery alone? Yet *Zhuangzi* says: "The ruler of Liang possesses a sage’s talent but not a sage’s Dao; I possess a sage’s Dao but not a sage’s talent." Thus, one who has the talent to aid the world and occupies the highest position may not necessarily know the Dao. One who dwells in the mountains and forests with a reputation for solitary integrity may not necessarily govern others.

Thus, after thirty years on the throne, the Yellow Emperor dreamed of the Land of Huaxu; only after Yao met the Four Masters did he gaze in wonder upon the southern banks of the Fen River. How then could it be strange that Shun had not yet awakened?

In *Zhenghe* it is said: Emptiness is loss, fullness is possession—whatever is grasped and held is subject to being seized. The Dao’s subtlety is formless, its depth unknowable. Since it cannot be possessed, how then can the human body—with its form, nature, and life—claim to endure when it arises and vanishes in an instant?

The body is but a vessel, and thus one’s person is Heaven and Earth’s entrusted form. Harmony is qi, and thus life is Heaven and Earth’s entrusted harmony. The birth of things follows the principle of nature and life, and thus nature and life are Heaven and Earth’s entrusted compliance. The body is the legacy of one’s parents, and thus descendants are Heaven and Earth’s entrusted shedding. If this is so, then none of it is truly one’s own. Thus, in walking one knows not where one goes, in dwelling one knows not what one holds, in eating one knows not the taste. There is indeed one who governs this, one who commands it—nothing more than the supreme yang of Heaven and Earth, the movement of qi alone. How then could one possess the Dao?

Yet the Dao is the shared path of all people. Thus it is said: “The Dao will make a dwelling for you.” Could it then remain forever unpossessable? To claim possession is to lose it; only the sage possesses it by not possessing.

Master Fan said: The Dao is originally without substance, and your body is also empty. If emptiness is not possession, where then could the Dao reside? Thus, of all things under Heaven, what you claim to possess most exclusively is your own body; of all that abides within the body, nothing is more precious than life; of the root of life, nothing is more fundamental than nature and destiny; of all that is closest to the body, nothing is dearer than one’s descendants. Yet you cannot truly possess any of these. Therefore, observing your own body, you see that it is fundamentally without knowledge: in walking, how could you know where you go? In dwelling, how could you know what you hold? In eating, how could you know the taste? All is but the supreme yang of Heaven and Earth, the workings of qi. How then could you claim to possess the Dao?

In Qi, the Guo family was immensely wealthy; in Song, the Xiang family was desperately poor. Traveling from Song to Qi, Xiang sought their method. Guo told him: “I am skilled in theft.” When I first began stealing, I had enough after one year, sufficiency after two, and great abundance after three. From then on, my bounty extended to towns and villages. Overjoyed, Xiang grasped the words of theft but not the Dao of theft. He scaled walls, broke through chambers, and seized whatever his hands and eyes reached. Before long, he was caught with stolen goods and forfeited his ancestral wealth. Believing Guo had deceived him, Xiang went to complain. Guo said: “How did you steal?” Xiang described what he had done. Guo said: “Alas! Have you lost the Dao of theft to this extent? Now I will tell you.

I have heard that Heaven has its seasons, Earth its advantages. Spring, autumn, winter, summer—each region yields what it produces. I steal Heaven and Earth’s timely benefits, the soaking rains of clouds, the nurturing bounty of mountains and marshes, to grow my grain, cultivate my crops, build my walls, and construct my dwellings. On land, I steal from birds and beasts; in water, I steal from fish and turtles—nothing is not stolen. Yet grain, crops, trees, birds, beasts, fish, and turtles are all born of Heaven. How could they be mine? Heaven cannot even produce itself—how could it produce things? People cannot even possess themselves—how could they possess things? This is to make clear that all things arise and possess themselves.

Yet when I steal from Heaven, I incur no misfortune. Heaven does not withhold its gifts, and I act with impartiality—where then could resentment arise? Gold, jade, treasures, grain, silk, and wealth are what people amass. How could they be Heaven’s to give? If Heaven cannot give them, how can people amass them? This too makes clear that all things gather themselves. If you steal them and incur punishment, whom should you resent?

People possess their wealth, and I violate their private holdings—this is why you bring trouble upon yourself.”

Master Lu said: Heaven and Earth are not benevolent; they treat the myriad things as straw dogs. Devoid of feeling in their nurturing, how could they have intent in giving or taking? The great consume the small, the clever exploit the foolish. Seizing opportunities to pursue profit, using force to serve private ends, all actions of taking and giving are theft. Yet when people hoard wealth, they do not act as its true masters. To take from them and be punished—whom else could you resent?

Master Fan said: Theft arises from covetous ambition, taking what is not one’s own. Yet there is what is called public theft and what is called private theft. Grain, crops, trees, birds, beasts, fish, and turtles are born of Heaven—Guo stole from them and incurred no misfortune. Gold, jade, treasures, grain, silk, and wealth are amassed by people—Xiang stole from them and was punished. Though the two differ, to take what is not one’s own is the same.

Bewildered, Xiang believed Guo had gravely deceived him. He encountered Master Dongguo and asked him about it. Master Dongguo said: “Is not your very body itself theft? You steal the harmony of yin and yang to form your life, to bear your form—how much more are external things not theft? If theft exists, then my body is but one thing among Heaven and Earth’s myriad things, and I cannot but claim it as my own. If theft does not exist, then inner and outer cannot differ. Truly, Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are inseparable. To measure and claim possession is delusion.

Heaven and Earth are the collective name of the myriad things; the myriad things are the alternate name of Heaven and Earth. Though each claims its own body, in principle they are not separate. To measure and claim possession is delusion. Thus, Heaven and Earth’s entrusted form is not mine. To adorn and cherish appearances, to boast and flaunt wisdom and ability—this is delusion.”

As for those who go to extremes, they arbitrarily measure and claim external things as their own, even inscribing names to set themselves apart, relying on kin to secure their position, adorning their robes to dazzle others, leveraging rank and status to sway the masses, amassing wealth and property, and establishing factions of power—all their lives they delight in such playthings, never awakening to the truth. Thus *Laozi* says: “The reason I have great trouble is that I have a body.” *Zhuangzi* says: “Of my hundred bones and six viscera, with whom shall I be intimate?” Grasping this principle, the mind’s square inch is as empty as the primordial void, and the bodily form exists equally among the myriad things.

Fan says: “To claim possession, whether great or small, is delusion.” Xiang’s parable speaks of theft in word but not of the Dao of theft, thus erring in pursuing wealth. Guo’s parable speaks of the Dao of theft but not of the reason for theft, thus erring in being burdened by things. If one attains the Dao, even the body within cannot be arbitrarily claimed as one’s own—how much less the myriad external things? Why? When a person is born, their hundred bones, nine orifices, and six viscera all exist together. With whom shall I be intimate? To recognize and claim them is great delusion. Only by not allowing profit to burden the form, nor the form to burden the mind, can one see that the myriad things and the self cannot be distinguished—when I have no self, what trouble can I have?

Guo’s theft is the public Dao, thus incurring no misfortune; Xiang’s theft is private desire, thus incurring guilt. “Public” is the opposite of “private”; without private desire, the name “public” vanishes. Now to call violating Heaven “public” and violating man “private” is not the ultimate principle. Lu says: “Heaven and Earth are without private desire; to take from them is without opposition, thus incurring no misfortune. The human heart has private desire; to take with attachment is theft. To take what is privately desired with a heart of private desire—private desire has its opposite, so how can guilt be doubted?” Thus, the law forbids private desire; without opposition, there is no prohibition. Those who act with public or private intent are still thieves—only the object of violation differs, not the nature of theft. Those who transcend public and private are also thieves. The body cannot but exist, and wealth cannot but be gathered; to then seek to abandon them is not the same as being without attachment from the outset.

Lu says: “The sage establishes laws and teachings, harming none and encroaching not upon the multitude—this is not theft. Those who do not violate the law act by the public Dao; those who violate the law act by the private Dao. Even if one does not violate the public but claims things for oneself, it is still theft—how much more so to violate the law and claim for oneself?” Public and private are the virtues of Heaven and Earth. Life is one principle of Heaven and Earth; the body is one thing among Heaven and Earth’s myriad things. What one now covets and clings to is but coveting and clinging to the life and body born between Heaven and Earth. In affairs, there is no public or private; in principle, there is no coveting or clinging. One who knows the virtue of Heaven and Earth—who is a thief? Who is not a thief? What is the virtue of Heaven and Earth? It is simply spontaneity. Where does it pass through the names of public and private? Once the names of public and private are abolished, theft and non-theft are without distinction in principle.

Lu says: “To know public and private yet be without private desire, to be of the same pattern as things without finding it strange—this is the virtue of Heaven and Earth. If one knows the virtue of Heaven and Earth and takes without private desire, this is not deceiving Heaven. To take without differing from others, to obtain without private desire, is not theft. If so, who is a thief? Who is not a thief? Only those who thoroughly comprehend the spirit and awaken to the Dao know this.”

*Zhenghe* says: “To take what is not one’s own is nothing but theft. Some act by the public Dao and incur no misfortune; others act by private desire and incur guilt—the difference lies in whether one is burdened by possession or by recognizing and claiming possession. Yet those who act with public or private intent cannot be without attachment, thus it is said: those with public or private intent are also thieves. Those who transcend public and private cannot be without lingering attachment, thus it is said: those who have transcended public and private are also thieves. To act with public or private intent as it arises naturally, without contrivance—this is to embrace the myriad things, which is called the virtue of Heaven and Earth. One who knows this merges into great unity. Though things differ in shape and form, they unite with Heaven and Earth. How can I discern their distinctions? Thus the chapter *Heavenly Auspices* concludes.”

Fan says: “To act for oneself is private; to turn away from private desire is public. Public originally has no name; it is only through private desire that it is known. When Heaven follows principle, it is vast and public—thus Guo’s theft of what Heaven produces is the public Dao. When man follows emotion, it is petty and private—thus Xiang’s theft of what man possesses is private desire. From the perspective of the Dao, both are partial. Those who act with public or private intent are thieves; those who transcend public and private are also thieves. Public and private are the virtues of Heaven and Earth. The sage does not discard either. Awakening to this and merging into obscurity, how can one discern the distinction between theft and non-theft?”

*The Four Explanations of the* Perfect Virtue of the Pristine Dao, *Volume Three* concludes.
*The Four Explanations of the* Perfect Virtue of the Pristine Dao, *Volume Four*

The *Harmonious Radiance Scattered One*, Gao Shouyuan, compiled:

What the Yellow Emperor received at birth is called *nature*; attaining the utmost of nature is called *harmony*. Thus, responding to principle and dwelling in compliance, one’s path is ever unobstructed; following emotion and turning from the Dao, one encounters things and is impeded.

Lu says: This clarifies forgetting form to nurture spirit, following profound silence to manifest true wisdom. At the outset of nurturing, one relinquishes the myriad existences to inwardly clarify the heart-mind; when wisdom is manifested, one transforms all living beings outwardly to engage with things. Thus, in its beginning, one purifies the heart-mind and disciplines the body, not mingling in governance; in its final chapter, one praises Confucius and Mozi to aid humanity. Such is its great purport.

In governance and harmony, those of old who illuminated the great Dao first clarified Heaven, then followed with virtue and the Dao. *Liezi* begins with *Heavenly Omens* and is followed by *The Yellow Emperor*—could this be the reason?

Fan says: The self-emptiness of the non-substantial flows unimpeded everywhere; clinging to the substantiality of things, one meets obstacles at every turn. Then is it easier to act from having? One must dissolve the heart-mind of right and wrong, gain and loss, abandon the fervor of cunning and decisiveness, uproot suspicion and anxiety, so that things cannot harm one’s essence, nor can others precede one. Rejecting the conduct of self-aggrandizement and upholding the constant Dao of victory, if you apply this among men, the afflictions of the human realm may well cease. The Land of Huaxu, the Mountain of Guye—are they far away? Such is the purport of this chapter.

Emperor Huangdi, having reigned fifteen years, rejoiced that all under heaven bore him allegiance—yet this was but worldly joy. He cultivated the mandate of correctness, which is precisely the cultivation of one’s nature. He delighted the ears and eyes, gratified the nose and mouth, yet his complexion withered and darkened, his five emotions grew dull and confused. The toil of heart and wit was insufficient to nurture his nature and life—it only sufficed to wither his form.

Lu says: The people of his age all sought to nourish their bodies with sounds and sights, with food and drink; only those of abundant means were deemed noble and wealthy. Yet the sage knew this path was enough to harm life, and so he grew withered and joyless.

The first chapter reveals the spirit as the master of life; the second seeks to clarify the Dao for nurturing the body. Thus it first shows what the multitude drowns in, then gradually guides them forward.

After another fifteen years, he grew anxious that all under heaven remained untended—yet this was but worldly anxiety. He exhausted his keen hearing and bright vision, advanced his wit and strength, and busied himself for the hundred clans. Again his complexion withered and darkened, his five emotions grew dull and confused. The use of keen hearing and bright vision was insufficient for governance—it only sufficed to disorder the spirit.

Lu says: Those of his age called themselves "noble men," scholars who ordered others. All wearied their hearts and wore themselves out, sacrificing their bodies for things, seeking fame and pursuing profit. Yet they knew not that toil of the spirit forfeits reality, and thus they drifted ever farther from the Dao.

Yellow Emperor then sighed in admiration—*sighed* should read *lamented*. "My errors run deep indeed—*deep* should read *excessive*. To nurture the self alone brings such calamity; to govern the myriad things brings such calamity. Only by entrusting without nurturing, by indulging without governing, may one’s nature and life remain whole, and all under heaven rest in peace."

Lu says: *Excessive* means straying from the Dao. All living things know to nurture themselves, selfishly preserving their lives, yet they know not that what gives life also leads to death. Those who grasp at benevolence and righteousness all know to pursue fame for profit, selfishly believing themselves capable, yet they know not how this exhausts the spirit, harms life, and hastens death. To serve the self and act selfishly is the way of the petty man; to aid others without selfishness is the way of the noble man. Outwardly, goodness and evil appear distinct, yet inwardly, the pursuit of fame and loss of reality both end in ruin. Now that the great Dao is made clear, both are condemned.

Fan says: To animate the myriad things without sharing the sage’s anxieties—this is the Dao. To share the people’s fortunes in good and ill—this is worldly affairs. Embracing the Dao, one knows no sorrow; engaging in affairs, one meets with calamity. The sage governs all under heaven only when unavoidable—how could he do so with indifference? Inwardly nurturing the self means preserving the true mandate, delighting the ears and eyes, sating the mouth and nose—yet its calamities are already thus. Outwardly governing the myriad things means exhausting keen hearing and bright vision, advancing wit and strength, and busying oneself for the hundred clans—its calamities are again thus. How could such trivial skills, which bind the body and startle the heart, inherently entail such manifold sorrows? Only when one takes the people’s heart as one’s own heart must one also share what they fear.

Thus, he set aside the myriad affairs, forsook the palace chambers, dismissed his attendants; stilled the bells and chimes, reduced the royal fare, and withdrew to dwell in leisure within the Hall of the Great Court. There, he purified his heart and disciplined his form—his heart free of desire, his form naturally compliant. For three months, he did not attend to governance.

Lu said: To set aside the myriad affairs does not mean doing nothing at all. Matters arise and are met as the four seasons turn. Thus it is said: What does Heaven say?
