The four seasons proceed, the hundred things are born—yet it does not toil in anxious thought to court empty fame, nor scheme for ability across a thousand ages to leave a fragrant name upon bamboo and silk. Rather, it responds in profound obscurity, moved only by necessity, neither exalting its own name nor increasing its cravings, neither enriching its flavors nor indulging in wanton sounds. It purifies the heart and subdues the body. Three months is but one season. Confucius said: Yan Hui did not stray from benevolence for three months—this is the same. Selecting worthy talents and entrusting them with rewards and punishments without partiality—this is not attending to governance oneself.

In daytime repose, he dreamed. Profound truths cannot be sought through emotion, so they are entrusted to dreams. The sage has no dreams. He wandered in the land of Huaxu. In perfect harmony: the perfected one does not burden the form with things, nor burden the heart with the form; above, he roams with the Creator, below, he befriends those beyond life and death, without beginning or end. Sorrow and joy do not alter him—what calamity could there be? The Yellow Emperor, by this means, cast off the burdens of the myriad things and returned to the One. Thus, purifying the heart and disciplining the form, he dreamed of wandering in the land of Huaxu.

Fan said: The governance of the sage, though daily handling ten thousand details, now setting aside the myriad affairs, no longer wearies the heart over the least trifles. Forsaking the palace chambers is not attachment to the comfort of dwelling; dismissing attendants is not delight in the multitude of servants. Stilling the bells and chimes, the ear is not ensnared by sound; reducing the royal fare, the mouth is not ensnared by taste. Withdrawing to dwell in leisure within the Hall of the Great Court, purifying the heart and disciplining the form, for three months not attending to governance—this is the utmost purity of fasting. Thus, the land of Huaxu is attained in dream.

The Classic says: When form meets, it is affairs; when spirit encounters, it is dream. All that one does must manifest in dream—thus the communion of the hun in sleep is not necessarily empty. All that one dreams at night must accord with the day—thus the awakening of the form is not necessarily real. Only one who comprehends the Way of day and night can know this. Merging dream and waking into one, regarding the world’s gains and losses, right and wrong, nobility and baseness, life and death as no more than dream and illusion—thus, dwelling in the palace of the transformed man, one wanders in dream; serving as the steward of Yin, one finds joy in dream; obtaining the deer of the man of Zheng, one litigates in dream. Whatever one encounters, one accepts—knowing it is illusion, not real. Why doubt this alone?

Yet the true person of old, in sleep, had no dreams. Liezi speaks of this to clarify that the utmost Dao cannot be sought through emotion, and so provisionally entrusts it to dream. The land of Huaxu lies west of Yan Prefecture and north of Tai Prefecture. It need not actually exist—it merely illustrates that the utmost principle must be thus. *Huainanzi* says: Due west is Yan Prefecture, northwest is Tai Prefecture. How many thousands of miles distant is this land? *Si* means "apart," *qi* means "center." It is beyond the reach of boats, carriages, or foot—only spirit may roam there. Boats, carriages, and feet are but the resources of form; the spirit’s way is subtle and indistinct, arriving without traveling.

Fan said: *Hua* means "to unfold and depart from the root," *xu* means "to emerge and meet." The principle is supremely subtle, yet need not be sought in the depths of darkness and silence. Though departing from the root of the Dao, it meets with things—this is what is meant by "returning to the root and recovering the mandate," which remains ever thus. Due west is Yan Prefecture, due north is Tai Prefecture; west of Yan and north of Tai is the place of returning to the root and recovering the mandate.

When the Dao descends to the mean, even those with feet may reach the mound. How many thousands of miles distant is this land? It is far removed from humanity—beyond the reach of boats, carriages, or foot, only spirit may roam there. Only spirit—swift without haste, arriving without traveling—thus it enters and exits the six directions, roams in the realm of nothingness. Where could it not go in leisure?

In that land, there are no teachers or leaders—only spontaneity. Its people have no cravings—only spontaneity. Spontaneity does not rely on externals. They do not know joy in life, nor aversion to death—thus no premature deaths; they do not know closeness to self, nor distance from things—thus no love or hatred; they do not know opposition or compliance—thus no benefit or harm. Principle knows no life or death—thus no joy or aversion; principle knows no love or hatred—thus no closeness or distance; principle knows no opposition or compliance—thus no benefit or harm. They cherish nothing, fear nothing. Entering water, they do not drown; entering fire, they do not burn; struck or flogged, they feel no pain; pricked or prodded, they feel no itch. In perfect harmony, nothing can harm them—heat, drowning, pain, or itch. In truth, these arise from anxiety and fear; the principles are detailed in the following chapter. *Xiao yang* means "aching itch," as explained in the *Rites of Zhou*.

They tread the void as if on solid ground, sleep in emptiness as if on a bed. Clouds and mist do not obstruct their sight, thunder and lightning do not disturb their hearing, beauty and ugliness do not confuse their hearts, mountains and valleys do not trip their steps—only spirit moves. In perfect compliance, nothing can oppose them.

Lu said: This is metaphorical speech. Purifying the heart and disciplining the form, when spirit unites with the Dao, one attains that great land. Spirit is the master of life. As the master of life, it governs spirit to nurture life. If nurturing is contrary to principle, it becomes harm instead.

The vulgar mistake indulgence in desires for nurturing life, yet this is precisely the root of premature death. Thus the noble person nurtures their nature, while the petty person nurtures their emotions. To nurture one’s nature is to be free of cravings, to preserve the natural, to feel no joy in life nor aversion to death, to hold no preferences or aversions, no fears or taboos—simply following the natural. One whose spirit moves is one whose spirit unites with the Dao. This is not some separate realm or distinct kind of person.

Thus it is said: the Way of humaneness is not distant—practice it and you arrive. A single word in accord, and you grasp it in an instant. *Zhenghe* lies west of Yan Prefecture and north of Tai Prefecture, thousands of miles from the state of Qi—its Way is secluded and boundless. Only those who wander in spirit can reach it. Without teachers or superiors, they govern themselves; without cravings, they are self-sufficient. Life and death bring no change to them, near and far impose no burden on their bodies. They do not pursue profit, yet profit does not come; they do not resist harm, yet harm does not arrive. They cherish nothing, fear nothing, and their hearts forget all things. They enter water yet do not drown, enter fire yet do not burn, suffer blows without pain, endure scratches without itching. Their forms are left behind, they tread the void and sleep in emptiness, unobstructed and untroubled. Wherever they go, they have no leisure—thus they come and go through the realms of yin and yang, beyond all reckoning, let alone human comprehension. This is what is meant by "spirit moving alone."

Fan said: In that land, there are no teachers or superiors—all is natural. Thus there is no labor of governance, yet the state governs itself. Its people have no cravings—all is natural. Thus there are no greedy impulses, and the people remain unspoiled. They do not know life in death, nor death in life, so they feel no joy in departure nor resistance to return. Who then could fear early death? They see others as themselves and themselves as others, so there is nothing too intimate, nothing too distant. Who then could harbor love or hatred? Anger arises from opposition, joy from compliance—so they do not turn away from difference nor incline toward sameness. Who then could be swayed by profit or harm? Free of love and hatred, their hearts know nothing; free of fear and taboo, their forms feel no alarm. Such beings are untouched by the greatest floods, though they reach the heavens; unburned by the fiercest droughts, though metal and stone melt and mountains wither. Though assailed by the brave, blades cannot pierce them; though struck by the strong, blows cannot land. They touch solidity yet are unobstructed, tread emptiness yet do not fall. They see without eyes, hear without ears. Their hearts are hollowed of all things—beauty and ugliness cannot disturb them. They move in concealment yet are not stifled; mountains and valleys cannot trip their steps. Their comings and goings are inexhaustible—such is the work of their spirit.

The Yellow Emperor, having awakened, knew this was but a dream—yet the sage is never truly asleep or awake. Serene and self-content, he summoned Tian Lao, Li Mu, and Tai Shan Ji—his three ministers—and said: "I have heard that after three months of purifying the heart and disciplining the form, one should seek the Way to nurture the self and govern all things, yet I have not attained its method. The self cannot be nurtured, things cannot be governed, and though I have sought it with utmost diligence, I have not found it. Exhausted, I slept, and dreamed thus. Now I know the supreme Dao cannot be sought through emotion. I have understood, I have attained it—but I cannot explain it to you. If it cannot be sought through emotion, then it cannot be conveyed through emotion."

Twenty-eight years later, the realm was greatly ordered, nearly like the land of Hua Xu. *Zhenghe*: Toil of form and agitation of heart, to know and debate—thus the method was not attained. Purification of heart and discipline of form, awakening to the obscure—thus the Way was obtained. Though it has reality and truth, it

The people mourned him for over two hundred years without cease.

Lu said: Having awakened to the Dao, one no longer relies on external things to attain it. Exhaustion leading to sleep is obscurity in principle, the abandonment of desires. The spirit of consciousness returns to its nature—this cannot be sought through emotion. That it cannot be conveyed to you is because the heart is stilled and words forgotten. All who govern the world through calculation merely achieve an empty name; when calculation reaches its limit, traces become visible, and what is empty cannot be made real. Those above demand empty names from those below, and those below respond with empty names to those above—superior and inferior deceive one another, accumulating emptiness as governance. How then can one seek purity and simplicity?

The Dao is a spiritual union with principle, responding to things with truth. It does not favor petty virtues nor rage at minor faults. Like spring spreading over all things, giving life, customs transform and winds shift—naturally and imperceptibly, without knowing how transformation occurs or how completion is achieved. Thus the people long for it, unaware of its limits.

Fan said: To have a body is to be burdened by things and lose the self. Turning inward to observe the body, one finds the body fundamentally non-existent; yet if one insists on nurturing it, one has not departed from the body. Looking outward to observe things, one finds things fundamentally non-existent; yet if one insists on governing them, one has not departed from things. Seeing neither self nor other, one drifts far from the Dao. Why? The essence of the supreme Dao is profound and obscure; its ultimate is dim and silent. Sought through emotion, the Dao will never be attained. What enables one to know it is no-knowledge; what enables one to attain it is no-attainment. Knowing is fundamentally no-knowledge; attainment is fundamentally no-attainment—how then could one convey this to others?

Truly understanding this, one roams the heart in blandness, unites qi in vastness, follows the natural course of things, and admits no selfishness—thus the world is ordered. After a thousand years, weary of the world, one departs to become an immortal of the highest realm, releasing the bow and ascending in transcendence. People follow this path—who would still take things as their concern?

I have examined the writings of Zhuang Zhou, who said that the Yellow Emperor first disturbed the human heart with benevolence and righteousness, then later inquired of Guangchengzi about the Dao, suggesting that only by abandoning sagacity and discarding knowledge could the world be ordered. Thus the Yellow Emperor, the so-called disturber of the human heart, must have been speaking in allegory. The meaning of this text aligns precisely with that.

Mount Liegu in the midst of the sea and rivers is recorded in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. Upon it dwells a divine person—serene and still, hence called a divine person. They inhale wind and drink dew, eating no grain; having abandoned grain, why would they still need to inhale wind or drink dew? This describes the appearance of breath cultivation, no different from other things. Their heart is like a deep spring, their form like a maiden—having reached the ultimate of softness and emptiness, their natural endowment is pure, not declining through cultivation. They show no partiality or attachment—partiality is also attachment—treating all things like straw dogs, with no favoritism. Immortals and sages serve as their ministers; immortals are the traces of long life, sages the names of ordered governance. They show no fear or anger, and the sincere and guileless serve them. How could there be any lordship or servitude here? High and low, long and short, each occupies its proper place—this is merely a figurative expression.

They do not bestow favors, yet all things are sufficient; they do not hoard or accumulate, yet nothing is lacking. Yin and yang are always harmonious, sun and moon always bright, the four seasons always in order, winds and rains always balanced, nurturing always timely, grains always abundant. The land knows no pestilence, people no premature death or misfortune, things no blemish or plague, and ghosts no supernatural manifestations. Heaven and humanity unite in virtue, yin and yang follow their sequence, darkness and light observe their measure, and disasters do not arise. Thus the Dao harmonizes the two modes and accords with the four seasons. As *Laozi* says: "Those who govern the world with the Dao—ghosts have no spirit."

Lu said: This speaks of the spirit uniting with the Dao, hence it borrows from the midst of what lies beyond form, designating it as a divine person. It does not rely on grain for nourishment but preserves wholeness through the inhalation and exhalation of true qi. The heart is like still water, incapable of stirring waves in the form; like a maiden, free from the entanglements of thought. Joy and anger do not enter their breast, right and wrong do not interfere with their function. Without seeking or desiring, they align with Heaven and Earth’s non-benevolence; without bestowing or giving, they rectify the generation of the myriad things by yin and yang, which cannot disturb them. Ghosts and spirits cannot attest to their truth—such is their achievement.

*Zhenghe*: The spirit is that which subtly brings forth the myriad things. Embodying the spirit’s subtlety and transcending form and calculation, one can overcome things without burden. Inhaling wind and drinking dew, eating no grain, one is not intent on nourishment. The heart like a deep spring, the form like a maiden—one is tranquil and undivided. Showing no partiality or attachment, immortals and sages serve as ministers; showing no fear or anger, the sincere and guileless serve them—thus one moves in harmony with the Dao. Such a one acts with ease and non-action, and yin and yang are harmonious and still; the multitude of beings is unharmed. Thus they do not bestow or favor, do not hoard or accumulate. Yin and yang are harmonious, the four seasons are in order, nurturing is timely, grains are abundant, people know no premature death or misfortune, things know no blemish or plague, and ghosts know no supernatural manifestations. This is how the sage subtly completes the myriad things without omission.

Fan said: The spirit is that which subtly brings forth the myriad things. The myriad things are at the end of the Dao. Those who embody the spirit dwell above the myriad things, seeing nothing in them that could disturb their heart—thus they overcome things without burden. If not a divine person, who could dwell on Mount Liegu? Inhaling wind and drinking dew, eating no grain, they are not intent on nourishment. The heart like a deep spring is still and reverent, not flowing; the form like a maiden is soft and compliant, without resistance.

Neither clinging nor coveting, they do not cultivate favor to draw near, yet immortals and sages truly establish this as their rule. Neither fearing nor resenting, they do not commit wrongs to estrange, yet the sincere and guileless truly serve as their instruments—detached from all things. They bestow no favors, yet all things naturally prosper; they take nothing for themselves. The Dao suffices to command yin and yang, so yin and yang remain harmonious, free from calamities of disorder. The Dao suffices to assist the sun and moon, so sun and moon remain luminous, free from eclipses or dimming. Moving the numbers with the Dao, one accords with the four seasons. Moving the qi with the Dao, one equalizes wind and rain. Thus, they never disrupt the tranquility of the six qi in nurturing all living beings, yet their nurturing is always timely. They never seize the essence of heaven and earth to aid the five grains, yet the harvests are always abundant, and the soil suffers no blight. People delight in their lives, knowing no premature death or misfortune, and fulfill their destinies. Things attain their natures, free from plague or blemish. Ghosts lose their numinosity, emitting no supernatural manifestations. This is the Dao—who but the most divine under heaven could partake in this?

Liezi studied under Master Lao Shang and befriended Bo Gaozi, advancing in the Way of these two masters until he could ride the wind and return. *Zhuangzi* says: Liezi traveled by riding the wind, soaring lightly and gracefully, returning after fifteen days. This is how the divine person, Yu Kou, described him.

Lu said: When the spirit is mired in being, the hundred limbs are all obstructed; when the spirit accords with the true, the five senses are all unobstructed. With unobstructedness, nothing is too distant to perceive; with no impediments, one rides the wind and travels. Those who don feathered robes to come and go, or entrust themselves to scales and fur to leap and bound, do so as a matter of constant principle—not because they prize the extraordinary, but because this is the meaning of their allegory.

*Zhenghe* says: The Classic states: "The good traveler leaves no tracks." Though riding the wind leaves no visible trace, it still depends on something. Only the spirit arrives without moving.

Yin Sheng, having heard of this, went to live with Liezi. For months, he did not return home. He then asked repeatedly to be taught the art, but after ten requests, Liezi refused ten times. Yin Sheng grew resentful and asked to leave, but Liezi gave no command. Yin Sheng withdrew. Months later, his resolve unbroken, he returned again. Liezi said: "Why do you come and go so frequently?"

Yin Sheng replied: "Formerly, Zhang Dai (Yin Sheng’s courtesy name) made a request of you, but you did not grant it, so I harbored resentment. Now, my heart is suddenly at ease, so I have come again."

Liezi said: "Before, I took you for one who understood. Now, do you show such vulgarity? Ji, I will tell you what I learned from my master."

*Zhenghe* says: Three questions met with no answer; ten requests met with no teaching—the Dao cannot truly be spoken. In the end, he told him only what he had learned from his master.

Fan said: The Dao does not answer questions; questions receive no response. Thus, the ancients had those who asked three times and received three "I do not know"s, or four times and four refusals. With Yin Sheng and Liezi, ten requests went unanswered—was this not because he wished him to grasp the Dao through his own heart and silently comprehend it? Yet he grew resentful, even indignant enough to ask to leave—how could his vulgarity be so great? Thus, Liezi had no choice but to tell him what he had learned, as if speaking without speaking.

Lu said: When you left before, I thought you had grasped my Way. Now, seeing you return to assuage your resentment, I know your shallowness.

Since I began serving my master (referring to Lao Shang) and befriending such a one (referring to Bo Gaozi), after three years, my heart dared not entertain thoughts of right or wrong, my mouth dared not speak of profit or harm—only then did my master deign to glance at me once. Truly harboring profit and harm yet not daring to speak is to conceal resentment and hide one’s feelings, so he merely glanced.

Lu said: Devoted and unwavering in reverence, one seeks only to observe my form and study my conduct.

After five years, my heart *geng*-thought of right and wrong, my mouth *geng*-spoke of profit and harm (*geng* should read *geng*, "again"). Only then did my master relax his expression and smile. Right and wrong, profit and harm are the common principles of the world—letting the heart think as it will, the mouth speak as it will, without arrogance or stinginess in the breast, inner and outer as one—is this not far beyond concealing and not revealing? Delighted by this restraint, he smiled in passing.

Lu said: After three years, devoted to stillness, he merely glanced. After five years, transcending devotion, his smile became clear.

After seven years, following the heart’s thoughts, *geng* without right or wrong; following the mouth’s words, *geng* without profit or harm—only then did my master draw me to sit beside him on the same mat. What is the heart? It is stillness without intention or thought.

What is the mouth? It is silence that naturally inhales and exhales. If one follows the heart’s utmost, then there is no right or wrong; if one follows the principle of the mouth, then there is no profit or harm. When the Dao harmonizes with master and friend, standing side by side in the same position, this is fitting.

Lu said: Examine carefully before speaking to ensure right and wrong, profit and harm do not lead one astray. After nine years, whether the heart’s thoughts are unrestrained or the mouth’s words are unchecked, one no longer knows one’s own right or wrong, profit or harm, nor that of others; one no longer knows the master as one’s teacher or others as one’s friends—inner and outer have advanced. Since the heart has no thoughts and the mouth no contradictions, one may indulge in thoughts and give free rein to speech, embodying the Dao and exhausting its essence, becoming a bridge for the world. Though one thinks all day, it is not one’s own thought; though one speaks all day, it is not one’s own speech. If one takes non-thought as thought and non-speech as speech, one has not yet reached the ultimate. The saying “non-action yet nothing is left undone” is like this—where then can one seek the difference between self and other? Where can the relationship between teacher and disciple be applied? Thus it is said: inner and outer are complete.

Lu said: With no heart at all, right and wrong, profit and harm are not chosen before speaking—words flow freely. When the heart is unrestrained and speech follows, all accords with this Dao.

Fan said: The manifestation of right and wrong is how the Dao becomes deficient; the arising of profit and harm is how emotions and falsity become entangled. In Liezi’s study, after three years, the heart dared not think of right or wrong, yet the heart still chose what to think; the mouth did not speak of profit or harm, yet the mouth still chose what to say. Thus, he merely received a glance from the master—yet the Dao resided in that instant of eye contact. After five years, the heart *geng* thought of right and wrong, so the heart no longer chose, though it had not yet dissolved right and wrong; the mouth *geng* spoke of profit and harm, so the mouth no longer selected, though it had not yet forgotten profit and harm. Thus, the master first relaxed his expression and smiled—his heart was in perfect accord. After seven years, following the heart’s unrestrained thoughts, *geng* without right or wrong, right and wrong were dissolved, though one had not yet reached non-thought; following the mouth’s unchecked speech, *geng* without profit or harm, profit and harm were forgotten, though one had not yet reached non-speech. Thus, the master first drew him to sit beside him on the same mat—understanding passed between them in an instant. When the heart’s thoughts are unrestrained, thought arises from non-thought; when the mouth’s speech is unchecked, speech arises from non-speech. Who is right, who is wrong? What is profit, what is harm? Self and other are both forgotten, merging into the Dao—how then could one know the master as one’s teacher or others as one’s friends?

The sequence begins with one, progresses through five, culminates at seven, and reaches completion at nine. Those who studied the Dao in ancient times: some attained mastery over life after nine days, some resolved their doubts after nine transmissions, some achieved great subtlety after nine years. This is because the order of entering the Dao reaches its end here, advancing into the realm beyond calculation. Thereafter, the eye is like the ear, the ear like the nose, the nose like the mouth—all are the same.

Lu said: The eye, ear, mouth, and nose do not employ their usual functions; each is entrusted without the heart’s involvement—thus it is said they are no different.

The heart congeals, the form dissolves, bones and flesh completely fuse. One does not sense the body’s support or the feet’s tread, drifting east and west with the wind like a dry leaf or husk. Does the wind ride me, or do I ride the wind? The eye, ear, nose, and mouth each have their appointed roles. When spirit congeals and form is abandoned, with no reliance on externals, then sight and hearing do not depend on the eyes and ears, scents and tastes do not rely on the nose and mouth. Thus, the six viscera and seven orifices, the four limbs and hundred joints, sit inert as a corpse, all becoming one thing. Where then does the body lean? Where do the feet tread? Whether I ride the wind or the wind rides me—who can distinguish?

Lu said: When spirit congeals, it does not move. When form dissolves, it is without obstruction. When bones and flesh completely fuse, one forgets the physical frame. Forgetting the frame’s destination, the spirit’s thoughts detach from attachment—then one rises and falls with the wind and air.

*Zhenghe* says: After three years, one is no longer deluded, so the master first glanced. After five years, one is no longer obscured, so he relaxed his expression and smiled. After seven years, one is no longer burdened, so he drew him to sit beside him on the same mat. After nine years, right and wrong, profit and harm cannot be distinguished—self and other are both forgotten, the five senses interpenetrate, the wind rides me, I ride the wind—where is there room for the heart’s involvement?

If you dwell in the master’s gate but have not yet passed a full cycle of time, yet resentment and grievance arise again and again—your single limb will not be borne by qi, your single joint will not be supported by the earth. Employing emotion and possessing a body, flesh and bone cannot coexist; the whole being will have nowhere to reside—how could heaven and earth cover and bear it? How could one approach riding the void and mounting the wind?

Yin was greatly alarmed, held his breath for a long while, and dared not speak again.

Lu said: Liezi could ride the wind because he forgot his body. *Laozi* says: “The reason I have great trouble is that I have a body. If I no longer had a body, what trouble would I have?” If one does not forget the physical frame, then the weight of a single joint is more than the earth can bear—how could one have leisure to ride the wind and soar through the void?

*Zhenghe* says: Those who attain the Dao forget the heart—how much more should they forget resentment and grievance? If a single limb is not borne by qi and a single joint is not supported by the earth, then your body will no longer be yours—how could you possess the Dao?

Fan said: When the six senses interpenetrate, all things communicate; when the six apertures conflict, all things are obstructed.

Those who advance in the Dao have eyes like ears, ears like noses, noses like mouths—unaware of how they see or hear, unaware of what they take in or taste, all merging seamlessly without the slightest doubt in the heart. Like dead ashes, the form is released; like withered wood, flesh and bone dissolve entirely, and one enters the great communion.

Thus, when the body leans, it leans without knowing the body; when the feet step, they step without knowing the feet. Drifting east and west with the wind, like a leaf or a dried husk. Then, in movement, the heavenly mechanism flows without tracks or traces—whether the wind rides me or I ride the wind, how can one distinguish the difference?

Liezi’s attainment of the Dao reached this point—it may be called perfection. When Zhuang Zhou spoke of being “dependent on something,” was he not referring to the very threshold by which one enters?

Liezi asked Guan Yin: “The perfected person moves in concealment without emptiness.” “Without emptiness” means possessing substance. The perfected person’s actions and stillness do not treat this substance as an obstacle. Guo Xiang said: “Their heart is empty, thus they can master all things substantial.”

They tread fire without feeling heat, walk above the myriad things without trembling. Xiang Xiu said: “The world delights in exalting them without tiring—it is not their own self-aggrandizement, hence they do not tremble.”

Liezi asked: “How does one reach this state?”

Guan Yin replied: “It is the preservation of pure qi, not the realm of cunning, skill, or boldness. Utterly pure and utterly true, this is one’s inherent nature, not sought from without. Guard it carefully without loss, and nothing can harm you—how could mere cleverness, calculation, or courage enable one to endure hardship and peril?”

Lu said: “The perfected person moves in concealment, accumulating virtue not as a foundation, but as emptiness. How can they tread fire without heat or ascend heights without trembling? This shows that pure qi arises from nature; preserving spirit to unite with the Dao enables such attainment—hence they are called perfected. How could cunning, skill, or boldness achieve this?”

Ji, the fish speaks to you. (Note: “Fish” should read “I.”) All things with form, sound, or appearance are merely objects. From the sage above to the insects below, all are beings of form and sound. Observing one another through form and sound, there is no absolute distinction. How can one object be distant from another?

Xiang Xiu said: “Only the heartless stand apart.”

What suffices to surpass others? Merely form. Xiang Xiu said: “All are beings of form and color—nothing suffices to surpass another. What surpasses is only spontaneity.”

Lu said: “All that has form is an object; all objects are equal—how can form be later or earlier? Those who arbitrarily rank things are deeply deluded. Only by forgetting form, preserving spirit, and cultivating stillness to generate wisdom can one communicate with the divine. Then one reaches the formless and rests where there is no transformation.

If existence has no beginning, then what is created is formless; if form has no end, then where it rests is without transformation.” *Zao* is pronounced like *zuo*.

Those who grasp this and exhaust it—how could they not be correct? To trace form and sound in pursuit of their beginning and end—how could one reach the ultimate? Lu said: “Forgetting form and preserving spirit, one reaches the formless. Treasuring truth and uniting with the Dao, one rests where there is no transformation. If one attains this Dao and exhausts principle to fulfill nature, how could one not be correct?”

They dwell in a measure not excessive, observing form and color without seeking their beginning or end, thus not losing the correctness of spontaneity. *Shen* should read *yin*. And they conceal themselves in the boundless thread, for where is the ultimate principle hidden? Acting without attachment, they are obscure and traceless, with no visible edges or limits.

Roaming through the beginning and end of the myriad things, those who ride principle without intention always journey alongside them—how could they not progress through beginning and end? Lu said: “The perfected person does not lose virtue, hence does not exceed their measure. Their conduct does not lose the path, hence they conceal themselves without bounds. Always returning to their root, they roam the beginning and end of the myriad things.”

Unifying their nature, nurturing their qi, containing their virtue, they communicate with what things create. Those whose qi is unified and virtue pure—how could they merely communicate with themselves? Where things reach, they remove all obstacles, then universally aid living beings. *Zao* is pronounced like *cao*.

Lu said: “Nature untainted and true unites with heaven and earth in virtue, communicating with the nature and destiny of the myriad things. Those like this preserve heaven completely, their spirit unobstructed—how could things enter? When the natural endowment is unharmed, form and spirit are whole and one—how could sorrow or misfortune enter?”

Lu said: “Treasuring the Dao preserves nature; eliminating emotion leaves no gaps, no traces. How could external things enter?”

Fan said: “The harmony of balanced qi is what all receive in common. When one engages with things and forgets to return, they may falter. Only the preservation of pure qi, single-minded and unadulterated, harmonious and without violence, attains utmost emptiness—how could cunning, skill, or boldness compare?

Why? Form, sound, and appearance—no thing is different. Thus, one thing is fundamentally no more distant from another.”

How could such things reach completion? They begin with their allure—mere appearance. Thus, the completion of things lies beyond form, not in being formed by form; it rests where nothing transforms, not in being transformed by transformation. If one attains this and exhausts it, how could external things find a place to halt?

They dwell within the measure of no excess, never transgressing. They conceal themselves in the boundless thread, never exhausting. Roaming through the beginning and end of the myriad things, they move with the Maker of Things yet do not act. In this way, they guard the spirit by withdrawing into seclusion, retreating into the hidden—how could things find a way in?

Thus, they move in concealment without obstruction, for solidity cannot hinder them; they tread upon fire without burning, for flame cannot scorch them; they walk above the myriad things without trembling, for height cannot endanger them. When a drunkard falls from a cart, though the speed be great, he does not die. His bones and joints are the same as others’, yet the harm he suffers differs—his spirit remains whole. He does not know he is riding, nor does he know he has fallen.

This uses the coarse to illustrate the necessity of the utmost principle. Life and death, alarm and fear do not enter his breast; thus, he clashes with things yet does not break.

Xiang Xiu said: “He encounters them without terror.”
Though he is preserved by wine, it is still so—
Xiang Xiu said: “Drunkenness causes him to lose what he knows, not that he is naturally without mind.”
How much more so when one is preserved by Heaven?
Xiang Xiu said: “One wholly preserved by Heaven is naturally without mind, yielding and following the utmost principle.”
The sage conceals himself in Heaven, so nothing can harm him.
Guo Xiang said: “Not peering beyond the bounds of one’s nature and destiny—this is called concealment.”

Lu said: “The drunkard’s spirit is not united with the Dao. Yet because he is wholly preserved by wine, fear and dread do not enter his celestial storehouse; life and death do not harm his form and spirit. If one is wholly preserved in spirit, then things cannot harm him.”

*Zhenghe* states: The perfected person is divine. “Purity” means not impairing one’s spirit. The Way of purity and simplicity guards only the spirit. Guard it without loss, and become one with the spirit. Thus it is said: The perfected person moves in concealment without obstruction, treads upon fire without burning, walks above the myriad things without trembling. This is the preservation of pure qi, not the realm of cunning, skill, or boldness.

Form, appearance, sound, and color—all have names and realities. Once names and realities exist, they cling to stillness and movement. Once they receive their completion, form does not perish until it is exhausted—how then could they be distant from one another? How could they alone reach what precedes them?

The Dao as a thing completes what is formless, yet does not pair with things; it rests where nothing transforms, thus standing alone and not being moved by things. If form, color, name, and sound are truly insufficient to grasp its essence, then those who attain this and exhaust it—how could they be considered correct?

The perfected person in this regard dwells within the measure of no excess, thus acting appropriately without transgression; conceals himself in the boundless thread, thus moving without exhaustion; advances through the beginning and end of the myriad things, thus roaming with the Maker of Things. Unifying his nature without duality, nurturing his qi without depletion, containing his virtue without dispersion, he connects with what is called “completing the formless” and “resting where nothing transforms.” His heavenly endowment remains whole, his spirit without gaps—how could things find a way in? This is why he moves in concealment without obstruction, treads upon fire without burning, walks above the myriad things without trembling.

The drunkard riding in the cart is preserved by wine, thus able to resist things without breaking. The perfected person walks above the myriad things, concealed in Heaven, thus able to overcome things without being harmed—both preserve pure qi and do not impair their spirit.

Fan said: Tracing form to its beginning, Heaven and Earth are born with me; tracing numbers to their origin, the myriad things are one with me. What thing can be called a cart? What thing can be called a person? What thing can be called a fall? What thing can be called harm? Once Heaven opens and humans engage in construction, they grasp things as real—what they see is truly a cart. Recognizing the self as substantial, what they know is truly a fall. Once knowledge and perception are established, riding and falling are distinguished—how could there be no harm?

Yet the drunkard wholly preserved by wine has his knowledge extinguished, his perception darkened. Riding, he does not know there is a cart; falling, he does not know there is the ground. Life and death, alarm and fear do not enter his breast. Thus, he clashes with things yet is not daunted. Even one who temporarily entrusts his wholeness to wine is like this—how much more so for one whose nature remains whole, never having departed?

The world is but a single cart. Those who rely upon it and ride atop it open the camp of knowledge and perception, which pursues the disturbance of illusory change. Once disturbed, it will topple into the realm of delusions—not merely harm to bones and joints. The sage conceals himself in Heaven, so nothing can harm him—this is what was meant earlier by “guarding wholeness without gaps.”

Yet to call it Heaven is to oppose it to humanity—if nature is one, how can there be Heaven? To call it concealment is to oppose it to openness—if Heaven is one, how can there be concealment? Examining what completes this, there are indeed things that words cannot discuss.

Lie Yukou shot for Bohun Wuren, drawing the bow to its fullest, the arrowhead reaching the very end. He placed a cup of water on his elbow, his hand steady and firm—the water did not spill. He loosed the arrow, and the arrowhead vanished into the distance.
Guo Xiang said: The arrow departs, the arrowhead gone, then returns in obscurity.
As the arrow was still lodged, another was already in place.
Guo Xiang said: The arrow has just departed, not yet reaching the target, yet another is already placed on the elbow—speaking of the marvel of swiftness.

At that moment, he was like a wooden figure.
Lu said: Drawing the bow to fullness, placing water on his elbow, loosing one arrow while another remains—like a wooden figure.

Will resolute, spirit settled, form unmoving—thus he attained this.

Bohun Wuren said: This is the shooting of one who shoots. Though it exhausts the principles of shooting, it cannot but rely on pride in the thing. It is not the shooting of one who does not shoot. Forgetting whether one can or cannot, though not shooting, one is equal to shooting. Now, let us ascend a high mountain, tread on perilous stones, stand at the edge of a hundred-fathom abyss—could you shoot then? If within there is fear, you will lose your shooting.

Lu said: Relying on one’s skill and resting in one’s form, one examines only what is fitting. This is not to speak of forgetting form and leaving things behind, moving by spirit alone.

Thereupon Wuren ascended the high mountain, trod on perilous stones, stood at the edge of a hundred-fathom abyss. His back receded, two parts of his feet hung over the edge. He bowed to Yu Kou and beckoned him forward. Yu Kou prostrated himself on the ground, sweat streaming down to his heels.

Lu said: To ascend heights and tread peril, yet fear as he did—this is to worry for one’s body, to cherish one’s life. Little does he know that he who is aware of his form only harms his life, while he who forgets his form is precisely the one who preserves it. Yu Kou is merely skilled in shooting, not one who accords with the Dao. Only he who forgets form and perfects spirit, unburdened by all under heaven, attains the shooting of one who does not shoot.

Bohun Wuren said: The perfected person gazes upward into the blue sky, plunges downward into the yellow springs, roams to the eight limits—his spirit and qi remain unchanged.

Guo Xiang said: To *hui chi* is to let go freely. When virtue fills within, spirit overflows without. No matter how distant, deep, or obscure, wherever one is, all is clear. Thus one discerns the pivot of safety and danger yet remains serene and self-possessed.

Now you are flushed with the ambition to follow the eye—within, you are already imperiled.

Guo Xiang said: Unable to grasp the ultimate distinction, you are beset by fear and lose much—how could this be limited to shooting?

Lu said: The person of utmost Dao is self-content between heaven and earth, spirit and qi sovereign alone. Sorrow and joy cannot enter. Now you still tremble with fear like this—how could you be near the Dao? In this, you have merely begun your first practice; you have not yet attained its marvel.

**Zhenghe’s Commentary:**
To draw the bow to full draw speaks of its power; to place a cup of water upon the elbow speaks of its precision. When loosed, the arrowhead and shaft follow in seamless succession—while the first arrow is still in flight, the next already rests upon the string. This describes one who, like a wooden figurine, remains unmoving, for their intent is utterly undivided. Yet this is still but a skill that has not yet penetrated the Dao. Thus it is called *the archery of archery*, not *the archery beyond archery*.

The perfected person gazes upward into the azure heavens—how could this be merely the height of climbing a mountain? They plunge downward into the Yellow Springs—how could this be merely the depth of standing at an abyss? They cast their spirit to the eight directions, their qi and spirit unchanging—how could this be merely turning their back in hesitation, with two parts of the foot still hanging over the edge?

When Liezi prostrated himself upon the ground, drenched in sweat, unable to shoot, it showed that though he guarded his purity without flaw, his spirit had not yet attained its utmost. Thus it is said: *Now you are imperiled within.*

**Fan’s Commentary:**
To draw the bow to full draw is the perfection of holding fullness; to place a cup of water upon the elbow is the perfection of levelness and straightness. When loosed, the arrowhead and shaft follow in seamless succession—this means the first arrow has just been released when the next already overlaps. The next arrow rests upon the string—this means the following arrow is already nocked while the first is still in flight. At such a moment, one resembles a wooden figurine, their intent undivided, just as the saying goes: *gazing upon them, they seem like a wooden rooster.* Such is the archery of archery, and nothing more.

As for the archery beyond archery, it is not merely standing upon a high mountain, treading upon perilous stones, or facing an abyss of a hundred fathoms—though the danger has reached its extreme, one’s heart remains unmoved, and every shot finds its mark. This is why it is called *the archery beyond archery.*

Extending this further, the perfected person gazes upward into the azure heavens and thereby exhausts its height; they plunge downward into the Yellow Springs and thereby fathom its depth. They cast their spirit to the eight directions, their qi and spirit unchanging—this is the utmost greatness and utmost firmness, filling the space between heaven and earth. Who among such as these would still tremble with the intent to follow the eye?

*The Four Commentaries on the True Scripture of Perfect Virtue and Emptiness, Scroll the Fourth—Concluded*
*The Four Commentaries on the True Scripture of Perfect Virtue and Emptiness, Scroll the Fifth*

Emperor Huang’s minister, the Fan clan, had a son named Zihua, who excelled at cultivating his private reputation and was a leader among the wandering knights. The entire state deferred to him; though favored by the Lord of Jin, he held no office yet ranked above the three ministers. Whatever he glanced at askance, the state of Jin ennobled; whatever he spoke of slightingly, the state of Jin demoted—*fei* meaning "to slight." Those who frequented his courtyard equaled the courtiers in number. Zihua had his knights attack one another with cunning and vulgarity, overpower the weak with strength, and though they suffered wounds before him, he paid no heed. Day and night, he made this his sport and amusement, until the state nearly became a custom.

Lu comments: *"Pian shi"* means an intense sidelong glance; *"pian fei"* means thick slander. The knights took his words as the measure of honor and disgrace, so those who frequented his gate were comparable to the court of Jin. Zihua allowed his retainers to indulge in unrestrained debate, sparing nothing, until mutual slander and humiliation became the prevailing custom.

Hesheng and Zibo, the Fan clan’s foremost retainers, went on a journey and passed beyond the outer fields—*shang* meaning the outskirts beyond the suburbs. They lodged at the hut of Tian Geng Shangqiu Kai (Geng should read *Yu*). In the dead of night, Hesheng and Zibo spoke to each other of Zihua’s fame and power, how he could make the living perish, the dead live, the rich poor, and the poor rich.

Lu comments: To make the living perish is to slander them; to make the dead live is to praise them; to make the rich poor is to seize from them; to make the poor rich is to bestow upon them.

Shangqiu Kai, a man of lowly origins and no understanding, took their words to be the work of sagely power. He had long suffered from cold and hunger and had been eavesdropping by the north window. Thereupon, he borrowed provisions and a basket and went to Zihua’s gate. Zihua’s retainers were all from eminent families, clad in white silk, riding in carriages, pacing slowly with haughty gazes. When they saw Shangqiu Kai—old, feeble, with a swarthy face, his clothes and cap in disarray—they all sneered at him (*nie*, pronounced *nu ge*). Soon they grew familiar, insulting and deceiving him, shoving and striking, pushing and dragging—nothing was left undone. Shangqiu Kai never showed anger, and the knights’ tricks were meager, exhausted by their mockery.

Lu comments: *"Fu nong"* means the height of frivolous play; *"xia wu"* means treating him with undue familiarity; *"qi dai"* means acting with reckless presumption; *"dang"* means jostling him; *"bi"* means twisting his limbs; *"ai"* means subjecting him to shame; *"du"* means resisting and repelling him.

Then they went with Shangqiu Kai to a high terrace and, before the crowd, casually declared: "Whoever can leap down will be rewarded with a hundred gold pieces." All clamored to accept.

Lu comments: These were fools mocking him, so they feigned eagerness to comply.

Shangqiu Kai took them at their word and leapt first, his body like a flying bird, alighting on the ground—his bones unbroken, his flesh unharmed. The Fan clan’s faction thought it a fluke and did not yet find it strange. Then they pointed to a secluded bend in the river and said: "There is a treasure pearl in that spot; you can retrieve it by swimming." Shangqiu Kai again dove in and swam beneath the water (*yong* meaning to move submerged). When he emerged, he indeed held a pearl. The crowd began to share their doubt. (*Fang* means "to begin.")

Then Master Hua of Fang ordered that Shangqiu Kai be provided with meat to eat and silk to wear. Soon after, a great fire broke out in the Fan clan’s treasury. Master Hua said: "Whoever can enter the flames and retrieve the brocade will be rewarded according to what they bring out." Shangqiu Kai went without hesitation, entering and exiting the fire unscathed—no soot clung to him, nor was his body singed. The Fan clan’s faction, believing him to possess the Dao, jointly apologized: "We did not know you possessed the Dao and thus deceived you—*dan* meaning 'to deceive.' We did not know you were a divine being and thus humiliated you. You must think us fools, deaf, or blind. We dare ask: what is your Dao?"

Lu comments: Leaping from the terrace as though flying, retrieving the pearl from the water as though on land, entering and exiting fire without harm—Master Hua’s disciples all took him for a divine being possessing the Dao. Yet this man, though deceived and angered, showed no resentment. Surely he must have thought us deaf and blind, yet he still deigned to ask of his Dao.

Shangqiu Kai replied: "I possess no Dao. Even in my own heart, I know not how this comes to be. Nevertheless, there is one thing I can tell you. When your two guests lodged at my home, I heard them praise the Fan clan’s power—how they could make the living perish and the dead live, the rich poor and the poor rich. I believed them without doubt and thus came without delay. Upon arriving, I took your faction’s words as truth, fearing only that my sincerity was insufficient, that my actions fell short. I knew not where to place my body or where harm and benefit lay. My heart was unified, and things offered no resistance—thus it was. Now that I know your faction deceived me, I harbor suspicion within and display caution without. I recall with relief how I was not scorched or drowned, yet my heart trembles with dread. How could I again approach water or fire?"

Lu comments: The *Daodejing* says, "Great wisdom appears foolish"—this resembles but does not fully capture it. Only one whose will is single, without other concerns, and who can suddenly forget their own form may remain untouched by life and death, fear and dread. How much more so for one who merges indistinguishably with the Dao, treasures their spirit to unite with the real, whose wisdom pervades the cosmos, and whose power suffices for all things—how could such feats be merely a matter of one or two? Yet when right and wrong arise in the heart, even water and fire become unapproachable.

Thereafter, when the Fan clan’s disciples encountered beggars or horse-doctors on the road, they dared not insult them but would alight from their carriages to bow. Zai Wo heard of this and reported it to Confucius. Confucius said: "Did you not know? A person of perfect sincerity can move things. They can stir heaven and earth, move ghosts and spirits, traverse the six directions without resistance—how could this be limited to treading perilous heights or entering water and fire? If Shangqiu Kai’s belief in false things did not meet with resistance, how much more so when both parties are sincere? Remember this, my disciples."

Lu comments: Beggars and horse-doctors are lowly people; even fools dare slight them. Yet the Master spoke of the power of perfect sincerity—his reasoning is exhaustive.

*Zhenghe* states: Sincerity gives rise to spirit, and one whose spirit is whole follows the Dao of the sage, embracing spirit in their wanderings. Amid the vulgar world, they remain vast and perfectly unadorned, forgetting schemes, profit, and cunning. In such a person’s heart, life and death, shock and fear cannot enter. Thus, they defy things without fear and walk above the myriad things without trembling. When others deceive them, they respond with sincerity—where could they not go? Thus, Shangqiu Kai leapt from the high terrace, his bones unbroken; he swam to the river’s bend and emerged with the pearl; he entered and exited the fire without soot clinging to him. That he was not scorched was due to sincerity. For the Dao of perfect sincerity enters and unites with spirit, unaware of where to place the body or where harm and benefit lie—thus it can overcome things without injury. Hence, the drunken man who falls from a carriage suffers no harm, and the Yellow Emperor lost his dark pearl only to have Xiangwang retrieve it. As for treading fire without feeling heat, Guan Yin surely deemed it the preservation of pure *qi*. But if cunning resides in the heart, purity is incomplete. When purity is incomplete, the spirit is unsettled, and the Dao cannot bear it. Thus, when Shangqiu Kai realized the deception, recalling with relief his past escapes from scorching and drowning, he trembled with dread—water and fire became unapproachable because cunning arose and doubt took hold. The distance between believing in false things with sincerity and doubting with suspicion is thus vast. Yet the person of perfect sincerity surpasses even this. They can stir heaven and earth, move ghosts and spirits, traverse the six directions without resistance—how could this be limited to treading perilous heights or entering water and fire?

Fan says: Those who wield fame and power to intimidate others can never truly subdue them. But those who embody sincerity in their dealings with things find ease in every encounter. Shangqiu Kai could be said to have embodied sincerity. Thus, hearing praise of the Fan clan, he believed it without doubt; enduring the crowd’s insults, he showed no resentment; leaping from the high terrace, he suffered no injury; retrieving the pearl from the river’s bend, he did not drown; taking the brocade from the fire, he was not scorched. Tracing the reason for this, sincerity resided in his heart, and things offered no resistance—thus it was. If his sincerity could not be harmed by others’ deceit, how much more so for a person of perfect sincerity? Stirring heaven and earth, moving ghosts and spirits, traversing the six directions without resistance—this is no wonder.

King Xuan of Zhou had a herdsman named Liang Yang who could raise wild birds and beasts. He scattered food in the gardens, and even tigers, wolves, eagles, and owls became gentle. Male and female gathered before him, breeding in groups. Different species lived together without attacking or biting. The king, fearing the art would die with Liang Yang, ordered Mao Qiu Yuan to learn it.

Liang Yang said: "I, Yang, am but a lowly servant—what art could I possibly teach you? I fear my lord suspects me of concealing something, so let me speak of my method for raising tigers.

All creatures with blood and qi rejoice when obeyed and rage when opposed—this is their nature. Yet their joy and anger are not wantonly aroused; both arise from opposition. When feeding a tiger, one must not offer it living prey, for it would kill in fury; one fears provoking its rage through slaughter. Nor should one give it whole prey, for it would tear it apart in fury; one fears inciting its rage through exertion. One must time its hunger and satiety, understand the source of its anger."

Xiang Xiu said: "This means avoiding what angers its heart and complying with its nature."

Though tigers and humans are of different kinds, they fawn upon those who raise them—this is compliance. Creatures of disparate natures lovingly fawn upon me because I comply with them. Thus, to kill is to oppose; harming creatures opposes their hearts. How then could I dare oppose them and provoke their anger? Yet neither do I comply to make them rejoice. For joy inevitably turns to anger, and anger inevitably turns to joy—both miss the mean. When extremes are reached, they reverse; this is the inevitable pattern.

Now, my heart holds neither opposition nor compliance, so birds and beasts regard me as one of their own. Thus, those that roam my gardens do not long for towering forests or vast marshes; those that rest in my courtyards do not yearn for deep mountains or secluded valleys—this is the principle at work. The sage’s way of nurturing all living things, allowing each to fulfill its nature, is like a servant’s ability to tend birds and beasts so they do not harm one another."

Lu said: "Though forms and substances differ, spirit and qi are not distinct. Thus, *Zhuangzi* says: 'Seeing their differences, the liver and gallbladder seem as distant as Chu and Yue. Seeing their sameness, the myriad things are one body.' The perfected person unites them through spirit. Birds do not stray in flight, beasts do not scatter in herds—this is because their wills align without opposition. Thus, fierce beasts may be tamed, and sea gulls may be befriended.

Birds and beasts retreat to deep mountains and secluded valleys to preserve themselves and escape human harm. Yet if no threat exists, what difference is there between gardens and forests?"

*Zhenghe* states: "The softest in the world gallops through the hardest—this is the sage’s great path to victory. Tigers and wolves are the fiercest of beasts; eagles and owls, the most rapacious of birds. Yet different kinds dwell together without attacking or biting, content within the gardens—this is because the method of harmonizing and taming them is rooted in principle.

The nature of life and destiny finds peace in compliance. Excessive joy or anger disrupts the balance of yin and yang. To provoke anger is not to comply with the nature of life and destiny. Thus, one who raises tigers times their hunger and satiety, understands the source of their anger—all to comply with their nature.

How could I dare oppose them and provoke their anger? This means not violating their nature. Nor do I comply to make them rejoice, for that would indulge their nature. Joy inevitably turns to anger, anger inevitably turns to joy—both are excesses of the Way. Now, my heart holds neither opposition nor compliance: I neither violate their nature to anger them nor indulge their nature to please them. Thus, their contentment and ease are fitting.

The sage’s way of nurturing life does not allow likes and dislikes to internally harm the body. Extending this to nurturing the myriad things and harmonizing the world—what other method is there but this?"

Fan said: "Creatures with blood and qi cannot be without joy and anger. Thus, though birds and beasts differ from humans, their joy and anger always arise from human opposition or compliance. One who skillfully raises tigers does not dare oppose them to provoke anger, nor does he comply to make them rejoice. Thus, birds and beasts of different kinds regard him as one of their own.

Yet when applied to human affairs, the decline of the human Way is often worse than raising tigers. Only the sage interacts with things without harming them, so things cannot harm him. *Zhuangzi*’s parables, such as the 'Human World' chapter, also speak of raising tigers—this is the principle it clarifies."

Yan Hui asked Confucius: "I once crossed the deep abyss of Shang. The ferryman handled the boat as if by magic. I asked him, 'Can one learn to handle a boat?' He said, 'Yes. Those who can swim may be taught; skilled swimmers quickly master it.'"

Xiang Xiu said: "Their skill comes naturally—speaking of their method, they must be unafraid of boats."

"As for divers, they have never seen a boat yet rise to handle it."

*Su* (起) is pronounced *su* (所六切), meaning "to rise."

Xiang Xiu said: "This refers to those who excel at diving."

*Wu* (骛) is pronounced *mu* (音木).

"I asked him, but he did not answer. May I ask what this means?"

Lu said: "Those skilled at handling boats can learn it. Those adept at swimming have practiced it repeatedly. As for divers, though they have never seen a boat, they rise to handle it—this is a spiritual communion they cannot defy."

*Zhenghe* annotates: "Handling a boat as if by magic means the Dao aids the world in ways beyond comprehension. 'Those who can swim may be taught' refers to their not being drowned by things. 'Skilled swimmers quickly master it' means they have long abided in the Way. As for divers, who have never seen a boat yet rise to handle it—this is wondrous and unknowable. Asking yet receiving no answer means the Dao has reached a point where it cannot be explained to others."

Confucius said: "Ah! You and I have long pondered its words, yet failed to grasp its essence—still, I shall now speak of it. *Ah* is pronounced *yi*. Seeing that handling a boat can be learned is merely pondering its words; not comprehending that divers act by nature is failing to reach its truth. Now I shall explain it to you.

'Those who can swim may be taught'—they treat water lightly. 'Skilled swimmers quickly master it'—they forget the water. Forgetting water means having no fear or caution. As for divers who have never seen a boat yet rise to handle it, they see abysses as hills and a capsized boat as a cart rolling back. Though ten thousand things lie before them, none enter their dwelling—for the spirit resides there. Where could they not find leisure? All they encounter is ease.

One who casts with tiles is skillful; with hooks, hesitant; with gold, confused. *Kou* means to cast with varying objects. Guo Xiang said: The heavier the stake, the more the mind clings. Lu said: Seeing that handling a boat can be learned is merely pondering its words. One who grasps its truth sees water as land, capsizing and drowning do not enter his spirit—where could he not find leisure?

One who casts with tiles sees only skill, hitting the mark without fear of losing the tile. With hooks, he is no longer focused on skill but fears clumsiness in losing the hook. With gold, he dares not hope to hit the mark, only dreads the loss of gold. Thus, past mastery lay in forgetting external things; present fear lies in cherishing them. People know the dual failings of clinging to externals yet covet things to their own ruin.

Skill is one, yet when one clings, externals grow heavy. All who prize externals neglect the internal. Only by forgetting both inner and outer, discarding light and heavy, does one transcend skill and clumsiness."

*Zhenghe* annotates: "*Ah* is the same as *yi*. It is like medicine—intent. Calling it *ah* may imply correcting one’s errors. 'Those who can swim may be taught'—they treat water lightly, so drowning does not burden their form. 'Skilled swimmers quickly master it'—they forget water, so treading its ways brings no change to themselves. As for divers who have never seen a boat yet rise to handle it, they unite self and world, doubting nothing in their actions. Life and death, fear and dread do not enter their breast—how much less the seeds of profit and harm?

This is why they see abysses as hills, a capsized boat as a cart rolling back. Though ten thousand things lie before them, none enter their dwelling—nowhere do they not find leisure. One whose inner resources in the Dao are shallow is easily swayed by external things. Thus, casting with tiles is skillful; with hooks, hesitant; with gold, confused.

Earlier scholars said *kou* means to cast with varying objects, referring to drawing lots or casting hooks. The heavier the stake, the more the mind clings. Thus, with tiles one is skillful; with hooks, hesitant; with gold, confused. Hesitation fears loss alone; confusion is as if lost. Hence, 'those who prize externals neglect the internal.' Neglecting the internal means the heart is bound and cannot rest.

Fan said: "The ancients who rode the Dao and virtue to roam freely emptied themselves of burdens, hollowed their hearts of things. They guarded the spirit, letting nothing enter—where could they not find leisure? Once one clings, how can there be no inner clumsiness?"

Confucius observed at Lüliang. The waterfall plunged thirty *ren*, its rapids surged thirty *li*, where turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles could not swim. He saw a man swimming there and thought him in distress, wishing to die. He sent his disciples along the current to rescue him. After several hundred paces, the man emerged, loosened his hair, and sang as he walked beneath the embankment.

Confucius followed and asked him: "The waterfall at Lüliang plunges thirty *ren*, its rapids surge thirty *li*—where turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles cannot swim. Just now I saw you tread it and thought you in distress, wishing to die, so I sent my disciples along the current to save you. You emerged, loosened your hair, and sang—I took you for a ghost! Examining you, I see you are a man. May I ask if treading water has a method?"

The man said: "No. I have no method. I begin with what is innate, grow with my nature, and complete it with fate. I enter with the swirl and emerge with the surge. *Ji* and *gu* describe water swirling in and surging out. I follow the water’s way without imposing my will—this is how I tread it."

Confucius said: "What do you mean by beginning with what is innate, growing with nature, and completing it with fate?"

The man said: "I was born on the hills and am at ease on the hills—this is innate. *Innate* is like plainness; abiding in true plainness, one finds ease in all encounters. I grew up in water and am at ease in water—this is nature. Following nature’s principles, nothing opposes it. Not knowing why I am so yet being so—this is fate. The principle of spontaneity cannot be known by wisdom; knowing it cannot be known is called fate."

Lu said: "To be born on the hills and find ease in their use, to be born in water and find ease in water—habit becomes constancy. Thus it is said: beginning with what is innate, growing with nature. By habituating to what is innate, one settles into nature; suddenly, the spirit converges to complete fate, gaining it without self-awareness.

Therefore, the *Yi* says: 'Exhaust the principles, fulfill nature, to reach fate.' Fate is the契合 of the spirit-Way. *Zhenghe* states: Where the soft-shelled turtle and fish cannot swim, he swims—thus forgetting the peril of fording. Taking you for a ghost, yet observing you, you are but a man—yet one suspects the numinous. Yet seeking his Way, it is simply following the water’s way without imposing his will.

Those who sink with the *ji* plumb the depths; those who rise with the *gu* skim the heights—all is left to spontaneity. This is what is meant by 'following the water’s way without imposing one’s will.'

Born on the hills and at ease on the hills, he does not lose what is thus; grown in water and at ease in water, he does not oppose what is natural. The spontaneous is not burdened by externals, nor altered within; that by which it is so cannot be known—thus it is said: 'Not knowing why it is so, yet it is so—this is fate.'

Fan said: What is caused by something is innate; what is spontaneous without cause is nature; not knowing why it is so, yet it is so—this is fate.

The Lord of Lüliang sinks with the *ji* and rises with the *gu*, following the water’s way without imposing his will—thus he treads where the soft-shelled turtle and fish cannot swim. Is this due to some partial ability? Then the one who embodies the Way is indeed nowhere unsuited."

Confucius traveled to Chu and, passing through a forest, saw a hunchback catching cicadas as easily as if he were plucking them up. Confucius said: "You are skillful! Is there a method to this?" The man replied: "I have a method. After five or six months of practice, if I stack two pellets and they do not fall, I lose only a fraction; as Xiang Xiu notes: 'If one stacks eighteen and they do not fall, it is due to the steadiness of the hand—thus in catching cicadas, what is lost is no more than a hair’s breadth.' If I stack three and they do not fall, I lose one in ten. If I stack five and they do not fall, I catch them as easily as plucking them. When the hand is perfectly steady, nothing is lost. My stance is like a stump or a broken tree—Cui Zhuan says: 'A stump or broken tree.'—and my arm is as still as a withered branch. Though the world is vast and things are many, I know only the cicada’s wings. I do not turn or sway, nor do I let the myriad things distract me from the cicada’s wings—how could I fail?"

Guo Xiang says: "Abandoning the other, one attains this." Lu says: "At first, when learning to stack pellets, one never succeeds. After half a year of practice, one can stack two without them falling. With unceasing practice, one reaches the point of stacking five without them falling—why is this? My body is like a stump, my arm like a withered branch, my mind fixed and unwavering, free from stray thoughts. Though things are many, my awareness rests solely on the cicada—how could I fail?"

Confucius turned to his disciples and said: "When the will is undivided, it congeals into spirit. 'Divided' means scattered; when intent is focused, it resembles the spirit." This is what the hunchbacked elder meant. Lu says: "With wholehearted focus, one congeals into spirit. The Master saw that the elder had not forgotten the cicada’s wings, thus he congealed into spirit—not that he had attained spiritual communion."

The elder said: "You are one of those in flowing robes—what do you know of such matters? Cultivate your own way, and only then may you speak of it." "Cultivate" means to refine. Refine the arts of benevolence and righteousness you employ, return to the natural Way, and only then may you embody these words. Lu says: "The Master’s disciples are all scholars in flowing robes, using benevolence and righteousness to teach the world, causing it to clamor after fame and profit, exhausting its wit and care, losing its true nature, and straining its spirit—what do they know of this Way? You who leave writings for later generations now wish to use the eloquence of your words to record this Way in writing."

*Zhenghe* says: "Will is the essence of single-minded focus. One who is precise in the Way is nowhere unsuited. His stance is like a stump, his arm like a withered branch—motionless and forgetting his own form. Though the world is vast and things are many, he knows only the cicada’s wings. Without turning or swaying, without letting the myriad things distract him from the cicada’s wings, his sincerity is unshaken, and external disturbances vanish. The longer his focus, the fewer his losses. Thus, at first, he loses only a fraction; with time, one in ten; and later, he plucks them as easily as if they were his own. There is no other reason—his will is single-minded. Single-minded will is called essence. Only the most refined essence in the world can penetrate, only the most divine spirit. Thus it is said: 'When the will is undivided, it congeals into spirit.'"

Fan says: "Catching cicadas is a minor skill. When the will is undivided, one attains the wonder of congealed spirit. Thus, what was earlier called the guarding of pure *qi*—its undivided will is now clear."

There was a man by the sea who loved gulls. Every morning, he went to the shore and followed the gulls, and hundreds of them would come without fail. When the heart is harmonious and the form compliant, creatures do not shun it. "Without fail" should read "in great numbers." His father said: "I hear the gulls all follow you—bring some back for me to play with." The next day, when he went to the shore, the gulls danced above but would not land. When the heart stirs within, the form changes without, and even birds sense it—how much less can human principles be feigned? Thus it is said: "The highest words transcend words; the highest action is non-action. What the wisdom of the world knows is shallow indeed." When words are utterly forgotten, things harbor no doubt. To be limited by what wisdom knows is to miss the mark by far.

Some doubt whether the elder feigned his form to catch cicadas or whether the boy’s harmonious heart allowed the gulls to approach—these two cases seem opposed, yet the boy did not offend the creatures. The foundation of these teachings differs, but though they appear contradictory, they converge in the same truth. The elder demonstrated that even with focused thought, one can avoid startling creatures—how much more so when one attains the profound, when one’s form is like wood or stone? As for the boy, his sincere heart filled his inner being, and his outward demeanor was open and unguarded. Though he had not yet forgotten both benefit and harm or dissolved all suspicion, he was at ease among different creatures, unperturbed by their strangeness. These two parables use the near to illustrate the distant, the minor to clarify the fundamental.

Lu says: "Spiritual communion can be understood through principle, not through emotion. When emotion arises, even flesh and blood suspect one another; when principle arises, all things are without barrier—only then can one discern the subtle and profound. To be one with the myriad things lies in spiritual communion; to be one with living beings lies in the extinction of emotion. To pride oneself on one’s mind and wit is to be far from the Way."

*Zhenghe* says: "The ancients who possessed the Way abandoned cunning and forgot schemes, their inner purity complete. Thus, they could enter the midst of beasts without disturbing the herd or join flocks of birds without disrupting their flight. Birds and beasts did not shun them—how much less humans? For their inner being was empty of intent, and creatures naturally trusted them. When inner purity is incomplete, schemes and stratagems multiply, and thus birds take flight to avoid the harm of nets and arrows. The gulls danced above but would not land because the boy had once abandoned cunning but now relied on it."

The sage does not govern the state through cunning. When he speaks, there is no burden of speech; when he acts, there are no traces of action. To equalize all that wisdom knows is but shallow.

Fan says: The *ou* is a seabird, emerging and submerging like bubbles. When scheming resides in the breast, even the seabirds on the sea merely dance above and do not descend—how much less humans? The sage who engages the world utters ultimate words yet leaves no words; though he speaks, he has never spoken. He performs ultimate non-action; though he acts, he has never acted. Suspicion and doubt do not arise, and his inner purity is fully realized. Entering flocks of birds, he does not disrupt their flight—who would dance above and not descend? But once one equalizes all that wisdom knows, speech may err and action may falter, and one has not yet escaped the burden—how could one deeply penetrate the Way?

Viscount Zhao of Zhao led a retinue of a hundred thousand to hunt in Mount Zhong, calling the great hunt a "royal chase." They set fire to the forests, fanning flames that scorched a hundred *li*. A man emerged from a stone cliff, rising and falling with the smoke and embers. The crowd called him a ghostly being. When the fire passed, he walked out unhurriedly, as if untouched by what he had traversed. Viscount Zhao, astonished, detained him and observed him closely: his form, complexion, and seven orifices were those of a man; his breath and voice were those of a man. He asked, "By what way did you dwell in the stone? By what way did you enter the fire?" The man replied, "What thing do you call stone? What thing do you call fire?" He was utterly unaware of stone or fire—what thing could obstruct him? Viscount Zhao said, "What you emerged from just now was stone; what you traversed just now was fire." The man said, "I do not know." Only through the extremity of not-knowing could he attain this.

Duke Wen of Wei, having heard of this, asked Zixia, "What manner of man is he?" Zixia replied, "From what I have heard of the Master’s teachings, one who harmonizes achieves great unity with all things—nothing can injure or obstruct him. To wander through metal and stone, to tread upon water and fire, all are possible."

Duke Wen said, "Why do you not practice this yourself?" Zixia answered, "To hollow out the heart and cast off knowledge—this I have not yet been able to do. Nevertheless, let me attempt to speak of it when there is leisure. For if one relies on the heart to hollow out the heart, borrows knowledge to discard knowledge, the burdens of heart and knowledge may indeed be exhausted—but the traces of what has been cast off still remain. To realize that the ultimate principle cannot be grasped by the exertions of the heart—this, in speech, is a matter of surplus leisure."

Lu said: The previous chapter spoke of wandering through water without obstruction; this chapter clarifies how fire and stone do no harm. Most men are accustomed to water, but few tread upon fire—lest the common mind cling to partiality, this further discourse on fire serves to elucidate. Within, he forgets his own form; without, he forgets all things. He does not know how stone obstructs or how fire injures.

Duke Wen, not understanding, raised the question. Zixia, having long known the matter, answered well. Thus the Duke pressed further: "Since you are one who knows, why do you not practice it?" Zixia replied, "To know and speak of it is already surplus for me. As for embodying and realizing it—this I have not yet been able to do."
