Duke Wen asked, "Why does the Master not practice it?" Zixia said, "The Master is capable of it but does not act upon it." Duke Wen was greatly pleased.

In the world, there are those capable of something yet able not to do it; those capable of something yet unable not to do it; those incapable yet who force themselves to do it; and those who do not act yet naturally possess the ability. As for the sage—what does he do? What does he not do? What is he capable of? What is he incapable of? He bows and rises with the common world, ascends and descends with all things. Extraordinary deeds and wondrous traces never briefly appear; the sublime subtlety within his being is not one in ten thousand to be seen.

From the beginning of this scroll to this chapter, it has expounded the Way of following one’s nature and destiny. One who is not entangled by the five emotions, who single-mindedly cultivates softness, and whose sincerity is without duality—such a one may dwell in water and fire without scorching or drowning, traverse wood and stone without hindrance, touch blades without injury, and tread perilous heights without falling. No thing in the world opposes his heart; entering the midst of beasts, he does not disturb their herds. His spirit roams alone; his body ascends lightly. His ears penetrate all sounds; his eyes illumine all things.

These words may seem unconventional, truly startling to the common mind. Let us then examine them:

Yin and yang alternate in transformation; the five agents pervade all nurturance. Metal and earth beget one another as mother and child; water and fire contend through dryness and moisture. Human nature diverges in stillness and agitation; ascent and descent differ in their capacities and dispositions. Thus there are winged creatures soaring through clouds, scaled ones lurking in the depths, fire-dwelling mice, and wood-concealing insects. Why? Hardness and softness, heat and cold—each has its proper place. To be confined to one realm is to be hindered in all others.

As for the perfected person, their mind merges mysteriously with primordial qi, their body harmonizes darkly with yin and yang. Neither square nor round conforms to a single form; neither warmth nor coolness is bound to a single vessel. With spirit settled and qi harmonized, all they traverse accords with the Way. Thus the five agents cannot oppose them, nor can cold or heat cause harm. This is called possessing profound virtue and perfect harmony. Hence they dwell perpetually beyond the realm of death—how could deliberate effort or withdrawal preserve them?

Treading water and fire, riding clouds and mist, scaling perilous heights, entering the midst of weapons—these are scarcely worth marveling at.

Lu [Huiqing] says: When the Master *could* act yet did not, it was to employ benevolence, righteousness, ritual propriety, and the Way of ruler and minister to rescue a declining age—not merely to perfect himself among flocks of birds and beasts.

*Zhenghe* [commentary]: When the mind merges with the Dao, they become one, never yet divided. When form shifts with things, the myriad transformations never begin differently. Subject and object stand opposed, touching all things as dualities. Harmonized by Heaven’s spontaneity, why seek distinction? Self and other share a single root; "that" and "this" are one in essence. Without the contrast of emptiness and fullness, passing through stone walls—what could obstruct? Without the friction of benefit and harm, ascending and descending through smoke and embers—what could cause injury? Thus it is said: Harmony unites with things; nothing can harm one so aligned. Obstruction means wandering through metal and stone, treading water and fire—all becomes possible.

Zixia understood this yet could not attain it; the Master could attain it yet did not act. For the Dao is not something the intentional can grasp from afar, nor the unintentional draw near. Thus Zixia said: "Carve out the heart and cast off wisdom"—yet Shang could not achieve this.

The sage conceals himself in Heaven without self-display. That the Master *could* yet did not act was true indeed. Those who toil to wander through metal and stone, tread water and fire, believing themselves possessed of the Dao—such a path opposes the world, making it possible for others to emulate them.

Thus the *Liezi* surveys the teachings of all masters and exalts the Master as the great completion.

Fan [Zhen] says: Stone is solid and obstructive; fire is fierce and burns. One who touches solidity yet is not obstructed—though emerging from stone walls, they know not the stone. One who treads fire yet feels no heat—though moving through smoke and embers, they know not the fire. To enter this Way, one must harmonize and unite with things—only then can it be achieved. For great harmony transcends the human; all things are viewed as one. Wandering through metal and stone, treading water and fire—nowhere is impossible.

If one seeks to carve out the heart yet cannot empty it of things, or to cast off wisdom yet cannot unite with the great flow, then though the words may be flawless, the deed remains unattainable. That the Master *could* yet did not act—was this not because he attained it through the heart?

A divine shaman from Qi arrived in Zheng, named Ji Xian. He knew the life and death, survival and demise, fortune and misfortune, longevity and early death of men, predicting them by year, month, and day with godlike precision. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled in avoidance.

Xiang Xiu says: No one delights in hearing their own death date.

Liezi met him and was utterly enchanted. Xiang Xiu says: He was deluded by his Way.

Returning, Liezi reported to Master Huqiu, saying: "At first, I took the Master’s Dao as the ultimate, yet now there is something even more ultimate."

Guo Xiang says: He meant that Ji Xian’s attainment surpassed even the Master’s.

Master Hu said: "I have shown you only its outward form, not yet its true substance—how then could you have grasped the Dao? A flock of hens without a rooster—how could they lay eggs?"

Xiang Xiu says: True substance is revealed through outward form; the Dao is made manifest through affairs. To possess the Dao yet engage in no affairs is like having hens without a rooster. Though you and I differ in depth, we both abide in the realm of substance—thus no outward form arises to manifest it. Therefore, I have not yet fully revealed the true substance of my Dao. This speaks of how the sage’s song must await a responsive echo. To oppose the world with the Dao is to be truly steadfast—only then can others perceive and assess you."

Xiang Xiu says: Lacking a single method to be steadfast in the world, one becomes assessable.

Lu says: Liezi was captivated upon seeing the Zheng shaman, believing his knowledge of life and death, fortune and misfortune encompassed the entirety of the Dao. Master Huqiu replied: "You and I have merely glimpsed its traces, not yet its true principle—how could you have attained my Dao? To still the spirit and quiet the mind is like a flock of hens; to act and achieve success is like a rooster. You now rest in stillness—how can you seek eggs without a rooster? To pit the ultimate Dao against a vulgar shaman is to be so deeply convinced that even spirits perceive you."

From the *Zhenghe* commentary: The *Zhuangzi* states—
Fan says: The term "spirit" describes the wondrous workings of all things. Shamans, however, deceive with false claims of divine speech. Thus, predictions of life and death, survival and demise, fortune and misfortune, longevity and early death—though seemingly precise to the year, month, or day—appear wondrous but are not truly so; they are merely "spiritual." Though called a "spirit shaman," Ji Xian embodies the wondrous responsiveness of things. To be "Xian" is to be responsive; to respond is to have intent. To impose one’s own intent upon others’ minds, to project one’s own perceptions onto others’ visions—these eight terms (life, death, survival, demise, fortune, misfortune, longevity, early death) are but empty names. Once names arise as empty, one falsely perceives them; once perceived falsely, one speaks of them falsely. Those in the world who cling to appearances and cannot transcend delusion then falsely accept them as true—hence they flee in avoidance. Though Liezi was enchanted upon seeing him, he could not master his own mind, for his understanding of the Dao, even when taken to Master Huqiu, had not yet cut off learning. He had grasped only its outward form, not its true substance. The Dao has never possessed anything, yet nothing exists outside the Dao. Thus, emptiness and voidness have no equal—like a flock of hens without a rooster. To cling to it alone within one’s breast is like seeking eggs without a rooster. To oppose the world with the Dao yet insist on being believed is to fail to stand in the unfathomable and wander in nothingness—thus others can perceive and assess you. Come, bring him to me, that I may show myself to him."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to meet Master Hu. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi: "Alas! Your master is dead—he cannot live. He will not last ten days. I saw something strange in him—like damp ashes."

Liezi entered, weeping so that his tears soaked the bedding, and told Master Hu. Master Hu said: "Just now, I showed him *Earth’s Pattern*."

Xiang Xiu says: Like a clod of earth.

"Motionless yet not inert."

The character *zui* (罪) may also be written as *meng* (萌).

Xiang Xiu says: Sprouting yet unmoving, neither acting nor ceasing—like a withered tree sharing its lack of bloom, like dead ashes sharing their lifeless stillness. This is the sage’s state of non-responsiveness. The sage’s movement is like Heaven; his stillness, like Earth; his conduct, like flowing water; his depth, like a silent abyss. The abyss’s silence and water’s flow, Heaven’s movement and Earth’s stillness—all are naturally one in non-action. Now, Ji Xian saw me dwelling as a corpse and sitting in forgetfulness, and so declared me near death. Yet when he saw my spirit move and Heaven follow, he took it as a sign of life. If one responds without intent, then one ascends and descends with change, measuring the world—only then can one master things and follow the timeless without limit. How could a diviner perceive this? He merely saw me *blocking the subtle stirrings of virtue*."

Xiang Xiu says: Virtue’s stirrings were unmanifest, hence "blocked."

Lu says: Spirits and gods perceive only the movements of the human heart. Where there is attachment, spirits know it. Master Hu’s countenance remained like damp ashes; his mind clung to the soil, sprouting yet without thought—hence *Heaven’s Pattern*. To stir is to live; to be still is to be profoundly quiescent—hence "neither moving nor inert."

From the *Zhenghe* commentary: To see something strange is to perceive the extraordinary; damp ashes cannot be rekindled. The ancient sages operated the pivot of the Dao in the boundless—thus no duality could match them. Blocking the subtle stirrings of virtue without manifesting them is like being lost in a daze, as if one’s counterpart were absent—hence showing him *Earth’s Pattern*. Seeing me *blocking the subtle stirrings of virtue*, he declared me unable to live. Earth shares its virtue with Yin, yet its affairs and patterns are one in stillness and manifestation—hence "neither quaking nor inert." "Not quaking" means not stirring; "not inert" means not clinging.

Fan says: All forms are of Earth. With form comes the strange; with mind comes the perception of strangeness. When Earth unites with emptiness and mind unites with nothingness, the Dao flows as one—what then is strange? The ancient sages’ minds were like dead ashes, their depths unfathomable. Those who perceive with delusion see only the strange.

"Not quaking" means it has never stirred; "not inert" means it has never clung. The subtle stirrings of that which is neither quaking nor inert—this is what *Earth’s Pattern* reveals, and it is precisely so. Is this not seeing me *blocking the subtle stirrings of virtue* without manifesting them?

Once again, he came. The next day, he was brought to see Master Huchi. Upon leaving, he said to Liezi: "Fortunate indeed that your master has met me—he will recover. There is life now, like cold ashes rekindled. I have seen *blocked potency*." It is useful yet without gain, hence called *blocked potency*.

Liezi entered and reported this to Master Huchi. The master said: "Earlier, I showed him *Heaven-and-Earth*. As Xiang Xiu says: *Within Heaven-and-Earth, the work of covering and bearing is manifest.* Is this *Earth’s Pattern* not also external? Names and realities do not enter, yet the mechanism arises from the heels." Guo Xiang says: *It constantly arises from the utmost.* This is *blocked potency*. Is this not seeing the subtle stirrings of my goodness? As Xiang Xiu says: *When there is goodness in another, they perceive it—how shallow was Jiyan’s vision!* Lu says: *To possess potency yet not employ it is to block.* Like Heaven’s covering, its work unseen, ascending from below—names and realities have not entered, hence "there is life now." *Zhenghe* says: *The rekindling of life within cold ashes may be understood as the intent of life persisting within what is no longer ablaze.* Earlier, seeing only damp ashes, the intent of life was extinguished. Extinguished, it was deemed lifeless. Now, seeing *blocked potency*, the function of movement is still concealed. Yet since movement exists, it is deemed alive.

I showed him *Heaven-and-Earth*—*Earth* being that which has breath. What is mandated by Heaven finds its breath here, hence *Heaven-and-Earth*. When names and realities do not enter, the true and the deluded are already obscured. When the mechanism arises from the heels, this is the origin of breath—this is *blocked potency*. For the Dao cannot be seen, yet its succession is like this alone. Hence, "Is this not seeing the subtle stirrings of my goodness?"

Fan says: *Names and realities are where things reside; namelessness and formlessness are where things are empty.* When names and realities do not enter and the mechanism arises from the heels, it is as if it has never been, yet never ceased to be. Though still and without action, qi naturally arises from this. This is what is meant by *the true person’s breath reaches the heels.* Showing him *Heaven-and-Earth* was indeed fitting. At first, Jiyan saw only damp ashes and, not recognizing *blocking*, deemed it death. Only upon seeing the wholeness of life did he realize that the earlier cold ashes were *blocked potency*, not true death. Is this not seeing the subtle stirrings of my goodness?

Once again, he came. The next day, he was brought to see Master Huchi. Upon leaving, he said to Liezi: "Your master sits without fasting—or perhaps the character for 'sits' is absent." Xiang Xiu says: *Without going, there is no imbalance—all is merged as one. Those who peer at Heaven through a tube see no bounds, hence the lack of fasting.* I can find no basis to assess him. Let him fast, and I shall assess him again.

Liezi entered and reported this to Master Huchi. The master said: "Earlier, I showed him *Supreme Vacuity without trace*." Xiang Xiu says: *Abiding in the utmost of Supreme Vacuity, the mind is vast and tranquil, darkly one with the ten thousand directions—no trace is visible.* Is this not seeing the subtle stirrings of my *balanced qi*? *Balanced* means level.

The whirlpool of the salamander forms a deep pool; the still water’s backflow forms a deep pool; the flowing water’s backflow forms a deep pool; the overflowing water’s backflow forms a deep pool; the gushing water’s backflow forms a deep pool; the dammed water’s backflow forms a deep pool; the dispersed water’s backflow forms a deep pool; the rich water’s backflow forms a deep pool—these are the *Nine Abysses*. The names and meanings of these waters are found in the *Erya*. Though water is one, following high and low, smooth and rugged, it differs in eddies, surges, flows, and stillness. This resembles the mind of the perfected person, which, in response to external things—difficult or easy—displays movements of activity, stillness, advance, or retreat.

Xiang Xiu says: *Whether water flows or stills, whether the salamander whirls or the dragon leaps, it remains profoundly self-possessed, never losing its quietude and silence.* Guo Xiang says: *The perfected person acts when employed, rests when set aside. Though waves surge through nine transformations, and order and chaos intermingle, abiding in the utmost, they remain serene, self-possessed, and free from action.* Lu says: *The mind moves with the qi of Supreme Vacuity, vast and traceless, yet manifesting form—its turning and flowing are unceasing, like water’s transformations in name, never departing from the clarity of the abyss. Thus, its appearance cannot be discerned or debated.*

*Zhenghe* says: *Earth’s Pattern leans toward Yin prevailing over Yang; Heaven-and-Earth leans toward Yang prevailing over Yin. As for Supreme Vacuity, it contains both Yin and Yang yet is neither Yin nor Yang. Hence, *Supreme Vacuity without trace.* *Trace* is the omen of spirit in things—Yin and Yang are unfathomable, so no trace can be perceived.* Moreover, *Vacuity* is the intersection of Yin and Yang. *Supreme Vacuity without trace* reveals only its perfect balance, hence called the *subtle stirrings of balanced qi*. The alternation of Yin and Yang, intersecting yet without trace, can be understood through the *Nine Abysses*.

*Backflow* means the reversal of current—only through reversal can wholeness be preserved. This backflow is what makes each an abyss. The whirlpool of the salamander forms a deep pool, signifying wholeness in utmost movement; the still water’s backflow forms a deep pool, signifying wholeness in possible stillness; flowing water illustrates interaction with things. Overflowing suggests excess in engagement; gushing water suggests exhaustion in reception. Damming means river water, having emerged, returns—different from exhaustion in reception. Dispersing means having emerged yet not flowing—different from returning after emergence.

Fatness means divergence in emergence yet convergence in return—here lies the full meaning of *backflow* preserving wholeness.

Yet *Zhuangzi* singles out three, for nine divided become three, and three united become one. Fan said: "Earth’s pattern is Yin; Heaven’s expanse is Yang. At their supreme intersection, the alternation of Yin and Yang is like the balanced scales of Wei—hence it is called the *subtle stirrings of balanced qi*. All dharmas are equal, without high or low; disparities arise only from deluded perception, thus nothing can be grasped or compared."

*Laozi* says: "The heart excels in abyssal depth—abyssal, empty, and still, unmingled with things. Though the waves’ transformations may differ, the abyss’s tranquil nature remains ever thus." The *supreme intersection* is much the same.

Once again, he came. The next day, he was brought to see Master Hu. Before standing firm, he lost himself and fled. Master Hu said: "Pursue him." Liezi chased him but could not catch up; returning, he reported to Master Hu: "He has vanished, he is lost—I could not reach him." Master Hu said: "Earlier, I showed him what had never yet departed from my source."

Xiang Xiu said: "Though moving and resting with the crowd, he ever roots deeply in tranquility’s utmost." I met him with emptiness and yielding compliance—Xiang Xiu said: "Without mind, one follows change." Not knowing who or what he was—Xiang Xiu said: "Adrift, unbound by anything." Thus he took me for withered grass, for flowing waves, and so he fled. *Withered grass* should read *crumbling decay*.

Xiang Xiu said: "Transforming like crumbling decay, worldly affairs like flowing waves—wherever one turns, one is shaped by causes, thus becoming what is not oneself. Though I do not act, I move and rest with the crowd. The perfected person is one, yet responds to worldly changes with timely action—thus the physiognomist, unable to employ his mind, lost himself and fled."

Lu said: "Abandoning thought, severing念, entering non-action, utterly empty and formless—his appearance unseen, like grass bending, like waves flowing, vast and drifting, beyond the ken of spirit-mediums."

Fan said: "Ji Xian, moved by intention and perception, each time he entered, declared he saw Master Hu. But Master Hu, without intention yet responsive, each time revealed to him one with intention gazing upon one without. Ji Xian was bound by forms and calculations, never free from seeing—where he saw, he took life and death to reside, with no escape. Thus, when shown *earth’s pattern*, he lamented death; when shown *heaven’s expanse*, he rejoiced in life; when shown the *supreme intersection* without trace, he named it *unsettled*, unable to grasp or compare.

He did not know that the perfected person’s heart, still, shares virtue with Yin; moving, flows in harmony with Yang. Sharing virtue with Yin, he could not be seen—thus he was shown *earth’s pattern*. *Pattern* is what things rely upon for coherence. Flowing in harmony with Yang, he could not be seen—thus he was shown *heaven’s expanse*. *Expanse* is what things rely upon for generation. When shown the *supreme intersection* without trace, Yin and Yang were perfectly balanced, with no partiality or dominance—this is what is called the *center of Heaven and Earth*. All three are called *subtle stirrings*. Movement’s subtlety is visible, hence he could perceive them. But as for *what had never yet departed from my source*—though shown, it remained hidden, and he could not glimpse it. Thus he lost himself and fled, and pursuit could not reach him.

Yet Master Hu told Liezi: 'You nearly saw my *power of closure’s stirrings*'; he also said: 'You nearly saw my *excellent stirrings*'; he also said: 'You nearly saw my *stirrings of balanced qi*'. All spoke of 'my'—thus establishing an 'I'. But when 'I met him with emptiness and yielding compliance, not knowing who or what he was'—even I lost myself. Who was the shower? Who was the gazer?

His stillness was like grass bending, yet he knew not why it bent; his movement was like waves flowing, yet he knew not why they flowed. Seeking me in movement and stillness, he found nothing—hence he fled. Thereafter, Liezi considered himself as one who had never yet studied and returned home, not emerging for three years."

Xiang Xiu said: "He abandoned the near tasks of human affairs." He cooked for his wife—Xiang Xiu said: "He forgot shame."

Feeding pigs as if feeding men—Xiang Xiu said: "He forgot distinctions of noble and base."

In affairs, he held no partiality—Xiang Xiu said: "Neither attachment nor aversion."

Carving and polishing, he returned to simplicity; solitary, he stood alone in his form—Xiang Xiu said: "The ornament of carving and polishing restored his true simplicity, and so external affairs fell away."

Sealed in primordial wholeness—Xiang Xiu said: "His truth remained undispersed."

*(Note: "戎" may also be written as "哉.")*

In this state he remained to the end—Xiang Xiu said: "Thus he attained the Dao."

Lu said: "He forgot right and wrong, equalized noble and base, leveled self and other, stilled external entanglements, ceased to be bound by the self, guarded unity to the end—only then did he accord with the real."

*Zhenghe*: "Never having departed from our origin"—for the sage takes Heaven as his origin. Concealed in Heaven, he never departed from our origin.

*(The remainder is seen in the *Zhuangzi Jie*.)*

Fan said: "To study is to study what cannot be studied. Liezi, in his approach to the Dao, had grasped its ornament but not yet its substance. Thus he considered himself as one who had never yet studied and returned home—to study what could not be studied. Three years he did not emerge, cooking for his wife, feeding pigs as if feeding men—this was the utmost forgetting of self. In affairs, he acted without partiality—this was the utmost attainment of emptiness. Carving and polishing, he returned to simplicity—having carved and polished, he reverted to unhewn wood. Solitary, he stood alone in his form—seeming to have left things and departed from men, standing in solitude. Sealed in primordial wholeness, he remained thus to the end—though myriad threads tangled and disturbed, he remained unshaken, unchanged to the last."

*End of the Fifth Scroll of the Four Commentaries on the* Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing.
*Beginning of the Sixth Scroll of the Four Commentaries on the* Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing.

Emperor Huangdi, on his way to Qi with Master Liezi, turned back mid-journey, startled that others deferred to him with such reverence—thus he dared not proceed. He encountered Bohun Maoren, who asked: "Why have you returned?"

"I was startled," he replied.

"Startled by what?"

"I dined at ten taverns selling broth, yet at five of them, the broth was offered to me first. All showed deference to one beneath them."

Bohun Maoren said: "If this is so, why are you startled?"

He answered: "Though inwardly I do not seek it, outwardly I am adorned with false dignity. My mannerisms become polished radiance, outwardly overawing others’ hearts—yet within, I am insufficient. This causes men to slight their elders and honor me instead, bringing upon me what I detest. Those broth-sellers deal only in food and surplus profit—meager gain, trifling influence—yet even they defer thus. How much more so a lord of ten thousand chariots, whose body toils for the state and whose wisdom is spent on affairs? He would entrust me with tasks and demand results of me. This is why I am startled."

Extending this principle: the lesser the gain, the slighter the expectation; the heavier the burden, the greater the demand.

The *Zhenghe* commentary states: The utmost person of antiquity, clear and plain, practiced non-action and returned to simplicity. The celestial mechanism remained unstrung; silently, he harmonized with the Dao—dim as if absent yet present, effortless as if formless yet spirit-filled. Thus, others knew him as rare and valued him. If inner sincerity remains unresolved, the heart cannot be forgotten. If mannerisms become polished radiance, the form cannot be transcended. Outwardly overawing others’ hearts, causing men to slight their elders and honor oneself—while one’s body toils for the state and one’s wisdom is spent on affairs—then the afflictions of sorrow and the comforts of joy will alternately assail the body, while the terrors of urgency and the joys of relief will mingle and drown the heart. Bringing upon oneself what one detests—how could one not be startled?

Master Fan said: The sage wears coarse cloth yet holds jade within, preserving his form. The living conceal themselves—not disdaining depth and obscurity alone. If inner sincerity remains unresolved, one has not attained the Dao and forgotten the heart. If mannerisms become polished radiance, one has not preserved inner light without display. Dining at ten broth-sellers’ stalls and having five offer first—this is to outwardly overawe others’ hearts, causing men to slight their elders and honor oneself. The utmost person dwells motionless in a humble chamber, yet the people wander wildly, knowing not where to turn. To now cause men to slight their elders and honor oneself is to bring upon oneself what one detests—self-inflicted harm. How could this be the way of one who wears coarse cloth yet holds jade within, concealing himself?

Bo Hun Maoren said: "Excellent observation! You recognize what startles men—this is true discernment. If you master yourself, others will safeguard you. Should you remain silent and unassuming, wherever you dwell—even in Qi—none shall fear you. But if you violate this principle, you will be guarded wherever you go."

Lu said: "When one displays imposing demeanor to others, men will risk death to honor him. Yet if a ruler wearies himself with affairs, he must delegate responsibility for achievements, and with rank comes increased anxiety—this is the source of their alarm."

Bo Hun replied: "If you withdraw to preserve your true nature, concealing your radiance to erase your traces, others will safeguard you. Why? The desire to advance goodness resides in all men. Where profit abounds, all compete for it. The nature of ordinary men may rise or fall; when they recognize that fame and gain cannot be forced, they advance goodness to cultivate themselves. The study of *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music* is no easy task. But if one retreats to obscurity, stilling the heart and extinguishing cleverness, free from the lure of ostentation, one gains the reputation of a good man. Thus, in the gateway of Dao-learning, both good and ill converge. The nobleman purifies his heart; the petty man reforms himself. Though they may not fully grasp it, they still benefit from following goodness."

He continued: "Thus I say, others will safeguard you." Soon after he departed, shoes filled the doorway. When the crowd returned, Bo Hun Maoren stood facing north, leaning firmly on his staff, pressing it against his chin. After standing for a while, he departed without a word.

The attendant informed Liezi. Liezi rushed barefoot, shoes in hand, and upon reaching the gate, asked: "Master, since you have come, have you not set aside your medicine?" (Here, *set aside* means *to neglect*—as if no healing words were offered in place of medicine.)

Lu said: "*Set aside* should read *dispense*—Master, since you have come, why not offer the medicine of your words, if only a little instruction?"

Bo Hun replied: "Enough. I already told you others would safeguard you—and indeed, they have."

"Yet it is not that you can make others safeguard you. When you align with principle in engaging with things, then things will not safeguard you. Now, turning your back on principle to move things, seeking that things not safeguard you—this cannot be attained. And you cannot cause others to not safeguard you.

Guo Xiang said: *When one acts with equanimity and transforms, there is no stirring, no seeking. Without stirring, without seeking, there is no mutual safeguarding.*

Lu said: *Your withdrawing the self and preserving integrity, severing learning and abandoning cunning—this is why others safeguard you, not because you can summon them. If you could erase traces and merge with the real, so that foolishness and wisdom were not manifest, others would likewise not know to safeguard you. From this we see: your conduct is precisely what makes others safeguard you, yet you cannot cause them not to do so. Why then stir them? By what art do you move things in this way?*

*Responding in advance with the extraordinary.*

Guo Xiang said: *Bestowing kindness before things arise—such kindness, not contingent on their prior response, is extraordinary. Yet there must be stirring. To shake the root of one’s own body—this is meaningless. If you must constantly make things stirred by you, then the natures of self and other are disturbed and altered.*

*Those who wander with you tell you nothing. They are all followers who shake the root, unable to awaken one another. Their petty words are all poison to others. Petty words, subtle and clever, easily move people—thus they harm others. Unaware, unenlightened, how can they examine one another? They cannot aid or complete one another.*"

Lu said: By what Dao do you move them? You must extol the virtues of preemptive kindness and expound extraordinary teachings, wagging your tongue to display your ability before all things. Though they may yield and follow you, what benefit does it bring? Those who dwell with you do not criticize your faults but only praise your strengths—this merely poisons your conduct, inflates your pride, and how can it lead to mutual completion?

*Zhenghe’s commentary: How excellent is the one who observes inwardly! He who masters himself is told to withdraw and conceal. The perfected person embraces the spirit and wanders among the mundane, allowing no one to glimpse his depths—thus he must master himself. If he does not master himself, others will seek to preserve him. For others to preserve you is not what is meant by "allowing no one to glimpse your depths."*

Therefore, respond only after being moved—do not seek to be extraordinary. This is how you ensure others have no means to preserve you. To respond preemptively is to act only after being moved; to display the extraordinary is to seek distinction. Those who do so not only invite others to preserve them and perceive them as exceptional, but they must also stir things, shaking the root of their own body, inevitably burdening others. Those who wander with you tell you nothing, leaving you without self-awareness. Their petty words are all poison to others, bringing only harm. Unaware, unenlightened, how can they return to the Dao?

Thus it is said: How can they examine one another? To "examine one another" means to mutually refine and bring to completion. *Fan’s commentary: To respond only after being moved, to embody the nature and embrace the spirit while wandering among the mundane—this is how you ensure others have no means to preserve you. To respond preemptively is not to act only after being moved; to display the extraordinary is not to wander among the world. If you employ such methods, you join the company of others, and ten thousand from Dengxu’s household will follow you, while the multitudes of Qi will value you thrice over. They will worship you as a corpse and sacrifice to you as a spirit. Even the humble folk of Weilei will whisper among themselves, eager to honor you among the worthy, removing their shoes at your door—such followers will soon fill your home.*

How can one who must stir things, shaking the root of their own body, ever harmonize and communicate without losing their true bearing? Thus, those who offer no guidance but only petty words become poison to others, harming their nature. And those who remain unaware and unenlightened cannot refine one another to completion.

Then, when you are offered ten jars of fermented drink and five are sent ahead as tribute, will you be truly startled?

Yang Zhu traveled south to Pei, while Laozi journeyed west to Qin, meeting by chance at the outskirts. They arrived in Liang and encountered Laozi. *The Zhuangzi states: Yangzi Ju—Ju being perhaps Yang Zhu’s courtesy name—did not wait for Laozi’s timing, for this is all allegory.* Midway, Laozi looked up to the heavens and sighed: "At first, I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot." For one who wanders with the perfected yet cannot shed their pride and boasting, it is said: "You cannot be taught." Yang Zhu made no reply.

Upon reaching the lodging, he presented water for washing, a towel, and a comb, removed his shoes outside the door, and advanced on his knees, saying: "Earlier, Master, you looked up to the heavens and sighed: ‘At first, I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot.’ Your disciple wished to ask for an explanation, but your journey afforded no pause, so I dared not speak. Now that you have stopped, may I inquire as to my fault?"

Laozi said: "Your haughty gaze and arrogant bearing—with whom do you dwell? How can you conduct yourself with such self-importance and boasting, inviting others to deceive you? The purest white appears tarnished; the greatest virtue seems insufficient. Not contending with things, one remains humble and restrained."

Yang Zhu’s expression changed abruptly, and he said: "I respectfully receive your instruction."

On his departure, the innkeeper’s entire household came to see him off—the master held the mat, his wife the towel and comb, the lodgers left their seats, and the cook stepped away from the stove. Such was their awe of his extraordinary demeanor. On his return, the lodgers vied with him for the same seat. He had become one with things, and things did not disdain him.

Lu said: The true recluse makes no sound of self-importance, bears no appearance of self-regard. Thus, when Yang Zhu departed, people came to send him off; upon hearing his virtue and governance, the lodgers vied for his seat. The previous chapter spoke of Liezi causing others to preserve him, while this chapter shows Yang Zhu enabling others to cease preserving him.

*Zhenghe’s commentary: A skilled merchant conceals his wealth as if empty; a noble person of great virtue appears foolish. Those who know me are few, and thus I am esteemed. Those who adorn their wisdom to astonish the foolish, who feign eccentricity to stand apart, who self-promote and self-display—how can they speak of the Dao? Even Liezi, who had no intention of startling others, was still criticized. How much more so Yang Zhu, who altered his appearance and bearing to distinguish himself from the crowd—his being deemed "unteachable" was fitting.*

"The purest white appears tarnished" means to cleanse the profound mirror and perceive no flaw, even bearing the world’s filth, yet not cultivating one’s person to expose its stains. "The greatest virtue seems insufficient" means that virtue cannot be surpassed, yet one does not consider oneself excessive—this is what is called "the highest virtue does not act as virtue."

Yang Zhu heard the instruction and departed, and those who harmonized with him vied for his seat—this was nearly it. For on his departure, they welcomed and served him, treating him as extraordinary; on his return, those who harmonized vied for his seat, showing how lightly the crowd regarded him. Thus the sage wears coarse cloth while holding jade within. He discards the former and embraces the latter.

*Fan’s commentary: There is no greater thief than virtue. If the heart has intent, and the intent has eyes, how much worse for one with a haughty gaze and arrogant bearing? A haughty gaze is rebellious and unyielding; an arrogant bearing is intrusive and unstraight. It is to inwardly see one’s own ruin while outwardly appearing to scheme. "The purest white appears tarnished"—though one cleanses the profound mirror and abides in constancy, the multitude despises it. "The greatest virtue seems insufficient"—though one daily renews without flaw, one does not consider oneself excessive. Discerning this truth, those who harmonize will vie for your seat.*

When ten jars of fermented drink are offered and five are sent ahead as tribute, will you be truly startled? Yang Zhu traveled south to Pei, while Laozi journeyed west to Qin, meeting by chance at the outskirts. They arrived in Liang and encountered Laozi. *The Zhuangzi states: Yangzi Ju—Ju being perhaps Yang Zhu’s courtesy name—did not wait for Laozi’s timing, for this is all allegory.* Laozi sighed to the heavens midway and said: "At first, I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot." For one who wanders with the perfected yet cannot shed their pride and boasting, it is said: "You cannot be taught."

Yang Zhu passed through Song and lodged at an inn traveling east. The innkeeper had two concubines—one beautiful, the other plain. The plain one was favored, while the beautiful one was slighted. Yangzi asked the reason. The innkeeper’s young attendant replied: "The beautiful one is beautiful in her own eyes, but I do not see her beauty. The plain one is plain in her own eyes, but I do not see her plainness." Yangzi said: "Disciples, take note. Conduct yourself with worthiness yet discard the conduct of self-regard—wherever you go, will you not be beloved? Pride, arrogance, self-importance, and boasting are what ghosts, spirits, and the human Dao reject. Empty yourself to cultivate principle, and the world will delight in exalting you. Proceed thus, and who can resist you?"

Lu said: This further binds the meaning of the two preceding sections. If one can make others cherish them, how could those who do not cherish them not be envious? If one does not presume to let things cherish them, then the world will forget their faults—how much more so the innkeeper’s concubines? *Zhenghe* states: From the Dao’s perspective, things have no inherent beauty or ugliness. To know beauty as beauty is to posit ugliness as its opposite. What the world deems beautiful is called the wondrous; what it deems ugly is called the putrid. Yet the wondrous transforms into the putrid, and the putrid transforms into the wondrous—how then can beauty and ugliness be distinguished? The sage does not cling to right or wrong, beauty or ugliness, but empties the self to wander through the world. Without pride or boasting, this is what is meant by "conducting oneself with worthiness yet discarding the conduct of self-regard." None in the world contend with them in ability, nor do they contend in merit—this is what is meant by "wherever you go, will you not be beloved?"

Fan said: The Dao has no distinct forms—who is beautiful, who is ugly? When the world knows beauty as beauty, ugliness already exists. The Dao has no separate grades—what is noble, what is base? Viewed through things, they deem themselves noble and others base. Thus, with the innkeeper’s concubines, who knows the reason for this? Only by conducting oneself with worthiness yet discarding the conduct of self-regard will the world delight in exalting you without weariness.

The world has a Way of constant victory and a Way of constant defeat. The Way of constant victory is called softness; the Way of constant defeat is called strength. Though these two are known, they should be treated as interchangeable—yet people do not understand this. Thus, the ancients said: Strength first concerns those not equal to oneself; what it overcomes lies beneath oneself. Softness first emerges from oneself, not contending with things, so things cannot overpower it. Those "first not equal to oneself" will, when they reach equality, be imperiled—encountering an adversary, they will surely be endangered. Those "first emerging from oneself" face no peril, for principle is ever at peace.

To overcome a single person is like nothing; to bear the world is like nothing—this is called overcoming without overcoming oneself, bearing without bearing. Embracing the Way of softness and emptiness, abiding in a place of non-contention, though it be the nobility of a single person or the vastness of the world, to guide it without intent is to be like nothing. "Nothing" means emptiness and humility.

Guo Xiang said: Listen to what the ears hear, see what the eyes see, know to stop where knowledge does not reach, and act only where ability suffices. Use what is naturally usable, do what is naturally doable, follow one’s nature without contending with things—this is the Way of utmost softness. Thus, lift what lifts itself, hold what holds itself. Without the weight of a hair’s tip, I exert no force.

Lu said: Strength and softness—these two are easily known. Why, then, do people not understand them? It is because the desire to prevail is too great. When encountering those not equal to oneself, one does not yet feel strong; but when meeting an equal adversary, one is constantly in peril. With this mindset, seeking to prevail over a single person or bear the world is to be ever like a servant without respite. Those who rest in softness do not even wish to prevail over those beneath them—how much less those who surpass them? People call it not prevailing, yet I prevail over myself; I bear, yet I have never faltered.

*Laozi* says: The soft and weak overcome the hard and strong.

Fan said: To aid things and prevail is superior; to prevail by force is inferior. Thus, the Way of constant victory is called softness; the Way of constant defeat is called strength. *Laozi* says: Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet in attacking the hard and strong, none can surpass it. Zhuangzi, in the *Outer Chapters*, discusses the mutual affection of Kui and She, saying: "Point at me, and I prevail; swallow me, and I prevail. To break great trees and topple great houses, only I can do this." This clarifies the matter. Yet though all know that the soft overcomes the hard and the weak overcomes the strong, none can practice it. Only the sage can accumulate many small non-victories into a great victory.

Zhouzi said: To seek hardness, one must guard with softness; to seek strength, one must preserve with weakness. Guarding softness not to seek hardness, one naturally becomes hard; preserving weakness not to seek strength, one naturally becomes strong. Thus, hardness and strength are not what desire can attain. Accumulate softness, and one becomes hard; accumulate weakness, and one becomes strong. Observe what is accumulated to know the realm of fortune and misfortune—for fortune and misfortune arise from what is accumulated.

Strength that prevails over those not equal to oneself will, when it reaches equality, become brittle and surely break. Softness that prevails by emerging from oneself has immeasurable power.

Fan said: Softness is the root of the Dao; weakness is its function. Thus, accumulating softness must yield hardness; accumulating weakness must yield strength. Observe what is accumulated, and fortune and misfortune arise from one’s own seeking. Using strength without knowing to preserve it with weakness, what one overcomes extends only to those not equal to oneself. Using softness, one ultimately prevails over the hard—thus, what one overcomes may surpass oneself.

The reason the ancients valued abiding in a place of non-contention is that through non-contention, none in the world can contend with them. Lao Dan said: "When weapons are strong, they perish."

Wang Bi said: What all things abhor must not endure. When wood is rigid, it snaps; when strength reaches its extreme, it destroys. The soft and weak belong to the company of life; the hard and strong belong to the company of death.

Lu said: The noble person declares: The overbearing do not meet their proper end; those who delight in victory will surely encounter their match. Accumulating virtue and amassing benevolence—this is the Way of softness. *Zhenghe* states: He who gathers many small victories to form a great victory—only the sage can achieve this. Laozi said: Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet in attacking the hard and strong, nothing surpasses it, for there is nothing that can replace it. If there were something to replace it, one would follow others and lose oneself—how then could one prevail over things? Only by having nothing to replace it does one remain constant amid ten thousand changes, so that nothing can overcome it. This is called the Way of constant victory. The Way of constant victory is called softness; the Way of constant defeat is called strength. These two are easy to know, yet people do not understand them—this is what Laozi meant by "softness overcomes hardness, weakness overcomes strength," which all under heaven know but none can practice. Strength first overcomes those not equal to oneself; softness first emerges from within. He who first overcomes those not equal to himself will, when he reaches those equal to himself, be in peril. He who first emerges from within will never be in peril. For one who opposes the world with the Dao will surely meet his match; the timid, yielding, and humble gallop through the world’s utmost hardness. This is precisely the meaning. To overcome a single person or a multitude with this, or to bear the world’s burdens with this, is to extend from the self to all under heaven—only those who follow the company of softness and weakness can truly bear such a task. For they do not seek to overcome things but overcome themselves; they do not rely on others but rely on themselves.

When armies clash, the side that grieves prevails—thus it is said, "When weapons are strong, they perish." The carpenter knows to nurture the young paulownia or catalpa, but when they grow strong, he fells and measures them. Thus it is said, "When wood is rigid, it snaps." All things bear yin and embrace yang, blending through the harmonizing qi. Yang’s virtue is to bring forth life; yin’s function is to bring stern execution. When stern execution prevails, the harmonizing qi is lost. Thus it is said: The soft and weak belong to the company of life; the hard and strong belong to the company of death.

Fan said: The tradition states: The hard and strong dwell below; the soft and weak reside above. For softness overcomes hardness, and weakness overcomes strength—this is where Laozi’s arts of the Dao lie. Thus *Zhuangzi* said: "Take timidity, weakness, and humility as one’s outward form." Appearance need not be uniform to share the same wisdom; wisdom need not be uniform to share the same appearance. The sage embraces childlike wisdom and discards childlike appearance, while the multitude clings to childlike appearance and distances itself from childlike wisdom. Do they love those whose appearance resembles their own and fear those whose appearance differs? Possessing a seven-foot frame, differing hands and feet, hair on the head and teeth in the mouth, standing and moving—this is called a human, yet humans are not without bestial hearts. Though they may have bestial hearts, their appearance makes them beloved. With wings and horns, fangs and claws, flying aloft or crouching to run—this is called a bird or beast, yet birds and beasts are not without human hearts. Though they may have human hearts, their appearance makes them estranged.

Fuxi, Nüwa, Shennong, and the Xia sovereigns—serpent-bodied with human faces, ox-headed with tiger noses—possessed forms not of humankind yet bore the great sagely virtue. Human appearances naturally include features resembling birds and beasts, and many ancient sages bore extraordinary visages. The so-called "serpent-bodied with human face" does not mean scaled and belly-crawling, devoid of limbs; "ox-headed with tiger nose" does not mean horned and dewlapped, with broad foreheads and slack jaws—just as physiognomy speaks of tortoise backs, crane strides, kite shoulders, and hawk beaks.

Jie of Xia, Zhou of Yin, Huan of Lu, and Mu of Chu—though their forms and seven apertures were identical to humans—harbored the hearts of birds and beasts. Yet the multitude clings to a single appearance in pursuit of supreme wisdom—how could this suffice?

Lu observes: What strange creatures cherish is spirit; when spirit departs, even the bond between father and child becomes estranged. Thus, in nights of terror, sharing a roof with living beings brings no fear, for their spirits are akin. Yet in an ordinary dwelling, sharing a room with a corpse induces dread, for their spirits differ. Though the dead and the living share the same former form, one feels secure and the other terrified—because kindred spirit departs, and form alone cannot foster closeness. How deluded are those who, ignorant of the shared spirit among living things, take the sameness or difference of appearance as the measure of intimacy!

Thus *Zhuangzi* states: What all things share is spirit, and spirit is the master of nurturing life.

Fan remarks: Mencius said, "That by which humans differ from birds and beasts is slight; the multitude abandons it, while the noble person preserves it." Seeking beyond the physical frame, one finds that though forms may be alike, wisdom need not be the same. Seeking within the physical frame, one finds that though wisdom may be alike, forms need not be the same. Thus, some ancient figures possessed non-human forms yet bore great sagely virtue, while others shared human forms yet harbored the hearts of birds and beasts—it lies solely in whether one preserves or abandons it.

When the Yellow Emperor battled the Flame Emperor at the wilds of Banquan, he marshaled bears, brown bears, wolves, leopards, boars, and tigers as his vanguard, and eagles, hawks, falcons, and kites as his banners—this was commanding birds and beasts by force. When Yao tasked Kui with overseeing music, striking and tapping the stones, the hundred beasts danced in unison, and when the *Xiaoshao* melody reached its ninth movement, the phoenix came to pay homage—this was summoning birds and beasts by sound. If so, how do the hearts of birds and beasts differ from those of humans? Their forms and voices differ from ours, yet we know not the way to connect with them. The sage, knowing all and penetrating all, thus draws them forth and commands them.

The wisdom of birds and beasts naturally includes aspects akin to human innocence. In aligning desires and preserving life, they require no borrowed wisdom from humans: male and female pair, mother and child bond; they shun flatlands and cling to perilous heights, flee the cold and seek the warm; at rest they form groups, in motion they march in ranks; the small stay within, the strong guard without; when drinking they accompany one another, when feeding they call in flocks. In the age of great antiquity, they dwelled alongside humans and walked among them. Where virtue was pure, birds and beasts harbored no fear. By the time of the Five Emperors, they began to scatter in alarm. By the final age, they hid and fled to evade harm. When humans harbor the intent to harm living things, those things too know to avoid them.

Fan observes: If they can be commanded by force and summoned by sound, then the hearts of birds and beasts naturally share something with humans. If in aligning desires and preserving life they require no aid from humans, then the wisdom of birds and beasts naturally includes aspects akin to human innocence. In an age of perfect virtue, when simplicity remained unspoiled, birds and beasts could be leashed and roam freely, and the nests of magpies and crows could be climbed to peer within. But in later ages, when cunning disrupted the world, when bows, crossbows, traps, and snares proliferated, birds were thrown into chaos above. When snares, nets, and pitfall traps multiplied, beasts were thrown into chaos in the marshes. Thus, even the creeping insects and fluttering creatures lost their true nature.

Now, in the eastern land of the Jie clan, there are those who frequently understand the speech of the six domestic animals—this is but a fragment of partial knowledge. The tortoise and dragon are the lords of shelled and scaled creatures; the qilin and phoenix are the chiefs of feathered and furred beings...

Where it extends to the flying midges and creeping worms, all cry out in mutual resonance, each with its own intent and inclination—how does their mutual mastery differ from that of humans? Yet men, unable to comprehend, claim the sounds of birds and beasts lack structure or pattern. Thus, those who exhaust principle and perfect knowledge open myriad paths of understanding; those who grasp but a single thread through partial experience may chance upon one strand, as in the *Zuo Tradition of the Spring and Autumn Annals*: Jie Gelu heard an ox low and said, "This one will sire four calves, all to be sacrificial victims."

The divine sages of high antiquity, knowing fully the dispositions of all things, understood completely the voices of different kinds. Gathering and assembling them, they instructed and received instruction, treating them as kin to humankind. Thus, they first convened ghosts, spirits, and mountain demons—Yu’s assembly of the host of spirits at Mount Kuaiji being one such instance. Next, they reached the peoples of the eight directions, and lastly, they gathered birds, beasts, insects, and moths—hence the saying that "the hundred beasts led the dance." This speaks to how creatures of blood and qi, though distant in form, share a commonality of heart and mind. The divine sages, knowing this, left nothing untaught in their instruction.

Lu notes: The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records Jie Gelu hearing an ox’s low and knowing it would bear four sacrificial calves; Yu’s assembly of spirits and Shun’s dance of the hundred beasts are such examples.

Zhenghe observes: Earlier scholars took *tong* (童) as "same," and this should be considered correct. In the age of perfect virtue, all was as if without knowledge, its power undivided; all was as if without desire, called "pristine simplicity." Thus, humans dwelt alongside birds and beasts in flocks, mingling with the ten thousand things. Birds and beasts could be leashed and roam freely; the nests of magpies and crows could be climbed to peer within. Might the battles of the Yellow Emperor at Banquan and the musical attainments of Emperor Yao have arisen from this?

Jie Gelu heard the lowing of an ox in the time of Cheng Zhou, when offices were established to raise and teach birds and beasts, tame them, and even converse with them. Thus, they fully understood the voices of different kinds, gathering and instructing them. Traces of this persisted into later ages, as in the *Liezi*, which laments the scattering of pure simplicity, seeking the intent of the Dao and virtue, and embedding it in its text. Though the age had shifted toward cunning, it still aspired to restore the realm of unadulterated thickness, and so its words touched upon this.

Fan notes: The *Rites of Zhou* records that the *lu* officials were tasked with speaking to beasts, showing that understanding the speech of the six domestic animals was indeed a matter of comprehensive knowledge. Yet these chirps and cries, without skilled discernment, could not be engaged. Thus, Boyi mastered the voices of birds in speech, while Gelu distinguished the sounds of lowing oxen.

In Song, there was a man called Master Ju, who loved raising monkeys and was thus named for them. He cherished the monkeys, keeping them in troops, and could understand their intent, just as they grasped his heart. He diminished his household’s provisions to satisfy the monkeys’ desires. Soon, however, he faced scarcity and sought to limit their food. Fearing the monkeys would not submit, he first deceived them, saying: "I will give you chestnuts—three in the morning and four at dusk. Is that enough?" The monkeys all rose in anger. Then he said: "I will give you chestnuts—four in the morning and three at dusk. Is that enough?" The monkeys all prostrated themselves in joy. Things that use their abilities to deceive and entrap one another all follow this pattern. The sage uses wisdom to entrap the multitude of fools, just as Master Ju used wisdom to entrap his monkeys. Names and realities remain unharmed, yet their joy and anger are manipulated.

Lu says: Though creatures possessing consciousness share the same spirit, those with round heads and square feet—humans—are the most intelligent. The most outstanding in wisdom become sages and worthies; the greatest in talent become kings. The sage assigns tasks according to ability, ensuring all—great or small—are rightly placed. Neither people nor things are discarded; all are entrapped by wisdom—how could this apply only to monkeys?

Zhenghe observes: The ancients who were skilled in the Way did not seek to enlighten the people but rather to make them simple. For the people may be led but not made to understand. Thus, those skilled in the Way merely guide them, reversing their usual state. The Way can be carried and shared with them, leaving no room for the exercise of cunning.

Fan says: In ancient times, those who spoke of deceit would call it "monkey deceit," for monkeys, by nature, are keen observers and fond of trickery. Thus, they exploit their cleverness, rely on their agility, feign appearances at odds with their true feelings, and harbor suspicion toward wisdom. Deceitful wisdom serves only to entrap. Though names and realities remain unharmed, joy and anger are put to use. Yet the monkeys, kept by Master Ju, were tossed between three and four, never realizing their plight—was this not because they were entrapped by what they loved?

Ji Xiaozhi raised fighting cocks for King Xuan of Zhou. After ten days, the king asked: "Are the cocks ready to fight?" He replied: "Not yet. They are still vain and arrogant, relying on their qi, lacking substance yet full of self-importance." After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: "Not yet. They still respond to shadows and movements, quick to take offense." After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: "Not yet. They still glare fiercely and bristle with qi, ever seeking an enemy and insisting on victory." After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: "Nearly so."

Though other cocks may crow, this one remains unchanged. When an opponent challenges, it does not respond—victory and defeat are forgotten. Beholding it, one sees but a wooden cock; its virtue is complete. To attain such wholeness is to transcend the realm of blood and qi. No other cock dares engage it; they turn and flee. One whose virtue is complete not only harbors no intent within but also prevents external things from arising in opposition.

Guo Xiang says: To nurture it to such completeness is to render it invulnerable to external foes—how much more so when one attains wholeness oneself?

Lu observes: Relying on qi and self-importance is no path to certain victory. Reacting to things with the swiftness of echo and shadow is to be turned by them, not to attain mastery. Fierce glares and surging qi betray a mind still entangled in calculation. Only one who forgets form and spirit, who remains unmoved by life and death, is truly invincible before external things.

In the *Zhenghe* edition: He who excels at overcoming foes does not contend. Precisely because he does not contend, none in the world can contend with him. To still the mind, one must first calm the qi; to attain spirit, one must follow the heart—this is the virtue of non-contention. Such a one preserves heaven’s wholeness, his spirit unassailable; how could external things find entry? Though he may clash with the world, he is not cowed, nor can the world harm him. This is the steadfastness of pure qi, not the realm of cunning, skill, or reckless daring. Such is the person of complete virtue.

Fan says: A cock, though domesticated by man, remains subject to greater forces—thus its nurture cannot be without dependence. Vain and reliant on its own qi, it cannot yet be free of self-importance; responsive to echoes and shadows, it cannot yet be free of reaction. Fierce glares and surging qi reveal a lingering desire for victory. But when, though other cocks may crow, this one remains unmoved—when it appears as a wooden cock—then its nurture is complete, and no foe dares challenge it. If mere physical wholeness suffices for this, how much more so for one of complete virtue?

Hui Ang, grandson of Hui Shi, was received by King Kang of Song. The king stamped his foot, cleared his throat, and spoke rapidly: "What pleases me is courage and strength, not benevolence and righteousness. What instruction do you bring?" Hui Ang replied: "I possess a Way by which, though a man be brave, piercing him will not penetrate; though he be strong, striking him will not land. Does Your Majesty not wish to hear of this?" The king of Song said: "Excellent. This is what I desire to hear."

Hui Ang replied: "That one may pierce without penetrating, strike without landing—this is still humiliation. I possess a Way by which, though a man be brave, he will not dare to pierce; though he be strong, he will not dare to strike. That he does not dare is not because he lacks the will. I possess a Way by which a man fundamentally lacks such will. When one lacks such will, there has never been a heart that loves or seeks advantage. I possess a Way by which all men and women under heaven, without exception, joyfully desire to love and benefit one another. This surpasses mere courage and strength—it stands above the four burdens."

(For the king ranks above ministers, grandees, officers, and commoners, hence the term "four burdens.")

The king of Song said: "This is what I desire to attain."

Lu observed: "Piercing without penetrating, striking without landing—this is the first. Not daring to pierce, not daring to strike—this is the second. Fundamentally lacking the will to strike—this is the third. Causing men and women to joyfully love and benefit one another—this is the fourth. If one achieves two of these four, how would that compare?"

Thus the king of Song inclined his mind to hear it.

Hui Ang replied: "Confucius and Mo Di embodied this."

Lu remarked: "This is the illumination of wisdom to harmonize all things, the exaltation of teaching to transform people—both are the residual affairs of the Way ascending to virtue. Confucius and Mo Di, though they had no land to rule as lords nor office to lead as officials, yet all men and women under heaven craned their necks and stood on tiptoe, yearning for their peace and benefit. Now, Your Majesty is a ruler of ten thousand chariots. If you truly possess such will, then all within your four borders will receive its benefit—your worth will far surpass that of Confucius and Mo Di."

The king of Song had no reply. Hui Ang hastened out.

The king of Song said to his attendants: "How eloquent! The guest has persuaded me with his discourse."

Lu observed: "This is the exaltation of the Way to manifest virtue, the bequeathing of traces to benefit people. The multitude sees only the transmission of Confucius’ and Mo Di’s teachings—how could they know the hidden Way that makes virtue resplendent? Thus, when one inquires after the ford, they do not flock with the birds and beasts. This is its great purport."

*Zhenghe* states: "The sage’s way in the world is divine martiality without killing, treasuring compassion as his jewel. Thus, when benevolence extends to all under heaven, none do not cherish it; when righteousness extends to all under heaven, none do not submit. This is called the constant Way of victory—it far surpasses mere courage and strength. This is why all under heaven love and benefit from it."

(That Confucius is mentioned alongside Mo Di: *Zhuangzi* discusses the arts of the Way in antiquity, where the Hundred Schools and various techniques each had their strengths. Though Mo Di’s grasp of the Way was not comprehensive, he was still a talented scholar with particular virtues.)

Fan remarked: "One who has a heart that loves and seeks advantage has not yet enabled others to forget the self. Hui Ang’s words stop here, merely addressing King Kang of Song. That this chapter concludes with these words—could it be that the constant Way of victory also lies herein?"

*True Classic of Perfect Virtue with Four Commentaries*, Scroll Six—concluded
*True Classic of Perfect Virtue with Four Commentaries*, Scroll Seven—begins

Gathered by He Guang the Scattered Light Daoist, Gao Shouyuan
*King Mu of Zhou*

That which receives life and accepts form is called "embodiment"; the transformations of bowing and rising are called "change." Where spirit intermingles is called "dream"; where form makes contact is called "waking." Tracing them to their ultimate, both converge in emptiness and illusion.

Why is this? The root of living substance is stagnant, and after a hundred years it comes to an end; the branches of transformative nature are shallow, vanishing in the blink of an eye. The Dao of spirit is indistinct, now present, now absent; the principles of form are manifest, now real, now solid. Thus, one who thoroughly perceives knows that the principles of arising and ceasing are equal, and the paths of waking and dreaming are one. Though ten thousand changes intermingle, they do not touch the spirit’s contemplation.

The deluded, taking the manifest and obscure as proof, hesitate over the slowness or swiftness of verification and thus doubt. Secretly, they cling to self-interest, taking the bodily frame as their true dwelling. Who recognizes that the root and return of life and transformation lie in nothingness?

Lu remarked: Heaven and earth form vessels, encompassing all things. Human beings live within them, merely preserving their existence. They never realize that spirit governs form, unbound by existence. This is why the sage nurtures the root, while the foolish only know how to nurture their form, constricting themselves to life. Greedy for life yet unaware of life’s master, they take the decay of form as death, ignorant of the spirit’s enduring presence. The deluded become ordinary people; the enlightened attain sagely wisdom. The confused are many. Thus, the enlightened are first discussed to clarify the distinction.

*Zhenghe*: The Dao has no true or false; things have that and this. Like dreams and waking, they generate their own confusion. Only the great sage knows them, uniting all as one.

Fan remarked: Those attached to existence are separated by the slightest thread; those awakened to nothingness see the ten thousand dharmas as one source. One who embodies the Dao roams freely in the ancestor of the ten thousand things, transcending form to wander with the transformed beings. Comprehending the subtleties of principle, they silently harmonize with the Creator, equalizing the principles of life and death, unifying the paths of dream and waking. Though existence and annihilation, gain and loss, sorrow and joy, love and aversion—none of these are known. Though heaven and earth, the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—none are distinguished. The Great Void is without thing; returning to the dwelling of nature, naturally—who would toil over things as matters of concern?

In the time of King Mu of Zhou, a transformed being came from the westernmost land. A master of illusion, he entered water and fire, pierced metal and stone, reversed mountains and rivers, and moved cities. Riding the void, he did not fall; touching the solid, he was not obstructed. His thousand changes and ten thousand transformations could not be exhausted. Having already altered the forms of things, he further transformed people’s thoughts, temporarily causing them to forget their long-held knowledge.

*Zhenghe*: One who knows the Dao of transformation—does this not mean knowing the workings of spirit? Unharmed by water or fire, unobstructed by metal or stone, high and low as one, void and solid both forgotten—his thousand changes and ten thousand transformations cannot be exhausted. This, too, is spirit. Yet spirit is that which subtly pervades the ten thousand things yet cannot be fathomed. Altering the forms of things and transforming people’s thoughts—this is merely exhausting calculations and mastering change, shifting and altering according to form. This is why he is called a "transformed being."

King Mu revered him as a spirit and served him as a lord. Lu remarked: The thoughts of ordinary people extend no further than desires, aversions, external gains, and benevolence and righteousness. The transformed being now returns them to their true nature—hence, it is said he "transforms" them. The transformed being is a body responsive to things, exhausting sagely wisdom and boundless application, his thousand changes and ten thousand transformations never reaching an end.

He spread out bedding for him to reside in, presented the three sacrificial animals to him, and selected female musicians to entertain him. The transformed being found the king’s palace crude and unfit to dwell in, the king’s kitchen fare rank and inedible (the stench of mole crickets), and the king’s consorts foul and unlovable.

Lu remarked: Finding the king’s palace crude, his kitchen fare rank, and his consorts foul clarifies that the transformed being did not value sensory pleasures, flavors, or dwellings.

King Mu then had it rebuilt for him. The labor of earth and timber, the red and white of ochre and plaster—no craft was spared. The five treasuries were emptied before the terrace was completed. A thousand *ren* high, overlooking the summit of Zhongnan, it was named the Terrace of Central Heaven. He selected the most graceful and delicate maidens from Zheng and Wei, their beauty enchanting and their forms supple. He applied fragrant unguents, painted their moth-like brows, adorned them with hairpins and earrings (earplugs), dressed them in fine silk and delicate linen, trailing gauze and fine silk. Powdered white and darkened with black, they wore jade pendants and rings, mingled with fragrant herbs, filling the terrace and pavilions. He performed the *Bearing Clouds*, *Six Radiance*, *Nine Shao*, and *Morning Dew* to entertain him (*Bearing Clouds* was the music of the Yellow Emperor; *Six Radiance*, that of Emperor Ku; *Nine Shao*, that of Shun; *Morning Dew*, that of Tang). Every month, jade robes were offered; every dawn, jade delicacies were presented—such were their precious rarities.

Yet the transformed being still did not appear pleased, only reluctantly ascending it.

Lu remarked: The king did not grasp his intent and further embellished it. The transformed being still remained unmoved, making clear that his heart was not in these things.

*Zhenghe*: What the world deems beautiful is called "divine and marvelous"; what it detests is called "foul and rotten." Divine and marvelous, foul and rotten—each transforms into the other. Then how can beauty and ugliness be distinguished? The transformed being found the king’s palace, kitchen fare, and consorts unacceptable and insisted on rebuilding and selecting anew before ascending. This shows he had not yet forgotten the passions of beauty and ugliness. Thus, King Mu revered him only as a spirit.

Fan remarked: Confucius said, "If one does not transform with the transformed being, how can one transform others?" The so-called "transformed being"—is he not also one who has transformed into a human? Thus, water and fire can be entered and pierced, mountains and rivers reversed and moved. With nothingness entering the spaceless, he does not fall into the void;

With nothingness of thickness entering the spaceless, he is not hindered by solidity. Myriad transformations, boundless and inexhaustible—he has no fixed form. There are those who alter the shapes of things, yet without thought. There are those who shift the concerns of men—these are called "transformed beings," and it is only fitting they be thus. King Mu then fully honored the Way of serving them, offering his own bedchamber for their residence, yet they knew not its baseness and unfitness; he presented the three sacrificial animals to them, yet they knew not the stench and foulness of what could not be consumed; he selected female musicians to entertain them, yet they knew not the rankness and repugnance of what could not be embraced. Thereupon he built the Terrace of Central Heaven, selected the bearing of Zheng and Wei, performed the music of *Yunshao*, presented jade robes, and offered jade delicacies—yet still they remained unmoved. Unable to do otherwise, he attended them. This was merely to follow their encounter and settle in one dwelling, abiding in what could not be avoided. After but a short stay, they requested the king join them in travel. The king grasped the sleeve of the transformed being—*qu*, meaning the sleeve of a robe—and soaring upward, they halted only at Central Heaven. When they reached the palace of the transformed beings, its halls were wrought of gold and silver, strung with pearls and jade, rising above clouds and rain, its foundations unseen below, appearing like piled clouds from afar. All that the eyes beheld and ears heard, all that the nose inhaled and mouth tasted, were unlike anything of the human realm. The king truly believed it to be the *Qingdu* and *Ziwei*, the abode of the Celestial Emperor, where the vast music of *Juntian* resounded. *Qingdu* and *Ziwei* are the dwelling places of the Celestial Emperor. Tradition records that Duke Mu of Qin, stricken with illness and insensible, awoke and said: "I was at the Emperor’s court, where it was most joyous. With a hundred spirits I roamed *Juntian*, where the vast music played nine suites and myriad dances, unlike the music of the Three Dynasties—its sound stirred the heart." Another account says the same befell Viscount Jian of Zhao. The king looked down and saw his own palaces and pavilions like heaped clods and piled straw.

The king believed he had dwelled there for decades without once longing for his kingdom—such is what is meant by "transforming a man’s thoughts."

Lu said: "The heart of the Central Heaven is the most numinous of minds. As King Mu could not instantly forget his desires, the transformation manifested in the splendor of palaces to seize his cherished heart." The *huaren* again invited the king to journey together. Wherever they went, above they saw neither sun nor moon, below they saw neither rivers nor seas. Where light and shadow fell, the king’s eyes blurred and could not see; where sounds arose, his ears confused and could not hear. His hundred bones and six viscera quivered and would not settle, his mind grew lost, his essence scattered, and he begged the *huaren* to return. The realm of the vast and indistinct is not a place mortals may tread—confusion of heart and eyes is the natural course.

*Zhenghe* said: "To say the king truly believed it the *Qingdu* and *Ziwei*, the *Juntian* and vast music, the Celestial Emperor’s abode, is to make clear it was not so. Wrought of gold and silver, strung with pearls and jade, all that eyes beheld, ears heard, nose inhaled, and mouth tasted were unlike anything of the human realm. Yet the king came to forget his kingdom—so great was his joy. But where they journeyed, above he saw neither sun nor moon, below neither rivers nor seas; his eyes could not see, his ears could not hear, his mind grew lost, his essence scattered, and he begged the *huaren* to return—so great was his distress. This is what is meant by 'altering the forms of things and transforming a man’s thoughts.'"

The *huaren* shifted him—*shifted*, meaning *pushed*. The king seemed to fall through emptiness. *Fell*, meaning *plummeted*.

Lu said: "The ultimate principle is precisely the place the *huaren* reached. All phenomena are utterly exhausted—how could sun, moon, rivers, or seas remain? All common delusions are swept away—how could light or shadow persist? The mortal form cannot abide there, the ordinary heart has never known it, and though his attachments were not yet forgotten, he begged to return."

When he awoke, he still sat where he had before, his attendants still the same as before. Before him, the wine had not yet cleared, the dishes had not yet spoiled. The king asked where he had come from, and his attendants said: "Your Majesty merely sat in silent contemplation." Thereafter, King Mu remained disoriented for three months before recovering. He questioned the *huaren* again, asking why his form had not moved.

Lu said: "Free from the cares of clinging, one enters the realm of serene illumination—within a single thought, a myriad ages cannot compare. The domain of the perfected, how could it be some other sacred realm? Thus the wine had not yet cleared, the dishes had not yet spoiled. The attendants saw the king sitting in silence, yet he had gone nowhere; the king, lost in sitting-forgetfulness for three months, dared not ask further."

The *huaren* said: "I traveled with Your Majesty in spirit—how could the form have moved? What is called 'spirit' moves not swiftly yet arrives instantly, goes not forth yet reaches all. To illustrate with a common example: in a single night’s slumber, one may dream a hundred years’ events, or behold things from the farthest realms. While awake, in the brief span of a bow or a glance, one may yet roam beyond the six directions.
