If wayward thoughts and wanton fancies can achieve this, how much more so when the spirit-heart moves alone, unshackled from form and vessel, perfectly penetrating and mystically illuminating, silently congealed in emptiness?

Moreover, was the place we dwelled before any different from Your Majesty’s palace? Was the garden we wandered any different from Your Majesty’s own?"

The king, accustomed to the familiar, doubted what briefly vanished. Yet that and this—both are not true things. To grow used to what always remains and doubt what briefly vanishes is the delusion of the heart. At the extreme of transformation, between swift and slow, can any pattern be grasped? Transformation cannot be exhausted, nor can the principle of swift and slow be divided—seeking to trace its image with the mind’s intelligence is beyond measure.

Lu said: The spirit’s divergence from form is thus made clearer. The king, merely habituated to common sights, harbors doubt about what briefly vanishes. But for one who has attained the utmost Dao and constantly dissolves his form—what doubt could remain? The spirit’s transformations, swift or slow, cannot be fully described.

*Annotation in Zhenghe*: When the spirit-heart is in a state of vagueness, weaving through the ten thousand directions, then the spirit’s journey is swift. In the span of a bow or a glance, one may yet roam beyond the four seas—no need for the form to move, for the heart moves with it.

People of the world take what is constantly present as real and what is constantly absent as illusory. Thus, they grow accustomed to what always remains and cast doubt upon what briefly vanishes. Clinging to being and rejecting emptiness, they are blinded by partiality, unaware that neither that nor this is truly real. Once this is understood, how then is the place we dwelled before any different from Your Majesty’s palace? How is the garden we wandered any different from Your Majesty’s own?

Fan said: The Transformed One’s palace was wrought of gold and silver, strung with pearls and jade, rising above clouds and rain, its foundations unseen below, appearing like a mass of clouds when viewed from afar. How would it compare to the terraces of mid-heaven? What the eyes saw and ears heard, what the nose inhaled and mouth tasted—none of it belonged to the human realm. How then would it compare to the postures of Zheng and Wei, the music of *Yunshao*, the presentation of jade robes and offering of jade food? The king truly believed it to be the Clear Capital, the Purple Tenuity, the vast music of the Heaven of Equal Power, the abode of the Emperor—never realizing that the forms of things could change and human thoughts be altered to such a degree.

Thus, when he looked down upon it, the palaces and pavilions seemed but piled clods and heaped straw—so the mutability of things’ forms became clear. The king had lived there for over a decade without longing for his kingdom—so the alteration of human thoughts became clear. Yet this was still but a beginning, not the ultimate attainment.

Therefore, the Transformed One again invited the king to journey together. Wherever they went, above they saw no sun or moon—so it was not the place said to "above, reveal light." Below they saw no rivers or seas—so it was not the place said to "below, become earth." The radiance that shone there dazzled the king’s eyes, so he could not see, for he had not yet attained the clarity of vision. The sounds that came assailed the king’s ears, so he could not hear, for he had not yet attained the harmony of hearing. His hundred bones and six viscera trembled without settling; his mind was lost, his essence scattered. He begged the Transformed One to return, terrified by the vastness, trembling in alarm, nearly undone—then suddenly awoke.

He found himself still seated where he had been before, his attendants unchanged, the wine before him still clear, the dishes uneaten. The king asked where he had come from, unaware that he had silently remained there while his form had never stirred. Thus was this called a spirit-journey.

Only the spirit—swift without haste, arriving without going, standing in the unfathomable, roaming in nothingness—can in a bow or a glance traverse the four seas, in vagueness weave through the ten thousand directions. How could form ever burden it? Truly understanding this, one sees that the extremes of transformation, the intervals of swift and slow, lie within oneself. How distant, then, is the Transformed One’s palace?

The king rejoiced greatly, no longer concerned with state affairs or delighting in ministers and concubines. Moved by these profound words, he abandoned all thought of the world’s order or disorder, forgot the hierarchy of ruler and subject. He gave himself wholly to far wanderings.

Lu said: *"The Zhuangzi* speaks of those who value the Dao, who abandon the world without a backward glance—this is like treating dust, grime, or husks as though they could mold a Yao or Shun. Who would take such things as their concern? Moreover, the drowning in sounds and sights, in cravings and desires—how could these be what the Dao delights in? Thus the king rejoiced greatly in this Way, no longer concerned with state affairs or delighting in ministers and concubines. To wander far is to forget what lies close at hand."

He commanded the harnessing of the Eight Steeds: on the right trace, *Lü* and *Green Ear*; on the left, *Red Qi* and *White Yi*. The chariot-master was Zaofu, his whip poised between heaven and earth. In the second chariot, the right trace bore *Quhuang* and the left *Yulun*; the left trace, *Daoli* and the right *Shanzi*. Baitian drove the lead chariot, Canbai served as charioteer, and Benrong rode at the right. They galloped a thousand *li* to the land of the Giant Sou clan. The Giant Sou presented the blood of a white swan for the king to drink and offered the milk of cows and horses to wash his feet—bestowing upon the Most Revered what they themselves held most precious, and likewise upon the two chariots’ attendants. After drinking, they departed and lodged at the slopes of Kunlun, on the sunny side of the Red Waters. *The Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: "Kunlun Mountain has waters of five colors." The next day, they ascended the heights of Kunlun, beheld the palace of the Yellow Emperor, and sealed it to bequeath to later ages. Then they were received as guests by the Queen Mother of the West, who feasted them upon the Jasper Pool. The Queen Mother of the West was a human figure with tiger’s teeth, disheveled hair adorned with a sheng-crown, and a talent for whistling—so *The Classic of Mountains and Seas* records. She sang for the king a wandering song, a day-song called *White Clouds*. The king harmonized with her, his response a poem titled *Returning East*. Its words were mournful. Then they beheld the place where the sun sets. *The Chronicle of King Mu* states: "He ascended Yan Mountain, traveling ten thousand *li* in a single day." The king sighed and said: "Alas! I alone am deficient in virtue yet in harmony with joy—harmony with eloquence. Will later ages recount my transgressions?" All from the passage beginning "He commanded the harnessing of the Eight Steeds" to this point is found in *The Chronicle of King Mu*.

King Mu was nearly a spirit-man! Yet he was not. As the *Zhenghe* edition notes: "The spirit is swift without haste, arrives without going." King Mu did not understand this. Though his comings and goings within the Six Directions were bound to this principle, he still commanded chariots and steeds, traveling ten thousand *li* in a day. Thus, though he reached the land of the Giant Sou, ascended the heights of Kunlun, beheld the palace of the Yellow Emperor, and was received by the Queen Mother upon the Jasper Pool, he did not ride the clouds and qi, mount the flying dragons, and wander beyond the Four Seas. Hence it is said: "Nearly a spirit-man"—he approached the spirit yet was not spirit. Able to exhaust the joys of his own lifetime, he knew that a hundred years would still pass, that worldly affairs are without constancy, and so he gave free rein to his heart. The world believed he had ascended in transcendence. The character *jia* should be read as *xia* ("far"); the world’s belief in his "ascension" makes clear that he truly died.

Lu said: To select the finest steeds and discern worthy talents, to employ them as circumstances demand without distinction between Chinese and barbarian lands—only the Dao’s direction is not distant. The palace of the Yellow Emperor exhausts all that heaven and earth possess, the utmost of what divine knowledge can expound. Yet it does not exalt virtue to boast of its use, but delights in the Dao to commune with the spirit. Transforming and ascending for a thousand years, the common people believed he had departed far away.

Fan said: King Mu, awakened by the words of the Transformed One, no longer concerned himself with state affairs, ministers, or concubines. He harnessed the Eight Steeds, journeyed to the land of the Giant Sou, lodged at the slopes of Kunlun, sealed the palace of the Yellow Emperor, and feasted upon the Jasper Pool. Giving free rein to his far wanderings, traveling ten thousand *li* in a day—this could indeed be called spirit-like. Yet to speak of riding the clouds and qi, mounting the flying dragons, and wandering beyond the Four Seas—this he had not achieved. Thus he was deemed merely "nearly a spirit."

Lao Chengzi studied illusion under Master Yin Wen for three years without receiving instruction. Lao Chengzi requested to hear his faults and sought leave to depart. Master Yin Wen bowed and led him into the chamber. Dismissing his attendants, he spoke: "When the Old Master Lao Dan departed for the west, he turned to me and said: 'The qi of living beings, the forms of shaped things—all are illusion. What the Creator begins, what yin and yang transform—this is called life, this is called death. Exhausting the numbers and grasping the changes, shifting and altering forms—this is called transformation, this is called illusion. Mastering the numbers of the Two Modes, holding fast to the principles of yin and yang—one molds and moves the ten thousand forms without sensing difficulty. The Creator’s skill is subtle, its work profound—truly difficult to exhaust or bring to an end. Does the Creator have a heart? It is naturally and wondrously so. When qi and substance are coarse and thin, congealing to form shapes, they follow transformation in their course, and thus do not immediately dissipate. That which relies on form displays its skill openly, its work is shallow, and so it arises and perishes in an instant. Those who borrow things to enact changes, who shift with the formation of shapes—these are born for a moment, vanish for a moment. Their work is manifest, their deeds conspicuous, and so all things are startled. Only when one knows that illusion and transformation are no different from life and death can one begin to study illusion.' The annotations in the chapter headings have already explained this in detail. You and I are also illusion—why study further? The body is illusion; to desire to study illusion is to have illusion study illusion."

Lu said: The changes of form and qi are ever-renewing, never abiding—how are they different from illusion? Thus, what the spirit and qi transform endures long and is difficult to know; what techniques and arts create is near and easy to see. Yet do people not recognize this? The difficult-to-know is the great illusion; the easy-to-see is the small illusion. If one knows that illusion and transformation are no different from life and death, why study further?

Lao Chengzi returned home and, applying Master Yin Wen’s words, pondered deeply for three months. Thereafter he could freely control life and death, reverse the four seasons—summoning thunder in winter, creating ice in summer—making the flying run and the running fly.

Deep contemplation for even a moment may yield the Dao—how much more so for those who attain it without conscious thought? Life necessarily follows principle; form necessarily follows life. There has never been life without principle, nor form without life. Life and form, form and principle—though differing in subtlety and coarseness, they alternate as guest and host, flowing and shifting without the slightest pause. Thus, they are ever-changing and impermanent, now gathering, now dispersing. When one grasps their order, their workings remain hidden yet their effects become manifest; when cultivation loses its measure, their traces appear and their transformations become evident.

Now, if the commands of the four seasons do not err, the three luminaries fall into disorder; when thunder and ice reverse their seasons, and vessels melt and scorch, then flying embers and drifting sands coalesce into surging waters. These are taken as ordinary, and none doubt them. Extending this principle to exhaust the subtleties of yin and yang and the nature of the myriad things, one may mold and shape all existence, matching the workings of creation itself.

As for those who master only partial arts, drawing upon qi and physical form—bowing and rising, they emerge and submerge; glancing about, they make the flying run and the running fly—these are but the lesser techniques. If one never clings to such arts throughout life, they will naturally remain untransmitted in the world. The Dao of the sage is used daily, yet the common people do not know it; displaying wonders to astonish the age is the way of ordinary men.

Lu said: To fathom the root of spirit and qi, to discern the source of life and death, is to attain boundless transformation—this is an inevitable principle. It must be realized through personal insight and embodied verification, for it cannot be transmitted through words or texts.

*Governance and Harmony*: One may journey with those who are ready, guiding them to the subtle Dao. But should one bow and usher them into the chamber? Those unready for this path must not be led. When one dismisses attendants to speak privately, it is because the movements of yin and yang, the progression of the four seasons, and the principles of the myriad things arise in an instant and vanish in a flash. Thus, it is said: the qi of life, the forms of existence—all are illusion. Things take life as their beginning and death as their end, life as the norm and death as change, yet all are obscured within the workings of creation and yin-yang. Therefore, what creation initiates and yin-yang transforms is called life and death. Having exhausted the numbers of creation and yin-yang, and comprehended the changes of qi and form, it is called transformation. When these are bound to numerical change and further altered by form, it is called illusion.

The creator is Heaven. Heaven is spirit, thus its craft is wondrous and unfathomable, its work profound and inexhaustible—hence its difficulty to fully know or comprehend. The shaper is man. Man is discerning, thus his craft is evident and swiftly accomplished, yet shallow and soon undone—hence its rise and fall in an instant.

Life and death are indeed so, while illusion and transformation are contrived. From the perspective of the Dao, none are truly constant. Knowing that illusion and transformation differ not from life and death, where is there anything that is not illusion? Now, you and I are both illusions—yet we study illusion. This is like interpreting a dream within a dream. To speak of things as sometimes existing or ceasing to those who are free in life and death—yet we ourselves remain. Reversing the four seasons, one commands yin and yang without being commanded by them; summoning thunder in winter and creating ice in summer, one governs the seasons without being governed by them; making the flying run and the running fly, one directs the myriad things without being directed by them. With wondrous skill and profound work, one roams with the creator. Yet if one never clings to such arts throughout life, the world will not transmit them, for they are difficult to fully know, fathom, or recognize.

Thus, those who excel in studying illusion establish it upon the constant of non-being, and only then can they exhaust its depths.

Fan said: To use one’s own illusion to invert things, though the root may be upright, is no way to comprehend them. Yet from the perspective of the Dao, even this comprehension is but illusion. Thus, in the faintest stirrings of qi and the division of form from primordial chaos, there is nothing that is not so. What is forged in the furnace and hammer is the beginning of creation; what is set in mechanisms and snares is the transformation of yin. Life and death are named as such, hence they are called life and death. Those who exhaust numbers and comprehend change have not yet transcended the bounds of the unnumbered. Those who alter forms have not yet wholly transformed beyond formlessness. Transformation and illusion are named as such, hence they are called transformation and illusion.

These two—one rooted in creation and attained naturally, thus wondrous, profound, and difficult to fully know or exhaust; the other rooted in form and unable to be without dependence, thus evident, shallow, and rising and falling in an instant. Yet if one truly grasps the Dao, then illusion and transformation differ not from life and death.

Was the automaton crafted by Yan Shi not capable of matching the work of creation? If one understands this principle, then even your body is not your own. Who possesses it? It too is but illusion. To study illusion with illusion—how is this different from interpreting a dream within a dream?

Lao Chengzi returned home and, applying Master Yin Wen’s words, pondered deeply for three months. Thus, he progressed from needing conscious effort to attain understanding. Thereafter, he could freely control life and death without being burdened by things, reverse the four seasons without being bound by numbers, summon thunder in winter and create ice in summer, make the flying run and the running fly, and never cling to such arts throughout his life. How much more so for those who embody the Dao? Wielding supreme authority to act alone, directing the myriad transformations in the unfathomable—there are indeed those who employ it subtly and transform independently, yet how could the world recognize them?

Liezi said: Those who excel in transformation follow a Dao that is subtle and unassuming, their work indistinguishable from others’. They seek only to benefit the world and bring peace to things, thus their achievements remain hidden, and none know of them. The virtue of the Five Emperors and the achievements of the Three Kings were not necessarily the result of wisdom or strength alone—some were accomplished through transformation. Who could fathom it?

The achievements and virtue of emperors and kings are named by the world, yet these names are not what make them emperors and kings. Bowing and yielding, halberds and shields—these are but borrowed paths; why should they not also borrow from illusions and transformations? Yet the traces that startle the world are employed subtly by the sage and never displayed.

*On Governance and Harmony*: The virtue of the Five Emperors and the achievements of the Three Kings—those whose Dao is subtle and unassuming speak of their Dao as hidden in its application. Those whose achievements are like others’ speak of their achievements as manifest in benevolence. The Five Emperors are called "virtuous," the Three Kings "achieving," yet these are but the traces they tread. Their hearts were never not one, yet once they were deemed the result of wisdom and strength, one dared not insist they were accomplished through transformation. Some doubted, so unfathomable was their mastery of transformation. Could this be called flowing in unison with Heaven and Earth?

Fan said: Their Dao is subtle and unassuming—hidden in its application. Their achievements are like others’—manifest in benevolence. This Dao is not easily attained without embodying the spirit and becoming transformation. Yet the Dao is the root; achievements are but its incidental affairs. Thus, though hidden in application—retiring into subtlety—what is usable and visible remains fundamental. Though manifest in benevolence—appearing no different from others—it never fails to equal the formless. If transformation is like this, then even dust, grime, chaff, and husks suffice to mold emperors and kings. Those who rely on wisdom and strength at such times leave only traces of responding to the world—how could this be their means of advancement? The ancients concealed their sharp instruments, never displaying them to others, yet in responding to the myriad changes, they remained serene and untroubled. Truly, this was because of this Dao. Thus, even the hidden depths of ghosts and spirits could not fathom it—how much less the benighted customs of the world? How could they plumb its mysteries?

Awareness has eight signs; dreams have six omens. *Signs* are verifications; *omens* are divinations. The divination of the six dreams is explained in the *Rites of Zhou*. What are the eight signs? The first is *the familiar*—past events. The second is *action*—what one does. The third is *gain*. The fourth is *loss*. The fifth is *sorrow*. The sixth is *joy*. The seventh is *life*. The eighth is *death*. These eight signs are what form engages. What are the six omens? The first is the *ordinary dream*—dreaming in calm repose. The second is the *startled dream*—the *Rites of Zhou* commentary states: *startled* should read as *alarmed*, meaning dreaming from alarm. The third is the *thought dream*—dreaming from reflection, as when Confucius dreamed of the Duke of Zhou. The fourth is the *waking dream*—dreaming while awake from what one has seen, as when Hu Tu dreamed of the crown prince. The fifth is the *joyful dream*—dreaming from happiness. The sixth is the *fearful dream*—dreaming from terror. These six are what the spirit encounters. Their portents can be divined, hence they are called the six omens. This chapter’s main point is also to clarify that awareness and dreams are not different.

Fan said: When King Mu of Zhou’s spirit wandered, it seemed to arrive yet did not; when the elder’s son learned illusions, they seemed real yet were not. Examining their essence, one sees that the myriad distinctions of the world are equal to awareness and dreams. Thus, this passage is followed by the discussion of awareness and dreams. Zhuang Zhou’s *Equalizing Things* chapter expresses the same idea. Therefore, in acting, one engages with affairs; in gain and loss, one differs from things; in sorrow and joy, one’s heart stirs; in life and death, one is bound by fate. These eight are what form engages—their events can be verified, hence they are called the eight signs. Dreaming in peace is the ordinary dream; dreaming from alarm is the startled dream; the thought dream arises from reflection, as when Confucius dreamed of the Duke of Zhou; the waking dream arises from what one sees while awake, as when Hu Tu dreamed of the crown prince; the joyful dream arises from happiness; the fearful dream arises from terror. These six are what the spirit encounters—their omens can be divined, hence they are called the six omens.

Those who do not recognize the origin of sensory changes are perplexed by events when they arise; those who recognize their origin understand events when they arise. Understanding their origin, there is nothing to fear. All transformations and actions arise from causes. Those who do not seek their root when events have yet to come will inevitably be perplexed. Truly recognizing their origin, even if myriad wonders arise, the heart remains unshaken.

Lu said: With an empty mind and tranquil thoughts, reflecting inward and preserving the spirit, one can penetrate all obstacles and transform all living beings. Yet people only see its application—the traces of transformation—and do not recognize it. Penetration is the root of transformation. Why? Because its Dao is subtly applied and difficult to know, its achievements no different from human affairs. Thus, the Five Emperors and Three Kings are known only for their wisdom and strength, unable to recognize those whose achievements arose from transformation. Yet awareness has eight signs, and dreams have six omens—these are all the traces of living beings. Past events, action, gain, loss, sorrow, joy, life, and death are what form engages; ordinary, startled, thought, waking, joyful, and fearful dreams are what the spirit encounters. What form engages is all deemed awareness; what the spirit encounters is felt as dreams. Though awareness and dreams differ in manifestation, in transformation they are never distinct. Knowing the eight signs and six omens as the constant transformations of the Dao is to recognize their origin. If one knows to guard the spirit without disturbance and that transformations have origins, then what signs or omens could cause alarm?

*On Governance and Harmony*: Awareness engages with affairs, hence it is verified by the eight signs. Dreams conceal principle, hence they are divined by the six omens. What one encounters is called *the familiar*; what one does is called *action*. *Gain* refers to what one acquires; *loss* refers to what one forfeits. Sorrow and joy burden the heart; life and death transform the self. These eight are what form reveals and can be verified, hence it is said: These eight signs are what form engages.

The six states—*uprightness*, *startlement*, *contemplation*, *awakening*, *joy*, and *fear*—are what the ethereal soul intersects and can be divined, hence it is said: These six omens are what the spirit engages. In dreams, the ethereal soul intersects; in wakefulness, form unfolds. The alternation of day and night is transformation. Those who do not recognize the origin of responsive transformation will, when affairs arise, be perplexed by their cause, for they do not know they are dreaming and take themselves to be awake. Those who recognize the origin of responsive transformation will, when affairs arise, know their cause—this is what is called *great awakening* and knowing this to be the *great dream*. One who comprehends the Dao of day and night and knows it perceives the myriad things as one: who is awake, who dreams? What transformation could cause alarm?

Master Fan says: Wakefulness has eight signs, though they are what form engages, yet by their eight signs they are verified, and they are never absent from dreams. Dreams have six omens, though they are what the spirit intersects, yet by their six omens they are divined, and they never begin apart from wakefulness. Thus, the origin of responsive transformation likely arises from some cause. Those who do not recognize its origin will, when affairs arise, be perplexed by their cause. Those who recognize its origin will, when affairs arise, know their cause. Knowing their cause, then life and death too are great matters—one cannot transform with them, let alone the beginnings of profit and harm. What could give rise to distress? The heart is already alarmed, as if alarmed by transformation. The heart has what it loves, then it forgets its worries and grows dim; the heart has what alarms it, then it ponders its troubles and grows clear. Recognizing the origin of responsive transformation, then there is no trouble—what alarm could there be?

The waxing and waning, ebb and flow of this single body all communicate with Heaven and Earth and respond to the classes of things. Humans share qi with yin and yang; the body parallels Heaven and Earth in form. Fortune and misfortune alternate, and cannot but be mutually connected. Thus, when yin qi is strong, one dreams of wading through great waters and feels terror, for losing the center of harmony brings drowning and fear. When yang qi is strong, one dreams of wading through great fires and being scorched, for fire’s nature is fierce and violent, and encountering it brings burning. When both yin and yang are strong, one dreams of life and death, for yin and yang function through harmony; when they contend, they harm each other, bringing either life or death.

When overly sated, one dreams of giving; when overly hungry, one dreams of taking. Surplus leads to the desire to bestow; deficiency leads to the desire to seize. This too resembles wakefulness. Thus, those whose illness is *floating emptiness* dream of soaring; those whose illness is *sinking fullness* dream of drowning. Sleeping with a sash beneath one dreams of snakes; if a bird clutches one’s hair, one dreams of flying. These arise from the stimulation of corresponding things. When yin prevails, one dreams of fire; when illness approaches, one dreams of eating. Those who drink wine grieve; those who sing and dance weep. These are all *lucid dreams*: some arise from affairs stimulating response, some reach extremes and invert, as in the *Rites of Zhou*’s six dreams and six principles—there is no principle without reason.

Master Lu says: When spirit and qi cling to what is fixed, transformation follows; when yin and yang stimulate, dreams change. Some respond after reaching extremes; some appear as likenesses and traces; some manifest reality from causes; some invert principles and do not reveal their nature. If one concentrates the spirit and aligns with the true, obscurely responding to the Dao, then clarity and stillness penetrate transformation, and sorrow and joy cannot enter.

Master Fan says: Form has waxing and waning; qi has ebb and flow. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, this truly communicates with them; though the classes of things are numerous, this truly responds to them. Dreams and wakefulness correspond—how could this be mere coincidence? Thus, what one sees in dreams may be assailed by yin and yang, or stimulated by the transformations of things. Some are the inverse of wakefulness; some resemble affairs—likely they arise from some cause. The ancients divined the fortune of the six dreams by the sun, moon, and stars—was this not for this reason?

Master Liezi says: *Spiritual encounter* is dreaming; *formal engagement* is affairs. *Zhuangzi* says: In sleep, the spirit intersects; in wakefulness, form unfolds. Thus, daytime thoughts become nighttime dreams—what the spirit and form encounter. These thoughts are the affairs of wakeful emotion and concern, not as in ordinary speech. Daytime thoughts of such affairs later follow in dreams. Thus, when the spirit is concentrated, thoughts and dreams dissolve of themselves. With no emotional concerns by day, there are no dreams by night. True wakefulness does not speak; true dreams are not expressed—these are the comings and goings of the transformation of things. To dream of being a bird and soaring in the sky, to dream of being a fish and diving into the depths—these are the reciprocal transformations of emotion. Did the true persons of old truly forget themselves in wakefulness and not dream in sleep? Could it be mere empty words? The true person forgets all comings and goings—how could they sleep, let alone dream? This too is figurative speech to clarify principle.

Master Lu says: When the six emotions are all employed, people take them as real intentions. Those who recognize only the solitary mind take them as illusory—both are like dreams. Those who take dreams as real are like those who call waking real. They do not know that wakefulness too is the spirit’s movement, and dreaming too is the spirit’s activity. To believe one and not the other is to fail to comprehend. If one forgets the self, then there is no dreaming—how could there be another principle?

*On Governance and Harmony*: The world is unified by a single qi. This is why waxing and waning, ebb and flow all communicate with Heaven and Earth and respond to the classes of things. When yin qi is strong, one dreams of great waters and feels terror; when yang qi is strong, one dreams of great fires and is scorched. When both yin and yang are strong and harmonious, one may dream of life; when both are strong and discordant, one may dream of death. Those whose illness is *floating emptiness* dream of soaring; those whose illness is *sinking fullness* dream of drowning—this is the principle of waxing and waning. When overly sated, one dreams of giving; when overly hungry, one dreams of taking. When yin prevails, one dreams of fire; when illness approaches, one dreams of eating—this is the principle of ebb and flow. Sleeping with a sash beneath one dreams of snakes; if a bird clutches one’s hair, one dreams of flying—this arises from correspondence with their kind.

Those who drink wine dream of sorrow; those who sing of love dream of weeping—this is the reversal of their kind. For what the form encounters by day is stored, and thus what the spirit meets arises by night. Hence, what spirit and form undergo are all but the spontaneous workings of waxing and waning, ebb and flow.

As for one who darkly abides in singular truth, ever moving with the Dao/Way, waking and dreaming become one, reality and illusion both forgotten—this is the true person.

Fan [Yizhi] says: In sleep, the hun-souls intermingle, and so encounters become dreams. In waking, the form opens, and so contacts become affairs. Daytime thoughts and nighttime dreams are merely what form and spirit encounter. Yet there must be those whose spirit is concentrated—who penetrate day and night with knowing, who merge dream and waking into a single whole.

Yet when one suddenly awakens from sleep, the transformations of things come and go without the mind ever intervening. Thus one dreams of being a bird and soars to the heavens, dreams of being a fish and plunges into the abyss. Who knows whether Zhou dreamed he was a butterfly, or the butterfly dreamed it was Zhou? The myriad learnings transform themselves; transformation knows no joy or sorrow. Who but the supremely awakened can compare to this?

Nevertheless, even if dreams reverse to oneness, there remains deluded perception. When the Dao/Way reaches the true person, in stillness they share virtue/power with yin, in movement they share waves with yang, using truth to darken illusion—truth and illusion become one truth. Waking and dreaming are no longer distinct. This is why their waking is free of delusion, and their sleep is dreamless.

Yet the Huaxu state of the Yellow Emperor is not unattainable—it is merely presented here to clarify the principle. *The Four Explications of the Perfect Dao-Virtue True Classic*, Scroll Seven, concludes.

*The Four Explications of the Perfect Dao-Virtue True Classic*, Scroll Eight.

In the southwestern corner of the Western Limits lay a land unknown where its borders touched, called the State of Ancient Wilderness. Yin and yang’s qi never mingled there, so cold and heat knew no distinction; the light of sun and moon never shone, so day and night knew no division. Its people ate no food, wore no clothes, and slept often, waking but once every fifty days. What they did in dreams they took for real; what they saw awake they deemed illusory.

The lands of the Four Seas—what they called the Central Kingdom—stretch across the north and south of the Yellow River, beyond Mount Tai to the east and west, spanning over ten thousand li. There, yin and yang are measured with precision, so one cold follows one heat; dawn and dusk are clearly divided, so one day follows one night. Its people range from wise to foolish; the ten thousand things flourish and multiply; skills and arts abound in countless forms. There are rulers and subjects in hierarchy, rites and laws to uphold order, and their deeds defy enumeration. Yet they too alternate between waking and sleep, taking what they do awake as real and what they see in dreams as false.

In the northeastern corner of the Eastern Limits lies a land called the State of Flourishing Desolation. Its earth is ever warm, bathed in the lingering glow of sun and moon, yet no fine crops grow there. Its people eat roots and fruits, ignorant of cooked food. Their nature is fierce and unyielding; the strong oppress the weak, prizing victory over righteousness. They run swiftly and rest little, ever awake and never sleeping.

Such differences in customs are like the reversal of learning and dreaming, where waking and sleeping diverge in nature—each suited to its own place, none to be faulted. Lu remarks: Thus, though these two lands differ, the spirit that unites them is never distinct. Therefore, we know that the workings of the spirit’s principle are not bound by dreams or waking, food or clothing. If one clings to desires without cease, the heart remains entangled in the mundane.

In the *Zhenghe* commentary: Yang is movement and light; yin is stillness and darkness. The southwestern corner of the Western Limits inclines toward yin, so its people turn toward darkness, still and given to sleep. The northeastern corner of the Eastern Limits inclines toward yang, so its people turn toward light, active and ever awake. The Central Kingdom lies where heaven and earth converge, where the four seasons meet, where wind and rain gather, where yin and yang harmonize. Why should it be bright and active? Why dark and at rest? Movement and stillness do not miss their time; waking and dreaming dissolve into real and illusory. Who but those who embody the true and constant and excel in transformation can attain this?

Fan observes: The rooster is bright by day and dim by night; the owl is dim by day and bright by night—such is the nature of creatures. So too with the people of the southwestern corner of the Western Limits, the northeastern corner of the Eastern Limits, and the Central Kingdom: their customs differ, their waking and dreaming diverge. How can one fathom the reason?

The Lord of Yin in Zhou greatly expanded his estate. His servants toiled from dawn to dusk without respite. An old laborer, his strength spent, was yet driven harder. By day, he groaned as he worked; by night, exhausted, he slept deeply. His spirit scattered and waned, and every night he dreamed he was a lord, ruling over the people, overseeing the affairs of the state, feasting in palaces and towers, indulging every desire—his joy beyond compare. When he woke, he returned to servitude. Someone consoled him for his toil, but the laborer said: “A human life spans a hundred years, divided equally between day and night. By day, I am a slave—it is bitter, to be sure—but by night, I am a lord, my joy beyond compare. What cause have I for resentment?”

The Lord of Yin, his heart consumed by worldly affairs and his mind fixed on his family’s wealth, exhausted both body and spirit. By night, he too slept deeply and exhausted. Every night, he dreamed he was a servant, running errands and laboring without cease, enduring every scolding and beating. In his sleep, he muttered and groaned, not resting until dawn. Troubled, the Lord of Yin sought counsel from his friend.

His friend replied: "Your rank suffices to honor your person, your wealth exceeds all needs—you surpass others by far. Yet in dreams you become a servant: such alternations of suffering and ease follow the constant course of things. For prosperity and decline succeed one another, and when joy reaches its height, sorrow arises. Thus what waking finds fair, dreams may find foul. Can one truly possess both waking and dreaming states at once?"

Upon hearing his friend’s words, the Lord of Yin eased the laborer’s tasks and lessened his own cares. His illness then abated somewhat.

This passage also shows that waking and dreaming are not distinct—suffering and joy each find their place. The laborer toils by day yet finds ease by night; the Lord of Yin enjoys honor by day yet suffers humiliation by night. If the principle does not permit both, neither state can claim superiority.

Lu said: "He who wearies the body yet rests the spirit will find waking wearisome but dreams peaceful. He who wearies the spirit yet toils the body will find waking pleasant but dreams bitter. The spirit is the master of life, yet men do not know to nurture the spirit to calm the body. The body is the vessel of the spirit, yet men do not know to sustain the body to ease the spirit. Thus when body and spirit are both exhausted, both exceed their proper measure. If labor and rest are balanced, illness will abate."

Zhenghe said: "The interplay of yin and yang, harmonious and even—this is the form Heaven bestows. When form loses its balance, it becomes biased and gives rise to illness. Some suffer by day yet find joy by night; some find ease by day yet toil by night. Such reversal from beginning to end is the inevitable principle. By easing the laborer’s tasks and reducing his own cares, each attains equilibrium, and so his illness abates.

Yet all things are of one equal nature—who is awake, who dreams? While dreaming, one does not know it is a dream; only upon waking does one recognize the dream. But even then, the fool believes himself awake. Only with great awakening does one realize this was the great dream. Was he lord or shepherd? How rigid the distinction!

Fan said: "The Lord of Yin—master of his domain—might be called at ease, yet night after night he dreams of being a servant. The laborer—one who hurries to serve—might be called wretched, yet night after night he dreams of being a lord. Thus suffering and ease alternate, as if some predestined pattern governs the exchange. What the world values is not truly valuable; what the world despises is not truly base. From the perspective of the Dao, things hold no inherent rank—they merely exalt one another as slaves do. Are their servants and concubines not fit to govern one another? Do they not take turns as lord and subject?"

The fool toils by day, yet by night may fancy himself awake, anxiously certain—was he lord or shepherd? How rigid the distinction!

There was a man of Zheng gathering firewood in the wilds when he came upon a startled deer. He pursued it, struck it down, and killed it. Fearing discovery, he hastily concealed it in a ditch and covered it with plantain leaves, overcome with joy. Soon after, he forgot where he had hidden it and took the whole affair for a dream. As he walked along, he recounted the tale aloud. A passerby overheard, followed his words, and took the deer. Returning home, he told his wife, who said: "The woodcutter dreamed he had caught a deer but did not know where it was. Now you have it—his dream was real indeed." The man replied: "Are you dreaming that the woodcutter caught a deer? Was there ever a woodcutter at all? Now that I truly have the deer, is your dream real?" The wife said: "I hold the deer—what need have I to know whether he dreamed or I dreamed?"

The woodcutter, returning home discontented at his loss, dreamed that night of the very place he had hidden the deer, and of the one who had taken it. At dawn, he followed his dream, found the deer, and brought suit against the man. The case was taken to the magistrate, who declared: "At first, you truly caught the deer but falsely called it a dream; now you truly dreamed of catching it but falsely call it real. He truly took your deer, and now you dispute it with him. Your wife, meanwhile, claims both dreamed of another’s deer—no one truly has it. Since I now hold this deer, let it be divided between you." He reported this to the Lord of Zheng, who exclaimed: "Ah! Is the magistrate about to dream he divides another’s deer?" He consulted his minister, who replied: "Whether it was dream or waking, your servant cannot discern. To distinguish dream from reality, only the Yellow Emperor or Confucius could suffice. How do the sages discern dream from waking? Simply by knowing they are not different. Now that the Yellow Emperor and Confucius are gone, who can decide? For the moment, let us trust the magistrate’s words." *Xun* means "to trust." Pronounced *gou*.

Amid joy and anger, one remains deluded, still unable to distinguish the emptiness or reality of waking and dreaming—how much less when waking and dreaming are fundamentally nonexistent? Lu said: Those who take it as a dream merely grasp at delusion. When the spirit’s perception is unclear, it becomes a false dream. The one who overheard and took the deer was likewise unclear—this too was a dream. The one who obtained the deer then dreamed of seeking it, bringing the matter before the magistrate: all were unclear, their deluded feelings the same. Thus, it was divided between them. Could any but the sage see through such delusion? Yet in an age without sages, affairs lack certainty, so the magistrate, unable to resolve what was unresolved, only added further delusion.

*Zhenghe* observes: From the Dao’s perspective, who is awake and who dreams? Right and wrong are but a single qi. Is there truly any distinction? With the rise of penal codes and names, truth and falsehood emerge. Thus, one truly obtains a deer, yet it is falsely called a dream. One truly dreams of shelter, yet it is falsely called real. The paths of right and wrong tangle and clash—how can their distinctions be known? The Yellow Emperor and Confucius, merging truth with delusion, truly had no "this" or "that." The magistrate’s words, using truth to discern delusion—truly had no "this" or "that." Thus, seeking verification from the Yellow Emperor or Confucius yields nothing, and so trusting the magistrate’s words suffices.

Fan said: One who embodies the Dao perceives solitude and embraces stillness, where gain and loss cannot burden the heart. The deer, a creature fond of herds, is not solitary. Swift of foot, it does not embrace stillness. Thus, one gathers firewood in the wild, obtains it, then suddenly loses it—concluding it was a dream. Another hears of it and takes it, then suddenly obtains it—likewise deeming the other’s account a true dream. Upon returning to tell his household, he too calls it a dream of another’s deer. Then who knows the reason for the deer’s gain or loss, the dream’s emptiness or reality? "This" and "that" attack each other, giving rise to deluded distinctions and the seeds of strife. Within the dream, one dreams of interpreting the dream—again dreaming of dividing another’s deer. How could one know this is the great dream? Only with a true person comes true knowledge. Thus, only the Yellow Emperor and Confucius could distinguish waking from dreaming. The magistrate acts to govern affairs; the minister of state discourses on the Dao.

In Song, in Yangli, there was a man named Hua Zi who, in middle age, suffered from forgetfulness. What he took in the morning he forgot by evening; what he gave at night he forgot by dawn. On the road, he forgot to walk; at home, he forgot to sit. He did not recognize what came before, nor what came after. His entire household was distressed. They consulted a diviner and sought a prognostication, but none was given...

They sought a shaman to pray for him, but the malady remained; they consulted a physician to treat him, yet it persisted. In Lu there was a Confucian scholar who offered his services, claiming he could cure Hua Zi. His wife and children offered half their household property in exchange for the remedy. The scholar said: "This is certainly not a matter for divination by hexagrams or omens. For the subtle workings arise there, while the oracle bones respond here—thus good and ill fortune may be inferred from hexagrams, and hidden truths traced through images and numbers. But now, the forgetful mind is as still as cold ashes, as vast and empty as an abandoned mansion. The sage would dance before its mirror—how could hexagrams or omens reveal it? Nor is it a matter for prayer and supplication. For trust and obedience may invoke blessings, and upright sincerity may dispel deceit and falsehood—this is the natural course of things. Thus those who betray their conscience before the gods or incur resentment in human affairs all bring ruin upon themselves through such means. But when the emotions are free of obsession and conduct free of madness, then it is not a matter for prayer and supplication. Nor is it a condition for acupuncture or medicine. When illness lodges in the organs or disperses through the flesh, one must examine the pulses to discern excess and deficiency, and administer medicine or stone needles to attack the affliction. Yet if the heart is not disordered by desires, nor the body harmed by cold or heat, then the physician’s art is rightly set aside.

I shall attempt to transform his mind and alter his thoughts—perhaps he may yet be cured. But if forgetfulness utterly lacks thought or concern, what is there to transform? This principle seems to suggest that it is easy to restore a mind where none exists, to restore thoughts where none remain—though the words may appear contradictory. Thus, I tested him: I exposed him to the cold, and he sought clothing; I starved him, and he sought food; I plunged him into darkness, and he sought light. First, I took away what he relied upon to provoke a response. The scholar joyfully told his son: 'The illness can be cured.' Yet my method is secret, passed down through generations and not disclosed to others. I asked that all attendants be dismissed, and that I remain alone with him in his chamber for seven days. They complied. None knew what he did."

The scholar’s methods were manifold, not confined to a single path of verification. Yet an illness of many years was dispelled in a single morning. The preceding lines speak of how shamans, physicians, and all arts of calculation had exhausted their thoughts, yet the scholar alone, by addressing what ailed him, first drew Hua Zi’s forgetfulness into alignment with the natural, illuminating the extremity of no-mind—a state not to be regained through mere techniques or repeated inference. The scholar’s achievement surpassed that of historians and shamans; when principle is not obscured, myriad methods may be employed to guide. He further sought to awaken the forgetful one to the realization that his former oblivion had nearly touched the ultimate truth.

Lu said: *"The* Laozi *states: 'In the pursuit of learning, one daily accumulates; in the pursuit of the Dao, one daily diminishes. Diminish and diminish again, until one reaches non-action.' Hua Zi studied the Dao and forgot his own existence; the scholar studied existence to augment his knowledge. To augment knowledge is to distinguish right and wrong before one’s eyes; to forget existence is to remain untouched by gain or loss in the celestial storehouse. How could divination or medicine cure such a state?"*

Thus, the scholar used manifold means to lure his mind, entangling his thoughts in right and wrong. Hua Zi thereby lost the Dao and afterward virtue; having lost virtue, right and wrong clashed within his breast, and the Way of sitting in forgetfulness was lost. Hua Zi, now awakened, flew into a great rage, cast out his wife, punished his son, and brandished a spear to chase the scholar away. The people of Song seized him and demanded an explanation. Hua Zi said: *"Before, I was forgetful—vast and boundless, unaware of the existence or nonexistence of heaven and earth. Now, suddenly recalling the past, decades of survival and loss, sorrow and joy, likes and dislikes—ten thousand threads of disturbance arise within me. I fear that future survival and loss, sorrow and joy, likes and dislikes will disorder my heart like this. Can I ever recover even a moment’s forgetfulness?"*

If even an illness resembling the ultimate truth could provoke such a response, how much more so when embodying its extremity?

Lu said: *"Hua Zi longed to return to the real but found no way, so he raged at his wife and son and drove out the scholar."*

Zi Gong heard of this and found it strange, reporting it to Confucius. Confucius said: *"This is not something within your grasp."* Then, turning to Yan Hui, he said: *"Record this."* This principle should indeed be within Ci’s reach; the reason for deflecting him was to entrust the subtle appreciation to a greater worthy.

Lu said: *"Zi Gong was a scholar of disputation, eager for advancement and diligent in learning—hence the words 'This is not within your grasp.' Yan Hui, who loved learning and was second only to the sage, never deviated from benevolence—thus he was instructed to record it, to make the Dao manifest to the great worthy."*

Zhenghe said: *"To know forgetfulness is the harmony of the mind of right and wrong. Cast aside the limbs and depart from form; dismiss cleverness and abandon knowledge. The celestial mechanism does not unfold; silently, one accords with the Dao. Dim and seemingly absent, yet present in the world—how could it be perceived? The common people are bright; I alone am as if benighted. The plain and unadorned scorn the root of established affairs, while knowledge penetrates the divine—this is why the sage does not fall ill. Yet the world deems ignorance an illness, calling Hua Zi’s state 'forgetful sickness.' Then they sought to transform his heart and alter his thoughts, causing survival and loss, sorrow and joy, likes and dislikes—ten thousand threads of disturbance—to arise and burden his form. By disturbing his heart, the scholar claimed to cure his illness. Yet to open a man’s mind and harm his life, and for Confucius not to speak of it to Zi Gong—because of its multiplicity and confusion—while instructing Yan Hui to record it, was due to his ability to sit in forgetfulness."*

Fan said: *"Song is where fire resides, and brightness follows; yang is by nature ever-floating and active. Within, it dwells but is not profound; like a flower, it blooms yet detaches from the root. The 'son' is but an infant, yet by middle age, one has already deeply immersed in human artifice. The illness of forgetfulness is a return to nature. A fleeting restoration of heaven—yet it is called an illness, revealing the world’s desires as the true sickness, not one of delusion without recovery. The ancients, in speaking of attaining the Dao, necessarily prized the forgetting of the heart. Was not Song Zi’s illness close to the forgetting of the heart, and thus to the attainment of the Dao’s reality? In reality, one attains unity. Taking in the morning and forgetting by evening—forgetting in the morning; giving in the evening and forgetting by dawn—forgetting in the evening. On the road, one forgets walking—forgetting on the road; in the chamber, one forgets sitting—forgetting in the chamber. Not recognizing the past—forgetting in the present; not recognizing the present—forgetting in the future. First, forgetting taking and giving—forgetting things; then, forgetting walking and sitting—forgetting place; finally, forgetting past and future—forgetting time. Yet how could this be considered an illness? Having called it an illness, there must be one who suffers it—yet if it is called 'forgetting,' who then suffers it? Not knowing that he was never ill, the household poisoned him; when poisoning failed, they consulted historians and divined; when divination failed, they consulted shamans and prayed; when prayer failed, they consulted physicians and attacked. None of the three could apply their arts, and then a Ru from Lu followed behind, self-promoting his ability to cure. Lu is a land of culture and refinement; Ru are the descendants of benevolence and righteousness. The great unity was thus sundered. Yet if he himself had no doubts, then fortune and misfortune could not be known—what use were the hexagrams? If he himself had no faults, then ghosts and spirits could not fathom him—what use were prayers? If he himself had no illness, then yin and yang could not invade him—what use were medicines? Desiring to cure his forgetfulness, they tested his heart and altered his thoughts, hoping he might yet be healed. Thus, they exposed him to the elements, making him aware of cold; starved him, making him aware of hunger; plunged him into darkness, making him aware of light. The heart, not being singular, became paired with things. Aware of cold, he sought clothing; aware of hunger, he sought food; aware of darkness, he sought light—perception was no longer solitary, and the heart had an opposite."*

When they chiseled for seven days, the seven apertures of Chaos opened at once; when they swept it away in a single morning, the ten thousand forms of the world arose all together.

In great fury he banished his wife and children, for they were close to him, and thus his reproach ran deep. Grasping his halberd he drove out the scholars, for they made demands of him, and thus his resentment cut keen.

Loss and gain, sorrow and joy, love and hate—once, each had been unknown to him; now, they ceaselessly swarmed within. How could even a moment’s forgetfulness be attained?

Zigong questioned Confucius in astonishment, for he was submerged in the disputations of broad learning. Confucius turned to Yan Hui and recorded it, for he had attained the marvel of sitting-in-forgetfulness. Yet was Huazi’s forgetfulness truly forgetfulness? His affliction was to forget in darkness, and upon awakening, to rage in sudden fury—he had not yet forgotten both and been transformed by the Dao.

Among the Feng clan of Qin there was a son who in youth was clever, but in manhood suffered from delusion. Cleverness is not delusion, yet the flaw of using cleverness must lead to delusion. He heard singing and took it for weeping; saw white and took it for black; smelled fragrance and took it for rot. The *Monthly Ordinances* says: its scent is rot. He tasted sweetness and took it for bitterness; took wrong for right. Wherever his mind wandered—heaven and earth, the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—nothing was not inverted.

Lu said: He who prides himself on petty cleverness is deemed wise by others. He who embodies the Dao and preserves harmony is deemed foolish. One who equalizes sound and color, who treats water and fire as illusions—such distinctions lie beyond the discernment of common folk. Thus they take the Dao for delusion.

Yang’s family told his father: “The gentlemen of Lu abound in arts and skills—might they cure him? Why not seek them out?” His father traveled to Lu, but passing through Chen, he encountered Lao Dan and recounted his son’s condition. Lao Dan said: “How do you know your son is the deluded one? Today, all under heaven are bewildered by right and wrong, benighted by profit and harm. Those sharing his affliction are many, yet none awaken. Moreover, the delusion of one man is not enough to overturn a household; the delusion of one household is not enough to overturn a village; the delusion of one village is not enough to overturn a state; the delusion of one state is not enough to overturn the world. If all under heaven were deluded, who then could overturn it? Were all under heaven like your son, *you* would be the deluded one.

Joy and sorrow, sound and color, scent and taste, right and wrong—who can set them aright? Even my words here may not be free of delusion, let alone the gentlemen of Lu, those couriers of confusion. The gentlemen of Lu extol benevolence and righteousness, proclaiming right and wrong with clarity—hence they are couriers of confusion. How could they resolve another’s delusion? Gather your provisions and return home. To ‘gather’ is to abandon.”

This passage clarifies that the principles of right and wrong cannot be wholly fixed, for all disputes arise from the many overwhelming the few. Lu said: “To gather is to abandon.” The world teems with vulgar scholars; those who awaken to the Dao are few. The multitude, lost in delusion, scorn the solitary wise as deluded. Thus the *Laozi* says: “When lesser men hear the Dao, they laugh aloud—were they not to laugh, it would not be the Dao.” To seek the scholars of Zhao Jing to rectify right and wrong is to stray ever farther from the Dao.

The Confucians of Lu, far from the Way of forgetting form and preserving spirit, are themselves profoundly deluded. How could they enlighten another’s confusion? Better to abandon your provisions and hasten home.

In the *Zhenghe* edition: The manifestation of right and wrong is the diminishment of the Dao. That side has its right and wrong, this side has its right and wrong—so too with the sounds of singing and weeping, the colors of black and white, the scents of fragrance and rot, the tastes of sweet and bitter. Within the four seas, each man affirms what he affirms and denies what he denies. Who then shall set them right? The people’s delusion...

The delusion has endured for long indeed. Those who whisper knowingly call *them* deluded—yet how could I claim to know their distinctions? This is why Laozi said to his father, “How could you presume to know your son’s delusion?” And again: “Grief and joy, sound and color, scent and taste, right and wrong—who can set them right?” The Dark Pearl was lost, and only Image-Net retrieved it. Yet the affliction of delusion and confusion—how could the world recognize it?

Fan said: Benevolence is the human heart. To act from the heart is what is called wisdom. To be wise from youth is merely to open to heaven. But when one reaches maturity and contracts the affliction of delusion and confusion, then knowledge becomes useless, and one drifts ever farther from the boundless Dao. In the northern lands, winter is called *wang*. *Wang* means the convergence of being. To be deluded to the point of *wang*—is this not nearly to reach the utter void, becoming the very source to which the myriad beings turn? Yet it is called an affliction because the world takes it as such.

To hear singing and mistake it for weeping is not to be without sound, but merely to fail to distinguish sounds. To see a white procession and call it black is not to be without color, but merely to fail to discern colors. To savor fragrance as rot is to approach the absence of scent. To taste sweetness as bitterness is to approach the absence of flavor. To take wrong for right is to arrive at the bounded Dao, where right and wrong have never yet arisen. At its root, wherever the mind turns—heaven and earth, the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—all are inverted. Is this not the state where the ten thousand things merge, utterly without distinction?

Yang told his father that the gentlemen of Lu prized many arts and skills, for they personally practiced benevolence and righteousness, clearly distinguishing right and wrong, thus dissecting the undivided wholeness of the Dao. His father, traveling to Lu, passed through Chen and encountered Laozi, to whom he recounted his son’s obstinacy. For Laozi’s way was rooted in profundity, ordered by simplicity, and embodied the vastness of the Dao.

At that time, the people of the world were bewildered by right and wrong, benighted by profit and harm. Those sharing the same affliction were many, yet none awakened. Who, then, was deluded? Who was not? From oneself to one’s family, from family to village, from village to state, from state to the world—none were not wholly deluded. Who could overturn this? Had the hearts of all under heaven been like your son’s, then *you* would have been the deluded one.

Moreover, grief and joy, sound and color, scent and taste, right and wrong—these incline and contend, yet who can determine which is correct? Who could I appoint to set them right, aligning them with me or with you? If they were aligned with me or with you, how could they then be set right to differ from me or from you? And if they were set to differ from me or from you, how could they be set right to avoid becoming the very messengers of delusion?

To seek to dispel another’s delusion only deepens the confusion, turning back upon your own nature and spirit, leaving no path to enter. Thus, abandon your provisions and hasten home. Laozi would surely say: “My words are not necessarily free of delusion—only I wish to cast off the burden of speech.”

A man of Yan was born in Yan and grew up in Chu. In old age, he returned to his homeland. Passing through the state of Jin, his traveling companions deceived him, pointing to a city and saying, “This is the city of Yan.” The man’s expression turned somber. They pointed to a shrine and said, “This is the village shrine of your homeland.” He sighed deeply. They pointed to a house and said, “This is your ancestors’ dwelling.” He wept silently. They pointed to a mound and said, “This is your ancestors’ grave.” He could not restrain his sobs. The companions burst into laughter, saying, “We were only teasing you—this is Jin!” The man was overcome with shame.

When he finally reached Yan and truly saw the city and shrines of Yan, truly saw his ancestors’ dwelling and graves, his sorrow was far less keen. This passage shows that emotions rise to a peak, but once grief and joy have passed, what once moved him now evoked no joy or sorrow.

Lu said: Human nature is fundamentally similar, but habits drive people far apart—each follows what their emotions and customs find familiar. Born in Yan, one does not depart from one’s roots. Raised in Chu, one grows accustomed to what one knows. Returning to one’s roots yet failing to recognize them, one is easily swayed by superficial resemblances. When deceived by what *seems* familiar, confusion arises. Realizing the deception, one feels shame, forgetting one’s emotions. Even upon seeing the truth, one still regards it as insubstantial. Thus, nothing is truly understood, and one’s emotions are swayed by others.

How much more so for the gentlemen of today, who cling to the cities and shrines of Jin as if they were real—how could they know the supreme Dao of nurturing the spirit and returning to the root?

Fan said: The realm of inherent nature spans the ages unchanged. Yet those who chase things and forget to return are many, drifting recklessly along the path of ceaseless change. Even those who seek to restore their nature through vulgar learning only deepen their confusion, mistaking things for themselves. Is this not because they know not where their true dwelling lies?

The man of Yan, born in Yan and raised in Chu, had chased things and lost the condition of his nature. In old age, returning to his homeland, he sought to restore his nature and recover his original state. Passing through Jin, his companions deceived him, pointing to a city and saying, “This is the city of Yan,” and his expression turned somber. Pointing to a shrine and saying, “This is the shrine of Yan,” he sighed deeply. Pointing to a house and saying, “This is your ancestors’ dwelling,” he wept silently. Pointing to a mound and saying, “This is your ancestors’ grave,” he could not restrain his sobs. Then, when told, “This is Jin,” he was overcome with shame—this was the condition of profound confusion, of mistaking things for oneself and failing to return to one’s true dwelling.

When he finally reached Yan and truly saw the city and shrines of Yan, truly saw his ancestors’ dwelling and graves, his sorrow was far less keen.

The gain and loss here may be what is meant by "entering and only then finding joy." This person, though said to have been deluded before returning, is fundamentally different from those who exhaust themselves in toil without knowing where to return. The *Xu Wugui* speaks of the man from Yue, and Zhuang Zhou offers the parable of the old capital—both align with this meaning.

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, with Four Commentaries, Volume Eight* concludes here.

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, with Four Commentaries, Volume Nine*
Compiled by He Guang, the Recluse of Harmonious Light

Confucius was a wise man who knew without being taught. Forgetting knowledge, he had nothing to know; employing knowledge, he had nothing to accomplish. Grasping the essence of spirit and moving alone, forgetting emotions and following principle, he was one who abided in serene and mysterious illumination.

Lu said: This chapter speaks of those who realize the Dao of non-action, who may then act without limitation. Worldly people see only the traces of the sage but do not know the root of what he has realized. Scholars merely grasp the beginning of transcending emotions but do not understand its application in saving all things—both miss the mean.

Zhenghe said: When acted upon, it is full; when left alone, it is empty. Observing subtleties in tandem, neither being nor non-being is obscured. Nothing is not forgotten, nothing is not possessed. In profound tranquility without limit, this is called uniting with principle.

Fan said: Only by forgetting knowledge can one refrain from using it; only by attaining the spirit can one avoid being named a sage. Such a one, turning inward in contemplation, finds right and wrong, profit and harm do not enter his breast. Thus, his eyes are like ears, his ears like nose, his nose like mouth—all are the same. What is within me is like this. Looking outward, praise and blame, sorrow and joy do not disturb his mind. Thus, he sees life as death, wealth as poverty, others as swine, and himself as one among them. What belongs to things is like this. Though his strength may subdue the world, he does not rely on it; though his eloquence may carve all things, he does not delight in it. True knowledge and true ability are found within oneself—this is what is meant by "non-action yet nothing left undone."

Confucius was at leisure when Zigong entered to attend him, bearing the weight of fame.

Zhenghe said: The sage retreats and hides in secrecy, thus his heart does not cling to joy or misfortune. Sharing the people’s concerns, he has sorrow.

Zigong did not dare ask. Though Zigong had not reached the level of nature and the Dao of Heaven, he had thoroughly heard the Master’s teachings. The sage’s freedom from sorrow was beyond the reach of common understanding—how much more so for Zigong? He did not dare ask because the elucidation of the profound principle would only arise after elevating one to the level of a great worthy, and only then could subtle words be proclaimed.

He went out and told Yan Hui, who took up his zither and sang. When Confucius heard this, he summoned Yan Hui to enter and asked, "Why are you alone in joy?"

Hui said, "Why is the Master alone in sorrow?"

Hui did not speak his intended question but used the zither and song to provoke the Master’s words.

Confucius said, "First, speak your own mind."

Hui said, "I once heard the Master say, 'Rejoice in Heaven and know fate, thus there is no sorrow'—this is why I am joyful. Heaven is the portion of nature; fate is the reckoning of success and failure."

Zhenghe said: Rejoicing in Heaven means not blaming others; knowing fate means not overrelying on effort. Following nature, the heart is not burdened—how could there be sorrow?

Confucius, his expression turning solemn, asked, "Did I truly say this? I will clarify that these words were incomplete." He showed a questioning demeanor to reveal the inadequacy of the statement. "Your understanding is mistaken. Those were my words of the past; let my words of today be taken as correct. The words of the past arose from circumstances; what I now clarify reaches their ultimate meaning."

Zhenghe said: Where does the Dao not go and yet not abide? What words abide and yet cannot be spoken? Adapting to the times is called "right"; embodying the constancy of the Dao is called "correct." Thus, the words of the past could be considered right, but from today’s perspective, they cannot be considered correct. Yet taking the past words as right and today’s words as correct, what is spoken remains unsettled. Knowing that the times have no fixed standard, how can those bound by teachings speak of this?

"You merely know that rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate bring no sorrow, but you do not know that rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate entail great sorrow. Knowing nothing that is not known, delighting in nothing that is not delightful, sorrowing in nothing that is not sorrowful—this is what is called 'great.'"

Zhenghe said: True joy is without joy, yet nothing is not joyful; true knowledge is without knowledge, yet nothing is not known. Cultivating it in oneself, there is no sorrow; cultivating it in the world, there is great sorrow.

Fan said: "Retiring from court" is called "leisurely dwelling"; "leisure" is called "at ease." Thus, there is "leisurely dwelling" and "dwelling at ease." Zigong’s learning grasped the words but not their underlying meaning. The Master’s teachings could be heard, but his words on nature and the Dao of Heaven could not be heard. Thus, regarding the sage’s sorrow, he dared not ask. Yan Hui’s learning was comprehensive yet subtle, attaining form and awakening—not truly foolish. "All day without opposition" was not to assist me; there was nothing not delightful. Thus, hearing the sage’s sorrow, he took up his zither and sang, intending to provoke something. This is why Confucius, upon hearing it, summoned him, and Hui entered with the question of "alone in joy."

The one who embodies the Dao is always joyful; the one who engages with affairs is always sorrowful. Heaven is what acts without acting—I rejoice in it and do not refuse. Fate is what gives rise to nature; thus, I know it without obstruction. Rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate, I set all things in motion yet do not share the sage’s sorrow. Looking down upon the world, all things arise, yet none burden my heart—this is why Hui is joyful. Yet this is only so much; it is not the ultimate.

Thus, Confucius took this as the words of the past.

Speech has no constant rightness; responding to the moment makes it correct. What was right in the past may now be deemed wrong; what was permissible then may now be rejected. You merely know that "rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate" brings no sorrow, yet you do not grasp its great sorrow—this is why you cannot take present words as correct.

Viewed thus, the *Liezi* may be said to delve deeper than the *Yi*. Wang Tong said: "All under Heaven are sorrowful; I alone attain freedom from sorrow." How could he have understood the sage’s intent? Now I tell you the truth: cultivating the self, accepting poverty or prosperity, knowing that going and coming are not of the self, and banishing disorder from the heart’s reckoning—this is what you call "rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate" without sorrow. This merely distinguishes the boundaries of inner and outer, discerns the realms of honor and disgrace, and no more. How could it attain the state of "no right, no wrong"?

Lu said: "To rejoice in Heaven, to know fate, and to be free from sorrow—this is the way of timely success, not the task of responding to things and saving the world."

Confucius said: "I once held these words, but now they differ from the past."

*Zhenghe* states: Those who understood the Dao in ancient times knew that action and stillness were not within human power but lay with Heaven, that rise and fall were not governed by effort but by fate. They neither rejoiced in success nor felt shame in failure, adapting to what came and went. To "adapt" is to follow. Abiding in the moment and dwelling in accord, sorrow and joy could not enter—what sorrow could there be? Yet this is only the cultivation of the self.

Formerly, I studied the *Odes* and *Documents*, rectified rites and music, intending to govern all under Heaven and bequeath them to later ages. The *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music are the instruments of governing the world. The sage employed them to remedy the ills of the time, but when their use strays from the Dao, they offer no benefit to order. It was not merely to cultivate the self or govern the state of Lu. The sage comprehends all things and saves the world through the Dao. To secure the self and rescue one state is not what makes a sage. Yet the rulers and ministers of Lu daily lost their proper order; benevolence and righteousness further declined, and human nature grew ever more shallow. If the Dao does not prevail in one state or in this generation, what of all under Heaven and future ages?

The arts of governing the world truly require benevolence and righteousness. Once the world is ordered, the arts employed should be set aside. If, when all affairs are concluded, one clings to them without letting go, those who follow the truth will be few, while those who seek profit will be many. The beginning of decline and shallowness truly lies here. Observing all under Heaven through one state, and future ages through the present, are the ills of governance not the same?

Only one who is perfectly adaptable and unobstructed can follow change as it arises, not lingering in one place. I once knew that the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music could not save the world from disorder, yet I did not know how to reform them—this is the sorrow of those who "rejoice in Heaven and know fate." Only by discarding the misuse of rites and music, not their proper use, can rites and music not be abandoned. Thus, I said I did not know how to reform them. Yet raising this as a sorrow lays the groundwork for what follows, which is why I spoke thus first.

Lu said: The *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music are not insufficient as the methods for governing all under Heaven, yet the theories of the world cannot be saved by the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music alone. If one discards these methods, there will be nothing left as the foundation of order. This is the profound sorrow of those who possess the Dao.

*Zhenghe* states: The *Odes* and *Documents* record the words of governance; rites and music are the instruments of governance. Confucius embodied the true Dao to cultivate himself, transcending form, color, fame, and reputation, yet he pitied the world’s ills. Thus, he spoke of benevolence and righteousness, clarified rites and music, and employed every means to console the hearts of all under Heaven. Yet the people of the world, losing their essence and drowning their minds, had no way to return to their nature and restore their original state. Benevolence and righteousness further declined, human nature grew more shallow, and the Dao did not prevail in that generation. Considering the world and later ages, this is the cause for sorrow.

Nevertheless, I have grasped it: those who "rejoice without joy" and "know without knowledge" do not refer to what the ancients called joy and knowledge. *Zhuangzi* said: "To rejoice in poverty or success, in things—this is not the sage." Thus, the ancients did not take the absence of joy as joy, nor the absence of knowledge as knowledge. Following what they rejoiced in, principle naturally has no joy; following what they knew, principle naturally has no knowledge. Without joy and without knowledge—this is true joy and true knowledge. To be entirely without joy and without knowledge is to be able to rejoice in the world’s joys, know the world’s knowledge, yet remain without self-interest. Thus, there is nothing not rejoiced in, nothing not known, nothing not sorrowed over, nothing not done. Abiding in the root and embodying completeness, one can act without acting and yet do all things.

What is there to discard in the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music? What is there to reform? If one seeks to diminish the *Odes* and *Documents* and alter the arts of governance, is this the way to remedy ills? Employ them without clinging, act without reliance, and things will be preserved of themselves.

Lu said: To know what Heaven’s decree allows no alternative and to accept one’s lot without sorrow—this is the constant heart of the noble person.
