Yet those in ancient times who opened things and accomplished tasks, who benefited people and improved customs, did not rest in their joy or rely on their knowledge. They preceded Heaven without opposing it, followed Heaven without defying its time—this is true joy and true knowledge. Being thus, there is nothing not rejoiced in, nothing not known, and thus nothing not done. How could they merely entrust themselves to it? Therefore, the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music are indeed the foundation for assisting transformation. What is there to reform?

Yan Hui faced north, bowed, and said: "I too have grasped it." This is what is meant by "not opposing yet seeming foolish."

He went out and told Zigong. Zigong was bewildered and lost, unable to fully align with the ultimate words, and thus fell into confusion. Returning home, he pondered deeply for seven days without sleep or food, until his bones stood out.

Fired with determination to seek the Dao, he forgot sleep and food. Yan Hui went again to enlighten him, whereupon Zigong returned to Confucius’ gate, strumming the zither and reciting the texts, never ceasing for the rest of his life. Once he had awakened to the ultimate principle, all other matters were forgotten.

Lu said: Yan Hui, in following the Way of the sublimely sagely, heard and grasped it. Zigong relied on the *Odes* and *Documents* for his wisdom, and thus when he heard the words, he lost what he had taken as fundamental.

Yan Hui enlightened him again, and he came to realize the benefit of learning, yet did not understand the Way of daily diminution.

*Zhenghe* says: If the Dao and its virtue are not abandoned, why seek benevolence and righteousness? If one’s nature and emotions are not estranged, why employ rites and music? Those who "rejoice in Heaven" rejoice in something—this is not the joy of the ancients. Those who "know fate" know something—this is not the knowledge of the ancients. One who rejoices in Heaven must ultimately reach a state of nothing to rejoice in—this is true joy. One who knows fate must ultimately reach a state of nothing to know—this is true knowledge. Being thus, there is nothing not rejoiced in, nothing not known, nothing not worried over, nothing not done, and one becomes a person in harmony with transformation. If one goes forth yet cannot transform, then governing with the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music may suffice. Retreating from benevolence and righteousness, discarding rites and music, may also suffice. Thus, in the beginning, the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music could not save the world from disorder. Yet once one has attained the Way, one asks: How could the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music be abandoned? Why reform them?

Zigong had not dared to ask before, but upon hearing this, he was bewildered and lost, his thoughts yielding nothing, so he merely recited the texts without ceasing. Was this not what is meant by "grasping the words but not their underlying meaning"?

Fan said: The true Dao governs the self, and thus cultivates the person. It neither exults in success nor feels shame in poverty, and thus embraces both prosperity and adversity. Coming, one accepts it; going, one follows it—thus knowing that going and coming are not of the self. Not seeking eminence, one indulges the will without constraint; in poverty and hardship, one follows custom—thus banishing disorder from the heart and thoughts. This is what is meant by "rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate without sorrow," and it is indeed found here.

Yet if even one general cannot be obtained to startle the world, then one must rely on remnants to govern the state and worthless chaff to order the realm. How can one who takes the hearts of the people as one’s own heart remain indifferent and without sorrow? Thus, the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music are the traces left by the former kings—tools for governance, not the Way of governance. Does one cultivate the *Odes* and *Documents* to govern? Yet benevolence and righteousness grow ever weaker. Does one rectify rites and music to govern? Yet human nature and emotions grow ever more shallow. If the Dao does not prevail in one state, what will become of the world? If the Dao does not prevail in the present age, what will become of future generations? I know that the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music cannot save the world from disorder, yet I do not know how to reform them. This is why those who "rejoice in Heaven and know fate" seem to bear so many sorrows.

Nevertheless, I have grasped it: "Rejoicing in Heaven" is not the joy of the ancients—true joy is joy without joy, allowing whatever brings joy, and thus there is nothing not rejoiced in. "Knowing fate" is not the knowledge of the ancients—true knowledge is knowledge without knowledge, allowing whatever is known, and thus there is nothing not known. Being thus, though there may be daily sorrows, how could these be the anxieties of those who fret over the world’s ills? Thus, one can act without acting yet accomplish everything.

As for the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music of old: one may use them without necessarily abandoning them, follow them without necessarily reforming them. Accord with things as they are, without imposing private intentions. Who could exhaust themselves in taking the world and future generations as their concern?

Yan Hui had attained the subtlety of "sitting in forgetfulness," and thus, upon hearing the sage’s words, he gained insight. Zigong was submerged in the disputations of broad learning, and thus he was merely bewildered and lost.

A minister from Chen visited Lu and had a private audience with the head of the Shu Sun clan. The latter said: "There is a sage in our state." The minister asked: "Is it not Kong Qiu?" "It is," he replied. "How do you know he is a sage?" "This is a profound question. The Way of the sage transcends the bounds of common understanding, beyond the reach of all things, yet one glimpses traces of its convergence and penetration—hence one calls him a sage. How could one truly know what makes him sagely?"

The head of the Shu Sun clan said: "I have often heard of him from Yan Hui." "This is a profound answer. If one has not embodied both aspects and taken form, how could one speak of his likeness or glimpse his precedence? Even with Yan Hui’s capacity, he could not fully describe him—how much less those beneath him?"

The minister said: "Kong Qiu can set aside the mind and employ the body." These were Yan Hui’s words. The sage neither sets aside nor employs anything—such terms arise only in response to circumstances. Thus, bending and rising to myriad affairs, engaging with worldly matters, are all matters of the body’s advance. One who is profoundly detached and still as cold ashes is indeed serene and unmoving.

Lu said: The sage responds to things and arises, aiding the times and guiding the multitude of beings, manifesting traces to reveal the truth and transform the ordinary.

The minister from Chen said: "There is also a sage in our state—did you not know?" "Who is this sage?" "Among the disciples of Lao Dan, there is one named Kǎng Cāngzǐ (also pronounced Gēng), who attained Lao Dan’s Dao. Lao Dan himself did not speak of attaining the Dao—how could Kǎng Cāng have attained it? This is merely a borrowed name to show that the ultimate principle is never severed from the principles of things. He could see with his ears and hear with his eyes."

The bodily form is the dwelling of the mind and intelligence; the ears and eyes are the windows and doors of seeing and hearing. If the spirit is thoroughly permeating, then seeing and hearing do not depend on windows and doors, and illumination is not obstructed by walls.

*Zhenghe* says: Confucius responded to things while forgetting the mind, and thus those who saw his sageliness thought he could set aside the mind and employ the body. Kǎng Cāngzǐ accorded with himself while forgetting the body, and thus those who saw his sageliness thought he could see with his ears and hear with his eyes. Setting aside the mind and employing the body is like the seesaw’s rise and fall.

What draws people is also drawn by them. Seeing with the ears and hearing with the eyes shares the same meaning as Liezi’s "mind congealed and form released, bones and flesh wholly dissolved." Yet what is set aside and what is employed? The Dao of the sage is without seeing and without hearing—how could it be subject to inquiry? This is merely how ordinary people perceive it.

Fan says: The mind is the master of the body, and the body is what the mind unites with. Only by controlling the mind can one set it aside, thus avoiding its burdens. Only by employing the form can one use it, thus avoiding its servitude. The sage’s sageliness lies in neither setting aside nor employing anything.

The hearing of Master Shusun was like this, and thus he praised Confucius in this way. The ears govern hearing, the eyes govern seeing. When essence is whole and unharmed, the ears become penetrating—then one may see. When spirit is employed yet inexhaustible, the eyes become penetrating—then one may hear. The sage’s sageliness lies in seeing without eyes and hearing without ears. The observation of Minister Chen was like this, and thus he praised Kǎng Cāngzǐ in this way.

The Duke of Lu, hearing this, was greatly astonished—not at Confucius’ employment of the body, but at the interchange of ears and eyes’ functions. His traces were the same as other things, so nothing startled his mind. He sent his highest minister with rich gifts to summon him. Kǎng Cāngzǐ accepted the invitation and arrived.

One who is utterly without intent finds nothing in the four directions that is not oneself. The Duke of Lu humbled his words and entreated him for instruction. Kǎng Cāngzǐ said: Those who transmit this speak falsely. I can see and hear without using ears and eyes, but I cannot alter the functions of ears and eyes. To alter their functions is not yet to be entirely without use. To be entirely without use would mean the instruments one relies upon are discarded.

Lu says: The ears and eyes are the instruments of seeing and hearing. Only spirit can employ them. If spirit is not present, even a corpse’s ears and eyes cannot see or hear. Kǎng Cāngzǐ understood what people are capable of, and thus did not use ears and eyes as the masters of seeing and hearing. Was he commanding the ears to see and the eyes to hear? This is merely how transmitters, not understanding, fabricated the account.

The Duke of Lu said: This is even more extraordinary—what is its principle? I still wish to hear it.

Lu says: The Duke of Lu still did not grasp this meaning and instead took it as an even greater marvel. He persistently requested its principle.

Kǎng Cāngzǐ said: My body unites with the mind—this is form and intelligence not opposing each other. The mind unites with qi—this is further distancing the employment of form and intelligence, entrusting it to the tranquil qi. Qi unites with spirit—this is stillness without movement, wholly forgetting intelligence. When intelligence is wholly forgotten, the workings of spirit alone respond without obstruction. Spirit unites with nothingness. To be one with nothingness is to be spirit; to be one with spirit is to be nothingness. How could these two have form? Only one who possesses intelligence cannot but draw near to nothingness to commune with it; one who forgets the mind becomes one with nothingness.

Lu says: The body, having substance, becomes obstructive; the mind, having attachments, becomes fixated. The body uniting with the mind does not lie in physical obstruction but in breaking fixation. Thus qi, though moving, has no fixation. The mind uniting with qi does not lie in fixation but in dynamic function. Thus qi uniting with spirit does not lie in movement but in clear perception. Spirit uniting with nothingness transcends all form, so it does not lie in perception but in profound union with the real.

When there is the slightest presence or sound—whether far away at the eight wilds’ edge or near at one’s brows and lashes—whatever comes to disturb me, I must know it. Only the vast openness of nothingness does not disturb the sage’s thoughts. In traversing the realm of being, the illumination of spirit and clarity knows no difference of far or near. Yet one does not know that this is what the seven orifices and four limbs perceive, what the heart, belly, and six viscera know—it is merely self-knowing. When all destinations are forgotten, how could there be any attainment of awareness?

Lu says: Thus, with form and sound, no matter how distant or near, whatever comes to disturb me, I can know it all—without employing the four limbs or seven orifices. Like a bright mirror hung high, it illuminates of itself; how could it rely on the ears?

The Duke of Lu was greatly pleased. Later, he told Confucius, who smiled but did not answer.

Kang Cang’s words are exhausted—what more could Confucius say? Yet by answering with silence, he answers indeed, entrusting it to a smile.

Lu says: To entrust it to a smile is to grasp the purport of forgetting words.

*Zhenghe* observes: Even when eyes see and ears hear, one remains bound to form. When seeing and hearing are not employed, the ears and eyes detach from form. For ears and eyes, though engaged in seeing and hearing, remain tied to form and thus still fall short. Only when ears and eyes are not used does form fill the void. One sees in darkness, hears in silence, and unites with spirit—who in the world could comprehend this? When the body aligns with the heart, one moves from externals inward; when the heart aligns with qi, one proceeds from solidity to emptiness. When qi aligns with spirit, one stands beyond reckoning; when spirit aligns with nothingness, one acts without fixed direction. Not listening with the ears but listening with qi, I encounter with spirit rather than seeing with the eyes—this is what is meant by entering the infinitesimal with what is not. Whether the solidity of *jieran*, the smallness of form, the resonance of *weiran*, or the subtlety of sound—whether far beyond the eight wilds or near between the brows—it is not that nothing exists, but that one is confined by form and moved by sound. Though I dismiss keen perception to merge with the great penetration, whatever comes to disturb me, I must know it—yet how could I know *how* I know? Thus it is said: it is merely self-knowing. Confucius’ smile without answer was a smile that did not know how to answer.

Fan says: Ears and eyes are form; seeing and hearing are their function. Only when spirit is utterly lucid, with no obstruction, can one see with the ears and hear with the eyes—yet this merely exchanges their functions, not transcends their use. But when, beyond form, one sees solely with spirit in the realm of the soundless and listens solely with qi in the domain of the formless, then ears and eyes are not used, yet keen perception endures. Thus the Duke of Lu marveled all the more. Tracing how this is achieved, there must be a Way. When the body aligns with the heart, its discernment is flawless, free from the heart’s delusions. When the heart aligns with qi, it congeals, free from forcing qi. Qi moves without cease, its transformations rooted in spirit; when qi aligns with spirit, they fuse without gap. Spirit is omnipresent, its function ever resting in nothingness; when spirit aligns with nothingness, they merge as one. Those who debate things as small cling to the solidity of *jieran*; those who respond to things are moved by the resonance of *weiran*. Though it lies beyond the eight wilds, can it be called distant? The Dao is present here, undivided by distance. Though it lies within the brows, can it be called near? The Dao is present here, undivided by proximity. With great wisdom observing all, nothing can interpose; without distinction of far or near, one knows whatever comes from without, yet the body is fundamentally no-body—thus what the seven orifices and four limbs perceive, I do not know. Observing within, the heart is fundamentally no-heart—thus what the heart, belly, and six viscera know, I do not know. True knowing is no-knowing, yet nothing is unknown—is this not the work of spirit? When the Dao reaches this point, it is complete, perfect, beyond augmentation. Thus the Duke of Lu told Confucius, and Confucius smiled without answering.

The Grand Steward of Shang asked Confucius: “Is Qiu a sage?”
Confucius said: “How dare Qiu claim sagacity? What the world calls sages merely follow their traces—how could they know what makes one a sage or not? Yet Qiu is one who has broadly studied and retains much.”
He merely appears to have broadly studied and retained much—truly, he has nothing he has studied, nothing he has retained.

*Zhenghe* notes: Though the Master was a sage, he said, “How dare I claim sagacity?”—for he did not presume to occupy sagacity. Though broadly learned, he left no name; though retaining much, he unified it with one thread—this is why Confucius is the culmination of greatness.

Fan says: The man of Daxiang knew enough to recognize a sage, thus he said, “broadly learned yet leaving no name.” The Old Man of Hanyin did not know enough to recognize a sage, thus he said, “broadly learned to emulate sages.” Yet if the sage has no name, who can name him? Broad learning and much retention were spoken only in response to the Grand Steward of Shang. Yet when he told Zigong, “Do you take me for one who has broadly studied and retained much?” he said, “I unify it with one thread—why?” Broad learning and much retention are how the *Doctrine of the Mean* aligns with humanity; unifying with one thread is how it reaches the utmost clarity to align with Heaven.

The Grand Steward of Shang said: “Were the Three Kings sages?”
Confucius said: “The Three Kings were skilled in employing wisdom and courage—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know.”
He said: “Were the Five Emperors sages?”
Confucius said: “The Five Emperors were skilled in employing benevolence and righteousness—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know.”
He said: “Were the Three Sovereigns sages?”
Confucius said: “The Three Sovereigns were skilled in employing what accords with the times—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know.”

Confucius’ broad learning, Tang and Wu’s martial might, Yao and Shun’s yielding deference, Fuxi and Shennong’s simplicity—these are all coarse traces of sages responding to the world’s affairs, not what makes one a sage. What makes one a sage is fundamentally beyond the reach of words and traces.

Lu says: To clarify that the great Dao is not a trace, one must replace what others expound, for they know only traces. Thus the Master, following what people commonly see, sought to illuminate the truly real sage.

*Zhenghe* notes: The Sovereigns spoke of the Dao, the Emperors of virtue, the Kings of enterprise. Skill in employing what accords with the times is how the Dao is enacted; skill in employing benevolence and righteousness is how virtue is perfected; skill in employing wisdom and courage is how enterprise is cultivated. Yet these are the burdens of action, not the affairs of non-action—thus he said, “whether they were sages, Qiu does not know.”

Though the Sovereigns, Emperors, and Kings established their roles in response to the times, these are but traces. What makes their traces possible is not what makes them sages.

Fan says: The Kings spoke of enterprise, hence their skill in employing wisdom and courage; the Emperors spoke of virtue, hence their skill in employing benevolence and righteousness; the Sovereigns spoke of the Dao, hence their skill in employing what accords with the times. Yet these are but coarse traces of responding to the world—how could they be what makes one a sage? Thus the Master declared he knew not whether they were sages, and thereby revealed that they did not embody sageliness.

The Grand Steward Shang was greatly startled: what the world called sages, Confucius dismissed as non-sages—this was why he was so astonished. He asked, "Then who is a sage?" Confucius altered his expression and paused before saying, "There are sages among the people of the West. Is sageliness bound to any fixed place?" He spoke thus to point toward what lies utterly beyond. "There are sages there who govern not, yet there is no disorder; they do not govern through governance, hence disorder cannot arise. They speak not, yet are trusted; those who speak are not trusted. They transform not, yet their Way is enacted; those who act cannot transform. This is the perfection of non-action. Vast and boundless, the people cannot name them."

He Yan’s *On the Nameless* states: "What the people praise has a name; what is unpraised is nameless. As for the sage, his name is no-name, his praise is no-praise. To call no-name the Dao and no-praise the great is to speak of the nameless as though it had a name, and the unpraised as though it had praise. Yet how could this be the same as what can be named or praised? Compared to having nothing, these all have something. Yet within what has something, one must align with what has nothing, differing from those who possess something. Like attracts like, no matter how distant; unlike repels, no matter how near. It is like the yang within yin or the yin within yang, each seeking its own kind. Summer is yang, yet its nights, far removed, share yin with winter; winter is yin, yet its days, far removed, share yang with summer. All differ from what is near yet align with what is distant. Only by examining this interplay of sameness and difference can the discourse on the nameless be understood. What brings one to this realization? The Dao alone is what has nothing. Since the beginning of heaven and earth, all things have had something. Yet we still call it the Dao because it can return to and employ what has nothing. Thus, though dwelling in the realm of names, it effaces the image of the nameless. It is like the distant body of yang forgetting that it shares a distant kind with yin."

Xiahou Xuan says: "Heaven and earth operate through spontaneity; the sage employs spontaneity. Spontaneity is the Dao. The Dao fundamentally has no name, hence Laozi said, 'Forced to name it.' Confucius called Yao 'vast and boundless, beyond naming,' yet later spoke of his 'lofty achievements'—these were forced names, taken from what the world knows. How could one who has a name then be said to be beyond naming? Only the nameless can be named with all the names under heaven, yet how could these be its true names? To grasp this analogy yet remain unenlightened is like gazing upon the towering Mount Tai and claiming the primal qi is not vast and boundless."

I suspect they may be sages—yet do I truly know they are sages? Or are they not sages? The principle of sageliness is profoundly obscure, hence it cannot be described in words; one can only remain in doubt.

The Grand Steward Shang fell silent, pondering in his heart: "Confucius has deceived me!" Such matters lie beyond common understanding, so he took them for deception.

Lu says: "Those who establish traces to exalt teachings and manifest conduct to inspire transformation are but worthy sages who save the age. As for those who embody the great Dao, they cover and support like heaven and earth, their transformations flow like the four seasons. One sees no governance, yet there is no disorder; no reliance on speech, yet trust is assured. Their bounty flows abundantly, benefiting the myriad things; their vastness embraces all beings, yet their profundity is hard to fathom. To ascribe a place to such sages or define them in words is still to fall short of the ultimate. Thus, the Master spoke in terms of doubt and semblance to transcend language. This is what the Grand Steward could not comprehend, taking it as mere deception by Confucius."

*Zhenghe* notes: "Zhuangzi spoke of Suiren, Fuxi, Shennong, the Yellow Emperor, and the sage-kings from Tang and Yu onward, all as declines in virtue. Confucius, in dismissing the governance of the Three Sovereigns, Five Emperors, and Three Kings as not truly sagely, then pointed to the people of the West, saying there are sages among them—this is because the Dao turns with the ages. The sage accords with the times. The governance of the Five Emperors and Three Kings gathered the myriad beginnings in the flourishing assembly of their eras, like spring and summer in the cycle of seasons. One sees only their outward royal enterprises, hence he said, 'I do not know if they were sages.' The people of the West, having turned from refinement back to simplicity, are like autumn and winter in the cycle—still and sagely. Thus he said, 'There are sages there.'

Only when there is no governance does governance arise; without the affair of governance, how can there be disorder? Hence, they govern not, yet there is no disorder. Those who require speech to be trusted lack true trust; silent understanding needs no words, hence they speak not, yet are trusted. The enactment of the Dao’s transformation leaves traces of action; transformation without trace—who could impose action upon it? Thus, they transform not, yet their Way is enacted. This is the meaning of 'nothing is done, yet all is spontaneously so.'

The Dao cannot be named, for it has no boundaries. Hence, 'vast and boundless, the people cannot name it.' Yet sageliness cannot be known—calling it sageliness, how could this be true? Thus, one can only doubt whether they are sages. The Grand Steward, finding these words far from human sentiment, was first shocked and startled. Seeking to measure them by conventional standards and finding no answer, he ultimately fell silent, pondering in his heart that Confucius had deceived him."

Fan said: Those who possess land possess great things, yet those who possess great things cannot be possessed by things. Only by not being a thing can one master things—this is how the ancients kept the world in its natural state, never hearing of "governing" the world. From the Three Dynasties onward, men abandoned the serene and effortless Way, delighting instead in the clamor of empty praise, bending and shaping rites and music to correct the world’s form. Alas! They soothed the world’s heart with benevolence and righteousness, calling this "governance"—what greater disorder could there be? Thus, the sage’s governance operates beyond rectification, ensuring only that those capable of their tasks perform them. Therefore, in leisurely non-action, the myriad things are freed from entanglement—why should I trouble myself with governing the world?

In establishing teaching, there is no burden of words, and so they trust themselves. In enacting transformation, there are no traces of transformation, and so it proceeds of itself. The Dao is eternally nameless; from a human perspective, though one sees its vastness, its great and nameless simplicity remains beyond all description. The people say, "We are naturally so—what has the emperor’s power to do with us?" Is this not what Zhuangzi meant by "the sage has no name"? Yet whether one is a sage or not, when doubt arises and knowledge fails, one approaches the divine mystery of the unknowable sage.

The Grand Steward was unfit to discuss such matters, hence he took Confucius’ words as deception.

Zixia asked Confucius, "What sort of man is Yan Hui?"
The Master said, "Hui’s benevolence surpasses my own."
Zixia asked, "What sort of man is Zigong?"
The Master said, "Ci’s eloquence surpasses my own."
Zixia asked, "What sort of man is Zilu?"
The Master said, "You’s courage surpasses my own."
Zixia asked, "What sort of man is Zizhang?"
The Master said, "Shi’s dignity surpasses my own." (Yet he prided himself on that dignity.)
Zixia rose from his mat and asked, "Then why do these four serve you?"
The Master said, "Sit, and I will tell you.

Hui can be benevolent but cannot adapt—
*Adapt* means to change. Clinging to one way without the wisdom to respond to things, wherever he goes, he will meet obstruction.
Lu said: One may journey with him toward the Dao, but not share in its flexibility.

Ci can be eloquent but cannot be reticent—
Lu said: He has the ability to advance, but has not yet ascended to the Dao.

You can be brave but cannot be timid—
Lu said: He knows only the masculine, not how to guard the feminine.

Shi can be dignified but cannot be harmonious.
Eloquence that cannot be reticent must lack the substance of loyalty and trust. Bravery that cannot be timid must violate the principle of the seven forbearances. Dignity that cannot be harmonious contravenes the meaning of blending with light. All these are fixations on one aspect.
Lu said: He guards his solemnity but cannot harmonize with things, losing the virtue of harmony.

Were I to exchange my Way for the possession of all four of these, I would not consent. These four each excel in one virtue; if they combined them and sought to alter my Way, I would not permit it. This is why they serve me without divided loyalty. The convergence of such qualities should be entrusted to the sage, and thus I cannot dismiss them.

Lu said: Though they possess benevolence, eloquence, dignity, and courage, I would not exchange my Way for them—how much less if they did not possess them all. The Master could encompass what the four could not, and so they served him without divided hearts.

This discussion concerns the greatest matters of the Dao, which are further expounded in the chapters on *Concealment and Display*.

Zhenghe: The sage’s Way is supremely lofty, yet it embodies the Mean. Whether in excess or deficiency, neither is the Dao. The worthy err in excess, and the sage takes nothing from them. Hui can be benevolent but cannot adapt—this is not great benevolence. Ci can be eloquent but cannot be reticent—this is not great eloquence. You can be brave but cannot be timid—this is not great courage. Shi can be dignified but cannot be harmonious—this is not blending with light. Though they do not encompass all aspects, each has a partial grasp of the Dao, and each has its use in its time. As for the Master’s great completeness, he embodies the Dao in its entirety.

Those four disciples, looking up to the sage, knew their own limitations. Thus, though Confucius deemed each superior to himself in their respective virtues, how could they serve him with divided loyalty?

Fan said: Yan Yuan grasped the *act* of the Way but not its *source*—thus, though he might not transgress for three months, he could not master himself for a single day. Is this not being able to be benevolent but unable to adapt? Zigong grasped the *words* of the Way but not their *source*—thus, Confucius ranked him among those skilled in speech, and Mencius praised his excellence in rhetoric. Is this not being able to be eloquent but unable to be reticent? When the Master spoke of sailing on a raft, some mocked his fondness for courage beyond their own; when asked about leading an army, some mocked his caution in the face of danger—thus, You’s ability to be brave but not timid is evident. Adorning his imposing demeanor made it difficult for him to practice benevolence; maintaining his lofty aspirations left his conduct exposed—thus, Shi’s ability to be dignified but not harmonious is evident.

These four were not complete talents, and so the sage would not exchange his Way for their combined virtues. Yet the Master’s Way is like the sea: whether small or great, whether much or little, each takes what they can and gains accordingly.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Its Virtue, Scroll Nine* concludes.
*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Its Virtue, Scroll Ten* begins.

Confucius, after Master Liezi had studied under Master Huqiu Zilin, daily diminished his reliance on teachers. Befriending Bohun Maoren, he then dwelled in Nanguo. Those who followed him grew daily in number, yet still could not be fully counted. Visitors came in an unbroken stream, and though he sorted and attended to them each day, he could not exhaust their multitude. Even so, Liezi remained unassuming—he himself did not know their number. Day after day, they debated together, and no discourse went unheard. Master and disciples lectured and practiced, their teachings resounding near and far.

Lu observed: Though the visitors were many and Liezi knew not their number, through daily discourse and instruction, no trace of the sage remained unheard. Yet for twenty years, he shared a wall with Nanguozi and never paid him a visit. Their Ways merged in profound harmony, and so, though they lived to old age, they did not call upon one another. When they met upon the road, their eyes seemed not to see each other, for when the Dao is present, sight is rendered void. The disciples and servants of his gate believed Liezi and Nanguozi must be adversaries without doubt—hostile enemies.

Lu noted: The multitude suspected enmity and resentment, seeing they did not visit one another. One who had come from Chu asked Liezi: "Master, what enmity do you bear toward Nanguozi?" Liezi replied: "Nanguozi’s appearance is whole, his heart empty; his ears hear nothing, his eyes see nothing, his mouth speaks nothing, his heart knows nothing, his form stirs not. What should I do when I go to him? *Whole* means complete. When the heart is empty, the form is whole—thus his ears are not deluded by sound, his eyes not ensnared by form, his mouth does not choose its words, his heart does not employ knowledge; inner and outer merge as one, and so his form knows no tremor."

Lu said: Though his appearance is whole and his heart utterly still, he is not swayed by the distinctions of ears, eyes, heart, or mouth. Thus his heart has no use, and his form no cause for anxiety or fear. Nevertheless, let us go together. He gathered forty disciples and set out as one.

This journey was not to select the superior or inferior, nor to measure the long or short—forty being a fitting number, it was thus recorded. Upon seeing Nanguozi, he indeed appeared like a *qipo*—unapproachable. *Qipo*: a scholar. Another explanation reads *qitou*. His spirit congealed, his form dissolved; external things could not glimpse him.

Turning to regard Liezi, his form and spirit were not paired, and he could not join the company. For his spirit commanded his form. With no thoughts in his heart, his appearance showed no movement—thus they seemed not to govern one another, and how could external things find fellowship with him?

Lu said: "The forty disciples who went to see him indeed found him like a *qipo* in the likeness of a man, or as one with a *qitou*—his form and spirit could not be engaged. Suddenly, Nanguozi pointed to one of Liezi’s disciples in the rear rank and spoke to him. Though he stood in the last row, there was no rank of noble or base. He responded to what moved him, not by intent toward things. He appeared *kankan*, as if single-minded and dominant.

When principle reaches its utmost, there are no words; when words arise, the distinction between self and other emerges. The sage responds and adapts, merging with things—thus to observe his form, one might think he seeks rightness and values victory.

Lu said: "Those in the rear rank still cling to judgments of right and wrong—*kankan*, overflowing with the air of seeking victory." Liezi’s disciples were startled. Seeing him dwell like a corpse, they took him for wood or earth; seeing him engage with things, they thought him entangled in right and wrong—hence their shock.

Returning to their lodging, all bore expressions of doubt. Lu said: "They doubt whether he has truly forgotten the heart of victory and defeat."

Fan said: "Nanming—dwelling in the southern quarter, he embodies the intent of seclusion yielding to illumination. Those who follow him count the days without ceasing, speaking of preserving your multitude. Daily they debate, hearing all—speaking of their diligent practice. Yet for twenty years, though he shared a wall with Nanguozi, they never visited or inquired. This was their Way’s mutual forgetting, no exchange between them. When they met on the path, their eyes seemed not to see each other—this was their Way’s silent accord, no need for recognition. The disciples of the gate thus took him for a rival without doubt, never realizing that one who embodies the Dao merges self and other, never establishing opposition."

One came from Chu to ask, and Liezi told him: "Nanguozi’s appearance is whole—complete and unblemished. His heart is empty—hollowed and devoid of things. His ears hear only by turning inward; his eyes see only by gazing within. In silence, he recognizes—thus there are no words. In awareness, he merges—thus there is no knowledge. If this is so, then stepping beyond form, who would tremble in fear? He has departed from men and abides in constant stillness. To go is delusion; to act is pretense—hence it is said: *What should one do when going?*

Yet the Dao is nowhere absent, and nowhere is it unfit to go. That forty disciples journeyed together speaks of those with feet arriving at the mound. Seeing Nanguozi, he indeed appeared like a *qipo*—unapproachable, seeming almost inhuman. Regarding Liezi, his form and spirit were not paired, and he could not join the company—appearing as if bereft of vitality.

Suddenly, Nanguozi pointed to one of Liezi’s disciples in the rear rank and spoke—emerging as if plucked, responding to what moved him, compelled to speak. His words were like those of a maiden—when did he ever intend to speak? Thus, from without, one might see him *kankan*, delighting in the Dao. In stillness, he was single-minded; in movement, direct. Though he seemed to value things and seek victory, what is called *not striving for dominance* remained undisturbed.

Those who came with him were still doubtful—how could they, opposing the world with the Dao, not be startled? Liezi said: "One who attains non-intent has no words; one who advances knowledge also has no words. Exhausting principle and embodying its utmost, words and intent are both forgotten. Using non-words as words is still words; using non-knowledge as knowledge is still knowledge.

Those who seek to abolish words with non-words and dispel knowledge with non-knowledge—followers of rare speech and peripheral teachings—remain bound to words and knowledge. Non-words and not speaking, non-knowledge and not knowing—these are also words, also knowledge. By comparison, one might wish to preserve the natural, dwelling in the realm of no words and no knowledge—yet this is again to dispel what cannot be dispelled, to know what cannot be known.

To dispel what cannot be dispelled is to fail to depart from dispelling; to know what cannot be known is never to forget knowledge. This is not the natural forgetting of words and knowledge. There is nothing not spoken, nothing not known; there is nothing spoken, nothing known. For non-words are the origin of words; non-knowledge is the master of knowledge.

The heart of the perfected one is vast and empty, responding to things with words—not his own words. Knowing through things—not his own knowledge. Thus, though he speaks all day, he bears no name of profound silence; though he employs knowledge all day, he bears no name of toiling thought. Hence, there is nothing not spoken, nothing not known. It is only thus.

Why this groundless alarm? Failing to grasp where the subtlest converges, one takes the coarse as finer; failing to reach the highest, one turns downward—and then marvels at the strange demeanor of one who responds in stillness, the differing mien of his movement and rest. What is this if not deluded shock?

Lu said: "Utmost knowledge and intent both forget words. If there were distinctions of superiority and inferiority, words would be needed to guide them. Using the words of non-words, the knowledge of non-knowledge—how is this different? Though words and knowledge pertain to the Dao, one should discern them—thus there are times when one does not speak, times when one does."

Unknowing, yet not unknowing. The principle is precisely thus—why this groundless wonder?

*Zhenghe* notes: "Those who count the days yet cannot reach him speak of how many come together, yet none can match the Master. Even Liezi is but faintly discernible"—meaning Liezi’s Dao cannot be seen. "Day after day they debate, yet hear nothing"—the Dao cannot be heard; to hear it is to miss it. When debate arises unavoidably, what is heard is but shallow. "For twenty years, though living side by side, they never visited one another"—this aligns with Laozi’s principle of non-intercourse. "Meeting on the Way, their eyes seemed not to see each other"—thus the Dao exists without need for visual confirmation. "The hundred bones, nine orifices—all are complete"—this is what is meant by "form fully realized." "Void and empty, invisible to the eye"—this is what is meant by "heart emptied." Though possessing human form, with ears, eyes, mouth, and features all intact, he has no human emotions—thus no hearing, no seeing, no speech, no knowledge, no fear, his chamber utterly vacant. The spirit seems present, yet the form is not true—like an image of a man. "Form and spirit not paired"—this means the spirit does not abide in the form. "Joyful and upright, as if steadfast and virile"—this means he cannot know the virile yet abides in the feminine. With Liezi’s Dao, even Nan Guo views him thus—no wonder his disciples were startled and all bore expressions of doubt.

Words serve to convey the Dao. Words reside in meaning; grasp the meaning and forget the words—hence it is said, "One who grasps the meaning speaks not." What can be discussed in words is but the ancestor of things, yet one remains ignorant of what lies within. Thus it is said, "One who advances in knowledge also speaks not." To use wordlessness as speech is the highest speech; to use unknowing as knowledge is the highest knowledge. Aligning my wordlessness with the Dao’s non-speech, my unknowing with the Dao’s unknowing—from the perspective of one who grasps meaning or advances in knowledge, this is also a form of speech, a form of knowledge. The Dao does not lie beyond this—what ground is there for groundless alarm?

Fan said: "What follows meaning cannot be transmitted in words—hence one who grasps meaning speaks not. What knowledge cannot know, debate cannot exhaust—hence one who advances in knowledge also speaks not. Wordlessness is the Dao. To use wordlessness as speech is still not to forget words. Unknowing is the Dao. To use unknowing as knowledge is still not to forget knowledge. Though words may not be forgotten, there is that which has never been spoken—this too lies within the realm of the speakable. Though knowledge may not be forgotten, there is that which has never been known—this too lies within the realm of the knowable. Speech and knowledge, in themselves, neither bind nor burden. Responding to things in speech, one speaks without limit—yet in truth speaks not. Engaging with things in knowledge, one knows without limit—yet in truth knows not. The Dao, as described above, is merely thus. What then is there to marvel at?"

Liezi was a student. As stated in the previous chapter, "Liezi studied the art of riding the wind." After three years, his heart dared not entertain judgments of right and wrong, his mouth dared not speak of profit and harm—only then did Old Shang grant him a single glance. After five years, his heart again pondered right and wrong, his mouth again spoke of profit and harm—only then did Old Shang relax his countenance and smile. After seven years, whatever his heart desired to ponder, there was no right or wrong; whatever his mouth desired to speak, there was no profit or harm. Only then did the Master draw him near to share his seat. Glances and smiles, shared seating—it seemed as though there were praise or censure, elevation or demotion. Yet the sage’s heart responds to affairs, gauging by the measure of external things—how could it be fixed to one side? After nine years, whatever his heart desired to ponder, whatever his mouth desired to speak, he neither knew his own judgments of right and wrong, profit and harm, nor those of others—inner and outer had merged. Only then were his eyes like ears, his ears like nose, his nose like mouth—all alike. His heart congealed, his form dissolved, bones and flesh wholly fused. He was unaware of what his body leaned against, where his feet trod, what his heart pondered, or what his words concealed. Merely thus—the principle was nowhere hidden.

This chapter also appears in the *Huangdi* section, where it is explained in detail. Its repetition here first clarifies the ultimate attainment of nature, enabling boundless transformation; later, it elucidates the principle of following the heart, revealing that nothing, however obscure, does not submit. The two chapters appear in tandem, each with its own intent—how can one fail to examine them closely?

Lu said: Laozi said, "Great wisdom appears foolish, great eloquence appears inarticulate." People only recognize the strangeness of speech, not the sameness of wordlessness and unknowing. Thus, the *Huangdi* section clarifies the use of wordless speech to aid others, while this section reiterates the discussion of speech, showing that its use is no different from wordlessness.

*Zhenghe* notes: "At first, a single glance—this speaks of the Dao residing in the meeting of eyes. A relaxed countenance and smile—this speaks of the heart’s attainment beyond form’s dissolution. Drawing him near to share a seat—this is advancement, joining with him. When inner and outer merge, the mystery becomes unfathomable. Form is full, the heart empty—thus the heart congeals, the form dissolves, bones and flesh wholly fuse, reaching the utmost of form—hence the principle is nowhere hidden."

Fan said: "Riding the wind still depends on something. Thus, the previous chapter discussed the art of riding the wind, while this one does not repeat it, to show that Liezi’s learning was not merely this." Initially, Liezi loved to wander. Huqiuzi said: "Yu Kou loves to wander—what does he love in wandering?" Liezi replied: "The joy of wandering lies in the ever-new delight of what one encounters." When others wander, they observe what they see...

My wandering observes its transformations. People call them "ordinary folk" or "petty men"—they see only glory and decay as distinct sights, taking them for joy and sorrow, unaware that all moves with the flux, its momentum never pausing for even a moment. Wandering, ah, wandering—none can discern the nature of this wandering. When people wander with Liezi, their paths are the same; what they wander toward differs. Thus it is said: "Wandering, ah, wandering," to clarify the divergence of these two perspectives. None have debated this, meaning few truly understand.

Lu said: "Delighting in the transformations of things, which shift and fade without constancy—others merely take pleasure in what they see, while I observe their changes. This is how I differ from them."

Huqiuzi said: "Does Liezi’s wandering truly align with others’, or is it fundamentally distinct?" All that is seen is ever seen in its transformations. If there is no momentary pause, then what is seen now is never what was seen before. Thus, to observe what is seen and to observe what changes—where lies the difference? To delight in the endless novelty of things yet not know that I, too, am without constancy. Both things and I move together with the flux. Those who wander outwardly do not know to turn their gaze inward. The outward wanderer seeks completion in things; the inward observer finds sufficiency within. Finding sufficiency within—this is the perfection of wandering; seeking completion in things—this is its failure.

Though man is but a seven-foot form, the principles of Heaven and Earth are complete within him. Thus, his head is round and feet square, taking their pattern from the Two Modes; his nose is high and mouth recessed, resembling mountains and valleys; his flesh and muscles connect to the earth’s soil, his blood and vessels belong to rivers and streams; his warmth and steam are one with scorching fire, his breath no different from wind and clouds. Inwardly observing the myriad forms, not a single thing is lacking. Why must one gaze upward and downward, tread through court and countryside, only then to complete what is seen?

He said: "You consider yourself different from others. Yet what others see has never differed from what you see. Why? You know things, know their transformations, but do not know your own lack of constancy. Thus, you wander only outwardly and do not observe inwardly. Though moved by things, you lose yourself—this is the utmost, so why seek completion in outward wandering?"

Thereupon, Liezi never left his home for the rest of his life, believing himself ignorant of wandering. Having heard these ultimate words, he abandoned his excursions and observations. Not leaving did not mean secluding himself within his gates.

* * *
What delights is without constancy, thus ever new. Others’ wandering observes only appearances—forms, sounds, and colors. My wandering observes transformations—flux and reflux, fullness and emptiness. None can discern the nature of this wandering, for the difference between the two is scarcely known. Yet even those who perceive through their nature, in what they see, ever see its transformations. Thus it is said: "All that is seen—is it not ever seen in its transformations?" If I follow after things, I merely see their lack of constancy. Turning outward reflection inward, then what is within me is never not ever new. Thus it is said: "Delighting in the lack of constancy in things, one does not know that I, too, am without constancy."

Those who wander outwardly follow things and cling to them, seeing things but not their nature; the inward observer turns to the self and finds sincerity, seeing nature but not things. Chasing echoes for their sound—this is the kind that seeks completion in things. Dwelling in shade to rest one’s shadow—this is the kind that finds sufficiency within. Thus, the perfection or failure of wandering lies solely in the distinction between inward and outward. Liezi never leaving his home for life—this is what it means to return and seek within oneself.

Huqiuzi said: "Is this the perfection of wandering?" Earlier, he questioned Liezi’s words on wandering, not yet discussing wandering in its perfection, hence the reiteration.

The perfection of wandering knows not where it goes; the perfection of observation knows not what it sees. Sufficient within oneself, thus knowing not where it goes; reflecting back upon the self, thus knowing not what it sees. All things wander, all things observe. Forgetting wandering, one can encounter things and wander; forgetting observation, one can encounter things and observe. This is what I call wandering, this is what I call observation. What I affirm is, in truth, to affirm nothing. Where one goes is ever unobstructed, without fixation—this is what I call wandering and observation. Thus it is said: "Is this the perfection of wandering? Is this where wandering truly lies?"

Lu said: "When the body goes nowhere, the eyes fix on nothing, then nothing is not seen, and nothing is not wandered. Such wandering and observation—is this not the true perfection of wandering?"

* * *
The perfection of wandering moves with one’s nature; the perfection of observation sees through one’s nature. To have a destination is to have limits—how can nature have limits? Thus, the perfection of wandering knows not where it goes. To have something seen is to have obstruction—how can nature have obstruction? Thus, the perfection of observation knows not what it sees. Wandering without bounds yet truly wandering nowhere, observing without bounds yet truly observing nothing—above, one wanders with the Creator. Such is all.

Thus it is said: "Is this the perfection of wandering?"

Fan said: "The transformations of things are endless—what is old there is new here. With yin and yang, the old and new replace each other—this is Heaven. With dwelling and parting, the old and new replace each other—this is man. The joy of wandering lies in delighting in what is without constancy, thus where one goes is ever new. Yet others’ wandering observes what they see, taking the glory of things as their sole delight. My wandering observes their transformations, thus wandering with the Creator and observing amidst the teeming multitudes. Wandering, ah, wandering—who knows how this is so? Liezi’s love of wandering clarifies this. Yet even so, the perspectives of things and self differ. This is still not the utmost. Thus, to take others’ wandering as observing what they see, not knowing they too ever see its transformations; to take the joy of wandering as delighting in what is without constancy, not knowing that I, too, am without constancy."

Those who merely strive for external wandering and neglect inner contemplation—how could they ever attain the carefree and effortless state, wandering where things cannot pursue yet all abide? *Zhuangzi* says: "If one has the capacity to wander, how could they not wander? If one lacks the capacity to wander, how could they wander?" Only those who embody the Dao can wander through the world without bias.

Thus, those who focus on inner contemplation proceed from victory within, moving through the nameless. Those who pursue external wandering seek completeness in things, proceeding from victory without, their aspirations fixed on extravagance. To find sufficiency within oneself, with one’s contemplation fixed on the Dao—this is the utmost wandering. To seek completeness in things, with one’s wandering fixed on things—this is wandering that does not reach its utmost. The joy of wandering differs so greatly.

Thus, Master Feng believed himself ignorant of wandering, and Master Huqiu further instructed him in the teachings of wandering and contemplation. To be wholly occupied with observing the unerring—this is called the utmost wandering, where one does not know where they go yet finds their own ease. To perceive all things as boundless—this is called the utmost contemplation. Not knowing where one’s limits lie, one merely polishes what is within.

In such a state, the Dao does not oppose things, and no thing is not the Dao. Then every thing becomes a place of wandering, every thing a subject of contemplation—this is what I call wandering, this is what I call contemplation. All things are complete within me; turning inward to find sincerity, no joy could be greater. Why then make external wandering one’s sole pursuit?

When the Dao reaches this point, it is perfect, complete—nothing can be added. The ancients, whether known or unknown by others, remained serene in this way. This is nearly the state of which I speak.

Longshu said to Wen Zhi: "Your art is subtle. I have an affliction—can you cure it?" Wen Zhi replied: "I await your command. But first, tell me the symptoms of your ailment." Lu said: "What Wen Zhi treats are merely illnesses of the organs, bones, and flesh. What Longshu speaks of is the heart that forgets form and transcends the common. Not belonging to the vulgar kind, he takes this as his affliction."

Longshu said: "Praise from my village does not bring me honor; slander from the state does not bring me shame. Gain does not make me rejoice; loss does not make me grieve. I regard life as death, wealth as poverty, and others as swine."

Wherever one goes, all is equal—thus one sees the myriad things as free from preference or aversion, nobility or baseness. One sees oneself as others; self and other are forgotten. Dwelling in one’s home is like meeting at an inn—one does not possess it as one’s own. Viewing one’s village is like gazing upon a land of barbarians. All under heaven is one.

These manifold afflictions cannot be swayed by rank or reward, nor cowed by punishment or penalty. Prosperity and decline, profit and harm cannot alter them; sorrow and joy cannot shift them. Thus one cannot serve a lord, befriend kin, govern a wife and children, or command servants. What binds a person to external things is the heart’s likes and dislikes, the body’s perceptions of benefit and harm. If one can regard the ten thousand differences as a single thread, how could one perceive any distinction between existence and nonexistence?

Therefore, the Son of Heaven cannot claim such a one as minister, the feudal lords cannot claim them as friend, wife and children cannot claim their intimacy, servants cannot claim their familiarity. What ailment is this? What method could cure it?

Lu said: *"The Zhuangzi* states: ‘Praise does not spur them, slander does not dishearten them. They settle the boundary between inner and outer, discern the realm of honor and disgrace.’ One who unites with the spirit while detaching from form sees poverty and wealth, life and death, human and beast, self and other as mere passing guests—what difference is there? Now, if one’s heart is thus engaged, the ways of ruler and minister, friend and companion are abandoned; the heart’s loves and hates, joys and angers are severed. What method could heal this?"

Wenzhi then instructed Long Shu to stand with his back to the light. Wenzhi himself faced the light from behind and observed him. After a moment, he exclaimed: "Ah! I see your heart now. The square inch within is empty—you are nearly a sage! Your heart has six apertures flowing freely, with but one still unopened. The old saying holds that the sage’s heart has seven apertures. Now, those who take sagely wisdom as an affliction may arise from this very cause. This is not something my shallow art can cure."

Lu said: "Standing with his back to the light means returning to the concerns of the common and vulgar. Facing the light to observe means inclining toward the heart of the supreme Dao. That the square inch is empty comes from clinging to written teachings. That one aperture remains unopened means perfection has not yet been attained. If all seven apertures were fully open, how could one still regard the Dao of sagely wisdom as a malady? This affliction is not something Wenzhi can remedy."

*Zhenghe* states: "Your art is subtle"—meaning it is wondrously refined. What Long Shu reported as an affliction, Wenzhi diagnosed as an illness, seeking only to understand its origin.

Praise and blame cannot bring him honor or disgrace; gain and loss cannot trouble or delight him; life and death cannot alter his heart; poverty and wealth cannot burden his form. Seeing others as swine, he forgets humanity’s superiority over things; seeing himself as human, he forgets his distinction from others. Dwelling in his own home as if in a transient inn, he does not linger; viewing his native land as a barbarian realm, he does not favor any place. For all these manifold afflictions, ranks and rewards cannot spur him, punishments and penalties cannot intimidate him—thus he is no longer subject to others’ control. Prosperity and decline, profit and harm cannot sway him; sorrow and joy cannot move him—thus he is no longer enslaved by things. Looking upward, he is truly unfit to serve a lord or befriend kin; looking downward, he is truly unfit to govern wife and children or command servants. Those of old who declined the world all claimed to suffer from a hidden sorrow—thus Wenzhi had Long Shu stand with his back to the light and face the light to observe him, suspecting he bore this hidden affliction.

The Dao of the sage holds nothing more precious than emptiness. Today, I see your heart—the square inch within is empty. You are nearly a sage!"

The ears, eyes, nose, and mouth are all connected to the heart; when the six apertures flow freely, the eye is like the ear, the ear like the nose, the nose like the mouth. If even one aperture is blocked, the heart congeals. To regard external things—how could they be a burden? Yet those who call this an affliction—are they not akin to those who suffer from the loss of the heart?

Fan said: The ancients who embodied the Dao regarded the myriad things as one, making no distinction between self and other, and saw all forms as equal, without favor or estrangement. Though the world praised them, they remained unmoved by its murmurs; though the world condemned them, they remained untouched by its reproaches. Gaining what they deemed right brought no joy; losing it brought no sorrow. They did not see life as a cause for delight nor death as a reason for grief—life and death were simply as they were. They did not desire wealth nor despise poverty—poverty and wealth were simply as they were. They saw others as swine, forgetting distinctions of noble and base; they saw themselves as others, forgetting the divide between self and other. Dwelling in their own homes as if in a travelers’ inn, they viewed all households as alike; observing their own villages as if they were barbarian lands, they saw all villages as the same. Such a one could not be swayed by chariots and crowns, nor deterred by axes and blades; prosperity and decline, profit and harm could not alter him, nor could sorrow and joy move him. The Son of Heaven could not make him a subject, the feudal lords could not befriend him; even his wife and children could not draw near, nor could his servants grow familiar. The great unity of his Dao was thus. To call this an affliction and seek to cure it with medicine is to fail to recognize that it is not a condition treatable by herbs or stones.

Wenzhi then had Long Shu stand with his back to the light, for one whose heart embodies the Dao seeks the deepest recesses of the hidden. Wenzhi himself stood behind, facing the light to observe him, for one who governs others seeks to depart from the path of the hidden. Then he said: Ah! I see your heart now. The square inch within is empty, the palace of perfect clarity gleams without the slightest speck of dust, and you have attained the subtlety of the hollowed heart. The Dao of the sage is nearly within reach—thus it is said: "Your heart’s six apertures flow freely, yet one remains unopened." This is what is meant by "not yet having bridged the final gap." How could such a state be remedied by shallow arts?

That which arises without cause and yet is ever-present is the Dao. To forget all cares and accept whatever comes, whether in success or adversity, is to be without a place of death—this is the Dao of the sage.

Lu said: The supreme Dao abides eternally, not dependent on external things. To live by life is to live, and thus though one may perish, one does not vanish—this is constancy. As the *Laozi* says: "One who dies yet does not perish is long-lived." To grasp the principle of life and not lose the meeting of primordial auspiciousness is to preserve the Dao of life even unto death. This is the portion of the worthy, not the state of one who forgets all cares and darkly attains spontaneity in its fullness.

Lu said: To follow principle in true constancy, to move with the forms of life and death yet never perish—this is the constancy of the Dao. To live yet perish is misfortune. To serve the intellect in pursuit of preservation, to value the self and belittle things, to shun harm and seek profit, to be inwardly bound while outwardly laboring—even unto death, this arises from misfortune.

Lu said: To covet life and lose the Dao is misfortune. That which arises from a cause and yet is ever-dying is also the Dao. To follow the principle of certain death and go to the place of certain death—this is the correspondence of fact and principle, the natural Dao.

Lu said: The common understanding of ritual and teaching is that one must distinguish and proceed to death.

To die by death is to die, and thus though one may not yet have perished, one’s principle of life is already exhausted—this too is the constancy of principle.

Lu said: To cherish the body of life and death, to practice the teachings of life and death, and to take no account of the Dao or the world as constant.

To die yet live is fortune. To violate principle and defy what is fitting, to deserve death yet not meet it—this is to be mistakenly alive.

Lu said: To dwell in the work of change and decline yet restrain one’s desires is also to know the fortune of life.

Thus, to live without use is called the Dao; to use the Dao and attain completion is called constancy. To employ the Dao of the sage is to preserve or perish yet remain in accord with principle.

To die through use is also called the Dao; to use the Dao and attain death is also called constancy. To ride the principle of peril and harm one’s own body—this too is the constancy of the Dao.

Lu said: Not to serve the intellect in pursuit of preservation is the Dao. To use this Dao and reach completion is constancy. The common man serves his intellect unto death, believing it the Dao of aiding others. To use this Dao and reach death is also called constancy.

What the multitude delights in, the multitude calls the Dao. What the multitude finds secure, the multitude calls constancy. Thus, the Dao of transcendence and the Dao of the world share the same name but differ in reality.

*Zhenghe* commentary: What is prized in the Dao is that it can determine life and death. The Dao alone abides and is ever-present, existing everywhere without exception. Abiding alone and ever-present, it is said: "That which arises without cause and yet is ever-living." Existing everywhere without exception, it is said: "That which arises from a cause and yet is ever-dying." To live by the Dao is to die yet not perish—this is the constancy of principle. Thus it is said: "To live by life is to live, and thus though one may perish, one does not vanish—this is constancy." As for living yet perishing, this is not the true mandate, hence it is called misfortune. To die by the Dao is to perish before completion yet not regard it as a change, thus it is said: "Though one may not yet have perished, one’s life principle is already exhausted—this too is constancy." As for dying yet living, this is a deluded life, a fortunate escape, hence it is said: "To die yet live is fortune."

The inception of creation and transformation, the changes of yin and yang—having transformed to give birth, and transforming again to transform—all proceed from the Dao, all submit to fate. To be born even as one dies is simply the constant state of things.

Master Fan says: The Dao encompasses but two things—death and life. Life is the inception of creation and transformation; death is the change of yin and yang. Those who embody the Dao comprehend the workings of all things, and thus death and life are but great matters to them, beyond the reach of change. Once one enters the Dao of day and night, falls into the mechanism of exit and entry, one is born into death without awareness or realization—some delight in life yet burden the body, some forget life yet pursue profit. How could they know the reason for this?

Thus Liezi expounds and clarifies this: That which arises without cause and is ever-living can be lived by living. To live by living is to live, and thus though one may reach the end of one’s mandate, there is a principle of non-perishing—this is what makes it constant. As for living yet perishing, this is simply misfortune, as in the early death of Yan Hui. That which arises from a cause and is ever-dying can be died by dying. To die by dying is to die, and thus though one may not yet have exhausted one’s natural span, there is a way of self-exhaustion—this is what makes it constant. As for dying yet living, this is simply a fortunate escape, as in the long life of Robber Zhi.

Those who live without purpose follow the Dao of spontaneity and thus can employ the Dao to attain their end. Those who die with purpose follow the Dao of certain death and thus can employ the Dao to attain death. Both may be called constant. As for fortune and misfortune, they merely describe change.

When Ji Liang died, Yang Zhu sang at his gate. Having fully followed the Dao of life and submission to the end, there was nothing to mourn. When Sui Wu died, Yang Zhu wept over his corpse. Having lived in misfortune and died, there was cause for grief. The life and death of the common folk—some sing, some weep. The common folk are but the multitude. They know not why they live, nor why they die, and thus their sorrow and joy lose their balance, now singing, now weeping.

Master Lu says: Those who attain the principle of perfect life and return to its end are why the sage and worthy do not mourn. Those who lose their true nature, violate principle, and reach death are why the wise and worthy grieve. The life and death, singing and weeping of the common folk are but the constant of things—how could they know their destination?

In the *Zhenghe* [commentary]: To die yet not perish is to find joy in death, hence singing at the gate. To die in misfortune is to find grief in death, hence weeping over the corpse. As for the life and death of the common folk—some join in song, some gather in weeping—how could they know where death and life reside?

Master Fan says: The tradition states: "If one weeps on a given day, one does not sing." Song and weeping follow different paths; the rites of felicity and mourning do not interfere. Only those who embody the Dao are not thus. Ji Liang entrusted himself to the Dao, and at his death, Yang Zhu sang at his gate—was this not because he accepted his fate with equanimity, and thus death was not cause for grief? The ancients had those who sang before the corpse—this was all. Sui Wu could not forget the self, and at his death, Yang Zhu wept over his corpse—was this not because he died in misfortune, and thus his death was not to be rejoiced? The ancients had those who wept in response to weeping—this was all.

Yet sorrow and joy—are these the virtues of the perfected? Do those who have attained the Dao act with intention in such things? Though they sing at the gate, they know not who now sings. Though they weep over the corpse, it is not the wailing of those who follow in weeping. This differs from the life and death of the common folk, where some sing and some weep.

When the eyes are about to go blind, they first perceive the tip of an autumn hair. Master Lu says: The vision of the elderly is clear at a distance but dim up close—this is the gradual loss of sight. When the ears are about to go deaf, they first hear the flight of gnats. Master Lu says: In Qin, mosquitoes are called *rui*. Those with afflicted ears hear the sound of insects flying within—this is the gradual loss of hearing. When the mouth is about to lose its taste, it first distinguishes the waters of Zhi and Sheng. "Losing taste" means deviation. The waters of Zhi and Sheng have different flavors, and once mixed, they are difficult to distinguish. Master Lu says: *Yu ling* (反). Two rivers in the land of Qi. When the nose is about to become blocked, it first detects the scent of scorched and decayed matter. The scent of scorched and decayed matter has a subtle, nodal quality, also faint and hard to distinguish. When the body is about to stiffen, it first hastens in restlessness. "Stiffening" means collapse. As when Yan Yuan knew that Dongye’s driving of the horses would lead to a runaway, so too with the principle in humans. When the mind is about to be deluded, it first discerns right and wrong.

The eyes, ears, mouth, nose, body, and mind—these six, when they maintain the Dao of harmony, cannot be altered or changed. Dwelling in the extreme of excess, none do not suddenly reach their end. Thus the decay of things must first begin with fullness, then proceed to deficiency. To reach the limit above is to return below; extreme flourishing must decline—this is the natural order. Therefore the sage dwells in the center and treads the path of harmony, seeing what the eyes see, hearing what the ears hear, employing the body’s abilities, following the mind’s discernment. Thus their wisdom encompasses all things, and they preserve their wholeness throughout life.

Master Lu says: When the mouth loses its proper taste, it distinguishes other flavors. When the nose loses its sense of smell, it detects other scents. When the body is about to collapse, it first hastens in restlessness. When the mind is deluded about the supreme Dao, it lies in right and wrong. Right and wrong thus become manifest, and the Dao is lost. Thus things do not return until they reach their limit. Only when they attain the extreme do they turn back. Therefore keen perception, strength, and discernment all become resources for obscurity, decline, and delusion.

Master Lu says: Returning to one’s constant nature is the path to the supreme Dao.

Thus it is said: One who gazes at the tip of an autumn hair cannot see Mount Tai; one who listens to the sound of a mosquito cannot hear thunder. Therefore *Zhuangzi* says: If you glue the eyes of Li Zhu shut, the world will all be bright; if you twist the fingers of Gongshu, the world will all be skillful. To combine the learning of Confucians and Mohists, to pride oneself on the fame of right and wrong as if it were wealth, to record the dregs of traces as if it were ability—how can one return to the real? By what means can it be attained?

Therefore the *Yijing* says: Without thought, without action, still and unmoving, yet responsive and penetrating. This is how the sage earnestly pursues the supreme Dao.

**Zhenghe’s Commentary:** When things reach their extreme, the mind turns back—this is the transformation of affairs. Once a form is established, it does not perish until it is exhausted, and thus it cannot escape entanglement. The sage does not fixate on form; in profound obscurity, he alone perceives the dawn; in utter silence, he alone hears harmony. How could form burden the spirit?

**Fan’s Commentary:** One who sees the tip of an autumn hair takes it for clarity, yet does not know that the five colors blind the eyes; one who hears the flight of a gnat takes it for keen hearing, yet does not know that the five sounds deafen the ears. The mouth is certainly capable of distinguishing the flavors of the Zī and the Miǎn, yet the five tastes muddy the palate, sometimes even dulling its sharpness. The nose is certainly capable of detecting the scent of burning or rot, yet the five odors choke the nostrils, sometimes even overwhelming the bridge of the nose. When the body is about to collapse, it first hastens in reckless abandon—this is why Dongye’s horse was bound to fail. When the mind is about to be deluded, it first discerns right and wrong—this is how right and wrong become manifest and the Dao is diminished.

When things reach their limit, they turn back—this is the principle of nature. The sage awakens to this and merges with obscurity. He flows with the waxing and waning of the times, advancing and retreating, preserving and perishing, without losing correctness. The seeing and hearing of the eyes and ears are not enslaved to anything; the intake and tasting of the nose and mouth are never indulged. The body harmonizes with the mind, the mind harmonizes with qi. Even life and death are great matters, yet they bring no change to him—how much less do gain and loss, fortune and misfortune affect him?

*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Sijie*, Scroll Ten—concluded
*Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing Sijie*, Scroll Eleven

In the marshes and gardens of Zheng, many worthies dwelled—those of Dao and virtue who remained hidden and silent. In Dongli, many talents resided—those with administrative skill who participated in state governance. Lu said: "Those who cultivate and exalt Dao and virtue are the worthy; those who study texts and scrutinize punishments are the talented."

Among the laborers of the marshes and gardens was a man named Bo Fengzi. While his cohort passed through Dongli, they encountered Deng Xi. Deng Xi, a master of disputation and wisdom in the state of Zheng, wielded arguments of dual possibility, and none in his time could oppose him. He composed bamboo texts, which Zichan put to use.

Deng Xi turned to his disciples and laughed, saying: "Let us perform a dance for him—what will that approaching one make of it?" Some say this refers to mocking banter as playful teasing. His disciples replied: "We wish to know." (Here, "know" means "hear.") Lu said: "Deng Xi, proud among his companions, sought to deceive and toy with Bo Fengzi, and his disciples all desired to see it."

Deng Xi addressed Bo Fengzi: "Do you understand the meaning of *yangyang* (nurturing)?" Lu said: "Zhang Zhan notes: the first *yang* is pronounced like *yang* (to raise), the second like *yang* (to itch)." "Those who nurture others but cannot nurture themselves are like dogs and swine. Those who nurture things so that things serve them exercise human effort. If your followers eat their fill and rest in their clothes, it is the achievement of governance." He implied they were like dogs and swine, while he himself was the one in power.

"When the young and old gather in pens and kitchens as provisions, how are they different from dogs and swine?" Bo Fengzi did not respond—not because he could not, but because he scorned the question. Lu said: "He deemed Deng Xi ignorant of fundamentals and unworthy of engagement."

A follower of Bo Fengzi stepped forward out of turn and said: "Has the master not heard of Qi and Lu, where many cunning artisans dwell? There are those skilled in earth and woodwork, those skilled in metal and leather, those skilled in music and calculation, those skilled in military affairs, and those skilled in ancestral rites—all talents are present. Yet none can occupy a position to coordinate them, nor can any direct the others. When tasks are established, they are not comprehensive; when skills are perfected, they are not all-encompassing. Those whose craft is partial and whose wisdom is matched cannot govern or command one another. The one who assigns positions lacks knowledge; the one who directs lacks ability. Yet knowledge and ability are made to serve. One who cannot know what others know, nor do what others can do, yet employs all talents—such a one does not occupy the place of knowledge or ability. Without aversion or preference, without distinction between this and that, he embodies a mind of non-action. Thus, the clear-sighted see for him, the keen-eared hear for him, the wise plan for him, the brave fight for him, and he remains without concern."

Gongchai said to Fu Gen and Xiahou Xuan: "In this world, your fame and achievements surpass mine, but your understanding falls short." Xiahou Xuan replied: "Success and fame depend on understanding. Who in the world has a deficient foundation yet excels in trifles?" He answered: "Success and fame depend on ambition, which is constrained by its own limitations. Thus, ambition and constraint are one and the same—certainly not the sole province of understanding. I can make you esteemed, but I cannot necessarily ensure the success of your endeavors. Those in power are but my instruments—why should you take pride in them?"

Deng Xi had no reply. He glanced at his disciples and withdrew. Lu said: "Only one who embodies the Dao can employ all talents for governance, utilize all things for their proper use, and ensure that chickens, dogs, oxen, and horses each fulfill their roles, while scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants each remain in their places.
