How could your lot achieve this on your own? You see only the branches and not the root. To raise the arm of a mantis against a chariot wheel is to know no measure." Deng Xi, humiliated before his disciples, glared at them and departed.

In the *Zhenghe* commentary: "The hundred schools and myriad arts cannot interconnect, just as the ear, eye, nose, and mouth each have their strengths and timely uses. Yet a true ruler exists above them. He employs forms: earth and wood, metal and leather are made into tools; music, calculation, and writing are made into instruments; military affairs defend against external threats; ancestral rites cultivate internal harmony—all talents may be said to be present. Yet those who possess them for gain must relinquish them for use, only then can they be unified. Where there is action, there is that which cannot be done; where there is non-action, there is nothing that cannot be done. Thus it is said: 'The one who assigns positions lacks knowledge; the one who directs lacks ability. Yet knowledge and ability are made to serve.' Deng Xi did not grasp this and took pride in his governance—no wonder he was ridiculed by those of great learning."

Bo Fengzi’s silence was the eloquence of restraint. Fan said: "The worthy are spoken of in terms of virtue; the talented, in terms of ability. Bo Fengzi is the disciple mentioned earlier. Deng Xi wielded arguments of dual possibility and crafted endless words—such was the domain of the disputers. Thus, he challenged Bo Fengzi with the meaning of *yangyang*."

The *Tradition* says: "The cunning labor, the wise worry; the incapable seek nothing, eat their fill, and wander freely." One who embodies the Dao sheds cleverness and returns to incapacity—eating to satiety, resting in clothes, yet never acting. To liken such a one to dogs and swine is why Bo Fengzi could respond with silence.

As for Qi and Lu, with their many artisans: some excel in earth and woodwork, some in metal and leather, some in music and calculation, some in military affairs, some in ancestral rites—all talents are present, yet none can encompass them all. Thus, there is no one to coordinate positions or direct others, much like the saying: "The cunning labor, the wise worry." Yet in the world, those with administrative ability are many.

The hundred schools and myriad arts each possess their strengths, serving their purposes in due time—like the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth, which cannot substitute for one another. Only the sage perceives the purity and wholeness of Heaven and Earth, grasping the great essence of the ancients. Thus, one does not strive to know what the multitude knows but employs those with knowledge; one does not force ability in what the multitude can do but enlists those with skill. Why then should one toil laboriously, exchanging techniques and exhausting form and mind?

This is what the Old Master meant by "using others’ strength." Gongyi Bo was renowned among the feudal lords for his strength. Tangxi Gong spoke of him to King Xuan of Zhou, who prepared gifts to summon him. When Gongyi Bo arrived and the king observed his appearance, he saw a feeble man—*feeble* meaning weak. King Xuan, perplexed, asked: "What is your strength like?" Gongyi Bo replied: "My strength can snap the leg of a spring katydid and bear the wing of an autumn cicada." *Bear* here means to withstand. The king’s expression changed as he said: "My strength can rend the hide of a rhinoceros or drag the tail of nine oxen, yet I still regret its weakness. You snap the leg of a spring katydid and bear the wing of an autumn cicada, yet your strength is renowned throughout the world—why?"

Gongyi Bo sighed deeply, withdrew from his mat, and said: "Excellent is Your Majesty’s question. I dare speak the truth. My teacher, Shangqiuzi, was unmatched in strength under Heaven, yet even his kin were unaware, for he never employed his strength. He governed things with the Way of utmost softness, and nothing could oppose him, so his achievements went unseen. I served him unto death. He instructed me: 'One who wishes to see what others cannot see must gaze where others do not look; one who wishes to obtain what others cannot obtain must cultivate what others do not practice. People always attack what is difficult, but I alone do what is easy.'"

Lu said: "What the multitude does and sees are but the paths of profit and fame, the traces of action. What the multitude does not look upon or practice is where those of the Dao roam. Thus, one who is unmatched under Heaven has no opposing force. Therefore, those who study dimness first see a cartload of firewood; those who study hearing first hear the tolling of a bell. One who has ease within encounters no difficulty without. The ancients said: 'One skilled in strength lifts autumn down; one skilled in hearing hears thunder.' This is what it means. With no difficulty without, one’s name does not extend beyond one’s own Way. The Way’s ultimate power is profound, so its name does not become manifest."

Lu said: "A cartload of firewood is a nearby thing; the tolling of a bell is a great sound. What is easily heard and seen begins with the near and extends to the far. One who is skilled in living first nurtures their spirit. When the spirit is whole, the power of non-action becomes evident, and external things are all penetrated. Thus it is said: 'One who has ease within encounters no difficulty without.' This is attained through unity of mind and perfected in the household, so outsiders do not know. Now my name is known among the feudal lords—this is my violation of my teacher’s teaching, the display of my own ability. I have not yet been able to prevent my name and traces from becoming manifest. Yet my name does not betray my strength; rather, it is because I am free from self-importance that I have attained this. It is by being able to use my strength—by being skilled in using strength without employing it—that I am better than one who burdens themselves with their strength. To flaunt ability is to display its use."

Lu said: "Though I do not match my teacher’s concealment of his traces, am I not still better than one who burdens themselves with their ability and displays it?" One who harmonizes with the great Dao and transforms the myriad things is truly strong. Thus Zhuangzi said: "Hide a mountain in a marsh, conceal a boat in a ravine. One with strength carries them away in the dead of night, yet the ignorant remain unaware." But King Xuan mistook this for mere physical strength.

*Zhenghe* said: "To accumulate many small, unbeatable things into a great victory—only the sage can do this. How could this be about prizing strength? This is why not using strength is the true strength. Scholars study what they cannot study." Thus it is said: "One who wishes to see what others cannot see must gaze where others do not look; one who wishes to obtain what others cannot obtain must cultivate what others do not practice." From having something to see, one discards and forgets it, reaching no-seeing—then one’s vision enters darkness, no different from seeing a dark corner or a cartload of firewood. From having something to hear, one discards and forgets it, reaching no-hearing—then one’s hearing enters silence, no different from hearing the tolling of a bell. When virtue does not take form, where can a name arise? Thus, to make one’s name manifest is to violate the teaching. Yet it is still better than prizing strength to seek fame—this is why one is valued in their time.

Fan said: "To snap the leg of a spring katydid and bear the wing of an autumn cicada may be called weak, yet weakness is the function of the Dao. To rend the hide of a rhinoceros or drag the tail of nine oxen may be called strong, yet strength is the path of death. Thus, under Heaven, the constant path of victory is softness, and the constant path of defeat is strength. Gongyi Bo’s teacher was unmatched in strength under Heaven, yet even his kin were unaware—was this not because he held to the constant path of victory and never employed his strength? The principle of the world is this: only one who can see what others do not look upon can see what others cannot see; only one who can cultivate what others do not practice can obtain what others cannot obtain. Seeing a cartload of firewood does not make one sharp-eyed, yet those who study vision must first see a cartload of firewood. Hearing the tolling of a bell does not make one keen-eared, yet those who study hearing must first hear the tolling of a bell. Why? One first practices what is easy and later what is difficult—then in the end, there is no difficulty. Only when there is no difficulty can no name be ascribed to one. Gongyi Bo’s strength being known among the feudal lords may seem like a violation of his teacher’s teaching and a display of his own ability, yet by being able to use his strength, he does not betray his strength."

As for those who pit strength against strength, gathering many forces to attack, even they may sometimes be subdued—how then could they gallop unchallenged beneath heaven to its most unyielding extremes? *Zhuangzi* says: "Those who employ it borrow from those who do not; by taking the long to gain its use, how much more so for that which has no unuse?" This is why Gongyi Bo’s ability to employ his strength does not compare to Shangqiuzi, of whom such things were never heard.

Zhongshan Gongzi Mou was a worthy prince of the state of Wei. He was the son of Marquis Wen and composed four chapters of writings, which were called *Daoist*. When Wei conquered Zhongshan, he was enfeoffed with its lands, and thus came to be known as Zhongshan Gongzi Mou. Lu says: "Gongzi Mou was the son of Marquis Wen, enfeoffed at Zhongshan, hence called Zhongshan Gongzi." He delighted in associating with worthy men and neglected state affairs, taking pleasure in the company of Gongsun Long of Zhao. Gongzi Mou and Gongsun Long seem to have lived after Liezi, yet they are mentioned here—perhaps later scholars added them to expand the text’s meaning. If such additions do not violate the overall principles and serve to clarify, what harm is there? All such passages are preserved and not removed.

Disciples of Yuezheng Ziyu laughed at him. Gongzi Mou said: "Why do you laugh at my delight in Gongsun Long?" Ziyu replied: "Gongsun Long’s character is such that his conduct has no teacher, his learning no companions. He does not follow the sagely and worthy of old. He is glib but misses the mark..."

Though gifted in debate, he fails to align with reason. He wanders aimlessly without a school—Confucianism, Mohism, Legalism, and the School of Names all jumbled together without a fixed doctrine. He delights in the bizarre and speaks recklessly, favoring the strange and embellishing his words with falsehoods. His aim is to bewilder men’s minds and silence their tongues, studying alongside men like Han Tan. Han Tan was a man’s name, and together they practiced his teachings.

As the *Zhuangzi* says: "The Duke of Huan’s Gongsun Long could overpower men’s mouths but not win their hearts—such is the stubbornness of debaters." Lu comments: "His conduct follows no master, his learning stands alone without companions. Though eloquent, his words miss the mark of reason; though expansive, he has no lineage to uphold. His Way may bend men’s tongues but cannot sway their hearts." Han Tan, as the *Zhuangzi* records, was also called Huan Tuan—both names referring to the same person, their sounds being close.

Gongzi Mou’s expression changed, saying: "Why do you depict Gongsun Long’s faults so harshly? Let me hear the truth of it." His displeasure showed in his face, for he found the accusations against Long excessive and demanded proof.

Ziyu replied: "I laugh at Long’s deception of Kong Chuan. Kong Chuan was Confucius’ grandson, as the *Records of the Generations* notes, and became Long’s disciple. The word *dai* means ‘to deceive.’ It is said that a skilled archer can make the rear arrow’s tip strike the front arrow’s nock, each shot following the last without break, the arrows linking seamlessly. The first arrow hits the target, and the next still clings to the bowstring, appearing as one continuous thing. The arrows connect without interruption—when the first strikes the mark, the next strikes the first, and the one behind it still seems to cling to the string, as if all were a single, unbroken line."

Kong Chuan was astonished. Long said: "This is not yet the marvel."

Yong Hongchao, a disciple of Feng Meng, grew enraged at his wife and sought to frighten her. He drew the Wuhao bow—Wuhao being the bow of the Yellow Emperor—and nocked a Qi-Wei arrow, Qi being a place renowned for fine arrows, and Wei referring to the fletching. He loosed the shaft at her eye. The arrow flew straight toward her pupil, yet her eyelid did not so much as flutter; the arrow fell to the ground, and not a speck of dust stirred. Though the arrow struck, its force was spent—just as the saying goes, "Even a mighty crossbow’s bolt, at its end, cannot pierce Lu gauze."

Could this truly be the words of a wise man?

Gongzi Mou replied: "The words of the wise are not something the foolish can comprehend. With this in mind, jest with Ziyu about the rear arrow striking the front nock, the later shot aligning with the earlier. When the later shot follows the earlier, there is no miss."

In recent times, there was a man who cast five dice, achieving a hundred "Lu" in a hundred throws. People marveled, believing him to possess some Dao, and reported it to Wang Yifu. Yifu said: "There is nothing extraordinary here—he simply made each later throw match the earlier one." When Yu Zisong heard this, he remarked: "Lord Wang’s words, though unassuming, grasp the principle. All such cases are of this kind."

The arrow flew toward the pupil yet the eyelid did not blink—this was the exhaustion of the arrow’s force. One who can gauge the force of bow and arrow, the measure of distance, will know whether the shot will penetrate or not, all determined by the mind and hand’s judgment. There is no fear of error.

Now, if even the clumsiest archer were to blindly shoot toward a target, the arrow would inevitably reach its limit. At that limit, not even a hair’s breadth more can be gained. By reckoning that limit, one adjusts depth according to distance.

Liu Daozhen once told Zhang Shuqi: "I once discussed this with Le Yanfu, who said this need not be the province of sages alone. Even if Confucius or Yan Hui were archers, they would surely understand this." I believe that matters of form and function are but coarse principles of the Dao. One who grasps even a part of the Way can master them fully. Just as Pao Ding wielded his blade or Carpenter Shi swung his axe—such mastery in a single craft does not require a sage.

Why doubt it?

As for aligning the later with the earlier, a hundred shots become as one, and thus appear as a single, unbroken line.

The archer whose eyes do not blink—when the arrow’s force reaches the lashes, it is already spent. Thus, not a speck of dust stirs upon the ground, for the arrow does not fall *because* it strikes the lashes. When Ziyu heard this, he saw it as one continuous motion, saying that from bowstring to target, the arrows linked in unbroken succession, as if never separate. But when he heard that an arrow could strike the pupil and yet not pierce the eye, he claimed the shot had missed its mark—this is a failure to grasp the ultimate principle, not some perverse sophistry of Gongsun Long.

Yue Zheng Ziyu said: "You, a disciple of Long, how could you not gloss over his flaws? I shall speak of his most egregious errors—errors most extreme." Long deceived the King of Wei, saying: "There is intention without mind." Yet the mind is still and thoughtless; if wayward intentions and concerns arise, the mind’s true nature is lost.

Lu said: "What moves in the mind is intention. The world knows intention but not the mind itself."

"There is pointing that does not reach." To seek arrival through pointing is to rely on the self to correct things. Relying on the self to correct things means one has not yet attained the ultimate. Only by forgetting what one relies upon can both self and other be mutually realized.

Hui Shi said: "Pointing does not reach." Lu said: "Wherever there is pointing, there is no arrival. Arrival means there is no pointing."

"There are things that cannot be exhausted." Within the realm of coarse existence, there is always *being*; at the boundary where things are exhausted, the One always remains. The One remains indivisible—though one may wish to diminish it, principle does not permit exhaustion. Only by following without diminishing, by aligning without opposing, do the towering heights of Mount Tai and the vast expanse of primordial qi merge indistinguishably into one.

Hui Shi said: "Take a one-foot staff and halve it daily—it will never be exhausted, even after ten thousand generations." Lu said: "If it could be exhausted, it would not be *being*. A one-foot staff halved daily will never be exhausted, for though the divisions grow ever finer, principle does not allow for total depletion."

"There is a shadow that does not move." Shadow arises from light; if the light does not shift, the shadow is reborn anew. All things undergo hidden transformation in this way, yet the deluded fail to perceive it—hence the analogy of the shadow.

Hui Shi said: "The shadow of a flying bird never moves." Lu said: "If it moved, the shadow would change. New shadows arise in succession, so its motion is unseen."

"A single hair can draw a thousand *jun*." Things break only where there is imbalance. Where all is balanced, nothing can break. Thus, though a hair is fine, it can lift a heavy weight—its force is perfectly even.

Lu said: "Though fine, a hair can bear many *jun*, just as individual strands may seem light, but when gathered, can snap an axle."

"White horse is not horse." This argument survives, and many have debated it. But those who debate it lack broad understanding, so I leave it unaddressed.

Lu said: "White designates color; horse designates form. 'White horse is not horse' distinguishes form from color."

"An orphaned calf has never had a mother." This meaning is not worth exposing.

Lu said: "To call it an orphaned calf—how could it have a mother? Such inverted logic defies reason beyond measure."

Gongzi Mou said: "You fail to grasp the profound and instead call it error—yet the greater error lies with you. The blame for misunderstanding returns to Ziyu. Without intention, minds are the same, united in nothingness. Without pointing, all things are reached. The mind points, and thus nothing is beyond its reach. One who seeks to exhaust things remains bound by *having*. With a mind set on exhausting things, things are never exhausted, and the mind remains burdened by possession. The shadow does not move—its explanation lies in change. The shadow changes and is reborn, not the same as before. *Mozi* says: 'The shadow does not move; its explanation lies in transformation.' A single hair draws a thousand *jun*—its force is perfectly equal. Because of this perfect equality, it does not break. Breaking arises from inequality. Thus, *Mozi* also holds this view. 'White horse is not horse'—form and name are separate. Separation means division. *The White Horse Discourse* says: 'Horse' designates form; 'white' designates color. What designates color does not designate form. How can such statements be resolved? They remain unclear. 'An orphaned calf has never had a mother'—it is not an orphaned calf. This saying verges on the vulgar and is incomprehensible."

Yue Zheng Ziyu said: "You rank Gongsun Long with the braying of horses. You claim his words are no different from a horse’s call, yet you say they have coherence. If such nonsense issued from another’s mouth, you would accept it. You resent Long’s sophistry and rage at Mou’s words, so you spew this crude and insolent language."

Gongzi Mou fell silent for a long while, then withdrew, saying: "Let me return another day to discuss this with you." His anger was still fierce, and reason could not prevail, so he retreated with these deferential words.

Lu said: "One who loses reason in anger cannot be reasoned with—hence his withdrawal."

*Zhenghe* states: "Conduct is ruined by following others—thus one desires a teacher. Learning is impoverished in isolation—thus one desires friends. Loquacity leads to exhaustion—better to abide in the center. Thus, glibness misses the mark. The hundred schools and myriad arts have their uses—thus, those who ramble lack a true school. There is the archery of shooting, and the archery of not shooting. The later arrow’s head aligns with the earlier arrow’s nock, no more than a hair’s breadth behind. The arrow strikes the pupil, yet the eye does not blink—thus, it does not exceed the arrow’s force. This is the archery of shooting—what doubt remains?

Intention arises from the mind; with intention, the mind is altered. Thus, 'intention without mind'—with intention, the mind is not itself. Without intention, minds are the same. To point at a thing is to fail to reach what is not pointed at—better to have no pointing, so all things are reached. Things cannot be exhausted."

To seek to exhaust things by their presence is to remain perpetually bound to existence—thus, where things are not exhausted, the exhaustion of things is ever present.

That a shadow does not move means: whether crooked or straight, its shadow remains the same—hence, the explanation lies in transformation.

To draw a thousand *jun* with a single hair means: the accumulation of small strengths that cannot prevail becomes a great strength that prevails—thus, it is said that power reaches equilibrium.

Yet though Gongsun Long could overcome men’s speech, he could not subdue their hearts—such is the confinement of the disputatious.

*The Liezi* records this, intending to expose the obscurity of perverse doctrines.

Fan [Zuyu] said: Tradition speaks of Huan Tuan, Gongsun Long, and their ilk—sophists who delude men’s minds, alter their thoughts, overcome their speech, yet fail to win their hearts. Gongsun Long himself claimed that by uniting sameness and difference, separating hardness and whiteness, affirming the unaffirmable, and negating the unnegatable, he could confound the wisdom of the hundred schools and exhaust the eloquence of all disputants—such were his tens of thousands of cunning words, yet none were worthy of emulation.

Prince Mou of Zhongshan, whom Zhuangzi deemed one who aspired to the Dao but had not attained it, delighted in Gongsun Long’s ultimate doctrines. Xun Qing, in denouncing the twelve philosophers, did so for this very reason.

As for Gongsun Long’s character: he followed no teacher, kept no companions, was glib yet missed the mark, rambled without a true school, indulged in the bizarre, and spoke recklessly—all to bewilder men’s hearts and silence their speech. This is why Yuezheng Ziyu condemned him.

Observe his letter to Kong Chuan: "A skilled archer can make the later arrow’s head align with the earlier arrow’s nock—this may be called 'the arrow’s head no more than a hair’s breadth behind.' When Hong Chao shot his wife, the arrow struck her pupil yet her eye did not blink—this may be called 'not exceeding the arrow’s force.' But 'intention without mind,' 'pointing without reaching,' 'things not exhausted,' 'shadows not moving,' 'drawing a thousand *jun* with a single hair,' 'a white horse is not a horse,' 'an orphaned calf never had a mother'—these defy categories and invert principles, and there is no end to such absurdities."

Yuezheng Ziyu said: "You take Gongsun Long’s clamor to be reasoned discourse—is it not rather like the wind howling through the reeds, devoid of meaning?"

Yet Prince Mou once delighted in Gongsun Long’s character. And Zhuangzi’s account of Gongsun Long questioning Wei Mou, concluding with the analogy of the frog in the shallow well, reveals an initial admiration followed by final rejection.

Yao governed the world because the world desired governance—thus, Yao governed it.

After fifty years, Yao knew not whether the world was well-governed or not, nor whether the myriad people wished to uphold him or not. For when the Dao governs things, the name of governance vanishes. Once the name of governance is extinguished, Yao does not perceive himself above things, nor do things perceive themselves below Yao. He turned and asked his attendants—his attendants knew not; he asked the outer court—the outer court knew not; he asked those in the wilds—they in the wilds knew not. Had there been any who knew, the Dao of governance would not yet have reached its perfection.

Yao then disguised himself and wandered the broad thoroughfares, where he heard children singing:

*"He raised us, the multitudes,
None but you set our bound.
Unknowing, unperceiving,
We follow Heaven’s decree."*

*"Multitudes"* means the many. To enable the ten thousand things all to attain their bound without violating their natural natures—is this not to follow Heaven’s Dao? If one were to regulate the natures of things through knowledge and perception, how could that accord with Heaven’s Way?

Pleased, Yao asked, *"Who taught you to say these words?"*
The children replied, *"We heard them from a grandee."*
The grandee said, *"It is an ancient poem."*

To speak of an ancient poem in the present is to make the present the same as the ancient.

Yao returned to his palace, summoned Shun, and thereupon abdicated the world to him. His work accomplished, he withdrew—Shun did not decline but accepted, for the moment had come and he responded.

Lu said: *"To prize one’s person while standing above the multitude is to live in constant fear of failing to command their respect. To cherish one’s person while standing above the multitude is to live in constant dread of failing to benefit things. But one who is indifferent to the world neither dominates nor abandons it, governs before disorder arises, takes without presuming honor, and relinquishes without feeling loss. Like Heaven’s revolutions, the four seasons come to be; like Earth’s bearing, the ten thousand things are born. Thus, one accomplishes the work, fulfills the task, and withdraws—this is to be without self. To be without self is to abdicate the great position without reluctance and to accept the great position without refusal."*

Zheng said: *"Yao was not someone who acted upon others, nor did he perceive others as acting upon him. Not acting upon others, he knew not whether the world was governed or not. Not perceiving others as acting upon him, he knew not whether the myriad people wished to uphold him or not. When he asked his attendants, the court, and those in the wilds, and none knew, then the people were vast and nameless—such was the effect. 'He raised us, the multitudes, none but you set our bound'—this means clothing and food were sufficient, and all received the mean of their mandate. 'Unknowing, unperceiving'—this means clothing and food were sufficient, and all followed Heaven’s principle. The hundred clans called it natural—this is the governance of the highest order."*

Fan said: *"One who is intent on governing will not necessarily govern the world; only one who takes no thought for the world can govern it. One who cannot be different will not necessarily be upheld; only one who is different will be upheld. Yet I have heard of letting the world be, not of governing it—thus, whether it is governed or not, I have no concern. When the noble man must assume office, he governs by returning to non-action. The spirit-man dislikes the crowd’s arrival, for when the crowd arrives, he does not compare himself to them—thus, whether they uphold him or not, he has no concern. When the world delights in pushing him forward and does not weary of him, then those who uphold him cannot let go. Thus, though Yao reigned for fifty years, he knew not whether the world was governed or not, nor whether the myriad people wished to uphold him or not. The hundred clans called it natural—what had the emperor’s power to do with them? When he asked his attendants, the court, and those in the wilds, none could explain why it was so. Only when he wandered the broad thoroughfares and heard the children’s song did he know that 'He raised us, the multitudes, none but you set our bound,' and that 'Unknowing, unperceiving, we follow Heaven’s decree.' Was this not the ultimate governance, where all traces vanish?*

*'He raised us, the multitudes, none but you set our bound'*—the *Ode to Wen* once praised Hou Ji with these words.
*'Unknowing, unperceiving, we follow Heaven’s decree'*—the *August* once praised King Wen with these words.
Thus, *Liezi* cited this to praise Yao, simply calling it an ancient poem.

That Yao returned to his palace, summoned Shun, and thereupon abdicated the world to him was because he had accomplished his work and did not dwell in it. That Shun did not decline but accepted was because the moment had come and he could respond. Moreover, when Yao was emperor, he regarded the imperial yellow canopy as no concern of his; when Shun was emperor, he held the world but did not possess it. Thus, their transmission and reception had their own Dao.

Yet the records of the historians say that Yao tested Shun through trials before yielding to him, and that Shun’s virtue was elevated before he received Yao’s abdication. Alas! How could this be the true nature of Yao and Shun?

Guan Yin Xi said: *"Abiding nowhere within oneself, empty and without attachment—how could there be any fixed grasp? Things take form of themselves. That things take form is like the unfolding of affairs and principles. Affairs and principles clarify themselves—it is not my doing. In movement, it is like water; in stillness, like a mirror; in response, like an echo. Moving like water—this is to move in accord with things. Still like a mirror—this is to respond without concealment. Echoing like an echo—this is to respond without leading.*

Lu said: *"The utmost of Heaven is spirit—subtle, wondrous, and profoundly penetrating, fathomless. Seen, it cannot be seen; heard, it cannot be heard. It ever resides within oneself yet none know its abode; it shapes the ten thousand things yet cannot be perceived. In movement, it is like water, moistening the lowly and aiding the exalted; in stillness, like a mirror, reflecting without weariness; in response, like an echo, leaving nothing unanswered—this is the highest principle of nurturing spirit. Thus, the Dao is like things, yet things depart from the Dao, while the Dao does not depart from things. Those who are one with the Dao, the Dao also attains."*

Lu said: *"This utmost Dao is not a thing with form, yet it responds perfectly without omission. Thus, things depart from it, but the Dao does not depart from things."*

One who excels in the Way does not employ the ears, nor the eyes, nor strength, nor the heart-mind. Only by forgetting all that is employed does one accord with the Way. Those who seek the Way through seeing, hearing, form, or intellect will never attain it.

Lu said: *"To excel in this Way, one must dismantle the limbs, silence brilliance, empty the heart-mind, and nourish the spirit—then one naturally turns toward realization. Gazing ahead, it suddenly lies behind; employed, it fills the Six Voids; abandoned, none know its whereabouts. Does the Way have front or back, much or little? It responds wherever sought."*

Lu said: *"This Way of nourishing spirit is hard to know, hard to see—neither existent nor nonexistent. To those who gaze upon it, it precedes the ten thousand things; to those who slight it, it does not contend with things. Employed, the Six Voids are all complete; abandoned, none know where it remains. It stands alone and does not change; it circulates everywhere without exhaustion—this is its perfection. Neither those with a heart-mind can draw near, nor those without a heart-mind can draw far. To seek the Way through having or lacking a heart-mind is to misjudge distance and nearness. Only by forgetting both existence and nonexistence, front and back, does one harmonize with the Heavenly Unification."*

Lu said: *"Those who seek with a heart-mind distance themselves from the Way—it is not the Way that distances them. Those who accord with the Way without a heart-mind draw near to it—the Way itself draws near. Having or lacking a heart-mind is a human distinction; the Way is never far. Only those who silently attain it and cultivate it through nature obtain it. Those who are naturally free of artifice lose nothing. To know yet be without emotion, to be capable yet not act—this is true knowing, true capability. Knowing to the utmost is to be as if without emotion; capability to the fullest is to return to non-action."*

Lu said: *"Only those who silently illuminate within and cultivate through nature attain it. Those who know through nature must forget emotion; those who forget emotion and act not—this is true knowing, true capability. If one arises from non-knowing, how can there be emotion? If one arises from incapability, how can there be action?"*

Lu said: *"Now, arising refers to what humans cannot know—how then could there be emotion? Arising from what humans cannot do—how could there be self-generated emotion? The deluded transform nature into emotion; the wise transform emotion into nature. Thus, the *Yi* says: 'If one does not restrain emotion through nature, how can one long practice correctness?' A clod of earth, a pile of dust—this is but a dwelling. Though one acts not, it is not the principle."*

Lu said: *"The non-acting yet accomplishes all. If one remains inert like a clod or dust, though emotion is abandoned and non-action upheld, it is not the highest principle."*

*Zhenghe* states: *"The Way moves above the ten thousand things. The sage embodies the Way, circulating without accumulation, yet things cannot depart from it. Thus it is said: 'Within oneself, it has no abode; in shaping things, it is manifest.' The movement like water means flowing with things, riding their waves, moving in accord with principle. Stillness like a mirror means neither advancing nor retreating, responding without concealment, still yet unchanging. Response like an echo means never initiating, only harmonizing with others—this is the Way, which responds to things without contrivance. The Way is like things, yet things grow distant from the Way—this is what is meant by 'things depart from the Way.' The Way is vast and one with things—this is what is meant by 'the Way does not depart.' Things have no beginning; to speak of the Way as audible is to hear falsely. The Way cannot be seen; to see it is to see falsely. Thus, one who excels in the Way does not employ the ears or the eyes."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Without place, without repose—only then is the Way settled. Without thought, without worry—only then is the Way known. Thus, one who excels in the Way does not employ strength or the heart-mind. Form, color, name, and sound are insufficient to grasp its essence. Therefore, it is said: 'Those who seek the Way through seeing, hearing, form, or intellect will never attain it.' Gazing ahead, it suddenly lies behind—this speaks of its immeasurability. Employed, it fills the Six Voids; abandoned, none know its whereabouts—this speaks of its inconstancy."*

Those who govern the Way forget the heart-mind; when the heart-mind knows nothing, this is called attainment. Those with a heart-mind and those without have not yet forgotten the heart-mind, and thus cannot attain. Only those who silently attain it and cultivate it through nature—who employ no heart-mind—can obtain it. To know yet forget emotion is to be free from the burdens of knowing—this is true knowing. To be capable yet not act is to be free from the artifice of capability—this is true capability. If one arises from non-knowing, how can there be emotion? If one arises from incapability, how can there be action?

The Way is ever non-acting yet not without action. A clod of earth, a pile of dust—though called non-acting, how can this be the Way? Thus it is said: 'Though one acts not, it is not the principle.'"

Fan said: *"To accumulate is to be insufficient; to store nothing is to have surplus. The perfected person accumulates nothing, is merely empty—thus embodying the Way within, never abiding in possession. Yet how could they constantly harbor intentions toward indiscriminate response? The manifestation in things is each receiving of itself. Thus, flowing without resistance, its movement is like water; responding without concealment, its stillness is like a mirror; harmonizing without initiating, its response is like an echo. Following things naturally, without room for selfishness—this is why the Way is like things. The Way is not far from humans; humans distance themselves from the Way. Thus it is said: 'Things depart from the Way.' Those who harmonize with the Way, the Way also attains—thus it is said: 'The Way does not depart.' One who excels in the Way does not employ the ears or eyes—if one seeks the Way through ears and eyes, though sight and hearing be detailed, they become but deafness and blindness. One does not employ the heart-mind or strength—if one seeks the Way through heart-mind and strength, though form and intellect toil, they become but exhaustion and withering."*

How then could one ever align with the Dao? Only the Dao’s movement is present everywhere. Gazing ahead, one cannot follow it; suddenly behind, one cannot meet it. Employed, it fills the six directions, pervading heaven and earth without revealing its bounds; abandoned, none know its whereabouts, entering the depths of darkness where no trace or sign may be glimpsed.

Those who distance themselves from the profound, the profound also distances itself—yet this is not something the intentional heart-mind can achieve. Those who draw near to the profound, the profound also draws near—yet this is not something the unintentional can attain. Only those who silently recognize it may grasp it; only those who follow their nature may fulfill it. The Dao within me is never lost.

Thus the ancients, knowing, forgot words—this is true knowledge, which knows all things. Acting, they did not act—this is true ability, which can do all things. As for clods of earth, heaps of dust, or obstruction by inaction—how could these be called the Dao?

*End of the Eleventh Fascicle of the Four Commentaries on the Perfect Dao and Its Virtue*
*Beginning of the Twelfth Fascicle of the Four Commentaries on the Perfect Dao and Its Virtue*

**Zhang Zhan of the Jin, Lu Chongxuan the Tang Palace Attendant, the Song *Zhenghe* Annotations, and Fan Zhixu the Left Minister of the Song—compiled by Gaoshouyuan the Radiant Scattered One**

**Tang’s Inquiry**

Zhang [Zhan] said: What wisdom knows is bounded by what it does not know. Yet the world takes its own limited vision to bound all things—thus the great sage poses questions, and those who exhaust principle respond.

Lu [Chongxuan] said: The nature of the myriad things each prizes its own life, yet knows not how to nurture what sustains it. Loving the body, they lose their lives. Thus this chapter discards form to preserve life and penetrate its essence; essence flows into nature, and nature unites with the Dao.

**Zhenghe [Annotations]:** What is above form is spirit, unfathomable; what is below form is matter, inexhaustible. Those of the world take what their ears and eyes reach to expect what lies beyond sight and hearing—how shallow!

Fan [Zhixu] said: Beyond the six directions, the sage acknowledges but does not discourse; within the six directions, the sage discourses but does not debate. Thus Tang’s questions and Ge’s answers are not easily rendered. Those of narrow vision and scant learning call the Dao shallow. Scholars of one corner, daunted by its vastness, dismiss this chapter’s discussions as absurd and fantastical, obscuring the words of the noble man—how is this different from the folly of those confined to a dried-up well?

King Tang of Yin asked Xia Ge (the character *Ge* in *Zhuangzi* is pronounced *Ji*): “Was there being in the primordial beginning?”
He replied: “It was but a boundless blur.”
Xia Ge said: “In the primordial beginning, there was no being—how could there be being now? That there is being today is because there was being in ancient times. Would it be right for later people to say there is no being now? Later ages will surely take this age as ancient, so past and present turn in a cycle. If later people were to say there is no being now, that would be wrong.”

**Zhenghe [Annotations]:** The *Book of Changes* speaks of the Supreme Polarity, which generates the Two Modes. Between heaven and earth, ancient is as present.

King Tang said: “Then is there no first or last among things?”
Xia Ge said: “The end and beginning of things have no limit. What begins may become an end, what ends may become a beginning—how can one know their order? What is now called an end may be the beginning of a thing; what is called a beginning may be its end. End and beginning follow one another, ultimately indistinguishable. Yet beyond things, before events—this I do not know.”
To say that beyond things and before events there is vast emptiness is to have nothing to say.

Lu [Chongxuan] said: Later ages will take today as ancient—how is this different from asking about ancient times today? How can there be no being? From Tang’s perspective, ancient times come first, but the beginning of things and the origin of events precede one another endlessly—this cannot be known.

**Zhenghe [Annotations]:** Without beginning or end, its limit is unknowable. Since beginning and end cannot be fixed, how can one know where before and after lie? Yet within things, though time has no fixed beginning or end, before or after, there is still measure—thus it is said: “How can one know their order?” But beyond things, before events, to exhaust what wisdom knows with what wisdom does not know is only confusion. Thus it is said: “This I do not know.”

Fan [Zhixu] said: Ran Qiu asked Confucius: “Can one know what was before heaven and earth?”
Confucius told him: “Ancient is as present.” For time moves without lingering, things pass without abiding. What was today in the past is now ancient; what is today will be ancient in the future. Then how is the future different from the present, or the present from the past? When the primal qi-mother first divided, the ten thousand kinds filled the space between. What has a beginning must have an end; what has an end must have a beginning. Beginning and end turn against each other like a ring without seam. Unless one is supremely enlightened about beginning and end, how can one know where it begins? How can one know where it ends? Yet though beginning and end have no fixed origin, the times and things within them may still be known. But as for what lies beyond things—something not a thing—or before events—something not an event—without past or present, without beginning or end, even the sage may not know it.

King Tang said: “Then do the eight directions above and below have a limit?”
Though Tang and Ge answered each other, their exchanges remained unclear to sight and hearing, so he asked again, seeking full clarity.
Ge said: “I do not know.”
It is not that he did not know, but that it cannot be known by wisdom.
Tang pressed him, and Ge said: “If there is nothing, then there is no limit; if there is something, then there is an end—how can I know this? To exhaust the limitless by bounding the limited is to reason from ignorance.”
Yet beyond the limitless there is no further limitless; within the endless there is no further endless. If it is called limitless, how can it have an outside? If it is called endless, how can it have a middle? What is called “no limitless” and “no endless” is truly limitless and endless. The limitless has no further limitless; the endless has no further endless. Some might say that beyond the limitless there is another limitless, or that within the endless there is another endless—thus it is reaffirmed that the limitless has no further limitless, and the endless has no further endless. From this I know that it is limitless and endless, but I do not know that it has a limit or an end. To know the limitless is to know all; not to know the limited is true knowledge.

**Zhenghe [Annotations]:** Within the realm, above and below, the eight directions exist; beyond the realm, above and below, the eight directions do not exist. From the perspective of being, there is limit and end; from the perspective of non-being, there is no limit or end. Thus Tang’s questions to Ge often met with “I do not know.”

Fan [Zhixu] said: The measure of things is boundless—where does one draw the line between great and small?

Seeking to exhaust the realm of the utmost greatness through the utmost smallness only leads to confusion and self-defeat. How can one easily attain knowledge of the eight directions above and below? Thus, in non-being there is no limit; the eight directions above and below do not transcend non-being. In being there is an end; the eight directions above and below have already fallen into being. From man’s perspective, one sees only its limitlessness, yet beyond this limitlessness, how could there be another limitlessness? One sees only its endlessness, yet within this endlessness, how could there be another endlessness? From this I know that it is limitless and endless, but I do not know that it has a limit or an end. Such is the discourse on exhausting principle.

Tang further asked: What exists beyond the four seas?
Ge replied: It is like the Central State.
*Central (齐, qí)* means the middle.

Lu said: If there is no being, how can there be limit or end? Thus the Dao pervades all—this is what is meant by "non-being." Its essence and function are both vast, not mere emptiness or nothingness.

Tang said: How do you verify this?
Ge replied: When I traveled east to Ying, the people were the same. If one asks about what lies east of Ying, it is still like Ying. When I traveled west to Bin, the people were the same. If one asks about what lies west of Bin, it is still like Bin. From this I know that the Four Seas, the Four Wastelands, and the Four Poles are no different.

*The meanings of the Four Seas, Four Wastes, and Four Poles are found in the* Er Ya.

Knowing that they do not differ from what lies between, then what lies between is thus. Lu said: The four directions cannot be exhausted, for all have life and death, love and hate, parents and wives, children. Thus one knows that beyond the Four Wastes and Four Poles, it is no different from within Ying and Bin—this is what is meant by "thus."

**Zhenghe:** Heaven and earth cover and bear all; the Dao makes them impartial. The four directions are boundless, without demarcation. Observing near and far, what difference is there?

Fan said: That which is at the center of heaven and earth is called the Central State; that which lies beyond is called the Four Barbarians. Though the natures of the five directions differ, and the suitability of the five lands varies, if we speak only of what exists, how is what lies beyond the Four Seas different from the Central State? Thus, east of Qi, traveling to Ying, the people are the same. Asking about what lies east of Ying, it is still like Ying—from this one may know the east extends to where the sun rises. West of Qi, traveling to Bin, the people are the same. Asking about what lies west of Bin, it is still like Bin—from this one may know the west extends to where the sun sets. Observing thus, one knows the Four Seas, Eight Wastes, and Four Poles are no different.

*Tradition says:* East to Taiyuan, west to the state of Bin, south to Puye, north to Zhuli—these are the Four Poles. Beyond the goblet bamboo to the north, west to the Queen Mother of the West—these are the Eight Wastes. The Nine Yi, Eight Di, Seven Rong, and Six Man—these are the Four Seas. Thus the great and small contain one another, without end.

That which contains the ten thousand things is like that which contains heaven and earth. That which contains the ten thousand things is heaven and earth; that which contains heaven and earth is the Great Void. It contains the ten thousand things, thus it is inexhaustible. Heaven and earth contain transformations; yin and yang receive qi; the myriad things take form in flux, succeeding one another without ceasing in one life or ending in one form—thus it is inexhaustible. It contains heaven and earth, thus it is limitless. Heaven and earth enclose the Three Luminaries and encompass the Four Seas—they are vast indeed, yet as formed and bounded things, they must have limits. To exhaust their limits, how could it not be void? Considering heaven and earth within the Great Void, they are as if existing and as if not. Thus, within the realm of bounded directions, all things compare in size and differ in quantity. Extending this to the utmost of non-being, how could it be exhausted by one heaven or bounded by one earth? Then heaven and earth and the ten thousand things mutually envelop one another, alternating as states and cities—how could one know their fullness or emptiness, measure their number? How could I know that beyond heaven and earth there are not greater heavens and earths? The Great Void is endless; heaven and earth have limits. Containing the limited within the endless, heaven is not necessarily the greatest of forms.

Thus, what Zou Yan spoke of was but what he grasped—this too is what I do not know. All things may be inferred by principle, not proven by tangible evidence. To trust only what the mind and intellect know, yet not know that knowledge has limits, is superficial understanding. To believe only what the ears and eyes perceive, yet not know that seeing and hearing have bounds, is the way of the vulgar scholar. As for the enlightened person, they dissolve the obstructions of the mind and intellect, profoundly comprehend the wondrous principles beyond wisdom, break through the barriers of seeing and hearing, and remotely grasp the forms beyond things. Those who confine their thoughts to bounded realms and follow the steps within the Six Directions will claim that all is recorded in the Three Tombs and Five Classics, that the *Guicang* is exhausted by the Four Seas and Nine Provinces. How could they know the vastness of the Great Void, the limitlessness of great and small, that heaven and earth are but a dwelling, and the ten thousand things but floating dust? All are constrained by short-sightedness in their time, dimly perishing together.

Thus Liezi expounded the ultimate words of no-inner, releasing the obstructions of the mind and intellect; proclaimed the grand song of no-outer, opening the barriers of seeing and hearing. Those who aspire to follow the wind unknowingly shed their self-importance; those bound by teachings unknowingly cast off their shackles. Thus he carved away the Confucians and Mohists, pointing directly to the great square—was this merely a love of the strange and a fondness for the extraordinary, vainly seeking grandeur? Alas! When Lao Dan and Zhuang Zhou were already mocked by worldly discourse, how could you alone escape it?

Lu said: The spiritual Dao contains the ten thousand things, thus it does not exhaust the yin and yang that contain heaven and earth. Thus beyond the limitless of heaven, earth, and the ten thousand things, what I do not know cannot be debated—this does not mean I know nothing at all.

Zhenghe: "Nameless, it is the beginning of heaven and earth; named, it is the mother of the ten thousand things." That which is the mother of the ten thousand things is heaven and earth, thus it contains the ten thousand things without exhaustion. That which is the beginning of heaven and earth is the Dao, thus it contains heaven and earth without limit. Heaven and earth are but a minute thing within the void, yet the Dao envelops them—thus beyond heaven and earth there must exist something greater still.

Fan said: The small differ and are distinct, while the great covers and unites them all. Only heaven and earth can cover and bear the ten thousand forms; only the great void can embrace the six directions. Great and small contain one another—who knows how this is so? Because it contains the ten thousand things, none know its bounds; this is why what fills the space between heaven and earth is only the ten thousand things. Because it contains heaven and earth, it has never had a limit; this is why heaven and earth, though vast, are not beyond its interior. Thus heaven and earth are merely a minute thing within the void, the largest of all that has form—how could one know that beyond the formless there exists something greater still? This may be grasped in the mind, yet when put into words, it often eludes understanding. Thus heaven and earth are also things. Things have insufficiencies, which is why in ancient times Nüwa smelted the five-colored stones to mend their flaws. When yin and yang lost their measure, the three luminaries waxed and waned—thus the flaws of heaven and earth need not be in their physical form. Nüwa was a divine being, so she could refine the essence of the five constants to harmonize yin and yang, ensuring the celestial markers followed their proper sequence, without needing to patch them with material substance.

Lu said: Zhang Zhan’s commentary here is apt. She broke the legs of the giant turtle to establish the four pillars. Later, Gonggong contended with Zhuanxu for the throne, and in his rage struck Mount Buzhou. Gonggong rose to power between Fuxi and Shennong, and his descendants, relying on their strength, later vied with Zhuanxu for rulership. Zhuanxu was the grandson of the Yellow Emperor. Mount Buzhou stood at the northwestern extremity. The pillar of heaven was broken, the cord of earth severed; thus heaven tilted to the northwest, and the sun, moon, and stars settled there; earth sank in the southeast, and so the hundred rivers and floodwaters flowed there.

Lu said: To disrupt the constant order and corrupt virtue is to break the pillar of heaven and sever the cord of earth. Thus the sage knows the Way of Heaven observes excess and supplements deficiency, so the three luminaries and the hundred rivers follow their great principles.

Zhenghe: Smelting stones to mend flaws and breaking the turtle’s legs to establish the pillars—these are the ways of the sage in shaping and aiding creation. The sun, moon, and stars settle in the northwest of heaven, and the hundred rivers and floodwaters flow to the southeast of earth—such is their inherent tendency.

Fan said: The *Book of Changes* takes Qian as the yang thing and Kun as the yin thing, so heaven and earth have not yet departed from being things. Thus the ancients sometimes smelted stones to mend flaws, broke the turtle’s legs to establish pillars, or shattered the pillar of heaven and severed the cord of earth. Though heaven and earth are vast, they cannot remain whole—how then could their encompassing and shaping be without dependence? The sun, moon, and stars revolve to the left because heaven is insufficient in the northwest; the hundred rivers and floodwaters flow eastward because earth is incomplete in the southeast. The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: "Heaven is insufficient in the northwest, hence the north is yin, and a person’s right ear and eye are not as keen as the left; earth is incomplete in the southeast, hence the southeast is yang, and a person’s left hand and foot are not as strong as the right." Taking the near to understand the body, the vastness of heaven and earth becomes apparent.

Tang further asked: Do things have greatness and smallness? Do they have length and shortness? Do they have sameness and difference?

Ge said: East of the Bohai Sea, beyond countless millions of *li*, lies a great abyss—truly a bottomless valley, as recorded in the *Classic of the Great Wilderness* and the *Odes Containing Divine Mist*, which states: "It pours eastward into the bottomless valley, its depths without end." To call it "bottomless" is merely to speak of its unfathomable depth. The preceding line says "without limit," meaning it cannot be exhausted. Even if truly bottomless, there is nothing to astonish. It is called the "Returning Gully." *Master Zhuang* refers to it as "Weilü."

All the waters of the Eight Corners and Nine Regions, the celestial streams of the Milky Way, pour into it without increase or decrease. The Eight Corners are the eight extremities; the Nine Regions, the eight directions of heaven and its center. It is said that the Celestial River flows into the sea.

Lu said: The bottomless great abyss speaks of the Dao’s inexhaustibility. The most subtle and fine, entering into the spaceless, is nothing but water. That it receives without increase or decrease is because the myriad existents are all contained within it.

Within it stand five mountains: the first is Daiyu, the second Yuanqiao, the third Fanghu, the fourth Yingzhou, and the fifth Penglai. Each mountain rises and encircles thirty thousand *li*, with its flat summit spanning nine thousand *li*. The distance between their midpoints is seventy thousand *li*, making them neighbors. Their terraces and pavilions are all of gold and jade; their birds and beasts, pure white. Trees of pearl and jade grow in clusters, their blossoms and fruits all flavorful—consuming them grants agelessness and immortality. The inhabitants are all of the seed of immortals and sages; those who fly between them day and night are beyond counting. Though the distance between two mountains is seventy thousand *li*, and the total span among the five is two hundred eighty thousand *li*, the number of those who travel back and forth day and night cannot be reckoned—even the swiftness of wind and clouds cannot surpass their speed.

Lu said: All things with form arise within the great Dao, adorned and embellished. Delighting in finery, they know neither aging nor death, their movements ceaseless, vanishing and appearing in an instant—without limit. Yet the roots of the five mountains have no attachment. Such mountains still float upon the sea; by this reasoning, all realms of form are lodged within the great void, thus without root or stem. They constantly rise and fall with the tides, never resting for even a moment.

Lu said: The eye, ear, nose, tongue, and body are the five roots, drifting with the waves without pause. This describes how the world is all swept along by sounds, colors, fragrances, and flavors, clinging without respite—even to the point of forgetting life and scorning death in pursuit of fame and profit, unaware of how to cease and reflect, to nurture the spirit and return to the Dao.

The immortals and sages lamented this and appealed to the Emperor. Fearing the mountains would drift to the four extremities and the abodes of the immortals and sages be lost, the Emperor commanded Yuqiang.

The *Classic of the Great Wilderness* states: The spirit of the Northern Culmen is named Yuqiang, and the divine turtle serves as his messenger. Fifteen giant turtles lift their heads to bear the weight. The *Li Sao* asks: "If giant turtles bear the mountains, how can they remain stable?" They take turns in three shifts, exchanging once every sixty thousand years. Thus, the five mountains stand firm and unmoving.

Lu said: Form and substance are the dwelling of spirit and illumination. If the five roots drift and lose their place of abiding, then the immortals and sages will have no dwelling. As *Zhuangzi* says: "Once receiving its completed form, it does not perish until its end." If the five roots float adrift, they follow delusion to the point of death. A life spent in vain—how can this not be lamentable? Therefore, the great sages established methods and teachings to halt this, and the five roots thereby found stability. The five dusts correspond to them, the five senses arise from them, hence the number fifteen. Through the heart they are discerned, thus the three shifts and the exchange every sixty thousand years. From this, one knows the Dao of restraining the five roots.

Yet in the land of Longbo there was a giant who, in fewer than several strides, reached the abode of the five mountains. With a single cast of his hook, he caught six turtles, bundled them together, and hastened back to his country, where he scorched their bones to divine by them. A mountain spanning ten thousand *li* in height, breadth, and circumference was borne upon the head of a single turtle, and these six turtles were drawn by a single hook. That the people of Longbo could shoulder them together and bore holes in their bones to cast lots—such a person’s form must measure over a million *li*. Compared to him, the *kun*-peng is but a gnat, flea, or gust of wind. Then what could the great void not contain?

Lu said: *Bo* means "elder," the greatest of the mighty dragons. This illustrates how, among the vulgar, craving, pride, love, greed, and indulgence become the hook that ensnares, bearing the six senses to their own gratification. Is it merely that they lose their place of abiding? They even destroy and make use of it.

Thus, the two mountains Daiyu and Yuanqiao drifted to the Northern Culmen and sank into the great sea, scattering immortals and sages beyond count. Lu said: What the vulgar heart drowns in, only sensory pleasures weigh heavily. Noble and base alike are ensnared by fame and profit—hence the two mountains flowed away. Love and indulgence run deep, likened to the great sea; the wandering of the numinous consciousness cannot be fully told.

The Thearch, in great wrath—*feng* meaning "great"—diminished the land of Longbo, making it straitened, and shrank its people, making them short. By the eras of Fuxi and Shennong, the people of that land still measured several tens of *zhang*. The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: "Beyond the Eastern Sea, within the great wilderness, lies the Land of Giants." The *River Chart Jade Tablet* records: "Nine myriad *li* north of Kunlun lies the land of Longbo, where people stand forty *zhang* tall and live eighteen thousand years before death." Lu said: The great sages abhorred the harm of craving and desire, so they established laws to restrain them. As the teachings of sagely wisdom spread, that land gradually shrank. Yet even under Shennong’s governance, its people still measured several tens of *zhang*, for while humanity cannot extinguish such traits, it can only pare them away.

Fan said: Tradition speaks of a great chasm beyond the Eastern Sea—this is the chasm now called. Its depths are bottomless, matching the so-called "valley that pours eastward without end." Named the "Returning Abyss," it is the same as the "tail’s vent" that drains all. The waters of the eight extremities and nine wilds, the flow of the Celestial Han—none fail to pour into it, yet it neither swells nor diminishes. It is filled yet never full, drawn from yet never exhausted, unchanging through time or measure, unaffected by quantity or flux, such is its nature.

I once examined the words of the Grand Historian: "Within the Bohai Sea lie the three divine mountains, where all immortals and the elixir of deathlessness reside. From afar, they appear like clouds. But upon approach, they recede and depart."

Shall these, then, be the so-called Five Mountains? Thus, none but the seed of immortals and sages may dwell upon them. Yet the roots of the Five Mountains had no firm anchorage, so the Thearch, fearing they would drift to the westernmost reaches, commanded the gods of Yuqiang to bear them upon the heads of giant sea-turtles. Only then did the Five Mountains stand firm and unmoving.

In the land of Longbo there lived giants, who with but a few strides reached the Five Mountains. One cast his hook and bore away six of the turtles, whereupon Mount Yuanqiao sank into the great sea, and the seed of immortals and sages were scattered and displaced. The Thearch, enraged, diminished their land and shrank their people, so that by the time of Fuxi and Shennong, the folk of that land stood but a few dozen *zhang* tall.

Is this not the "land of giants" spoken of in tradition—beyond the Eastern Sea, within the Great Wilderness? From the Central States, forty thousand *li* eastward lies the land of Jiao Yao, whose people stand but a foot and a half tall. This is recorded in the *Poems Containing the Divine Mist*. In the far northeast there are beings called the Contentious Folk, who measure but nine inches. They are mentioned in the *Classic of Mountains and Seas*. The *Poems Containing the Divine Mist* also states: "In the far northeast dwell such people." Having spoken of the great, it thus clarifies the small.

Fan remarks: The Five Mountains rest upon the heads of giant turtles, and a single hook of the Longbo giant could lift them—thus are the vastness of things made plain. The people of Jiao Yao are short, standing but a foot and a half; the Contentious Folk of the northeast measure but nine inches—thus are the minuteness of things made plain. The sage observes from afar and from near, so that the small is not deemed insignificant, nor the great excessive. How, then, can one know that the tip of a hair suffices to define the limit of the finest? And how can one know that heaven and earth suffice to exhaust the realm of the greatest?

South of Jing lies the Dark Numinous, for whom five hundred years make a spring, and five hundred years an autumn. In high antiquity there was the Great Camphor, for whom eight thousand years made a spring, and eight thousand years an autumn. Upon rotting soil grow fungi and mushrooms that sprout at dawn and perish by dusk. In the months of spring and summer there are midges that arise with the rain and die at the sight of the sun.

Lu says: If one harbors desires and loses their true nature, then what difference is there between the great of form and the small, the long-lived and the short-lived? Thus, whether one ascends to the heights of divine immortals or descends to the level of ants, to lose the true and forsake the Way, to be driven by the passions—this loss is one and the same.

Fan says: The Dark Numinous and the Great Camphor are what Zhuangzi called the "great years." The fungi and midges are what he called the "small years." Time has its durations, number its quantities. To awaken to this and yet remain unfathomable—distant yet not perplexed, grasping yet not straining—why then should the common folk trouble themselves with knowing Pengzu as long-lived and comparing themselves to him?

At the northernmost reaches of the northern lands, as Zhuangzi says, "beyond the farthest growth," lies the Dark Sea, the Celestial Pool. There dwells a fish, thousands of *li* in breadth, its length commensurate, called the Kun. There is also a bird, called the Peng, whose wings are like clouds trailing from the sky, its body commensurate. As Zhuangzi says: "The Kun transforms into the Peng." Yet how could the world know of such creatures? They amuse themselves with what they commonly see, grow accustomed to what they commonly hear—even if told of these things, they would still disbelieve.

Great Yu beheld them in his travels, Boyi knew and named them, and the sage Yijian heard and recorded them. Strange sights and wondrous tales are met with doubt by the multitude. Yet how could Yu, Boyi, and Jian have merely spun empty words of marvels to startle their age? Rather, they made plain that such creatures exist, to dispel the stubborn narrowness of those who cling to their views, and to remove the blindness and deafness of those who perceive and hear.

Though Yijian’s name is not widely known, he was one of the ancients well-versed in the myriad things.

Fan says: Scaled creatures blaze below, able to dive but not to fly. The Kun is the diver, bound to the yin. Feathered creatures soar above, able to fly but not to dive. The Peng is the flier, bound to the yang. Though vast, the Kun and Peng are still confined to the categories of yin and yang. The vulgar scholars of the world, constrained by what lies near to their ears and eyes, thus deem such creatures nonexistent—how then could they know of that which is without limit or end?

Thus, Liezi must attribute his words to figures like Great Yu, Boyi, and Yijian, for his account is rooted in antiquity, not merely spun as empty words to startle the age.

Between the riverbanks there breeds a tiny creature, so minute it is called the Jiao Ming. They gather in swarms upon the eyelashes of mosquitoes, yet never collide. They alight and depart, and the mosquito remains unaware. Li Zhu and Zi Yu, even as they wiped their eyes and raised their brows to gaze by day, could not discern their forms. Li Zhu, a man of keen sight in the time of the Yellow Thearch, could see the tip of an autumn hair at a hundred paces. Zi Yu’s identity is unknown. Chi Yu and Shi Kuang, even as they pricked their ears and bent their heads to listen by night, could not catch their sound. Chi Yu’s identity is unknown. Shi Kuang lived in the time of Duke Ping of Jin; Xia Ge had no reason to mention him, so later writers embellished the account.

Those who rely on the mind’s intelligence and depend on the ears and eyes for seeing and hearing cannot perceive the most minute of things. Only when the Yellow Thearch and Rong Chengzi dwelt upon Kongtong, fasting together for three months, their hearts dead and their forms abandoned—what is called "the heart like dead ashes, the form like withered wood"—did they slowly begin to see with the spirit...

The spirit is but silent, darkly illuminating—no need for the eyes. Suddenly one sees it, vast as the slopes of Mount Song; yet to bring form into the realm of spirit is like comparing Mount Song to its true immensity. Slowly one listens with qi—qi follows its natural course, not relying on external use. Suddenly one hears it, resounding like thunder; yet to bring sound into the domain of emptiness is like comparing thunder to its true vastness.

Lu said: If there be the obstruction of form and sound, then piled earth becomes mountains, swarming mosquitoes become thunder—suddenly one sees it, suddenly one hears it, and there is nothing strange in this.

Fan said: Li Zhu and Zi Yu, men of keen sight in ancient times, could see only what had form; beyond form, there was nothing they could perceive. Chi Yu and Shi Kuang, men of sharp hearing in ancient times, could hear only what had sound; beyond sound, there was nothing they could detect. Only the Yellow Thearch and Rong Chengzi, dwelling upon Kongtong, fasting for three months, their hearts like dead ashes, their spirits congealed, their forms like withered wood, their countenances still—seeing with spirit rather than eyes, listening with qi rather than ears—then between the riverbanks, swarms of midges gathered. Before, Li Zhu and Zi Yu, even as they wiped their eyes and raised their brows by day, could not perceive their forms; now, suddenly, they saw them as vast as Mount Song. Before, Chi Yu and Shi Kuang, even as they pricked their ears and bent their heads by night, could not catch their sound; now, suddenly, they heard it resounding like thunder. Who can fathom the principle behind the minute and the vast?

Moreover, from the perspective of ordinary people, the leviathan and the tiny insect are so far apart that their difference cannot be expressed in words. Yet from the boundless, limitless vantage, both are but things—what superiority is there between them?

In the lands of Wu and Chu, there is a great tree called *you* (sounding like "pomelo"). Its leaves are jade-green, and it bears fruit in winter; its berries are crimson and sour. Drinking its sap cures the illness of oppression. The people of the Central States prize it, yet when it crosses the Huai River to the north, it transforms into the trifoliate orange. The myna does not cross the Ji River; the raccoon dog dies if it crosses the Wen River—such is the influence of the earth’s qi. Though this matter is also recorded in the *Rites of Zhou*, the forms and qi differ, yet their natures remain the same. They do not change one another; all life is complete, all portions are sufficient.

How am I to know the great from the small? How am I to know the long from the short? How am I to know the same from the different? The ten thousand kinds, ten thousand forms, ten thousand natures, ten thousand dispositions—each rests in what it knows, following without grasping, then all are equal in completeness and sufficiency, without desire to change one another. Can this be discerned by wisdom?

Lu said: What is born of yin and yang, what suits the land, what connects with spirit and qi, what is altered by habit and influence—all are like this. What is there to debate?

*Zhenghe* commentary: The great and small belong to form; the long and short belong to number. With form and number, the names of sameness and difference are established. Beyond the four directions, within the six directions, there are ten thousand differences—who knows their limit? Yu the Great and Bo Yi named what they saw, yet this still lay within the realm of ears, eyes, heart, and knowledge. The Yellow Thearch and Rong Chengzi, seeing with spirit and listening with qi, had already transcended into the subtle, mysterious, and penetrating. Those who behold the Dao do not follow what is abandoned, nor trace what arises. All natures are equal, all lives are complete, all portions are sufficient. Knowing this alone, why must one discern where the great and small, the long and short, the same and different come to rest?

Fan said: The *Artificers’ Record* states: "The orange crosses the Huai to the north and becomes the trifoliate orange; the myna does not cross the Ji River; the raccoon dog dies if it crosses the Wen River—such is the earth’s qi." Its words are based on this. Things born between heaven and earth vary in fullness and emptiness, in growth and decline, in qi; yet the natures they are endowed with are natural and cannot be altered. All life is complete, without the slightest deficiency; all portions are sufficient, without the slightest lack. The great and small, the long and short, the same and different—awake or obscure, there is no boasting, so why must one seek knowledge of these?

The meaning of *Zhuangzi*’s "Free and Easy Wandering" accords with this.

The two mountains, Taihang and Wangwu—*Taihang* should read *Taihang* (the Taihang Mountains are in Heyang County, Henan; Wangwu is in Dongyuan County, Hedong)—span seven hundred *li* and rise ten thousand *ren*. They originally lay south of Jizhou and north of the Yellow River. There was an old man of the Northern Mountains, called the Foolish Old Man—though he was called foolish, he was not necessarily unwise. Nearly ninety years of age, he lived facing the mountains.

Lu said: *Cheng* (惩), pronounced *chí gāng*.

"Cheng" means caution, wound, or governance. The mountains blocked the north, making all journeys circuitous. He gathered his household to consult, saying: "I and you shall join our strength to level the perilous terrain, opening a path south to Yu and reaching the southern bank of the Han—would this be acceptable?" They all assented in unison. *Za* means "the rest."

His wife raised a doubt—*xiànyí* means to present a difficulty—and said: "With your strength, you cannot even reduce the mound of Kuifu; what can you do to Taihang and Wangwu? Kuifu is a small hill, located in the borders of Chenliu. Moreover, where will you put the earth and stones?"

They replied in unison: "Cast them into the far reaches of the Bohai Sea, north of the Hidden Land." *Huainanzi* states: "The prefecture obtained in the northeast is called the Hidden Land."

Thereupon, he led his children and grandchildren—three men able to bear the load—striking stones and breaking soil, transporting them with baskets to the far reaches of the Bohai Sea. The widow of a neighbor from the Jingcheng clan—a widow is a woman without a husband—had an orphaned boy who had just lost his milk teeth; he leapt forth to assist them. As the seasons changed, they had only just made one return trip.

The Wise Old Man of the River Bend laughed and tried to stop them—those commonly called wise are not necessarily unwise—and said: "How extreme is your lack of wisdom!"

With his remaining years and waning strength, how could he harm so much as a single blade of grass upon the mountain—what then of its earth and stones?

The Foolish Old Man of North Mountain sighed deeply: "Your heart is so obstinate, so utterly impervious—you are not even the equal of a widow or a frail child. Though I should die, my son remains. My son will beget a grandson, my grandson will beget a son, and that son will beget yet another son. Sons beget sons, grandsons beget grandsons, without end or exhaustion. Yet the mountain will not grow—why then should we not level it?"

The Wise Old Man of the River Bend had no reply. He yielded in reason but submitted to the other’s resolve.

The Spirit Who Grasps the Serpent, hearing of this—*The Classic of Great Wilderness* states: "The spirits of mountains and seas all grasp serpents"—feared that the old man would not cease. If he did not cease, the mountains would surely be leveled.

The world knows well that small accumulations can build great heights, yet fails to grasp that great losses can dwindle to near nothing. The nine-tiered tower rises from heaped earth; the lofty bank becomes a hidden ravine. If effort is never abandoned, if one does not demand instant results, then no trifle will fail to accumulate, no vastness will fail to diminish. Now, when whetstone and blade grind ceaselessly against each other, one knows they will eventually wear away. If two things are thus, then hills and valleys must likewise shift—there is no cause for doubt. Those who are confounded by questions of scale or speed have simply failed to extend the principle.

He reported the matter to the Emperor. Moved by the old man’s sincerity—touched by the depth of the Foolish Old Man’s devotion—the Emperor commanded the two sons of Kuafu. (Kuafu is unrecorded in the annals; he must have been a being of divine strength.) They bore the two mountains away, placing one in the northeast, the other south of Yong. From that time on, south of Ji and north of the Han River, there were no more obstructing ridges.

Those who seek merit in a single morning or evening will resort to deceit upon hearing the year’s end draw near; those who covet beauty in their own time will grieve endlessly after their death. Such is the shallow mind of the common man, the ordinary sentiment of a single generation. As for the great person, they take heaven and earth as a single dawn, myriad ages as a fleeting instant, act with utter forgetfulness, and achieve without deliberate intent. Whether it lies with me or with another, whether it belongs to this life or to others, they perceive no distinction, nor do they know what comes before or after. Thus, the Foolish Man of North Mountain and the widowed wife’s orphan are enough to mock the cunning of Hequ and deride the delusions of an entire age. O you aimless multitudes—can you not reflect upon this?

Lu says: This chapter is an *xing*. The vulgar follow what they are accustomed to and conform to the crowd; what the crowd deems right, they take as right.
