Though entangled in desires from birth to death, living in ceaseless drift and dying without knowing their destination, the foolish toil endlessly for food and clothing until their end, while the gentleman toils endlessly for fame and form until his death—both believe they are content with fate, as it has ever been. Yet the one who attains the highest learning must seek the Dao, forget life, and merge with the real. Upon hearing the Way, they act without regard for age; if they exhaust life without hearing it, their spirit may yet be moved to spontaneous understanding. Thus the *Yijing* says: "Abiding in stillness, yet when stimulated, they penetrate all." Only then can the hindrances of form be forgotten, and the principle of perfect equilibrium flow unimpeded.

*Zhenghe* says: To level the perilous and make it passable is to remove the obstruction of form. That the Emperor was moved by sincerity is to reach the wondrous realm beyond form. The old man of Hequ was shackled by the finitude of form and unaware of the Dao’s boundlessness; in his cleverness, he mocked the foolish, never realizing that pure, unadulterated foolishness is the greatest wisdom.

Fan says: The trickle from a cliff’s edge bores through stone; a single strand of hemp severs a tree trunk. Water is not a drill for stone, nor is rope a saw for wood—yet they wear away by ceaseless effort. The one who embodies the Dao discerns this principle clearly, taking ancient and modern as a single breath, life and death as a single thread. With steadfast resolve, they act without expecting results in an instant; with unwavering sincerity, they seek no merit within the span of a year. How could they ever deem worldly affairs difficult? Thus, the two mountains of Taihang and Wangwu stood south of Jizhou and north of Heyang, spanning seven hundred *li*, towering ten thousand *ren*—truly high and vast. Yet they had not transcended form and measure, and so could be leveled. The Foolish Old Man of North Mountain, nearly ninety years of age, dwelt facing the mountains. Vexed by the blockage to the north and the circuitous paths of his comings and goings, he gathered his clan to deliberate. With all their strength, they set about leveling the perilous heights. Shouldering baskets and wicker hampers, they carried earth and stone, dumping them north of Yintu and at the edge of the Bohai Sea—truly a distant removal. Some approved with a chorus of voices, while young boys just losing their baby teeth came to help. Those who raised doubts were not heeded; those who laughed and tried to stop them were ignored.

With descendants endless in number, leveling earth and stone that would never increase—such perseverance might well endure forever. His unceasing toil, thus carried to its end, moved even the Spirit Who Grasps the Serpent, who in turn stirred the son of Kuafu. Their strength bore the peril of the two mountains, smoothing the way so that no ridges remained to block the ascent. Ah! What seemed folly yet reached the height of wisdom—is this not the very essence of the Foolish Old Man?

---
*The Perfect Dao and Its Power: True Classic with Four Commentaries, Scroll Twelve ends*
*The Perfect Dao and Its Power: True Classic with Four Commentaries, Scroll Thirteen*

**Tang’s Questions**

Kuafu, overestimating his strength, sought to chase the sun’s shadow, pursuing it to the edge of Yu Valley. Yu Valley is Yuyuan, where the sun sets. Thirsting for drink, he rushed to the Yellow River and the Wei. When the Yellow River and the Wei proved insufficient, he set out northward to drink from the Great Marsh. Before he arrived, he perished of thirst along the way. He cast aside his staff, and where his corpse’s fat and flesh soaked the earth, Deng Forest grew. Deng Forest spread wide, spanning thousands of *li*.

*The Classic of Mountains and Seas* states: “Kuafu died, discarded his staff, and it became Deng Forest.”

Lu says: When one arrives at the Dao with a single-minded resolve, one departs with a bond to the Real. Yet if one relies on ability to seek victory, one chases shadows and never catches them. In attaining the Real, all forms are leveled; in pursuing the superficial, one loses life and leaves only traces. The traces’ manifestation gave rise to Deng Forest; the bond to the Real leveled hills and ridges.

Zhenghe: Transformations and shifts proceed without end—who can fathom their limits? The Zheng man’s autumn cypress, Kuafu’s birth of Deng Forest—do these not arise from some hidden mechanism, some inevitability?

Fan says: Tradition holds that Kuafu died, discarded his staff, and it became Deng Forest—this is the very Kuafu in question. Chasing the sun to the edge of Yu Valley, rushing to drink between the Yellow River and the Wei, he finally set out northward for the Great Marsh, only to die before arriving. Was this not the consequence of burdening himself excessively, failing to measure his own strength?

Great Yu declared: Within the Six Directions and the Four Seas, illumination comes from the sun and moon, alignment from the stars, order from the four seasons, and summation from the Great Year. What the divine and numinous engender assumes diverse forms—some perish young, some live long—only the sage can fathom their Way.

The sage follows the Dao of Heaven and Earth, accords with the nature of the myriad things, allows each to find its place, and harmonizes their reversals and sequences, so that all differences find their proper measure and all lives, long or short, fulfill their allotted span.

Zhenghe: The sun and moon possess radiance, hence "illumination"; the stars form ranks, hence "alignment"; the four seasons proceed in sequence, hence "order"; the Great Year encompasses all, hence "summation." "What the divine and numinous engender" refers to what Heaven and Earth bring forth—Heaven as divine, Earth as numinous. The myriad things assume diverse forms, some perishing young, some living long. The sage roams the beginning and end of all things and penetrates their origins, thus it is said: only the sage can fathom their Way.

Xia Ge replied: Yet there are things that arise without relying on the divine or numinous, take form without depending on yin and yang, and shine without awaiting the sun and moon. That which lives lives of itself; that which takes form forms of itself; that which shines shines of itself—suddenly, spontaneously, inherently without cause or reliance. They perish young without awaiting violence, live long without awaiting nurture; those that perish young do so not through misfortune, those that live long do so not through care. They eat without relying on the five grains, clothe themselves without awaiting silk or floss, travel without awaiting boats or carriages—their Way is spontaneous. The spontaneous requires no reliance whatsoever. This is not what the sage can fathom.

The sage does not contravene the spontaneous, and the myriad things move of themselves—how could he delight in fathoming things? All the passages above were Xia Ge’s counsel to Tang of Yin.

Lu said: The beings that move and take form each have their proper place; the sage can follow their arising to fathom their Way. Yet the numinous consciousness is utterly transcendent, requiring nothing, not nurtured by the host of beings—it moves of itself, spontaneously. How could the sage fathom it?

Zhenghe: Beyond the Six Directions, the sage preserves without discussing—how could he fathom each and every thing? Is it not that which arises without reliance? Who knows why it is so? Allowing things to be spontaneous, leaving them to themselves—this is to delight in fathoming things, not the sage’s way.

Fan said: What Great Yu spoke of remained within the bounds of limits and ends; what Xia Ge spoke of reached beyond the boundless and endless. Thus one says: only the sage can fathom their Way; another says: this is not what the sage can fathom. In general, whether beyond things or within them, where do we see their difference?

When Yu governed the waters and lands, he became lost and strayed, leading an entire state astray. Those who wander beyond the farthest bounds are beyond the reach of deliberate thought, hence his words were those of one lost and mistaken. North of the Northern Sea, how many thousands of *li* distant from the Central States? "Distant" means "remote." That land is called Zhongbei.

Lu said: Zhongbei signifies the utmost depths of the obscure and subtle, a realm of profound stillness. Its boundaries and limits are unknown. No wind or rain, frost or dew arise there; no birds or beasts, insects or fish, grasses or trees are born.

The four directions are all level, encircled by towering heights—these are the layered ridges of mountains. Lu says: In this realm of profound stillness, without boundaries or limits, where wind and rain, birds and beasts, and all moving things cannot reach, there is only perfect flatness. The lofty land is but a hindrance of form and vessel.

At the center of the land stands a mountain named Huling, shaped like a *dan* (an earthen jar). Its peak has an opening, resembling a ring, called Zixue. Water gushes forth from it, named the Divine Spring—*fèn* refers to a mountain’s summit spring. Its fragrance surpasses orchid and pepper, its taste surpasses unstrained wine. Lu says: The mountain symbolizes the mind, while water serves as the function of wisdom, for it is the Dao’s divine spring that wells forth.

A single source divides into four channels, flowing down the mountain. The watercourses on the mountain are called *lie*. They nourish the entire land, reaching everywhere without exception. Lu says: They flow through the four limbs, permeating the hundred parts of the body, thus encompassing all forms and vessels.

The earth’s *qi* is harmonious, free from plague or pestilence. The people’s nature is gentle and yielding, following things without contention; their hearts are soft and their bones weak, neither arrogant nor envious. Young and old dwell together without rulers or subjects; men and women mingle freely without matchmakers or betrothals. They dwell along the water without plowing or sowing. The earth’s *qi* is warm and agreeable, so they know neither knowledge nor clothing. They live a hundred years without premature death or illness. Their people multiply without count, knowing only joy and happiness, free from aging, sorrow, or suffering. Lu says: The hundred bones and nine orifices respond to affairs as they are used.

They neither contend nor compete, nor boast or vaunt; embracing both yin and yang, they move with the turning of the seasons.

The Dao there is utterly yielding—no clothing, no food—neither aging nor death pursues them, neither short nor long life constrains them. The highest adept who diligently seeks it will reach that land.

Their custom delights in sound: hand in hand they take turns singing, their voices ceaseless all day. When hungry or weary, they drink the divine spring; their strength and will remain harmonious and peaceful. If they overindulge, they become intoxicated, and only after ten days do they awaken. Bathing in the divine spring, their skin glows with luster, their fragrance lingering for ten days.

Lu says: Humans live by *qi*, hence their love of sound. Their breath flows in and out without cease, thus their voices never falter. Drinking and eating with true wisdom, free from stray thoughts, they become intoxicated. When thoughts arise, they immerse themselves again, hence their radiant fragrance.

*Zhenghe* says: This land is one of delusion, showing it is not a realm the sage can fathom. Without knowing the boundaries that align and limit, there are depths within the six directions that cannot be exhausted. The *Book of Changes* says: "What is unfathomable in yin and yang is called the divine."

In the heavens above, there is no wind, rain, frost, or dew; on the earth below, there are no birds, beasts, grasses, or trees. The people drink the divine spring and bathe in it, thereby sating hunger and weariness, nourishing their skin, preserving life, and fulfilling their years. This is called the divine spring—it is what makes them unfathomable.

King Mu of Zhou traveled north and passed through this land, remaining for three years before remembering to return. Once back in the Zhou court, he longed for that country and was lost in melancholy.

They consumed no wine or meat and summoned no concubines or attendants, and only after several months did they recover. Lu said: King Mu of Zhou had indeed once reached that land, yet could not remain there permanently, hence it is said they "recovered" thereafter. Fan said: When the House of Si regulated the waters and lands, their influence extended far, yet there were remote corners and foreign realms beyond the reach of human footsteps. Thus, this is spoken in allegory of delusion.

North of the northern sea, beyond countless thousands of miles from the Central States, its distance from mankind is vast indeed. The land is called Zhongbei, for it is the place where all return to the root and restore their mandate. Without knowing the boundaries that align and limit, one wanders through an expanse of boundless wilderness. Without wind, rain, frost, or dew, the qi of yin and yang do not intermingle. Without the birth of birds, beasts, insects, fish, grasses, or trees, the myriad scattered kinds do not flourish. The land is level in all four directions, flat and never sloping. Encircling it are towering ridges, so high they cannot be surmounted.

Within the land stands a mountain, and at its summit lies an opening from which water gushes forth, called the divine spring—this is what the tradition refers to as "the great outflow beneath the tail." A single source divides into four streams, flowing down the mountainside, just as the tradition speaks of "water beneath the mountain." It circulates throughout the land, reaching every part without exception, thus illustrating its pervasive flow and beneficent nourishment of all things.

For this reason, the land knows no plagues or afflictions, and all beings are free from blemish or blight. Those who are gentle and compliant harbor no competitive impulses; those who are soft and tranquil exhibit no arrogance or envy. Without rulers or subjects, there is no hierarchy of elder and younger; without matchmakers or betrothals, there is no distinction between male and female. They dwell along the water, needing no grains to eat; the earth’s qi is warm and agreeable, requiring no silk or wadding for clothing. They dwell in the realm of benevolence and longevity, free from the suffering of premature death.

Drinking the divine spring, their strength and will become harmonious and balanced, as though their minds and wisdom were cleansed and opened. Bathing in the divine spring, their skin becomes sleek and lustrous, as though all dust and grime were washed away by goodness.

King Mu of Zhou traveled north and passed through this land, remaining for three years before remembering to return. Once back in the Zhou court, he longed for that country and was lost in melancholy.

After several months, he at last recovered. Such is the state of one who has yet to fully attain the Dao.

Guan Zhong urged Duke Huan of Qi to journey to Liaokou, intending to reach that land together, nearly succeeding in their quest. Lu observed: Guan Zhong could describe its location—hence the phrase "journey to Liaokou"—yet though they wished to go, they could not arrive, thus "nearly succeeding."

Xi Peng remonstrated: "My lord, why forsake the vastness of Qi, its teeming populace, its majestic mountains and rivers, its abundance of resources, its flourishing rites and virtues, its splendid robes and ornaments? Your court overflows with beauty and grace, your halls brim with loyalty and wisdom. With a single shout, a million soldiers stand ready; with a mere gesture, the feudal lords obey. Why then covet that land and abandon Qi’s altars of soil and grain to follow barbarian ways?"

Lu remarked: "The words of worldly gentlemen concern only populace, rites, robes, and sensual pleasures—these they deem noble and worthy of desire."

Duke Huan desisted and relayed Xi Peng’s words to Guan Zhong. Guan Zhong replied: "This is truly beyond Peng’s understanding. His knowledge extends only to Qi; how could he grasp the grandeur of that land?" He mocked Peng: "I fear that land is beyond comprehension. Why cling to Qi’s wealth? Why heed Peng’s words? That land cannot be attained in life—would I halt for Peng’s sake?"

Lu explained: "Xi Peng’s understanding does not reach so far. Guan Zhong said: From what I have heard, I only fear it may not match the legends. Thus he said, 'I fear it is unknowable.' If the reality matches the tales, and one could journey there, then why cling to worldly wealth and glory? Why regard rites, virtue, or loyalty?"

*Zhenghe* states: Where does the Dao not reside? It is present in nothingness and in being alike. Free the mind and release the form, and neither nothingness nor being is obscured; toil the body and rest the mind is to cling to being and abandon nothingness. The sage, in relation to the Dao, embraces both presence and absence, perceiving the subtle and the manifest together—thus nothing is beyond reach. Those who cling to a state’s riches are far from the Dao, yet those who forsake their diverse people to yearn for an unfathomable land—how can they attain the Dao? King Mu’s intent, Guan Zhong’s words, and Xi Peng’s admonitions—none of these grasped the truth.

Fan observed: "Xi Peng was a man who forgot both high and low, ashamed to fall short of the Yellow Emperor yet pitying those beneath him. In governing the state, he heard nothing amiss; in managing his household, he saw nothing awry. Did Guan Zhong truly entrust the state to such a man? Yet his wisdom sufficed only to govern Qi. Thus, in the end, he opposed the northern journey and remonstrated against it."

This discourse contrasts those like Yu the Great, who deeply attained the Dao, with King Mu, who reached its shores yet turned back, and Duke Huan, who merely glimpsed the Dao from afar without ever beholding it.

The people of the southern lands wear their hair loose and go bare; the northerners don fur-lined robes and wrap their heads in felt. The Central States’ people wear caps and robes. The nine lands sustain themselves variously—some by farming, some by trade, some by hunting, some by fishing. Like winter furs and summer linen, boats for water and carts for land, these arise naturally, shaped by nature itself.

The resources of each region emerge from its inherent capacity. The people of Wu and Yue use boats, while those of Yan and Shuo ride horses, adapting to the suitability of water or land without need of learning from the wise. Shen Dao said: "Those who control water build dikes and clear blockages—even among distant tribes, the method is uniform. They learn from water itself, not from Yu."

*Zhenghe* states: The southern climate is hot, so its people wear their hair loose and go bare, as in the *Book of Islands*’ "barbarians clad in grass." The northern climate is cold, so its people wrap their heads in felt and wear furs, as in the *Book of Islands*’ "barbarians clad in hides." The Central States, called Xia, are civilized and grand, donning caps and robes, practicing refined rites. The nine lands’ resources—farming, trade, hunting, fishing—along with winter furs and summer linen, are all prepared by the labor of a single body and the work of a hundred craftsmen. Yet all arise silently, shaped by nature, differing only in suitability.

Fan observed: "The southerners’ loose hair, the northerners’ felt wraps, the Central States’ caps and robes—these are differences of custom. The nine lands’ varied livelihoods—farming, trade, hunting, fishing—reflect differences in skill. Winter furs and summer linen suit different needs; boats and carts serve different purposes. Such things arise naturally and cannot be altered. Who knows their origins? They emerge silently, shaped by nature alone."

To the east of Yue lies the state of Zhexiou, where the eldest son is slaughtered and eaten at birth, deemed "fitting for younger brothers." When the grandfather dies, his widow is carried away and abandoned, for "a ghost’s wife cannot dwell with the living." South of Chu lies the land of the Yan people, where upon a relative’s death, the flesh is carved and discarded before the bones are buried—only then is one called a filial son. West of Qin lie the states of Yiqu and Kang, where the dead are heaped with firewood and burned. The rising smoke is called "ascending to distant realms," and only then is one deemed filial. Such practices are enforced as governance above and adopted as custom below, yet they are scarcely deemed strange. These accounts also appear in the *Mozi*.

Lu observed: "What the multitude deems right becomes correct; what the multitude practices becomes normal. Thus, the most correct and most common things go unquestioned. Those bound by custom are many—how could they know the ultimate principle?"

Zhenghe remarked: "East of Yue, south of Chu, west of Qin—where rites are absent above and learning is absent below—such acts are still enforced as governance and practiced as custom. These are perversions of human relations, unworthy of instruction."

Fan said: "East of Yue, south of Chu, west of Qin—all are distant, alien lands beyond mutual reach. Thus, what is enforced as governance above and practiced as custom below may differ in ways beyond comparison. Confucius, traveling east, encountered two children arguing and asked the reason. One child said: 'I believe the sun is nearer at dawn and farther at noon.' The other insisted the sun was farther at dawn and nearer at noon. The first child argued: 'At dawn, the sun is as large as a carriage canopy, but at noon, it is no bigger than a plate—is this not proof that what is far appears small, and what is near appears large?' The second child countered: 'At dawn, the sun is cool and refreshing, but at noon, it burns like boiling water—is this not proof that what is near is hot, and what is far is cool?' Confucius could not decide. The two children laughed: 'Who says you know so much?'

What lies beyond the six directions, the sage acknowledges but does not discuss. The two children’s mockery may not have missed this point—they may have been testing each other. Lu said: 'The sage’s life values the illumination of the Dao. When successful, they aid all under heaven; when obstructed, they cultivate themselves alone. Cultivating alone means nurturing the Dao to preserve authenticity; aiding all means establishing teachings to benefit others. If advancement does not fulfill the Dao and retreat does not benefit life, such one-sided debates are why the sage does not engage. Not deciding is not the same as not knowing. The world assumes that asking without knowing is ignorance, but the sage refrains from debating what is unprofitable. If every matter—whether reasonable or not—were debated, the sage’s labor would indeed be endless. Yet the children’s dispute can be clarified: at dawn, the sun’s light is not yet far-reaching, and people stand beyond its glow, seeing it as large. At noon, the light fills all things, and people stand within its brilliance, seeing its true form. It is like gazing at a candle’s flame from afar—it appears larger, but up close, the flame itself is small. Such is the principle of things—what is gained by debating it?'

Fan said: 'The sun rises in the east and sets in the westernmost reaches. In its roundness, it does not depart from form; in its singular circuit, it does not depart from measure. Once bound by form and measure, distance—near or far—can be calculated. Yet Confucius could not decide—was it truly beyond his ability? Or did he simply acknowledge it without discussion? Balance is the ultimate principle of all under heaven. When every thing and every affair is level and equal, the principle is fully realized. This applies even to material objects. "Connected" means attached—what is attached to implements must also be balanced. When hair is evenly drawn and evenly suspended, it snaps under the slightest weight due to imbalance. Yet if the hair is extremely fine and delicate but does not break, it is because of perfect balance. What snaps now does so because of imbalance—like a scale tipped by unequal weight. If balanced, how could it break? It would never snap. Some dismiss this, but those who grasp the principle know it to be so. There are those who understand this truth. The *Mozi* also records this idea.'

Lu said: 'The ultimate principle is such that all under heaven is naturally balanced, not requiring adjustment to be so. Material things are the same—when aligned with principle, they are naturally balanced. Like hair suspending a weight: though fine, it does not break. What breaks is unbalanced; what is balanced does not break. The world may deny this, but they do not know the necessity of principle.'

Zhenghe said: 'The alternation of yin and yang is called the Dao. The benevolent see it as benevolence; the wise see it as wisdom. The radiant light of the sun and moon appears differently to every eye. Judging by form, one assumes what is far is small and what is near is large; judging by qi, one assumes what is near is hot and what is far is cool. Yet the sun itself cannot be measured by near or far.'

Fan said: 'Hair lifting a thousand jun—this is the power of equal force. The principle aligns with this.' Zhan He..."

Jen Ho, a man of Chu, was renowned throughout the kingdom for his skill in angling. He used a single silkworm’s thread for his line, a needle’s barb for his hook, a stalk of thistle for his rod, and a split grain for his bait—yet drew forth fish as large as a cart. As the *Family Sayings* states: "The kun-fish is so vast it fills a carriage." In the swirling currents of a hundred-fathom abyss, his line never snapped, his hook never straightened, his rod never bent.

He adorned his bait with fragrance, dangled his delicate hook, sank it into the dark silt, and rode the churning waves. Following the water’s flow, he cast and retrieved, rising and falling with the current. Only when the fish had fully taken the bait did he strike, thus capturing it alive.

The King of Chu, hearing of this, found it extraordinary and summoned Jen Ho to ask the reason. Jen Ho replied: "I once heard my late master speak of Pu Juzǐ’s archery. Pu Juzǐ was a master archer of old. With a supple bow and fine cord, he rode the wind and loosed his arrow, bringing down two cormorants amid the azure clouds. His mind was single, his movements perfectly balanced. I took his method as my model and applied it to angling, studying for five years before mastering its principles.

When I stand by the river with rod in hand, my mind is free of distractions, fixed solely on the fish. Casting my line and sinking my hook, I feel no weight, and nothing can disturb my focus. The fish see my bait as mere drifting dust or foam, swallowing it without hesitation. Thus I am able to subdue the strong with the weak, and draw the heavy with the light.

If Your Majesty could govern the state with such skill, the world could be held in the palm of your hand—what further effort would be needed?"

The King of Chu said: "Well said." He approved of this analogy, using it to admonish his approach to governance.

Lu said: "When the sage orders the customs of the world, he must first examine the nature of all things and establish teachings to guide them. Then the people go about their daily lives unaware, effortlessly responding to his influence. It is like the supple bow and fine cord riding the wind, or the light hook and delicate bait moving with the waves—nothing can escape being mastered.

The Daoist nurtures life, preserves authenticity, and embraces all beings, drawing them naturally toward transformation. Thus, governing the world follows the same principle.

Zhenghe said: "From the age of penal codes and names onward, the great controls the small, the strong subdues the weak. The Dao manifests through weakness and humility, its subtlety and wonder lying in its profundity. Thus, nothing can escape it, and all things endure.

A hundred-fathom abyss may be measured in depth, the heights of azure clouds may be reached, yet the Dao encompasses and sustains them all. Those who embody the Dao hold fast to simplicity, and the myriad things submit of their own accord. The metaphor lies here: drawing a cart-sized fish with a fine line, or bringing down two cormorants amid the clouds with a weak bow. The sage’s governance of the world is no different.

Fan said: "The King of Chu, hearing of Jen Ho’s angling, understood how to govern the state. The Yellow Emperor, questioning a child herdsman, grasped how to rule the world—the principle is the same.

Gongyu of Lu and Qiying of Zhao both fell ill and sought treatment from Bian Que. After curing them, Bian Que said: 'Your former ailments, which arose externally and afflicted the organs, were easily remedied by medicine and stone. But now you have a congenital illness that has grown with your body. If I were to treat it now, what would you say?'

The two replied: 'We would first like to hear its symptoms.'"

Bian Que said to Gongyu: "Your will is strong but your qi is weak, so you excel in planning yet falter in decisiveness. *Will here refers to the mind’s intelligence; qi to one’s inherent nature. With abundant intelligence comes overthinking; with a weak nature comes indecision.* Qiying’s will is weak but his qi is strong, so he lacks forethought yet errs in recklessness. *With scant intelligence but a dominant nature, one acts boldly yet self-servingly.* If we were to exchange your hearts, you would both attain balance in virtue."

Thereupon, Bian Que gave the two men a toxic draft, rendering them unconscious for three days. He then split open their chests, extracted their hearts, swapped them, and replaced them. After administering a divine medicine, they awoke as before. The two took their leave and returned home.

Yet Gongyu entered Qiying’s house and claimed his wife and children, who did not recognize him. Likewise, Qiying entered Gongyu’s house and claimed his wife and children, who also did not recognize him. *As their hearts were exchanged, they followed their original memories, each returning to the other’s home. Since their forms were no longer their own, their families failed to recognize them.* The two households then brought suit against each other, seeking Bian Que’s judgment. Bian Que explained the cause, and the dispute was resolved.

This account is fantastical, rarely found in records. Yet during the Wei dynasty, Hua Tuo could split open abdomens, exchange viscera, and cleanse the five organs—proof that the world holds principles beyond human comprehension. One should not dismiss such matters out of hand; better to preserve them without debate.

Lu said: "The body is an insensate thing; the spirit is the sentient master. Abiding in one’s true nature preserves wholeness and aligns with the Dao, while clinging to form loses one’s nature and succumbs to emotion. The common folk see only the body’s attachments, unaware that the spirit governs. This tale of exchanged hearts and estranged families illustrates that the heart rules the emotions, while the body is truly insensate. Thus, the Daoist values the cultivation of spirit."

Zhenghe said: "Illness arises when form loses balance; so too when nature loses balance. Physical illness is treated with medicine and stone; illness of nature requires Daoist arts. The will commands qi, and qi fills the body. Will governs planning; qi governs decisiveness. When balance is lost, strength and weakness invert. Thus, one with a strong will and weak qi excels in planning but lacks decisiveness, while one with a weak will and strong qi lacks forethought but errs in recklessness. The heart is the master of the five senses. Exchanging hearts adjusts their strengths to harmony—this is the marvel of the illness-free, the profound work of medicine. The sage governs the world through the mysterious Dao, unifying balance and imbalance—how is this different?"

Fan said: "Mencius said: 'The will arrives first; qi follows.' He also said: 'Hold fast to your will; do not coerce your qi.' The will is the mind’s direction; qi is the will’s instrument. Any imbalance disrupts nature’s harmony, creating illness. Thus, the will is like a general; qi like the troops. A strong will with weak qi leaves a general without an army—hesitant and indecisive. A weak will with strong qi leaves troops without direction—reckless and self-serving. Without medicine to transform the heart and alter its thoughts, how could such illness be cured? I have observed that Hua Tuo of the Wei dynasty possessed the arts of splitting abdomens, exchanging viscera, and cleansing the five organs—thus this account is hardly strange.

When Hu Ba played the zither, birds danced and fish leapt. *Hu Ba was a master zitherist of old.* Zheng Shiwen, hearing of him—*Shiwen was a music master of Zheng*—abandoned his home to study under Master Xiang. *Master Xiang was also a renowned zitherist of old.* For three years, he placed his fingers on the strings and plucked, yet could not form a melody. *Adjusting his fingers and tuning the strings, he spent three years without mastering a single piece.* Master Xiang said: 'You may return home.' *He despaired of teaching him.*

Shiwen set down his zither and sighed: 'It is not that my fingers cannot pluck the strings, nor that I cannot form a melody. My focus lies not in the strings; my aspiration not in sound. Only by forgetting the strings and sound can one fully realize their use. If the heart is not at ease within, nor the instrument responsive without, how dare I move my hands to play?' *When heart, hand, and instrument harmonize without discord, then perfect music arises.*

Lu said: "People know how to train the body to produce sound, but do not realize that discerning sound and moving the body is the work of the spirit. If the heart does not respond to the instrument, the music, though formed, lacks precision. To exhaust the instrument’s potential and perfect the body’s artistry, one must achieve spiritual resonance and inner attainment."

Master Xiang granted him a brief respite to observe his progress. Before long, Shiwen returned. Master Xiang asked: 'How is your zither playing?' Shiwen replied: 'I have grasped it. Please allow me to demonstrate.' *Lu said: When the heart is attuned and the instrument responsive, one may then practice sound to commune with the spirit.*

Thus, in spring, he struck the *shang* string to summon *Nanlü*—*Shang, the metal tone, belongs to autumn; Nanlü is the eighth-month pitch.* A cool breeze suddenly arose, and plants bore fruit. *He captured autumn’s essence, bringing maturity.* In autumn, he struck the *jiao* string to arouse *Jiazhong*—*Jiao, the wood tone, belongs to spring; Jiazhong is the second-month pitch.* A warm breeze gently swirled, and plants flourished. *He captured spring’s essence, bringing vitality.*

In summer, he struck the *yu* string to summon *Huangzhong*—*Yu, the water tone, belongs to winter; Huangzhong is the eleventh-month pitch.* Frost and snow descended together, and rivers and ponds froze solid. *He captured winter’s essence, bringing congealed yin and frozen waters.* In winter, he struck the *zhi* string to arouse *Ruibin*—*Zhi, the fire tone, belongs to summer; Ruibin is the fifth-month pitch.* The sun’s rays blazed fiercely, and hard ice instantly melted. *He captured summer’s essence, bringing dissolution.*"

At this moment of playing the zither, there was no means to suddenly alter the four seasons. Yet by demonstrating the effect of one season, the other three could be inferred—this was merely to convey the marvel of the responses evoked. As the performance drew to a close, he commanded the *gong* string to harmonize all four, and then the *jing* wind soared, auspicious clouds drifted, sweet dew descended, and sweet springs welled forth. Such perfect harmony moved Master Xiang to press his hand to his heart and exclaim in exaltation: "How subtle is your playing! Even the *Qingjiao* of Master Kuang—when he performed it for Duke Ping of Jin, the first playing summoned white clouds from the northwest; the second brought gale winds and rain; the third tore the curtains, shattered the ritual vessels, sent roof tiles flying, and sent all attendants fleeing in panic. Duke Ping cowered in terror, and Jin suffered a great drought, its lands scorched for three years—could only produce sounds that brought either fortune or misfortune. Or like Zou Yan’s blowing of the pitch-pipes—there was a land in the north, fertile yet frigid, where the five grains would not grow. Zou Yan blew the pipes to warm it, and suddenly millet and sorghum flourished. Nothing surpasses this; they would but clutch their zithers and flutes to follow in your wake."

Lu said: "When one acts in accord with one’s inherent nature, the responses of yin and yang’s numerology and the sequence of the four seasons arise spontaneously. Even water and fire cannot drown or burn one—how much less the qi of wind, rain, cold, or heat? Thus the *Book of Changes* says: 'Act before Heaven, and Heaven does not oppose; how much more so for man? How much more for ghosts and spirits?' This is what it means. Such transformations through sound and pitch do not depend on the four seasons."

*Zhenghe* states: "The supreme music regulates the four seasons, harmonizes the myriad things, and as the four seasons succeed one another, the myriad things follow their cycles of life. In its subtlety, the two kinds [of sound and qi] merge, the two interact. What some call death, others call life; what some call substance, others call flourishing. Moving or lingering, scattering or shifting, it does not adhere to ordinary sounds. Thus, when sound stirs qi, it responds to the stirring—this is the inevitable principle. In spring, strike the *shang* string to summon *Nanlü*, and autumn’s qi answers; in autumn, strike the *jiao* string to arouse *Jiazhong*, and spring’s qi answers; in summer, strike the *yu* string to summon *Huangzhong*, and winter’s qi answers; in winter, strike the *zhi* string to arouse *Ruibin*, and summer’s qi answers. Thus, music’s influence can even alter the sequence of the four seasons and seize the mechanism of creation—its marvel is such. When all are harmonized together, they intermingle to form perfect accord, and so the *jing* wind soars, auspicious clouds drift, sweet dew descends, and sweet springs well forth. For the qi of all under Heaven is one. When sound aligns with qi, the response is natural. Birds dance and fish leap—these are but its incidental effects."

Fan said: "The zither is the gentleman’s constant companion in music. Its construction is refined, its meaning profound; its sound subtle, its power great. It begins by guarding the heart to restrain oneself and ends by transcending the instrument to attain enlightenment. Thus, a sound produced in the space of a single step resonates to the heights of the Great Purity. Of old, the Yellow Emperor played the *Qingjiao* zither to assemble the ghosts and spirits, and phoenixes blotted out the sun. Yao played the zither, and the celestial spirits descended; Shun sang the *Nanfeng*, and all under Heaven was transformed. How could this be mere bird dances and fish leaps like those of Hu Ba? Yet the zither’s construction rests on silk strings stretched over wood—its sound emerges thus. For fire finds voice through wood, and spirit responds to form in the world, harboring ineffable marvels within. If one clings rigidly to the nearness of form and instrument or is blinded by the superficiality of sound, how can one grasp the Dao of music?

When Master Wen first studied, his fingers pressed the bridges and hooked the strings, but in the end, he set the zither aside and sighed in realization. What he preserved was not in the strings; what he aspired to was not in the sound. He may be said to have attained this. Thus, striking the strings to summon the pitches transforms the four qi. When he commanded the *gong* string to harmonize all four, the *jing* wind soared, auspicious clouds drifted, sweet dew descended, and sweet springs welled forth—so harmonious was the sound that it brought forth such auspicious signs. Compared to this, Master Kuang’s *Qingjiao*, which summoned wind and rain when played, or Zou Yan’s pitch-pipes, which warmed the land so millet and sorghum flourished—how could they surpass it? It is fitting that Master Xiang pressed his hand to his heart and danced in exaltation.

Xue Tan studied singing under Qin Qing—both were renowned singers of the state of Qin. Before exhausting Qin Qing’s art, Xue Tan believed he had mastered it and took his leave. Qin Qing did not stop him but saw him off at the crossroads in the suburbs. There, he beat time and sang a mournful song; its sound shook the trees, its resonance halted the passing clouds. Xue Tan then apologized and begged to return, never daring to speak of leaving again. Qin Qing turned to his friends and said: 'Long ago, Han E, a skilled singer of Han, traveled east to Qi. Running out of provisions, she passed through Yongmen, where she sold her songs for food. After she left, her lingering notes coiled around the beams, lingering for three days without ceasing. The people thought she had not gone. When she passed an inn, the innkeeper insulted her. Han E then sang a long, mournful lament. The old and young of the village grieved and wept, facing one another in sorrow, and for three days they ate nothing. They hastily pursued her. Han E returned and sang a long, joyful song. The old and young of the village leapt and danced for joy, unable to restrain themselves, forgetting their former sorrow. They then richly rewarded her and sent her on her way. Thus, the people of Yongmen to this day excel in singing and weeping, emulating Han E’s lingering tones. In the time of the Six States, there was a man of Yongmen named Zhou, skilled in the zither and also in weeping, who wept before Lord Mengchang.'"

Lu said: "The six senses, when employed, can all achieve communion. When communion is achieved, marvelous responses arise without limit—not merely through the mind’s perception. Thus, Gongyu of Lu spoke directly of the heart’s function, while those like Hu Ba attained communion through sound."

*Zhenghe* states: "When sound shakes the trees and halts the passing clouds, this is but the strength of its emission and the distance of its lingering resonance. When long, drawn-out songs and weeping move the old and young of a village to joy or sorrow, stirring their spirits and altering their thoughts—this is the sublime mastery of singing."

Fan said: "When Qin Qing struck his rhythm, his sound shook the trees and halted the passing clouds; when Han E’s drawn-out song transformed the old and young of a village—this arose from sincerity within that evoked response without, and could not but be so. How much more, then, when one’s own sincerity is expressed?

Boya was skilled in playing the zither, while Zhong Ziqi was skilled in listening. When Boya played, his intent was to ascend a high mountain. Zhong Ziqi said: 'Excellent! Lofty as Mount Tai!' His intent turned to flowing water. Zhong Ziqi said: 'Excellent! Vast as the Yangtze and Yellow River!' Whatever Boya conceived, Zhong Ziqi invariably grasped.

Once, Boya traveled to the northern slopes of Mount Tai. Suddenly, a violent storm arose, and he took shelter beneath a cliff. His heart filled with sorrow, he took up his zither and played. First came the melody of ceaseless rain, then the sound of crumbling mountains. With every piece he performed, Zhong Ziqi exhausted its essence.

Boya then set aside his zither and sighed: 'Excellent! Excellent! How well you listen! Your thoughts and imaginings are as my own heart—our minds align in secret, no different from mine. Where could I hide my sound from you?' Once a note was struck, Zhong Ziqi had already grasped his intent, leaving no place to conceal its meaning.

Lu said: "The formation of sound follows what moves it; the heart’s arising is accompanied by sound. Thus, the five senses all commune, wholly transmitted by the perceiving mind. For one skilled in listening, even a cough reveals its meaning—how much more when sound is shaped by pattern? How could it not be discerned?"

*Zhenghe* states: "What resides in the heart is intent. All sound arises from the human heart. Moved by things, it then stirs, and the heart’s direction may be examined. Yet if listening stops at the ear, one knows sound but not music; if listening unites with the heart, one examines music to know its meaning. This is how Ziqi’s listening attained the subtlest depths."

Fan said: "The Dao cannot be heard; what is heard is not it. Yet beyond sound, there are indeed those who reach the realm of listening. What Boya played and Ziqi heard never departed from the realm of form and sound—high mountains and flowing waters—yet with every performance, its essence was exhausted. What sound could possibly escape?"

Yao asked:

When King Mu of Zhou made his western tour of inspection, he crossed the Kunlun Mountains but did not reach Yan Mountain. On his return journey, before reaching the Central States, he encountered someone presenting a craftsman named Yanshi. Along the way, a certain state had offered this master of ingenuity. King Mu received him—*jian* here should be read as *jin*, "to present." The king asked, "What skills do you possess?"

Yanshi replied, "Your servant awaits only your command to demonstrate. However, I have already created something; I humbly request that Your Majesty first observe it."

The king said, "Come together on another day"—*ri* here meaning a separate day. "I shall view it with you."

Lu commented: "The marvel of spiritual application—how could it be limited to sound alone? Color, fragrance, and flavor all attain their principles. Thus, this chapter speaks of carving and shaping to the utmost subtlety."

The next day, Yanshi sought an audience with the king. The king received him and asked, "Who is the person accompanying you?"

He replied, "It is a singing performer I have created"—*chang* meaning an actor or entertainer.

King Mu gazed at it in astonishment: its gait, bowing, and posture were truly those of a human. When the skillful craftsman stroked its chin, it sang in perfect harmony; when he took its hand, it danced in time. With a thousand transformations and ten thousand changes, it followed intention as desired. The king took it for a real person and, together with Sheng Ji, his inner consort, observed it closely. *The Chronicle of King Mu* states: "Sheng Ji was King Mu’s beloved beauty."

As the performance neared its end, the performer winked at the king’s attending concubines on the left and right. The king grew furious and immediately sought to execute Yanshi.

Yanshi, greatly terrified, immediately disassembled the performer before the king to reveal its construction: all were joined from leather, wood, glue, lacquer, white, black, red, and blue pigments. The king examined it closely—within were liver, gallbladder, heart, lungs, spleen, kidneys, intestines, and stomach; without were sinews, bones, limbs, joints, skin, hair, teeth, and hair—all artificial yet perfectly complete. When reassembled, it appeared as it had at first sight, just as when it had first met the king.

The king tested it: when he removed its heart, its mouth could not speak; when he removed its liver, its eyes could not see; when he removed its kidneys, its feet could not walk. All these were controlled by mechanisms, and without the master mechanism, they could not govern one another—just as when a person’s five viscera are afflicted, the seven orifices and four limbs outwardly respond.

Lu said: "The inner liver, gallbladder, heart, and lungs enable the outer functions of sight, hearing, movement, and perception; when set in motion, they become living things. Without spirit, they cannot employ their five senses. Now, the creation of living things by the forces of nature—how is it different from what Yanshi has made? If devoid of spirit, they would be no more than plants and trees; but if spirit is present, what is so extraordinary about their movement? The wooden figure moves by Yanshi’s spirit, so it fittingly resembles a living being. The craft of spirit reaches its utmost—how far removed is it from transformation?"

King Mu, now pleased, sighed and said: "Can human skill truly match the work of creation?" He ordered his secondary chariot to carry it back.

In recent times, some have claimed that human intelligence arises from mechanisms—what does this mean? The work of creation is supremely subtle, thus all things are nurtured, moving without fixed form. Human artistry is crude, merely shaping form from inert matter. Yet when skill reaches its utmost, it nearly approaches creation. Perhaps such claims arise from this idea, though this text does not endorse them. To clarify this point, one should consider that supreme skill transcends conventional limits, so examples of extreme craft are given to dispel common delusions—not to suggest that things lack a spiritual essence, which would be a grave error.

Ban Shu’s cloud-ladder and Mo Di’s flying kite were each deemed the pinnacle of their skill. Ban Shu’s cloud-ladder could ascend the void for assault; Mozi’s wooden kite flew for three days without landing. When disciples Dongmen Jia and Qin Guli heard of Yanshi’s craft, they reported it to the two masters, who thereafter never dared speak of their own arts and constantly held compass and square. "Constantly held compass and square" means they dared not boast of their skills.

Lu said: "Yanshi’s subtlety unites with the spirit of creation; Ban Shu and his like merely exhaust mechanical ingenuity. This shows that supreme skill cannot be confined to mere tools."

*Zhenghe* states: "Borrowing from strange materials, entrusting to a shared form, lodging within a hundred bones, imitating ears and eyes—its sight, hearing, speech, appearance, gait, bowing, and posture seemed as if naturally its own, unaware of its own actions. Such exquisite skill, such profound workmanship, uniquely achieves its own nature, possessing human form—how could it merely approximate reality? Yanshi’s creation truly matches the work of creation.

Yet living beings are but temporary assemblages. The Dao grants them appearance, Heaven grants them form—how is this different? Fang said: "Kunlun is the mound of tranquility; Yan Mountain is where the sun sets. To pass Kunlun without reaching Yan Mountain is to seek stillness while still in motion, unable to depart brightness for darkness—thus one turns back. Yanshi’s performer equals the work of creation: when its chin is stroked, it sings in harmony like the spontaneous music of nature; when its hand is taken, it dances in rhythm like the self-moving mechanism of Heaven. With a thousand transformations and ten thousand changes, it follows intention as desired. King Mu gazed in astonishment, truly believing it a real person—never realizing it was merely an assemblage of leather, wood, glue, lacquer, white, black, red, and blue pigments. Such is the advancement of skill—how much more so for one who embodies the Dao and comprehends the workings of things?"

Gan Ying, an ancient master archer, drew his bow and beasts crouched, birds descended. His arrows never missed, and birds and beasts dared not flee. *The Strategies of the Warring States* records: "Geng Lei shot without drawing the bow, yet birds fell." His disciple, Fei Wei, studied archery under Gan Ying and surpassed his teacher. Ji Chang, in turn, studied under Fei Wei.

Fei Wei said: "First learn not to blink, then we may speak of archery." Ji Chang returned home, lay beneath his wife’s loom, and fixed his gaze on the heddle rod. After two years, even if a cone’s tip touched his eyelashes, he did not blink. He reported this to Fei Wei, who said: "Not yet."

Lu said: "Drawing an empty bow to bring down birds is the subtlety of skill; surpassing one’s teacher is the penetration of spirit. Subtlety lies in practice, spirit in precision. Learning not to blink is the utmost precision; fixing the gaze without movement is the settling of spirit. Yet if settled but not applied, it is still insufficient—you must learn to see."

Lu said: "This means using unblinking eyes to see." "See the small as large, the faint as clear—then tell me." Lu said: "To see clearly is to perceive the small as large."

Chang suspended a louse from the window with a yak’s hair and faced south to observe it. Within ten days, it gradually grew larger; after three years, it appeared as big as a cartwheel. Viewing other things, they all seemed like mountains. Seeing the louse as a wheel, other things proportionally enlarged. Then, with a bow of Yan horn and arrows of northern reeds, he shot through the louse’s heart without breaking the suspension—demonstrating the marvel of his hand’s skill.

He reported this to Fei Wei. Fei Wei leapt up, clapped his chest, and exclaimed: "You have grasped it!" Lu said: "To see the small as large—piercing it is no longer difficult."

Once Ji Chang had mastered all of Fei Wei’s techniques, he calculated that in all the world, only one man could rival him, and so he plotted to kill Fei Wei. Lu said: "To crush his ability to compare, one must surpass the master’s method. To erase Fei Wei’s name does not mean severing his life."

They met in the wild, and the two exchanged shots. Midway, their arrowheads clashed and fell to the ground without stirring a speck of dust. Fei Wei’s arrows were exhausted first. *Exhausted* means depleted. Ji Chang loosed one final arrow, and as it flew, Fei Wei intercepted it with the tip of a thorn, not missing by a hair. Lu said: "Two arrows followed the same path, meeting as their momentum faded—thus they fell to the earth without raising dust, so minute was the difference. To deflect with a thorn without error is the height of precision."

Thereupon, the two wept, cast aside their bows, and bowed to each other on the road, pledging to become father and son. They cut their arms and swore an oath never to reveal their art to others, guarding its secrecy. This chapter’s principles are already detailed in the *Zhongni* section. Lu said: "This is what is meant by spiritual communion and mutual understanding—it transcends the art of archery. Thus they cast aside their bows and swore, for only then could the profound connection be transmitted, and so they could not pass on the technique."

*Zhenghe* states: "Only when the inner is settled can the outer respond. Only when the minute is scrutinized can what is manifest be grasped without omission." Bohun Maoren said to Liezi: "You still harbor the intent of a squinting eye within—this is perilous." He referred to the inability to settle the inner. Ji Chang first learned not to blink, training his eyes to follow the suspended hair for this reason.

*Zhuangzi* says: "One who views the great as small lacks clarity." This refers to the inability to scrutinize the minute. Chang, gazing at the louse suspended by yak hair until it gradually grew large, did so for this reason. Truly, those who do not comprehend this yet excel in archery are rare.

Fan said: "When shooting at a hundred paces, the arrow’s arrival depends on your strength, but its hitting the mark does not. Thus, those who teach archery must focus on the draw, and learners must likewise focus on the draw. Is this not because what lies within the division of strength can be learned, while what lies beyond it cannot be forced?" Fei Wei studied archery under Gan Ying and surpassed his master, for he had naturally attained the innate essence of the art.

In the human body, the heart governs the inner, while the eyes lead in engaging the external. Thus, spirit unites with the heart, and its mechanism is ever present in the eyes. When Ji Chang studied under Fei Wei, he was first instructed to learn not to blink, so that his spirit might be whole within, and he would not be daunted when confronting things. Yet merely not blinking was not the ultimate attainment. Among the ancients who cultivated courage, there were those who practiced not averting their gaze. But to see the small as large, the faint as clear, and to perceive a louse on the eyelash as towering Mount Song—this approaches the realm of gazing with tranquil spirit. Thus, he could draw the Yan horn bow, loose the northern reed arrow, and pierce the suspended louse, achieving the "shooting that does not shoot," with mastery residing within himself.

Yet in time, having perfected the skill of piercing willow leaves, he bent the bow of Hou Yi—how vainly he relied on strength and strove for supremacy in technique! Thus, at first they met in the wild and exchanged shots, their arrowheads clashing; later, they bowed on the road, cast aside their bows, and cut their arms to swear an oath. If even the humble art of archery must not be revealed to others, how much more so for one who embodies the Dao and advances into the untransmittable mystery?

Zaofu’s master was Taidou Shi, mentioned in various miscellaneous records. When Zaofu first began learning charioteering, he observed the rites with great humility. Taidou taught him nothing for three years. Zaofu’s observance of the rites grew even more reverent, and then Taidou said: "An ancient poem states: *The son of a good bowmaker must first learn to weave baskets; the son of a good smith must first learn to sew fur.* Baskets and furs require flexibility and patching before they become tools. For bowmakers and smiths, harmonizing sinew and horn or blending metal and iron is the same. Thus, the learner must first master what is easy before achieving what is difficult—this is the lesson."

Lu said: "The basket is the tool for making bows; the fur is the tool for fanning the forge. Laozi likened it to a bellows—today’s leather bag. The one uses a bow-bending frame, the other a fire-fanning bag; though not bows or smelting, bowmaking and smithing rely on them. Now observe my movements." *Movements* means actions. "Move as I do, and then you may hold the six reins and drive the six horses."

Zaofu said: "I will follow your command." Taidou then set up wooden planks as a path, just wide enough for his feet. Having secured his footing, he measured steps and placed them at intervals matching his stride. He walked upon them, running back and forth without stumbling. Zaofu learned this, and in three days mastered its skill.

Taidou sighed: "How quick you are! You grasped it swiftly." *Quick* means fast; *swift* means rapid. "All charioteering is like this." Lu said: "Setting up wood like feet, arranging them like steps. *Zhuangzi* says: *Beyond the space for one’s feet, all earth is removed—those who cannot walk it are unsettled.* If driving horses is like guiding one’s feet, then it is sublime."

"Previously, your movements were guided by your feet and responded to by your heart. Extending this to charioteering, you harmonize the reins and bit, adjust the tension in the lips and mouth, align the measure within your chest, and grasp the rhythm in your hands. Within, you attain the center of your heart; without, you unite with the horse’s intent. Thus, you can advance and retreat along the line, turn and curve within the compass, take the road to distant places, and still have strength to spare—truly mastering the art."

Perceiving it in the bit, responding with the reins; perceiving it in the reins, responding with the hands; perceiving it in the hands, responding with the heart. Then one need not rely on the eyes to see, nor use the whip to drive. With a tranquil heart and upright body, the six reins remain untangled, and the twenty-four hooves land without error. In turning or advancing, retreating or curving, all align with the rhythm, harmonizing with the chime of the *heluan* bells. Only then may the wheels leave no excess track beyond their path, the hooves tread no ground beyond their place. Unaware of the peril of valleys or the levelness of plains, one regards them as one. My art is exhausted—you must grasp this.

What one treads is merely the space for one’s feet. Beyond the feet, there is no excess, yet others dare not step there—this is because the heart is not at ease, the body not tranquil. When the heart is at ease and the body tranquil, advance and retreat follow constant measure, speed and slowness adhere to fixed degree. If one attains its subtlety, this is not only applied to the self but may be extended to things. Though the six reins be tangled and the horse’s hooves numerous, with proper regulation, disorder is no concern. Thus, beyond the wheels, one does not rely on useless tracks; beyond the hooves, one does not depend on wasted ground. Can this not be so?

Lu said: *Zhuangzi*’s butcher explains: "Between the bones there is space; the blade of the knife has no thickness. With no thickness entering where there is space, how vast the room for movement!" This speaks of the principle—abundant leisure. Not regarding the ground beyond one’s feet keeps the mind focused; with focused intent, the feet move as the heart wills. If this is applied to the reins and bit, exchanged for a spirited steed, where tracks align with steps, what peril could there be?

People of the world seek the branches yet know not the root. Those who recognize truth must seek the root before applying it. Thus, even the minor arts of archery and charioteering must accord with the Dao.

**On Governance:** Without an inner master, there is no stillness; without outer correctness, there is no movement—this is the way of nature and practice mutually completing each other. Thus, governing the Dao begins with practice, and when the Dao is attained, it is perfected in nature. The son of a skilled archer first learns to weave baskets; the son of a master smith first learns to sew furs—this illustrates the importance of practice.

Setting wood as a path, just enough to hold the feet, placing them step by step, treading and walking—this is how one practices. For setting wood as a path, just enough for the feet, when extended to charioteering, the wheels leave no excess track, the hooves tread no wasted ground. Placing them step by step, treading and walking, when extended to charioteering, the peril of valleys and the levelness of plains are seen as one. Harmonizing the reins and bit, adjusting the tension in the lips and mouth—this is what is meant by "externally uniting with the horse’s intent." Aligning the measure within the chest, grasping the rhythm in the hands—this is what is meant by "internally attaining the center of the heart." Advancing and retreating along the line, turning and curving within the compass—this describes the mastery of driving. Taking the road to distant places with strength to spare—this describes its smoothness.

The reins connect to the bit, so perceiving it in the hands responds to the reins; the hands hold the reins, so perceiving it in the reins responds to the hands; the heart moves the hands, so perceiving it in the hands responds to the heart. To the point of not relying on the eyes to see or the whip to drive, where turning and advancing, retreating and curving all align with the rhythm—this is as if by nature’s spontaneity, unaware of one’s own doing. This is called skillful driving.

Fan said: When Jichang learned archery from Fei Wei, Fei Wei did not teach him archery but first instructed him to learn not to blink. When Zaofu learned charioteering from Taidou, Taidou did not teach him driving but first told him to observe his intent. This is drawing the bow without releasing, opening the door without leading through—allowing deep mastery of self-attained subtlety. Even in the minor arts of archery and charioteering, it is so—how much more for the Dao, which can be transmitted but not received? Thus, what is shown here is merely the form to awaken understanding; what is observed there is seen with the eyes yet retained in the mind. This is called "self-attainment"—how profound must its attainment be?

Observing Taidou’s charioteering: at first, he harmonized the reins and bit, adjusted the tension in the lips and mouth, aligned the measure within his chest, and grasped the rhythm in his hands. Internally attaining the center of his heart, externally uniting with the horse’s intent, he could advance and retreat along the line, turn and curve within the compass, take the road to distant places with strength to spare. This was within the bounds of method, never daring to transgress—such was his discipline.

As time passed, perceiving it in the bit, responding with the reins; perceiving it in the reins, responding with the hands; perceiving it in the hands, responding with the heart. No need to rely on the eyes to see or the whip to drive. With a tranquil heart and upright body, the six reins remained untangled, the twenty-four hooves landed without error, and in turning or advancing, retreating or curving, all aligned with the rhythm. This transcended method, effortlessly hitting the mark—such was his mastery.

To summarize in the end: beyond the wheels, no excess track; beyond the hooves, no wasted ground. Unaware of the peril of valleys or the levelness of plains, regarding them as one. Then there is no place that is not movement, no movement that is not the Dao. Whether level or sloped, the Dao unifies all—where could there be no leisure? In driving, one has reached this point—this is beyond mere skill.

Wei Heilu killed Qiu Bingzhang out of personal enmity. *Nixian* refers to private grudges. Lu said: Those who kill or harm out of personal enmity or indulge desires to destroy things are but common folk, not men of the Dao. Qiu Bingzhang’s son, Lai Dan, plotted to avenge his father’s death. Dan was fierce in spirit, exposed in form, ate by counting grains, and ran with the wind. Though enraged, he could not wield a weapon to exact revenge...

The man had courage and spirit, yet his body was frail and weak, unable to lift a weapon. Ashamed to borrow strength from others, he swore to slaughter Heilu with his bare hands and a sword. Heilu’s ferocity surpassed all others, his strength equal to a hundred men. His sinews, bones, flesh, and skin were not of mortal kind. He would stretch his neck to meet the blade, bare his chest to receive arrows—edges bent and points shattered, yet his body bore no mark. Relying on his natural strength, he regarded Lai Dan as but a fledgling chick.

Lai Dan’s friend Shen Ta said: “Your hatred for Heilu is extreme, and his contempt for you surpasses all bounds—what plan will you devise?” Lai Dan wept and replied: “I beg you to devise one for me.” Shen Ta said: “I have heard that Kong Zhou of Wei, a descendant of his lineage, obtained the treasured swords of the Yin Emperor. When a mere boy wielded one, he repelled the masses of three armies. Why not seek his aid?”

Lu said: The most refined objects of Heaven and Earth serve only to overawe the multitudes with their majesty. If their merit lay in cutting and severing, they would not be of the utmost refinement.

Lai Dan then journeyed to Wei, where he met Kong Zhou. Assuming the role of a servant and charioteer, he first offered his wife and children in service before stating his desire. Kong Zhou said: “I possess three swords—choose whichever you wish. None can kill a man, but first let me describe their forms.

The first is called *Han Guang* (Concealed Light): invisible when gazed upon, unfelt when wielded. Where it touches, all boundaries dissolve—it passes through things, yet they remain unaware.

The second is called *Cheng Ying* (Bearing Shadow): at the cusp of dawn’s first light or the dimming of dusk, when facing it, one perceives only a faint presence, its form unrecognizable. Where it touches, a faint whisper sounds—it passes through things, yet they suffer no harm.

The third is called *Xiao Lian* (Night Forged): by day, its shadow appears but its light does not; it shares the hue of sun and moon. By night, its light appears but its form does not. It illuminates the darkness. Where it touches, it severs with a *huo* sound—*huo* (the noise of splitting). It cuts and instantly mends, swift yet bloodless.

These three treasures have been passed down for thirteen generations, yet they have never been put to use, for they cannot harm living things. They remain sealed in their caskets, never once opened.”

Lai Dan said: “Even so, I must request the lowest of them.”

Kong Zhou then sent his wife and children away and fasted for seven days. During the quiet of dusk—*yan yin*, meaning the dimming of late evening—he knelt and presented the lowest of the swords. Lai Dan bowed twice and received it before departing. Since it could be grasped and seen, it was the lowest of the three.

Lu said: “The more precious the weapon, the deeper its harm to living things. The ultimate Dao and its utmost refinement inflict no injury upon them.”

Lai Dan then took the sword and followed Black Egg. At the time, Black Egg was drunk and sprawled beneath the window. Lai Dan struck him three times from neck to waist. Black Egg remained unaware. Believing Black Egg dead, Lai Dan hurried away. At the gate, he encountered Black Egg’s son and struck him three times as if thrusting into emptiness. Black Egg’s son merely laughed and said, “Why do you act so foolishly, waving at me three times?” Lai Dan, realizing the sword could not kill, sighed and returned home.

When Black Egg awoke, he raged at his wife: “You let me drink until I exposed myself! Now my throat aches and my waist is stiff.” His son said, “Just now, Lai Dan came and met me at the gate. He struck me three times, leaving my body sore and my limbs stiff. He must have cursed me!”

Lu said: “The man who has attained the utmost Dao harms nothing in the world, nor can the world’s harms injure him. Thus, venomous creatures do not sting him, fierce beasts do not seize him. Even the most refined of things cannot harm him. As *Laozi* says: *His spirit does not injure; therefore, he is sagely.* What is prized in man is his ability to remain unharmed by all things. Why is this so?

*Zhenghe*: The *Han Guang* (Containing Light) blends with brightness yet does not shine; the *Cheng Ying* (Bearing Shadow) abides in darkness yet does not shift. At the darkest hour, *Xiao Lian* (Night Forged) possesses tangible form, while *Han Guang* has none. Thus, it cannot be seen, its motion leaves no trace, and though it passes through things, they remain unaware. *Cheng Ying* exists in a state between presence and absence—though its form may not be recognized, its sound may sometimes be heard. *Xiao Lian*, however, is already something, for it has tangible form. Thus, by day, its shadow appears. Since it operates in darkness, by night, its light appears.

The Dao values non-action above all; existence between being and non-being is secondary, while confinement to form is lowest. Therefore, of the three swords, *Han Guang* is supreme, *Cheng Ying* comes next, and *Xiao Lian* is the lowest. Was the sword Lai Dan received the *Xiao Lian*?

Yet these three do not excel in cutting or severing—they are all divine swords. There exists in the world a constant path to victory: advance without obstruction, move without hindrance, and all under heaven will submit. How could it lie in mere striking and combat? Thus, *Zhuangzi* deemed the sword that severs necks and pierces lungs as the weapon of commoners. Alas, Lai Dan did not grasp this. Knowing the sword could not kill, he still deceived himself—how could this be the constant path to victory?

Fan said: Black Egg embodies the Dao’s return to utmost obscurity; Bing Zhang manifests the Dao’s brilliance in utmost clarity; Lai Dan, in turn, represents the hue of utmost yang. Thus, seeking vengeance for his father’s foe, he requested a sword from Wei Kong Zhou. One who possesses a sword from Yue keeps it sheathed and hidden, never daring to use it—such is its supreme value. The Dao’s utility is like this: advance without obstruction, lift without limit, press without descent, move without constraint. It parts floating clouds, severs earth’s foundations, and in its vastness, leaves ample room for the play of the blade.

Lai Dan received the sword from Kong Zhou yet used it only to avenge his father, striking Black Egg three times from neck to waist while he was drunk. In this, he differed not at all from those who sever necks above and pierce livers below—how could he have understood the way to wield it?

King Mu of Zhou launched a great campaign against the Western Rong, who presented him with the *Kun Wu* sword and fire-washed cloth. The sword measured a foot and a half, its finely wrought steel blade cutting jade as easily as mud. The fire-washed cloth, when washed, must be cast into fire: the cloth turns the color of flame, while dirt takes on the cloth’s hue. Removed from the fire and shaken, it gleams as white as snow. This is recorded in the *Book of Zhou*.

The Crown Prince believed no such things existed, dismissing the accounts as fabrications.

Xiao Shu said: The Crown Prince was obstinate in his self-assurance, obstinate in his denial of reason! This passage concludes the chapter, yet it recounts the jade-cutting sword and fire-washed cloth to demonstrate that all the preceding records are factual. If these two things are not false, then none are.

Lu said: If metal cannot cut jade, it is not because the blade is dull; if cloth cannot be washed in fire without burning, it is due to the object’s peculiar nature. Within heaven and earth, among the myriad things, some can be understood by reason, while others lie beyond its reach. Thus, though jade is hard, it has veins that can be carved; though a sword is iron, it has an edge that must sever. When an edge that must sever meets a thing that can be carved, failure to penetrate arises not from the blade’s lack of sharpness but from the absence of a principle of cutting. Yet if such a principle exists, why doubt it?

Moreover, among living things, their natures differ: some thrive by water and fire, others perish by them. The fire-mountain rat lives by fire, the wind-born beast lives by wind. Man depends on empty space to stand, fish depend on water to exist. Thus, the fire-washed flax is neither ramie nor hemp, yet its name as cloth is the same as those of the Central States. Its nature is akin to the fire-rat’s fur—what is so strange about this? To be obstinate in self-assurance is truly to lack understanding.

*Zhenghe*: The people of the world take what their ears and eyes perceive as real and dismiss what they cannot as nonexistent. Yet beyond the eight wilds, the limits cannot be fathomed—how can one assume that what ears and eyes cannot reach does not exist?

Fan said: The jade-cutting blade and fire-washed cloth exist by principle, yet those confined to the use of their ears and eyes insist such things do not exist. How could they ever comprehend the farthest reaches of knowledge?

This passage concludes the preceding records to clarify that all previously documented matters are grounded in the ultimate principle, not merely extravagant empty words meant to astonish the world.

*The Four Commentaries on the True Scripture of Perfect Virtue, Volume Fourteen* ends here.
*The Four Commentaries on the True Scripture of Perfect Virtue, Volume Fifteen*
Compiled by Gao Shouyuan, the Recluse of Harmonious Light

**On Power and Fate**
Zhang said: Fate is the inevitable term, the predestined portion. Though the matter has yet to manifest, the principle is already so. To claim that longevity or early death depends on nurture, or that success and failure hinge on knowledge and effort, is to be deluded by heavenly principle.

Lu said: Fate is the fixed portion, yet without power it cannot be fulfilled. Power is the force of advancement, yet without fate it cannot be realized. Those endowed with fate must rely on their power; those with power may or may not align with their fate. There are things power cannot attain—this is the absence of fate. Waiting for fate without striving is to await the hour; trusting fate but not power is a grave error, while trusting power but not fate is likewise misguided.

*Zhenghe*: Power may be wise or foolish, fate may be prosperous or impoverished; the disparity between gain and loss cannot be opposed. Unwavering in resolve is how one establishes fate.

Fan said: The ancients had a saying: "That which arrives without knowing how is fate." Another: "Not knowing why it is so, yet it is so—this is fate." Fate resides in Heaven; before form takes shape, its portion is already set—how could power ever control it? Only by knowing there is nothing to be done and accepting it can one attain this, and none but the virtuous can achieve such equanimity.

Power said to Fate: "What is your merit compared to mine?"
Fate replied: "What have you ever done for things that you dare compare yourself to me?"
Power said: "Longevity or early death, success or failure, nobility or baseness, wealth or poverty—these are all within my power."
Fate said: "Peng Zu’s wisdom did not surpass Yao and Shun’s, yet he lived eight hundred years; Yan Yuan’s talent did not exceed that of ordinary men, yet he died at thirty-two. Confucius’s virtue did not fall below that of the feudal lords, yet he was besieged in Chen and Cai; King Zhou of Yin’s conduct did not surpass the Three Benevolent Ones, yet he occupied the throne. Ji Zha held no rank in Wu, while Tian Heng usurped the state of Qi. Boyi and Shuqi starved on Mount Shouyang, while the Ji clan grew wealthier than Zhan Qin. If these were within your power, why grant longevity to one and early death to another, obstruct the sage while elevating the wicked, debase the worthy while ennobling the foolish, impoverish the good while enriching the evil?"
Power said: "If what you say is true, then I have no merit in things—yet why do things turn out as they do? Is this not your doing?"
Fate said: "Since it is called fate, how could there be one who controls it? I merely follow the straight and yield to the crooked. Whether long-lived or short-lived, successful or failed, noble or base, rich or poor—things are so without knowing why, and this is fate. How could it be controlled? How could I ever comprehend it? How could I ever comprehend it?"

This passage clarifies that all things have their fate, rendering wisdom and power ineffectual. The *Yang Zhu* chapter speaks of people indulging their desires, implying that control does not derive from fate. The principles differ and seem contradictory. Yet as order and chaos shift, love and hate contend, emotions and deceit multiply, and the times compel competition—who can truly know the reason? Thus, the sage preserves both without debate, for the great support of the moral order cannot entirely block the paths to corruption. Some rely on cunning and force to defy fate, like King Zhuang of Chu inquiring about the Zhou tripods or Wu Zhi causing chaos in Qi. Others feign natural integrity to pursue fame, like Boyi and Shuqi starving on Western Mountain or Zhong You being minced in Wei. Therefore, Liezi examines both extremes, allowing all things to seek their own middle way.
