If one attains this middle, then the cunningly active will not disrupt their inherent portion with power, nor will the proud and fame-seeking harm their lives through artificial restraint. The intent of this discourse lies herein. Alas, can readers fail to discern this?

Lu said: Fate is Heaven; power is man. Fate can fulfill, power can move—thus it is called "moving fate." *Zhuangzi* said: "Knowing the inevitable and accepting it as fate"—this is when power cannot move it. Confucius said: "At fifty, I understood Heaven’s mandate. Without understanding fate, one cannot be a noble person." Yet when traveling through the states and seeking office, he longed to hold the whip of a charioteer—this was not forgetting power.

*Zhenghe*: Fate lies in Heaven, power lies in man—power may achieve its ends. Yet what lies in Heaven is beyond human mastery, so the noble person does not rely on power but submits to fate. What lies in man is beyond Heaven’s opposition, so the noble person does not rely on fate. Longevity or early death, success or failure, nobility or baseness, wealth or poverty—these are what all things receive, for there is indeed one who ordains them. Because one dares not resist, one follows the straight. Because it cannot be halted, one yields to the crooked. It is neither within power’s ability to control nor fate’s power to oppose—it is simply natural. Why trouble oneself with excessive knowledge? Thus it is said: "How could I ever comprehend it?"

Fan said: Time does not halt, so years vary in length—Peng Zu and Yan Yuan’s differing lifespans illustrate this. Portions are not constant, so circumstances vary in gain and loss—Confucius and King Zhou’s differing fortunes show this. Ji Zha held no rank in Wu, while Tian Heng monopolized Qi—how vastly different their nobility! Yet from the Dao’s perspective, there is such a thing as equal nobility. Boyi and Shuqi starved on Mount Shouyang, while the Ji clan grew wealthier than Zhan Qin—how vastly different their wealth! Yet from the Dao’s perspective, there is such a thing as equal poverty and wealth.

The boundaries of nature’s allotment are scarcely knowable, so one may only push forward in straightness, allowing each to find its proper place without partiality; or yield in flexibility, allowing each to follow its course without resistance. Longevity and early death, success and failure, nobility and baseness, poverty and wealth—all are self-determined. Who, then, is the agent behind them? Only those who grasp the truth of fate do not strive in vain, knowing what lies beyond human control. Thus, life and death are of great consequence. If one cannot alter them, how much less can the gains and losses, fortunes and misfortunes that lie between disturb the heart?

Beigongzi said to Ximenzi: "You and I share the same era, yet you are esteemed while I am not. We come from the same clan, yet you are revered while I am slighted. Our appearances are alike, yet you are beloved while I am ignored. Our words are similar, yet yours are valued while mine are dismissed. Our conduct is the same, yet yours is trusted while mine is doubted. We both serve in office, yet you are exalted while I remain lowly. We both till the soil, yet you grow wealthy while I remain poor. We both trade, yet you profit while I do not. I wear coarse hemp, eat coarse grain and oysters, dwell in a thatched hut, and walk on foot. You wear embroidered silks, feast on fine millet and meat, live in a mansion with towering beams, and ride in a carriage drawn by four horses. At home, you wear a carefree expression as if casting me aside; at court, you bear a haughty demeanor as if scorning me. We no longer seek each other’s favor, nor do we travel together—this has been so for years. Do you truly believe your virtue surpasses mine?"

Ximenzi replied: "I have no way of knowing the truth of this. You pursue affairs and meet with failure, while I pursue them and meet with success—is this not proof of the disparity in our virtue?"

Lu said: "All I pursue meets with success, while all you pursue meets with failure. The thickness or thinness of virtue is plain to see. Yet you claim to be my equal—how shameless you are!"

Beigongzi had no reply. He departed in dismay, and on the road, he encountered Master Dongguo.

The master asked: "Where have you been that you return with such a halting gait and a look of deep shame?"

Beigongzi recounted what had transpired.

Master Dongguo said: "I shall relieve you of your shame. Let us go together to question Ximenzi. Ask him: ‘Why do you humiliate Beigongzi by probing so deeply?’ Speak plainly."

Ximenzi replied: "Beigongzi says we share the same era, clan, age, appearance, words, and conduct, yet our nobility and baseness, wealth and poverty differ. I told him: ‘I have no way of knowing the truth of this. You pursue affairs and meet with failure, while I pursue them and meet with success—is this not proof of the disparity in our virtue?’ And you claim to be my equal—how shameless you are!"

Master Dongguo said: "Your notion of ‘thickness’ and ‘thinness’ refers merely to differences in talent and virtue. My understanding of ‘thickness’ and ‘thinness’ differs. Beigongzi is thick in virtue but thin in fate; you are thick in fate but thin in virtue. Your success is not due to wisdom, nor is his failure due to folly. Both are matters of Heaven, not of man. This is simply the way of nature, not a result of human skill or clumsiness. Yet you take pride in your thick fate, while Beigongzi feels shame for his thick virtue—both fail to recognize the inherent principle of things."

Ximenzi said: "Master, you have silenced me. I dare not speak further." He heard the truth and submitted.

Lu said: "Ximenzi’s success in his pursuits is a matter of fate. Beigongzi’s failure in his pursuits is also a matter of fate. Those who do not understand fate bear an air of self-importance; those who understand it harbor no sorrow or shame. Success or failure is not a matter of wisdom or folly, nor of talent or virtue. Ximenzi dared not speak further because, knowing his success was a matter of fate, he dared not rely on his virtue."

After returning home, Beigongzi donned his coarse hempen robe, yet it warmed him like fox or marten fur. He ate his coarse beans, yet they tasted as fine as rice and millet. His thatched hut sheltered him like a grand hall, and his humble cart rode as smoothly as an ornate carriage. For the rest of his days, he remained at ease, unaware of honor or disgrace residing in others rather than himself. Once enlightened to the principle, the abundance or scarcity of external things no longer weighed upon his mind.

Lu said: "To know fate is to be free from sorrow and shame, and also to be unaware of the thickness or thinness of one’s virtue."

Master Dongguo, upon hearing this, said: "Beigongzi has slumbered for so long—yet a single word awakened him. How easily he was startled!"

Lu said: "To slumber is to remain unenlightened. When awakened, one is merely startled by what is ordinary."

*Zhenghe* states: Though they shared the same clan, words, conduct, age, and appearance, their nobility and baseness, wealth and poverty, success and failure differed. Beigongzi’s failure was not due to folly, nor was Ximenzi’s success due to wisdom. The one who failed felt shame for his thick virtue, while the one who succeeded took pride in his thick fate—both were confined to a single corner of things, failing to comprehend the Dao. Who but Dongguo could have awakened them? Ximenzi dared not speak further, knowing only that his former view was mistaken. To awaken is to dissolve one’s intentions into the Dao—is this not the way to near-perfection?

Fan said: "Fate lies with Heaven; virtue lies with oneself. The noble person of old cultivated what lay within their power and awaited what lay with Heaven. Though they might prosper in their undertakings, they did not pride themselves on their abundant fate; though they might falter in their undertakings, they did not shame themselves for their abundant virtue. Abiding in their time and dwelling in accord, neither sorrow nor joy could enter them.

Beigongzi wore coarse hemp, ate coarse grain, dwelt in a thatched hut, and traveled on foot—one might call him impoverished, yet he did not know his virtue was abundant and thus felt shame for this. Ximenzi wore embroidered silks, ate fine meats, dwelt in a mansion, and rode in a carriage of four—one might call him prosperous, yet he did not know his virtue was meager and thus took pride in this. How could they recognize the principle that is inherently so?

Master Dongguo declined and withdrew, and only then did those who heard his words and awakened show no deep shame, and those who heard his reasoning and submitted abandon their self-aggrandizing conduct. In applying this to oneself, one does not covet another’s brocade robes—clad in coarse hemp, one has the warmth of fox and badger fur; why should one inherently feel shame for plain garments? One sated within does not covet another’s rich fare—partaking of coarse beans, one tastes the savor of fine rice; why should one inherently feel shame for simple food?

A hall towering several *ren* high—I, should I attain my will, would not build it; though sheltered in a thatched hut, it is as though shaded by a vast mansion. A thousand chariots in procession—I, should I attain my will, would not ride in them; though traveling in a humble cart, it is as though adorned with ornate carvings. All my life, I remain at ease, knowing neither honor nor disgrace lies with others but with myself. Thus, within the bounds of my allotted span, I move through namelessness. My view of things that come and go by chance is like watching sparrows, mosquitoes, gnats, and flies pass before me—how could they suffice to alter my plain path? Without understanding fate, one cannot advance to this."

Guan Yiwu and Bao Shuya were close friends, dwelling together in Qi. Guan Yiwu served Prince Jiu, while Bao Shuya served Prince Xiaobai. The Qi royal clan was favored with many scions, and the lines of legitimate and lesser heirs advanced side by side. Yi Zhong, younger brother of Duke Xi of Qi, sired Gongsun Wuzhi; Duke Xi doted on him, granting him rites and rank equal to the crown prince. The people of the state feared disorder.

Guan Zhong and Shao Hu escorted Prince Jiu in flight to Lu. Jiu was the younger brother of Duke Xiang. Bao Shuya escorted Prince Xiaobai in flight to Ju. Xiaobai was Jiu’s younger brother. Soon after, Gongsun Wuzhi stirred rebellion. When Duke Xiang ascended, he demoted Wuzhi’s rank and robes, whereupon Wuzhi killed Duke Xiang and seized the throne. The people of the state swiftly slew him. With Qi left without a ruler, the two princes vied to return. Guan Yiwu clashed with Xiaobai on the road to Ju, shooting an arrow that struck Xiaobai’s belt hook. Xiaobai feigned death and thus secured the throne—he was Duke Huan. He coerced Lu into executing Prince Jiu; Shao Hu died for his lord, while Guan Yiwu was imprisoned.

Qi sent word to Lu: “Prince Jiu was our lord’s brother; we cannot bear to execute him ourselves, so we ask you to kill him. Shao Hu and Guan Zhong are our enemies—we demand their bodies to be minced into meat sauce. If you refuse, we shall destroy Lu.” Lu, alarmed, put Prince Jiu to death. Shao Hu took his own life, while Guan Yiwu requested imprisonment.

Bao Shuya said to Duke Huan: “Guan Yiwu is capable—he can govern the state.” Duke Huan replied: “He is my enemy; I wish to kill him.” Bao Shuya said: “I have heard that a worthy lord harbors no private grudges. Moreover, one who serves his own master loyally will surely serve a lord well. If you seek hegemony, without Yiwu it cannot be done. You must spare him.” Thereupon, Duke Huan summoned Guan Zhong. Lu returned him to Qi, and Bao Shuya welcomed him beyond the outskirts, releasing him from his bonds. Duke Huan treated him with courtesy. Bao Shuya personally met Guan Zhong at Tangfu, removed his fetters, and presented him to Duke Huan at the Qi outskirts. He was elevated above Gao and Guo—the great clans of Qi—while Bao Shuya humbled himself beneath him. Entrusted with the governance of the state, he was styled “Father Zhong.” Duke Huan thus became hegemon.

Guan Zhong once sighed: “In my youth, when I was poor and troubled, I once traded goods with Bao Shuya, taking the larger share for myself. Bao Shuya did not deem me greedy, knowing I was impoverished. I once devised a plan for Bao Shuya that led to great hardship; he did not deem me foolish, knowing that times may be favorable or unfavorable. I served three times and was thrice dismissed by my lord; Bao Shuya did not deem me worthless, knowing I had not met my time. I fought three battles and fled thrice; he did not deem me cowardly, knowing I had an aged mother. When Prince Jiu was defeated, Shao Hu died for him, while I endured imprisonment and humiliation; Bao Shuya did not deem me shameless, knowing I did not scorn minor setbacks but felt shame if my name were not renowned throughout the world. My parents gave me life; Bao Shuya knows me.”

Thus the world praises the fine friendship of Guan and Bao, and Xiaobai’s skill in employing the capable. Yet in truth, there was no fine friendship, nor any skill in employing the capable. If there was no fine friendship and no skill in employing the capable, it was not that there existed some other fine friendship or skill in employing the capable. This makes clear that in the Dao of principle, there is no fine friendship or skill in employing the capable—this is not limited to Guan, Bao, and Duke Huan.

Lu said: “When the mandate calls for one’s use, then through friendship one attains advancement—this is not a matter of some other fine friendship or skill in employing the capable. Thus, those who rely on talent to gain office do so by mandate; those who advance through friendship do so by effort. Yet it is not solely a matter of Heaven’s timing, but also of human planning. When human effort succeeds, all is ascribed to mandate. When the mandate comes, Bao Shuya could not but exert his utmost, and Duke Huan could not but employ him—all was mandate. Shao Hu had no choice but to die; Bao Shuya had no choice but to recommend the worthy; Xiaobai had no choice but to follow counsel. All were driven by unseen forces, not controlled by human effort.”

Lu said: “Mandate is perfected through effort, and effort is perfected through mandate—there is no partiality in this.”

Fan said: “Guan Zhong’s relationship with Qi was such that to Bao Shuya he was a friend, and to Duke Huan he was a lord. He took the larger share of profits yet was not deemed greedy; his plans led to hardship yet he was not deemed foolish; he was dismissed thrice from office yet not deemed worthless; he fled thrice in battle yet not deemed cowardly; he endured imprisonment and humiliation yet not deemed shameless. Thus, Bao Shuya’s treatment of Yiwu truly embodied the Dao of fine friendship. At first, there was the battle on the road to Ju, where an arrow struck the belt hook; in the end, there was the welcome at Tangfu, where his fetters were removed.”

Holding a position above the state’s highest ranks, he bore the honorific “Uncle-Father”; nine times he assembled the feudal lords, once he rectified the realm—thus Duke Huan’s treatment of Yiwu truly embodied the Dao of employing the capable.

Yet in truth, there was no “fine friendship” in Guan and Bao’s bond, nor was there “skill in employing the capable” in Duke Huan’s use of Yiwu. If one were to set these aside and seek elsewhere, could there be any finer friendship or any greater skill in employing the capable? Such things arise not from deliberate action but always occur naturally—indeed, there is something in them that defies comprehension.

When Guan Yiwu fell ill, Duke Xiao Bai visited him and said: “Uncle-Father’s illness is grave. There is no need for reticence.” This meant the sickness was so severe that it could no longer be concealed.

Lu said: “When death is near, one cannot avoid speaking of it.”

“In the event of a fatal illness,” the duke asked, “to whom should I entrust the state?”

Yiwu replied: “Whom does Your Lordship desire?”

Duke Xiao Bai said: “Bao Shuya would be suitable.”

“No,” Yiwu said. “He is a man of purity and integrity, content with keeping himself unsullied. Toward those who do not measure up to him, he refuses to associate. He seeks to make all things equal through his own virtue. Once he hears of another’s fault, he never forgets it. He cannot overlook flaws to recognize merit. If he were to govern the state, above he would constrain his lord, below he would alienate the people. He would insist on enforcing the ruler’s decrees, yet his Way would not be expansive. If the Way is not expansive, he would oppose the people and fail to win them over. His offense against the ruler would not be long in coming.”

Duke Xiao Bai said: “Then who would be suitable?”

Yiwu replied: “If there is no alternative, then Xi Peng would do—not that he is inherently suitable, but he will suffice. His character is such that above he forgets himself and below he does not incite rebellion. Though he occupies a high position, he does not dwell on his status, so he need not fear the people’s defection. He feels shame for falling short of the Yellow Emperor and pities those who do not match his own abilities. He is humbled that his Way does not reach the sages and pities the people for not keeping pace with him. Thus, he does not discard others.”

Lu said: “When one forgets one’s own loftiness and feels shame for lacking virtue, the resolve to promote goodness runs deep. Those who are not one’s equal are met with compassion

Fan said: “The Old Master said: ‘Capacity leads to impartiality, impartiality leads to kingship.’ Impartiality and kingship—these names arise from reality. Only through impartiality can one be impartial; only through kingship can one be a king. As for Bao Shu’s character, he refuses to associate with those inferior to himself, and upon hearing of another’s fault, he never forgets it for the rest of his life—how can this be called capacity? As for Xi Peng’s character, above he forgets himself and below he does not incite rebellion. He feels shame for not matching the Yellow Emperor and pities those who do not match himself. What he does not hear in the state, what he does not see in his household—this is the capacity that embodies the Way of impartiality.

When Guan Zhong answered Duke Huan, he deemed Bao Shu unfit to entrust with the state—not out of deliberate disparagement, but because he could not but disparage him. He deemed Xi Peng fit to entrust with the state—not out of deliberate esteem, but because he could not but esteem him. The ebb and flow of esteem and disparagement—how could one harbor intentions in their midst? It is simply a matter of following nature and nothing more.

Deng Xi wielded arguments of dual possibility, crafting endless words. During Zichan’s governance, he composed the *Bamboo Penal Code*—a simplified law. The state of Zheng adopted it. He repeatedly challenged Zichan’s administration, and Zichan yielded to him. Zichan seized and executed him, swiftly putting him to death. This tradition states that Zichan killed Deng Xi, while the *Zuozhuan* records that Sima Duan executed Deng Xi yet adopted his *Bamboo Penal Code*. Zichan died twenty years before Deng Xi’s death. Thus, Zichan did not willingly adopt the *Bamboo Penal Code*—he could not but adopt it. Deng Xi did not willingly yield to Zichan—he could not but yield. Zichan did not willingly execute Deng Xi—he could not but execute him. The principle of this passage mirrors that of the preceding one.

Lu said: “To establish laws is a matter of effort; to suffer execution is a matter of fate. To employ those laws is also a matter of effort; to execute the man is also a matter of fate. To apply effort to affairs is a matter of talent; for talent to go unrecognized is also a matter of fate.”

*Zhenghe* commentary: “To be unable not to adopt the *Bamboo Penal Code* is a matter of the times; to be unable not to execute Deng Xi is a matter of circumstances.”

Fan said: “Carefully examining the *Zuozhuan*: In the twentieth year of Duke Zhao, Zichan died. In the ninth year of Duke Ding, Sima Duan killed Deng Xi and adopted his *Bamboo Penal Code*. Thus, the claim that Zichan killed Deng Xi is merely a figurative expression to clarify the principle. Some live because they are meant to live—whether through accumulating virtue and practicing benevolence, or by encountering the right moment and achieving success, gaining the favor of their time and fulfilling their personal aspirations—it may seem like retribution, as if it arose from wisdom and effort.”

Heaven’s blessing—simply to live, simply to be at peace, not necessarily through virtue or wisdom, nor through power and effort. Yet through faithful and compliant conduct, one may fulfill personal aspirations, live out one’s years without care or fear—what else but Heaven’s blessing could this be?

To die when one might die:
Some accumulate evil and act with violence; some suffer hunger, cold, and destitution, thus scorning punishment and scorning life, so that death is imposed upon them—it may seem self-inflicted, as if it came in response to their deeds.
Heaven’s blessing—simply to die, simply to be brought low, not necessarily through cruelty or folly. Yet by indulging a cruel heart, dwelling in a place where life is not worth living, and having death imposed—such a death, fitting for one who dies, is also called Heaven’s blessing.

Lu said: To dwell in a time when one may live and obtain life is Heaven’s blessing; to dwell in a time when one may die and obtain death is also Heaven’s blessing. Like Yiwu, who sought life in the time of Duke Huan of Qi and succeeded in preserving his life—truly Heaven’s blessing. Like Chu Ni, who dashed his head against a locust tree to meet his end, neither dishonoring his lord’s command nor harming a worthy man, thus fulfilling his death and leaving behind an undying name—this too is Heaven’s blessing.

To live when one might not live:
Dwelling in honor and prosperity, wishing for long life yet dying early—this is Heaven’s punishment. To wish for life yet not obtain it is called Heaven’s punishment.

To die when one might not die:
Dwelling in hardship and disgrace, yet not wishing for a long life and yet not dying—this is Heaven’s punishment. To treat death lightly yet not die is also Heaven’s punishment.

Lu said: Dwelling in honor and prosperity, occupying a lofty position, yet not obtaining life when one might live—such as Dong Xian and the like. Dwelling in hardship and disgrace, enduring suffering and distress, yet not obtaining death when one might die—such as Ren Jing and the like. When desires are not fulfilled, all are Heaven’s punishment.

There are those who may live or die and do live or die—this is to live when one should live, to die when one should die. There are those who may neither live nor die, yet live or die nonetheless—this is to live when one should die, to die when one should live. These two cases seem to repeat what was already encompassed in the previous principle; perhaps there is an error in the text.

Yet in life and death, neither things nor the self hold sway—all is fate, beyond the reach of wisdom. Since the principles of life and death are unfathomable, death does not come from things, nor does life reside in the self—how could wisdom control them?

Lu said: Not from things, nor from the self; knowing cannot direct it, strength cannot achieve it—only then can one submit to fate.

*Zhenghe* commentary: That which comes through compliance is called blessing; that which descends through adversity is called punishment. To live when one may live, to die when one may die—both follow their natural course, thus they are called Heaven’s blessing. This speaks of Heaven’s favor, which comes in accordance with what is fitting. To not live when one may live, to not die when one may die—both defy what is inherently so, thus they are called Heaven’s punishment. This speaks of Heaven’s punishment, which descends according to the nature of things. Yet some may die when they might live, or live when they might die—such is the workings of fate. Who knows the reason? Thus even the wise are greatly perplexed, and only the enlightened understand its flow.

Fan said: Life is the companion of death, death the beginning of life—who knows their order? Only those who trace things back to their origin and return to their end, understanding the discourse on life and death, grasp the essence of fate and do not strive after what wisdom cannot control. Thus it is said: Profound and boundless, the Way of Heaven meets of itself; vast and undivided, the Way of Heaven moves of itself. Boundless and undivided—this is the ultimate of the natural. Meeting and moving of themselves—how could they be compelled?

Heaven and Earth cannot transgress it;
Though Heaven and Earth are vast, they cannot defy the natural.

The sage and the wise cannot interfere with it;
Though the sage’s spirit is subtle, they cannot go against the course of time.

Ghosts and spirits cannot deceive it.
Though ghosts and spirits are uncanny, they cannot falsify what is true.

The natural silently completes it—
Silence, nothingness.

Levels it, pacifies it—
Leveling and pacifying require no action.

Guides it, welcomes it.
Yet in its lossless efficacy, it seems as if guided and welcomed.

Lu said: When one aligns with the Dao and fulfills fate, even Heaven and Earth cannot defy it; when one applies principle and responds to change like a spirit, not even ghosts and spirits can deceive it—how much less human affairs?

*Zhenghe* commentary: "Profound" speaks of depth and obscurity; "vast" speaks of boundlessness and indefinability. The wondrous essence has no essence, thus it is profound and boundless, and its Way meets of itself through the natural. The wondrous function has no function, thus it is vast and undivided, and its Way moves of itself through the natural. Given this, who can know the cause of life and death’s transformations?

That Heaven and Earth cannot transgress means that though Heaven and Earth are vast, they cannot violate the principle of the natural. That the sage and the wise cannot interfere means that though the sage and the wise are subtle, they cannot go against the principle of the natural. That ghosts and spirits cannot deceive means that though ghosts and spirits are hidden, they cannot delude the principle of the natural.

The natural silently completes it, existing without speech. In its non-action, it is still and unmoving—thus to level and pacify it is to abide in quietude, leaving no traces, and not be burdened by being. In its action, it responds and thoroughly penetrates the world’s affairs—thus to guide and welcome it is to respond to things without concealment and not be obscured by non-being.

Fan said: "Profound" is the utmost of obscurity; "vast" is the ultimate of stillness. The Way of Heaven is profoundly obscure, and things cannot fathom it—thus it is said to meet of itself. Without boundaries or limits, the Way of Heaven is utterly still, and things cannot disturb it—thus it is said to move of itself.

Without discrimination, though Heaven and Earth be vast, there are things they cannot transgress; though sagely wisdom be wondrous, there are things it cannot interfere with; though ghosts and spirits be uncanny, there are things they cannot deceive. In life and death alike, all follows the natural. To silence it requires no reliance on argument or explanation; to complete it demands no meddling with excess or deficiency. Merely level it, and there is neither lack nor surplus; merely pacify it, and there is neither disturbance nor agitation. With something to guide it, one can abide in harmony and depart in accord; with something to welcome it, one can rest in ease and meet the moment of its coming. Knowing that nothing can be done, one thus accepts it as it is—who but those of utmost destiny could attain this?

*End of the Fifteenth Scroll of the* True Classic of Perfect Virtue Emptied and Subtle, *Four Commentaries*

*Sixteenth Scroll of the* True Classic of Perfect Virtue Emptied and Subtle, *Four Commentaries*
**Strength and Destiny**

Yang Zhu’s friend, Ji Liang, fell ill, and after seven days, his condition grew critical. *Gradual* here means severe. His sons gathered around him, weeping, and summoned physicians. Ji Liang said to Yang Zhu: “My sons are so foolish—why do you not sing for them to enlighten them?” Yang Zhu sang:

Does Heaven not know man—how could man awaken?
No blessing descends from Heaven, no calamity arises from man.
Is it I or you who does not understand?
Physicians and shamans—do they truly know?

He meant that only he and Ji Liang understood that life and death are a matter of destiny, not something physicians or shamans could comprehend. His sons did not understand and ultimately consulted three physicians, failing to grasp the meaning of Yang Zhu’s song and assuming it aligned with their own views.

Lu said: His sons sought physicians—could even Heaven’s mandate not be known? How then could men perceive it? Heaven does not bestow extra fortune, nor do men commit excesses. To encounter illness is simply one’s destiny. If even I and you cannot know it, how could physicians or shamans? Moreover, the song’s meaning was that neither I nor you could cause this illness, nor could shamans cure it.

The first was Master Jiao, the second Master Yu, and the third Master Lu—they diagnosed his illness. Master Jiao said to Ji Liang: “Your cold and warmth are unbalanced, your deficiency and excess out of measure. The illness stems from hunger and satiety, lust and desire, the scattering of essence and the turmoil of thoughts—neither Heaven nor ghosts are to blame. Though severe, it can be treated.” Ji Liang said: “A common physician!” and swiftly sent him away.

Master Yu said: “At your beginning, your fetal qi was insufficient, while your mother’s milk was excessive. This illness did not arise in a single day or night—its origins have grown gradually, and it cannot be cured.” Ji Liang said: “A fine physician!” and bade him stay to eat.

Master Lu said: “The illness of which Master Jiao spoke arises from human affairs and the misapplication of strength. The illness of which Master Yu spoke is born with the body, a congenital insufficiency of qi—it cannot be remedied.” Master Lu continued: “Your illness comes neither from Heaven, nor from man, nor from ghosts. From the moment of receiving life and form, there has been one who ordains it, and one who knows it. The division of life and death, the span of long and short—all are fixed by non-action, by the ordinance of Heaven’s principle. This is what the benighted find confounding, and what the profoundly enlightened come to understand. What can medicine or stone do for you?”

Ji Liang said: “A divine physician!” and sent him away with generous gifts. Soon after, Ji Liang’s illness resolved of itself.

Master Lu said: “The illness of which I spoke arises from the spirit. What the spirit creates may bring merit or fault. The spirit is the vessel through which the divine repays—it is the spirit that ordains. Before form was received, the spirit already knew. When the spirit is insufficient, the form follows. Long or short, beautiful or ugly—one’s material form is already determined. How could medicine or stone cure this?”

Ji Liang, taking him for a divine physician, cultivated the spirit and nurtured virtue, and his illness healed itself.

Fan said: “Movement and transgression give rise to illness. A physician may cure those who are ill and await treatment, but it is better to hear of it and take medicine beforehand. Yet for illnesses that come without cause, do not take medicine. Even when one is ill, if one can speak of the illness, there is hope for recovery—yet such cases cannot be treated with medicine or stone.”

Ji Liang’s illness worsened over seven days. His sons gathered around him, weeping—such is the confusion and attachment of ordinary men. Ji Liang said to Yang Zhu: “My sons are so profoundly unworthy. Why do you not sing for them to enlighten them?” He wished to impress upon them the principle of life and death to awaken them. Yet their simple, rustic hearts remained unchanged, so he summoned three physicians.

Master Jiao went against the principle of life and nature in attempting to treat it, so he was called a common physician. Master Yu followed the natural course of life and did not seek to cure it, so he was called a fine physician. As for Master Lu, he traced things back to their origin and returned to their end, knowing there was one who silently ordained them. Decoctions and acupuncture could add nothing more—who but one who transcends skill and aligns with the divine could reach such understanding? Thus Ji Liang took him for a divine physician.

In ancient times, when illness entered the spirit or settled in the vital organs, even divine physicians deemed it incurable and trusted in this—was this not akin to Master Lu’s approach?

Life is not preserved by esteem, nor is the body enriched by love. Life is not cut short by contempt, nor is the body diminished by neglect. Thus, esteem may not preserve life, nor contempt bring death; love may not enrich, nor neglect diminish. This appears contradictory, yet it is not—life and death, enrichment and diminution, arise of themselves. Esteem may bring life, or contempt death; love may enrich, or neglect diminish. This appears natural, yet it is not—life and death, enrichment and diminution, still arise of themselves.

Fan said: “Life is not yours to possess—it is the harmony bestowed by Heaven and Earth. The body is not yours to possess—it is the form entrusted by Heaven and Earth. Whether one lives or dies, whether one is enriched or diminished—how can man defy this? All is simply natural.”

Yuxiong said to King Wen: “Yuxiong was King Wen’s teacher. What is long is not thereby increased, what is short is not thereby diminished. What is lost to calculation—what can be done about it?” *Calculation here means wisdom.*

Master Lu said: “If one knows the retribution of form, one acts without private sentiment. Private sentiment is what gives rise to esteem and love, contempt and neglect. The body does not follow the thickness or thinness of sentiment—what seems to follow or oppose, to be contradictory or natural, is in truth neither. It is like length and beauty—how could they be altered by sentiment or love? They are beyond the reach of wisdom and calculation.”

Fan said: “Though the crane’s neck is long, to sever it brings sorrow—this is not to increase it. Though the teal’s legs are short, to lengthen them brings grief—this is not to diminish them. Length and shortness stand in relation to one another, with a fixed number between them. Even the most skilled reckoner cannot calculate it—how much less the ordinary man? Thus, the great tree and the morning mushroom differ in duration, Pengzu and the short-lived child differ in lifespan. In the unseen, all has its fixed portion—how can it be perceived by calculation?”

Laozi said to Guan Yin: “Who knows the reason for Heaven’s dislikes?” Wang Bi said: “Who can know Heaven’s intent? Only the sage.” To anticipate Heaven’s will, to weigh advantage and harm, is not as good as ceasing. One who follows Heaven’s principle without intent cannot be violated by ghosts or spirits, nor disrupted by human affairs. But to anticipate Heaven’s will, to calculate fortune and misfortune, to act in accord with the natural while avoiding the contrary, to pursue advantage and shun harm—this merely sets one in great opposition to adversity and misfortune. It is the height of cleverness in the use of wisdom, yet it cannot prevent the arising of good and ill fortune, nor the exhaustion of blessing and calamity.

Master Lu said: “How can one who does not know the Way know what Heaven loves or hates? To anticipate Heaven’s will, to weigh advantage and harm in service of private sentiment, is to fail to follow principle and entrust oneself to fate. This chapter speaks of how strength cannot defy destiny, and destiny cannot be foreknown. To entrust oneself to it is to be late; to strive against it is to defy destiny—thus one is shamed.”

One who knows the Way cultivates the spirit and nurtures the true. One who creates karma at the outset strives to alter destiny—this is to seek to shorten what Heaven has ordained.

Under the *Zhenghe* reign: When destiny is constrained, even Heaven cannot know it—how much less can man? If blessings do not come from Heaven, and misfortunes do not arise from man, then fortune and misfortune are but what one summons, and calamity and auspiciousness respond in kind. They are neither Heaven’s private favor nor something man can bring about. Diviners and physicians are but the basest of craftsmen, yet even wisdom cannot know what destiny constrains. When illness strikes and one seeks a physician, does one suppose the physician truly knows? There are three schools of medicine: the Jiao, the Yu, and the Lu. The Jiao school speaks of resisting and treating; the Yu school, of following and regulating; the Lu school, of unifying principle and obscuring distinctions.

For instance, when cold and heat are imbalanced, deficiency and excess are disordered, one knows the illness lies in man alone—yet failing to comprehend destiny, this is the way of resisting and treating. When prenatal qi is insufficient and breast milk excessive, one knows the illness is received from Heaven alone—accepting it as destiny, this is the way of following and regulating. If illness does not arise from Heaven, it is not what the Yu school can treat; if illness does not lie in man, it is not what the Jiao school can attack. When none know why things are as they are, this is destiny at work—what use are medicines and stones? This is what it means to unify principle and obscure distinctions, and such a one is rightly called a divine physician.

Those who prize life nourish the form to preserve life; those who cherish the body do not harm it through nurture. Yet this is not the way to perfect the body and cultivate life. For in pursuing things, one cannot avoid endangering the body; in living with sorrow and anxiety, one cannot escape harming life and body. Only one who does not treat life as something to be prized surpasses those who prize it. For life is not preserved by valuing it, nor cut short by despising it. Only one who forgets the body can be free from harm, for the body is not made substantial by cherishing it, nor diminished by slighting it.

Thus, prizing life may not preserve it, despising it may not end it; cherishing the body may not make it flourish, slighting it may not make it wither. From the perspective of constant principle, this seems contradictory, yet accepting destiny, it is not. Some prize life and live, some despise it and die; some cherish the body and it flourishes, some slight it and it withers. From the perspective of changing principle, this seems natural, yet constrained by destiny, it is not. All this arises of itself, so it is said: one lives or dies, flourishes or withers, of itself.

Yu Xiong said: “What is long is not made so by addition; what is short is not made so by reduction.” What is natural cannot be increased or diminished. The *Classic* says: “What Heaven detests—who knows the reason?” What is natural cannot be fathomed. This is why the true person of old did not destroy destiny for the sake of causes.

Fan said: “What is equal in man is equal in Heaven. What man deems beneficial, Heaven detests—who can know which to follow? Anticipating Heaven’s will, weighing advantage and harm, wisdom may yet falter. This is why foresight is the flower of the Way and the beginning of folly. Heaven’s workings are soundless and scentless—how can they be hastily measured? Thus it is said: ‘Better to desist.’”

Yang Bu was the younger brother of Yang Zhu. He asked: “Consider two men here: in years, they are brothers; in speech, they are brothers; in talent, they are brothers; in appearance, they are brothers. Yet in longevity and early death, they are father and son; in nobility and lowliness, father and son; in fame and reputation, father and son; in love and hatred, father and son. I am perplexed.”

Master Lu said: “In years, speech, talent, and appearance, they are alike—hence brothers. In longevity and early death, nobility and lowliness, they are vastly different—hence father and son. This is the unknowability of destiny, so you are perplexed.”

Yangzi said: “The ancients had a saying I once understood and shall tell you: ‘What is not known to be so yet is so—this is destiny.’ The principle of spontaneity cannot be known by wisdom. Now, in confusion and obscurity, in tangled disorder, whether one acts or does not act, days pass and return—who knows the reason? It is all destiny.”

Master Lu said: “What the multitude does not know they take as natural: in confusion and obscurity, days passing and returning, ceaseless in motion—do they take this as destiny? One who trusts in destiny denies longevity and early death; if there are longevity and early death, it is not destiny. One who trusts in principle denies right and wrong; if there are right and wrong, it is not principle. One who trusts in the heart denies adversity and favor; if there are adversity and favor, it is not the heart. One who trusts in nature denies safety and danger; if there are safety and danger, it is not *zhu* [nature]. Then one may speak of having nothing to trust, nothing not to trust. Principle, too, has no trust or distrust.

Truly sincere and earnest—what to leave, what to approach? What to grieve, what to rejoice? What to do, what not to do? If principle has no heart, then there is nothing not done, and nothing done.”

Master Lu said: “Longevity and early death are destiny; right and wrong are principle; adversity and favor are the heart; safety and danger are nature. If one who trusts in destiny denies longevity and early death, one who trusts in principle denies right and wrong, one who trusts in the heart denies adversity and favor, one who trusts in nature denies safety and danger—then one may speak of having nothing to trust, nothing not to trust, and thus attains the true Way. What to leave, what to approach? What to grieve, what to rejoice? What to do, what not to do? This is the ultimate Way.”

Fan said: “Brothers are of heaven’s bonds—this is why we speak of their sameness. Father and son are of heaven’s ties—this is why we speak of their difference. Consider this man: though their years are equal, their longevity and early death differ; though their words are equal, their nobility and baseness differ; though their talents are equal, their fame and repute differ; though their appearances are equal, their love and hatred differ. What causes this? It is simply the natural course. The principle of the natural course cannot be fathomed, so in dimness and obscurity, one remains in darkness alone, with nothing to be seen; in confusion and disorder, one follows along alone, with nothing to oppose. Whatever is done, whatever is not done, I harbor no intent. Knowing that going and coming are not of the self, the heart’s deliberations are not disturbed—who then can know its cause? That which arrives without knowing how is fittingly ascribed to destiny. Thus, one who trusts in destiny denies longevity and early death, and Peng and Shang’s fixed allotments become one thread; one who trusts in principle denies right and wrong, and Yao and Jie’s natural sameness becomes one continuum; one who trusts in the heart denies adversity and favor, never turning from one to embrace the other; one who trusts in nature denies safety and danger, never departing from peril to seek safety. When the Way reaches this point, trust and distrust will also dissolve. Therefore, the true is but one and does not stray; the sincere is whole and solid, without flaw. Going and approaching, grief and joy—no distinctions remain. What to do? What not to do?”

Thus it shall transform of itself. The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* states: "The perfected person dwells as though dead, moves as though a machine." This illustrates the ultimate of non-mind. They know not why they dwell, nor why they do not dwell; they know not why they move, nor why they do not move. They do not alter their countenance for the gaze of the multitude, nor do they claim that their countenance remains unchanged when none observe. They do not allow external things—sights or sounds—to alter their measure. Alone they come, alone they go, alone they enter, alone they depart—who can obstruct them? Things go, and they go; things come, and they come, letting things enter and depart as they will, thus nothing can obstruct them.

Lu says: "Dwelling as though dead" means having no mind. "Moving as though a machine" means employing mechanisms. Like a wooden figure’s movements—what knowledge does it possess? It lies neither in emotion nor in appearance, but only in spirit wandering free. Who could obstruct it?

In the *Zhenghe* edition: Brothers are spoken of in terms of the order of precedence; father and son, in terms of the hierarchy of rank. Talent, appearance, speech, and age belong to oneself; longevity and early death, nobility and lowliness, fame and reputation, love and hatred belong to the external. Years may be the same, yet longevity differs; words may be the same, yet reputation differs; talent may be the same, yet rank differs; appearance may be the same, yet love and hatred differ. All that belongs to oneself arises from what is inherently so, while what is external cannot be made certain. Principle has its natural course and its induced course—this is what is meant by "knowing not why it is so, yet it is so." Destiny, then, is that of which we know neither its natural nor its induced causes.

Dim and obscure, it cannot be made clear. Confused and shifting, it cannot be fixed. Following what is done, following what is not done, it responds without concealment. Day by day it departs, day by day it arrives, ever in motion yet never accumulating. In this manner, who can know it? Thus it is said: "Who knows its cause?" All is destiny.

Longevity and early death are not two; cultivate the self and await it—this is how one establishes destiny. Therefore, one who trusts in destiny denies longevity and early death. "Right and right, wrong and wrong" is called wisdom, and wisdom is what exhausts principle. Therefore, one who trusts in principle denies right and wrong. The human heart delights in compliance and rages at opposition—thus one who trusts in the heart denies adversity and favor. Human nature finds peace in what it attains and peril in what it loses—thus one who trusts in nature denies safety and danger. Trust means yielding to what is natural.

Though unified in trust, one is still bound. Only when there is neither trust nor distrust does one attain the utmost truth, free from delusion and flaw. In such sincerity, there is no entry where one does not find contentment. Thus it is said: "What to leave? What to approach? What to grieve? What to rejoice? What to do? What not to do?"

The perfected person dwells as though dead—this is called stillness without motion. When moving, they are like a machine—like the operation of a mechanism. They do not know why they dwell, nor why they do not dwell; though abiding in stillness, they have never clung to stillness, and the reason for their stillness remains unknown to them. They do not know why they move, nor why they do not move; though emerging in motion, they have never been swept away by motion, and the reason for their movement remains unknown to them. They do not alter their demeanor when observed by the multitude, nor do they remain unchanged when unobserved—thus the myriad forms cannot sway them. Alone they come, alone they go; alone they emerge, alone they enter. Their movement is present everywhere—who can obstruct them?

Master Fan says: Those who attained the Dao in ancient times came to rest in profound obscurity, standing like withered wood; they moved with effortless grace, trailing like a dead branch. In dwelling, they were unbound; in action, they pursued nothing. Truly, they never allowed the vicissitudes of external things to lodge as thorns in their hearts. Roaming within the Six Directions, wandering across the Nine Provinces, alone they came, alone they went; alone they emerged, alone they entered. None could pair with them—this is to cast off things and stand in solitude, to attain clarity at dawn and perceive the One. Who then could oppose the great Dao or invite trouble by colliding with its path?

Mo Yin: "Brow-tilted, stern, and sternly restrained."
Lu: "The appearance of silence, deception, and feigned foolishness."

Single sounds: *Zhàn* to *zhì*, *Dí*—
Lu: "The appearance of light, impulsive movement."

*Chǎnchǐ* to *xuānhuǎn*, *Xuányuán*—
Lu: "The appearance of meandering slowness."

*Biēfáng* to *mièfū*, sound *fū*—
All these are the guises of silent deception, sudden outbursts, sluggishness, and haste.
The four roamed together in the world, each following their own will;
though they spent their years side by side, they never knew one another’s hearts, each believing their own wisdom to be profound.
Lu: "Though they wandered together in the world, through all their years they never learned each other’s names, each convinced of their own excellence."

*Qiǎonìng*—
Lu: "The appearance of cunning flattery."

*Yúzhí*—
Lu: "The appearance of guileless simplicity."

*Nǎnzhuó* to *lüèzhuó*—
*Nǎnzhuó*: the look of one who fails to comprehend.
Lu: "The appearance of blunt obstinacy."

*Biànpì*—
Lu: "The appearance of fawning compliance."
The four roamed together in the world, each following their own will;
though they spent their years side by side, they never exchanged a word of their arts, each believing their own skill to be most subtle.
Lu: "Though they wandered together in the world, through all their years they never visited one another, each convinced of their own cunning."

Wondrous indeed!
*Kǔjiāo* to *kǔ*, *yá*—
Lu: "The appearance of stubborn defiance and unyielding force."

*Qínglù*—
Lu: "The appearance of unhidden feelings."

*Jiǎnjí*, sound *jiǎn*—
Lu: "The appearance of halting speech."

*Língsuì*, sound *suì*—
All these are the guises of sharp reproach, stammering hesitation, and contentious eloquence.
Lu: "The appearance of seizing an opening to censure another."
The four roamed together in the world, each following their own will;
though they spent their years side by side, they never came to mutual understanding, each believing their own talent to be perfectly realized.
Lu: "Each convinced of their own abilities."

*Miánmòdiǎn*—
*Niǎn*, *tújiǎn*—
Lu: "The appearance of lifeless dullness."

*Zhuìzhǐ* to *lěiwěi*, like *wěi*—
Lu: "The appearance of burdensome complexity."

*Yǒnggǎn*—
Lu: "The appearance of bold vigor."

*Qièyí*—
*Miánniǎn*: the look of one who fails to open or connect.
*Zhuìwěi*: the appearance of burdensome complexity.
Lu: "The appearance of timid indecision."
The four roamed together in the world, each following their own will;
though they spent their years side by side, they never reproached one another, each believing their own conduct to be free of fault.
Lu: "Each convinced they had attained the perfect mean."

*Duō’ǒu*—
Lu: "The appearance of harmonious conformity."

*Zìzhuān*—
Lu: "The appearance of self-reliance."

*Chéngquán*—
Lu: "The appearance of wielding power."

*Zhīlì*—
Lu: "The appearance of solitary integrity."
The four roamed together in the world, each following their own will;
though they spent their years side by side, they never glanced at one another, each believing the time to be perfectly suited to them.

These are the manifold dispositions—countenances not one in kind—yet all converge in the Dao, for this is where their fates return.
Lu: "Deceptive cunning, clever eloquence, foolish clumsiness, flattering sincerity—though their dispositions differ, all attribute it to fate. Such reasoning is unsound. Those who speak now say that the qi one receives is thick or thin, and thus their differences arise, all resigned to Heaven with nothing to be done. This is the view of the unknowing."

Thus the scholar who knows the Dao nurtures their spirit, preserves their true nature, transforms their thoughts, and alters their form. Their bodily frame is self-fashioned. They strengthen their conduct and shift their fate—all these are the splendid achievements of those who give life to life.

Yet if one relies on form to discern fate, then effort is no match for fate;
if one relies on effort to contend with form, then fate is no match for effort.

*Zhènghé*:
*Mòxī* speaks of one whose nature admits no passage;
*Dānzhì* speaks of one whose conduct finds its way;
*Chǎnxuān* describes the slowness of temperament;
*Biēfū* describes the urgency of the heart.
Those who believe their wisdom to be profound do so because each measures by what they know.

The cunning and flattering are deceitful;
the foolish and upright are sincere.
*Nǎnzhuó* is the name for unyielding resolve;
*biànpì* is the look of pliable compliance.
Those who believe their skill to be most subtle do so because each measures by what they can do.

*Kǔjiāo* conceals what is in the heart;
*qínglù* leaves nothing hidden in affairs.
*Jiǎnjí* is the height of stammering;
*língsuì* is the fluency of argument.
Those who believe their talent to be perfectly realized do so because each measures by what they have attained.

*Miánniǎn* seems to lack vigor in action;
*zhuìwěi* seems slow in speech.
The bold and daring press forward;
the timid and hesitant fall behind.
Those who believe their conduct to be free of fault do so because each measures by how they act.

Those who conform to the crowd are *duō’ǒu*;
those who stand apart are *zìzhuān*;
those who ride power act by clinging to influence;
those who stand alone dwell in solitude.
Those who believe the time to be perfectly suited to them do so because each measures by what they encounter.

These manifold abilities and dispositions arise from what the heart can do.
Since hearts differ, appearances are not uniform—
this is why they follow their own faces and bear different names and reputations.

Yet all who act must proceed from the Dao;
those who proceed from the Dao must heed their fate.
These manifold dispositions—some find their end, some go astray—
yet all equally proceed from the Dao and heed their fate.
Thus it is said: all converge in the Dao, for this is where their fates return.

Fan:
Diverse wills and thoughts—some find their end, some go astray;
varied paths and pursuits—some clash, some align.
Yet those who believe their wisdom to be profound spend their years without knowing one another’s hearts;
those who believe their skill to be most subtle spend their years without exchanging their arts;
those who believe their talent to be perfectly realized spend their years without mutual understanding;
those who believe their conduct to be free of fault spend their years without reproaching one another;
those who believe the time to be perfectly suited to them spend their years without glancing at one another.
The teeming manifold dispositions—each with its own countenance—
yet all roam together in the world, each following their own will.
Perhaps this is simply the result of following their true nature.

*Guǐguǐgūwēi*—
*Chéng* is *qiǎochéng*; *qiǎo*, pronounced *xiào*, means "to resemble."
Lu: "*Wēiwēi* is the appearance of one on the verge of desire;
*qiǎo* means 'to resemble.'"

At first, it is not truly success—*guǐguǐ* failure is but a semblance of failure, yet at first, it is not truly failure. The world speaks of near-gains and near-losses, yet in truth, there are scarcely any such things.

Lu says: One who desires success yet does not achieve it resembles success but is not success; one who nearly fails yet does not fail resembles failure but is not failure. Thus, delusion arises from semblance, for one is confused by success and failure and cannot discern the source of the error. The moment of semblance is obscure. If, in the midst of semblance, one is not obscured, *jì* means "convergence"—it speaks of the murky indistinction. Lu says: Human delusion arises from resemblance. If one does not comprehend, then all remains obscure. But if one perceives resemblance without obscurity, this is called clarity. Then one is not startled by external misfortune nor delighted by inner fortune. Are misfortune and fortune truly external or internal? All is determined by the profound constancy of principle. Seeing their sudden rise, one calls them externally arrived; seeing their gradual manifestation, one calls them internally formed. Moving with the time, halting with the time—wisdom cannot comprehend it. Movement and stillness are not mine, so they are not what knowledge claims to grasp.

Lu says: What is called clarity is thorough understanding of one’s nature and penetration of the spirit. Here lies the source of strength, the completion of destiny. Thus, there is no external misfortune to fear, no inner fortune to rejoice in. Movement and stillness follow the time, requiring no calculation of wisdom.

*Zhenghe* says: *Guǐ* is the appearance of near-desire; *qiào* is the term for resemblance. *Guǐguǐ* success is near-success yet not success—thus it is called *qiào* success, for at first, it is not success. It resembles success but is not. *Guǐguǐ* failure is near-failure yet not failure—thus it is called *qiào* failure, for at first, it is not failure. It resembles failure but is not. Viewed by its traces, the moment of success or failure is as narrow as a hair’s breadth; viewed by principle, the slightest deviation swiftly leads a thousand *li* astray. This is why the benighted fall into delusion at the threshold of resemblance, never awakening. Thus it is said: Delusion arises from semblance, and the moment of semblance is obscure. Yet the vision of the enlightened is like the dawn breaking upon solitude. Therefore, in the midst of semblance, they are not obscured. The inception of success or failure brings no change to themselves—thus they are not startled by external misfortune nor delighted by inner fortune. Movement and stillness do not transgress their time, nor do they cling to sentiment. Thus they move with the time, halt with the time—this is not something wisdom can grasp. Hence it is said: Wisdom cannot know it.

One who trusts destiny harbors no duality between self and other, no emotions of joy or fear. For one who maintains duality between self and other, it would be better to cover the eyes and plug the ears, to turn one’s back to the slope and face the moat—yet even then, one would not stumble or fall. This shows that relying on cunning calculation is inferior to following nature.

Lu says: If one can maintain no duality between self and other, then words of misfortune, regret, or shame do not arise. If one cannot know destiny and follow principle, then preserving the body and avoiding harm is already enough to escape collapse and ruin. Thus, people of the world never cease striving by force, yet cannot entrust themselves to destiny.

Fan says: The ten thousand things under heaven rise and fall in alternation, suddenly appearing and vanishing—mechanisms of success and failure follow one another endlessly. Thus, near-success is not truly success, but only *qiào* success; near-failure is not truly failure, but only *qiào* failure. At the threshold of resemblance, doubt lingers unresolved—here is the source of delusion. The sage perceives the endless transformations, recognizes the numerical patterns of fullness and emptiness, and remains transcendent and self-possessed, unburdened by things. Misfortune does not arrive, fortune does not come; movement and stillness follow the time without resistance. How could private wisdom intervene? Trusting destiny, there is no duality between self and other—nothing is done, yet all is naturally so.

Thus it is said: life and death follow destiny; if it were not so, the benevolent and wise would surely live long, while the cruel and foolish would surely die young—but this is not necessarily the case. Poverty and hardship follow the times; if it were not so, the diligent and frugal would surely grow rich, while the extravagant and indolent would surely remain poor—but this, too, is not necessarily the case.

Lu says: Zixia said, "Life and death are a matter of destiny; wealth and honor lie with Heaven." "Heaven" here means the times. When yang warmth spreads its *qi*, all things come to life. The sage appears at the opportune moment, and all beings with spirit flourish. What is fixed by the self is called destiny; what is reached by transformation is called the times. Those who resent early death do not understand destiny; those who resent poverty do not understand the times. These are all failures to recognize the principle of naturalness. To face death without fear when it is time, to remain untroubled in hardship—this is to know destiny and be at peace with the times.

Lu says: To know destiny and be at peace with the times is the greatest virtue. When the time comes, it cannot be met with resistance; when destiny arrives, it cannot be refused. Thus it is said: abide in the time and follow the natural course, and sorrow and joy cannot enter. Grief arises from resemblance, delusion from ignorance of the times.

*Zhenghe* says: That which is direct and without gap is called destiny. Thus, one who trusts destiny harbors no duality between self and other; to hold such duality is to introduce a gap. Those with intent cannot attain the Dao, while those who govern the Dao forget intent. Therefore, those with duality are no better than one who covers their eyes and plugs their ears, turns their back to the slope and faces the moat—yet does not fall. For one who covers their eyes and plugs their ears has dimmed their clarity; one who turns their back to the slope and faces the moat has nothing to look back upon. In this way, shock and fear do not enter the heart, and thus they do not fall. Though they have not yet fully grasped destiny, they are still better off than those who do not.

"Life and death follow destiny" means they are determined by that which is beyond the self. "Poverty and hardship follow the times" means what one encounters does not lie within the self. Destiny lies beyond, yet the noble person awaits it without doubt—how could one who resents early death understand destiny? The times lie beyond, yet the noble person waits for them without complaint—how could one who resents poverty understand the times? Only one who faces death without fear treats life and death as day and night—this is one who knows destiny. One who remains untroubled in hardship treats poverty and prosperity as the sequence of cold and heat—this is one who is at peace with the times.

If a person of great wisdom weighs gains and losses, assesses emptiness and fullness, and measures human nature, they may succeed half the time. If a person of little wisdom does not weigh gains and losses, does not assess emptiness and fullness, and does not measure human nature, they may still succeed half the time. What difference is there between weighing and not weighing, assessing and not assessing, measuring and not measuring?

Lu says: One who predicts the affairs of the world and is correct half the time must be skilled in prediction. Yet one of little wisdom who does not predict may also succeed half the time—what difference is there? Only when there is nothing to weigh does one not labor the mind. When there is nothing not weighed, one relies on the mind’s knowledge—then one is whole and without loss. Yet this is neither true wholeness nor true loss. One is whole of oneself, perishes of oneself, loses of oneself. Wholeness does not come from the exertion of the mind, nor does failure arise from misconduct.

Lu says: Even if one strains the mind and exhausts the will to predict half correctly, it is still inferior to not predicting at all and preserving one’s life. Those who labor their thoughts do not understand destiny; those who make no predictions do not rely on their own strength. Those who do not rely on their own strength draw near to the Dao—thus, one must abandon the former and embrace the latter.

*Zhenghe* says: Affairs have gains and losses, principles have emptiness and fullness, human nature has sincerity and deceit—these are not things that great wisdom can measure, nor are they beyond the reach of little wisdom. This is why success and failure are each half. To insist that great wisdom must succeed is to deny early death; to insist that little wisdom must fail is to deny humanity. Thus it is said: What difference is there between weighing and not weighing, assessing and not assessing, measuring and not measuring? Only by merging them into one and entrusting them to their natural course can one approach the truth.

Fan says: Destiny may be heeded but not opposed; the times may be followed but not defied. Those who resent early death do not know destiny; those who resent poverty do not know the times. Only the noble person can know destiny and be at peace with the times. Thus, they regard life and death as the constant alternation of day and night—facing death without fear, never despising death; regarding poverty and prosperity as the sequence of cold and heat—remaining untroubled in hardship, never disdaining poverty. How could they exhaust their spirit in vain conjectures, forcing wisdom where none exists?

Duke Jing of Qi was traveling on Ox Mountain. Looking north over his capital, he wept and said: "How beautiful is this land! So lush and verdant—how could I leave it and die? If the ancients had never died, where would I go from here?" Shi Kong and Liang Qiu Ju both wept with him, saying: "We rely on Your Lordship’s grace. Even coarse meat and broken-down horses are gifts we receive—how could we wish to die? How much more so for our lord?" Only Yanzi laughed to the side.

The duke wiped his tears and turned to Yanzi: "Today I am overcome with sorrow. Kong and Ju weep with me, yet you alone laugh—why?" Yanzi replied: "If worthy rulers always preserved the state, the Great Duke and Duke Huan would still rule today. If brave rulers always preserved the state, Duke Zhuang and Duke Ling would still rule today. Had these rulers remained, Your Lordship would now be wearing a straw raincoat and hat, standing in the fields, concerned only with your affairs—when would you have had time to brood over death?"

Lu says: Those who speak of death and rebirth draw from Buddhist and Daoist texts, echoing Confucian teachings but never stating it plainly. Now, to say that Your Lordship would be standing in the fields wearing a straw raincoat and hat is to make the principle of life and death perfectly clear.

Thus we know that effort brings destiny to fruition, and only when destiny is fulfilled does life arise—here the workings of life’s perpetuation become visible. Then how could our lord have attained this position and stood in it? For it is but a succession of occupying and relinquishing. That our lord alone weeps for this is unkind. To see an unkind ruler and flattering, fawning ministers—when I behold these two, I cannot but laugh in secret.

Duke Jing felt ashamed and raised his cup to punish himself, then penalized his two ministers with two cups each.

*Zhenghe*: Master Yanzi said: "How excellent! In ancient times, death was the repose of the virtuous. Thus to weep was deemed unkind."

*Fan* said: Heaven and earth are without end, but human death has its appointed time. Wielding the instruments of this appointed time, one lodges within the boundless—suddenly, no different from a swift steed galloping past a crack. Then even a ruler who holds a state may long to preserve it—can he truly do so? Duke Jing wept on Ox Mountain, for he did not understand this; no wonder Master Yanzi laughed in secret.

There was a man of Wei named Dongmen Wu whose son died yet he felt no sorrow. His steward said: "Your Lordship’s love for your son was unmatched in the world. Now that he has died, why do you not grieve?" Dongmen Wu replied: "I was once without a son, and in that time of childlessness I felt no sorrow. Now that my son has died, it is the same as when I had no son—why should I grieve?"

*Zhenghe*: "Children and grandchildren are not your own; they are the cast-off husks of Heaven and Earth. Their coming cannot be refused, their going cannot be halted—thus to have a child is no cause for joy, and to lose a child is no cause for distress. Without penetrating the truth of life and destiny, how could one attain this?"

The farmer hastens to the season, the merchant pursues profit, the artisan chases skill, the official chases power—circumstance compels them. Yet the farmer meets with flood or drought, the merchant with gain or loss, the artisan with success or failure, the official with favor or disfavor—destiny compels them. This is the dark workings of nature.

*Lu* said: "Scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants—each pursues profit and chases power through their own effort. But flood or drought, success or failure, favor or disfavor lie beyond what effort can achieve; thus one entrusts oneself to destiny and finds peace. This is to gather one’s fallen elms without harming the sheltering tree. Yet people all call what cannot be helped ‘destiny.’ Those who do not strive with effort are deluded by what seems like attainment.

Dongmen Wu was skilled at resting in destiny, but not at understanding it. If the living possess life, and the living grasp what creates their destiny, how could they willingly entrust their destiny to the living? They grasp what brings the succession of occupying and relinquishing. One who truly understands destiny entrusts it to fate while still exerting effort."

*Zhenghe*: "Power lies with man; destiny lies with Heaven. What lies with man can be achieved through effort; what lies with Heaven cannot be attained through effort. Scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants—these four encompass all the people under Heaven. They hasten to the season, pursue profit, chase skill, and seek power—this is what we can do. Flood or drought, gain or loss, success or failure, favor or disfavor—this is what we cannot do. What we can do lies with man; what we cannot do lies with Heaven. Man’s inability to overcome Heaven has been so for ages. Thus Liezi concludes his discourse on effort and destiny with this."

*Fan* said: "To hasten is to pursue at a measured pace; to chase is to pursue more urgently. Those who open land and plant grain are called farmers, thus what they hasten to is the season. Those who circulate goods and sell wares are called merchants, thus what they pursue is profit. Artisans gather in government offices to discuss techniques, so they merely chase skill. Scholars gather in banquets to discourse on benevolence and righteousness, so they merely chase the times. Day and night they engage in these pursuits, never turning aside to other things—circumstance compels them. But when farmers meet with flood or drought, merchants with gain or loss, artisans with success or failure, officials with favor or disfavor, this is not due to circumstance but to destiny at work."

*End of The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Four Commentaries, Scroll Sixteen*
*Beginning of The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Four Commentaries, Scroll Seventeen*

Master Heguang’s Collection
Yang Zhu’s Chapter, with Zhang’s Commentary:

What is life? A momentary gathering of qi, a fleeting spark of awareness in a single thing. What gathers will scatter, what flickers will return to emptiness. Yet all things share one constant nature: they shun toil and crave ease. Thus, what delights them in life is nothing more than rich flavors, fine garments, beautiful forms, and harmonious sounds. But then they cannot freely follow what soothes their nature or pleases their senses. Instead, they use benevolence and righteousness as bolts, ritual and music as sashes, withering themselves in their prime while chasing posthumous fame—this is to miss the true joy of living.

Lu’s Commentary:
The noble pursue fame, the base pursue profit; both fame and profit are passions that drown men, what the vulgar world contends for. Thus, one who embodies the Dao does good without seeking renown, avoiding what the crowd competes for; does ill without courting punishment, shunning what the crowd condemns. To violate the Dao for fame or drown in passion for desire is to lose the mean—hence the man of Dao dwells not there. This teaching seems perverse, and scholars often doubt it. Yet how does the *Yang Zhu* chapter differ from the way of Robber Zhi?

Zhenghe’s Commentary:
When sagely kings do not arise, recluses offer reckless opinions to flatter their own tastes. Lie Yukou saw how heterodox doctrines obscured one corner of the truth, and how scholars of the age, unlucky not to see the vastness of heaven and earth, had torn the Dao into fragments for the world. Thus, he spoke out to refute them.

Fan’s Commentary:
Those who serve cunning and deceit to seize the times do so through power, disrupting their natural allotment. Those who violate their innate truth to chase falsehoods do so through forced restraint, harming their bodily life.

Only these two paths both deviate from the central Way. Thus, the *Strength and Fate* chapter pushes all to their allotted fate, while the *Yang Zhu* chapter exalts only unbridled indulgence. Some suppose these two doctrines clash, unlike the teachings of a single school—how could they know the intent of the perfected in setting forth their words is to preserve both without discarding either?

Yang Zhu traveled to Lu and lodged with the Meng family. Meng asked him: “Man is but man—why strive for fame?”
Yang Zhu replied: “Those who seek fame do so for wealth.”
“Once wealthy, why not stop?”
“For honor.”
“Once honored, why not stop?”
“For death.”
“Once dead, what then?”
“For descendants.”
Yet those who endlessly pursue affairs never satisfy their desires. What benefit does fame bring to descendants?
Yang Zhu said: “Fame torments the body and scorches the heart. Fame seeks truth through falsehood, summons reality through emptiness, and forces conduct against one’s nature—how could it not bring ruin through care and sorrow? Yet those who ride fame bring blessings to their clans, profit to their villages, and all the more to their descendants.”

Fan’s Commentary:
Fame is a public vessel—one cannot take too much of it. Thus, those who maim their lives and harm their natures, offering themselves as sacrifices, are what the perfected deeply lament. Yet with fame comes honor, without it comes disgrace. That one’s name goes unspoken after death is what the noble man detests. Thus, to seek wealth and honor in life and leave it to descendants after death, fame cannot be entirely abandoned. All who pursue fame must be incorruptible—yet incorruptibility leads to poverty. Those who pursue fame must yield—yet yielding leads to lowliness. These are the words of a troubled household. Now, one may have the fame of generosity and yielding yet still suffer poverty and lowliness—this is doing good without seeking profit.

Lu’s Commentary:
In this world of men, there is only fame and profit. The sage uses fame and profit as hooks: petty men die for profit, noble men die for fame—none fail to reach their end. Though good and evil differ, both involve seeking. Yet those who seek fame and succeed—do they not gain wealth and honor, bringing glory to descendants and profit to their villages? Though they torment body and heart, toil in incorruptibility and yielding, their aspirations have their hopes and their passions their forgetfulness—both lose the mean.

It is said: When Guan Zhong served as minister of Qi, if his lord was dissolute, he too was dissolute; if his lord was extravagant, he too was extravagant. He did not claim sole credit for good or evil. His will aligned with his lord’s words, and the Way was practiced—Qi became a hegemon. Yet after his death, only the Guan family remained.

Lu’s Commentary:
The profit of true fame is meager. When the Tian family served as ministers of Qi, if their lord was full, they diminished themselves; if their lord took, they gave. This was shifting blame to the lord. The people all turned to them, and thus they took Qi for themselves. Their descendants enjoyed it, and it continues unbroken to this day.

Lu’s Commentary:
The profit of false fame is deep. If true fame brings poverty, false fame brings wealth. Doing good without seeking fame, yet naturally arising—this is true fame. Seeking fame to attract profit, yet the world remains unaware—this is false fame. False fame is what brings profit.

It is said: Truth has no fame, fame has no truth. Fame is but falsehood. Without falsehood, one cannot attract profit.

Lu’s Commentary:
Those who act with truth have no fame; those who seek fame have no truth. Thus, without falsehood, profit does not become manifest. Formerly, Yao and Shun falsely yielded the world to Xu You and Shan Juan yet did not lose the world, enjoying a reign of a hundred years. The traces of falsehood and truth arise from circumstances. Those who create falsehood follow the traces of Yao and Shun, yet the sage has no falsehood. Boyi and Shuqi truly yielded the rule of Guzhu yet ultimately lost their state, starving to death on Mount Shouyang. The distinction between truth and falsehood is as clear as this. *Sheng* means to examine.

Lu’s Commentary:
The false seek fame without truth; the true act with truth yet forget fame. The contrast between Yao, Shun, Boyi, and Shuqi is so luminous—yet the traces of truth and falsehood are not easily examined! If the world’s people do not lose their truth by chasing fame and profit, they drown in passions and forget the Way. The good are few in the world, the ungood many—thus those who chase fame are rare, while those who follow desires are legion. Even the wise can do nothing about it, for both lose the mean.

Zhenghe’s Commentary:
The sage has no fame, yet others give him fame—thus all so-called fame is but a guest to truth. The worthy chase fame, yet fame may exceed truth—thus so-called fame is often taken through falsehood. Nevertheless, the sages of old did not act as vessels for fame, only fearing that fame would burden them. Once fame exists, how can truth and falsehood be distinguished? Thus, some gain fame through truth, some through falsehood; some use truth to create falsehood, some use falsehood to create truth. Yet Guan Zhong and the Tian family still contend with Yao, Shun, Boyi, and Shuqi over the distinctions between truth, falsehood, fame, and reality—this is why the *Zhuangzi*’s discussion of nurturing life desires that one do good without drawing near to fame.

Fan’s Commentary:
Generosity without seeking may not avoid poverty; yielding without contention may not avoid lowliness—if so, what benefit is fame? Yet fame is one, though truth and falsehood differ. True fame brings poverty, as with Guan Zhong; false fame brings wealth, as with Tian Cheng.
