Extending this upward, if Yao and Shun yielded the world, or Boyi and Shuqi yielded their state—some did not lose the world but enjoyed a hundred years of emolument, while others lost their state and starved to death—is this not also a matter of truth and falsehood?

Yang Zhu said: “A hundred years is the greatest span of life. Of a thousand, not one attains it. Even if one does, half is spent in infancy and senility. Half of what remains is lost to nightly sleep and daily oblivion. Half of what remains is consumed by pain, illness, sorrow, loss, care, and fear.”

Within the span of a decade or so, one may find fleeting moments of ease and self-contentment, free from the slightest care—yet even these endure but a brief while. Then for what does man live? What joy does he seek? It is for rich pleasures, for sounds and sights. Yet rich pleasures cannot always sate, nor sounds and sights always delight. Thus one is further bound by rewards and punishments, advanced or restrained by fame and law; anxiously vying for an ephemeral reputation in one’s time, scheming for posthumous glory; fretfully conforming to what the ears and eyes perceive, clinging to the judgments of body and mind—all the while forfeiting the supreme joy of one’s prime, unable to indulge even for a moment. How does this differ from a prisoner weighed down by fetters?

*Yi* (异) here means "to differ"—an ancient character.

Lu observes: The common crowd all take a hundred years as the span of a lifetime, yet even this is diminished by the sorrows and toils of day and night. Moments of unburdened contentment are few. The joys one seeks are no more than sensual pleasures and fine flavors, yet these are constrained by rewards, punishments, fame, and moral teachings, preventing the free expression of one’s nature. How then does this differ from being shackled in prison? Such are the words of those ensnared by emotion.

*Zhenghe* cites *Zhuangzi*: "The supreme joy in ordering one’s life lies only in non-action, where something of the self may yet remain." Man’s life is born with care; what he delights in is bodily ease, rich flavors, fine garments, beauty, and music. If the body knows no comfort, the mouth no rich taste, the form no fine attire, the eyes no beauty, the ears no music, then great anxiety and fear arise. One toils a lifetime in pursuit of the supreme joy, yet the joy thus attained is but meager. Therefore, only one who takes no joy as joy attains the supreme joy. Now, if one is urged or restrained by rewards and punishments, advanced or withdrawn by fame and law, conforms to what the ears and eyes perceive, and clings to the judgments of body and mind—all in pursuit of one’s own joy—how does this differ from a prisoner weighed down by fetters? How can such a life truly nourish the self?

The people of high antiquity knew that life is but a brief sojourn, and death a brief departure. That life is a fleeting arrival and death a long departure is common talk. Yet to say that death is also a "brief departure" may, at first glance, seem a textual error. However, the overarching theme of this work holds that existence and annihilation, form and qi, alternate and continue; life and death transform without ceasing. This principle has already been expounded in detail in the commentary on the "Heavenly Tally" chapter. Thus, one follows the heart’s impulses without resisting what is naturally desired; the joys of the present are not to be forsaken, and so one is not swayed by the allure of fame. Those who do good do not seek fame. One roams in accord with nature without opposing what all things desire; posthumous renown is not to be pursued, and so one is not subject to punishment. Those who do evil do not invite punishment.

Fame before or after, the length or brevity of life—these are not matters of concern.

Lu says: The people of high antiquity acted in accordance with the mean. That which is born and dies is form; that which emerges into life and enters death is spirit. Knowing that life and death are but brief arrivals and departures, one does not hastily seek fame. Knowing that spirit is neither born nor dies, one does not anxiously strive for the Dao. Thus, one follows the heart’s impulses without resisting what is naturally desired—merely delighting the self. What need is there for fame? One roams in accord with nature without opposing what all things crave—merely pleasing the self. What fear is there of punishment? Therefore, fame and reputation, whether before or after, and the length of life are not matters of calculation. To delight the self and please the mind is to act in harmony with the Dao, not to be drowned in emotion.

*Zhenghe* notes: To die here is not necessarily not to be born elsewhere; thus, life and death are but fleeting transitions. The heart may arise and cease, but nature suffers no gain or loss. Therefore, one who follows the heart’s impulses does not forsake the joys of the present; one who roams in accord with nature does not seek posthumous fame. To follow the heart’s impulses without resisting what is naturally desired is to act in accordance with what is inherently so within oneself. To act in accord with nature without opposing what all things desire is to follow what is spontaneously so in the external world. Not to be swayed by the allure of fame is what *Zhuangzi* calls "not drawing near to fame"; not to be subject to punishment is what *Zhuangzi* calls "not drawing near to punishment." For such a one, posthumous fame is certainly not a concern, nor do the joys of the present become a burden. Then how could one trouble oneself over fame before or after, or the length of life?

Fan says: Man’s life in the world is like a bubble on water—born, it transforms and dies; formed, it swiftly decays. Dying, it transforms and is born; decayed, it swiftly forms anew. Only by tracing things to their origin and returning to their end can one understand the discourse on life and death. To follow the heart’s impulses and roam in accord with nature is to not forsake the joys of the present. One who does good does so without drawing near to fame, and thus is not swayed by its allure. Posthumous fame is not to be pursued, and so one is not subject to punishment. Fame and reputation, whether before or after, and the length of life are never entertained in the mind. Why then would one be constrained, anxious, or hurried, placing oneself in the bonds of a prisoner weighed down by fetters?

Yang Zhu said: What distinguishes all things is life; what they share is death. In life, there are the worthy and the foolish, the noble and the base—these are their distinctions. In death, there is putrefaction and dissolution—this they share. Yet the worthy and the foolish, the noble and the base are not of one’s own making; putrefaction and dissolution are also not of one’s own making. Thus, life is not what one lives, death is not what one dies; worthiness is not what one makes worthy, foolishness is not what one makes foolish; nobility is not what one makes noble, baseness is not what one makes base. All is spontaneous, not the work of one’s own ability. Yet all things equally live, equally die, are equally worthy, equally foolish, equally noble, equally base—all return alike to spontaneity.

Ten years, and one dies. A hundred years, and one dies. The benevolent sage dies, the cruel fool dies. Alive, they may be Yao and Shun; dead, they are but rotting bones. Alive, they may be Jie and Zhou; dead, they are but rotting bones. Rotting bones are all the same—who knows their distinctions? Moreover, one should hasten to the present life—why idle over what comes after death? This mocks those deluded by postmortem reckoning.

Not scheming for what came before, not fretting over what comes after, without clinging to the present—this is the perfection of virtue.

Lu said: Life is the recompense of a single body; death is the end of that recompense. Worthiness and foolishness, nobility and baseness—these are the distinctions of living things, hence their differences. Putrefaction and dissolution—these are the constants of dead things, hence their sameness. The world points to form as death, yet in life, none know there is spirit beyond form. When the spirit departs, all is ignorance. Thus, worthiness and foolishness, nobility and baseness, putrefaction and dissolution—none are of form’s own making. If not of its own making, then the essence of all living things has never not been equal. People know what is equal but not what makes them distinct. Thus, they vie in the present life and have no leisure to nurture what gives them life. This is why those who embody the Dao differ from such pursuits.

*Zhenghe*: Those who grasp the truth of life know that life comes but briefly—how much less should they exhaust themselves over death? Knowing that life must end, that all beginnings have an end, equalizing life and death, sameness and foolishness, nobility and baseness—then a hundred cares become one. To scheme for what comes after death is delusion.

Fan said: Bound by the mechanisms of yin and yang, molded in the crucible of creation, treating the body as a great affliction, life as finite—unable to delight the mind or nurture longevity—these are not those who have mastered the Dao. Why? Worthiness and foolishness, nobility and baseness differ in life, but in death, all share putrefaction and dissolution. Ten years, and one dies; a hundred years, and one dies—Peng Zu and the short-lived child have no difference in duration. The benevolent sage dies, the cruel fool dies—Confucius and Robber Zhi have no distinction between good and evil. Who would then sacrifice the body to pursue posthumous glory?

Yang Zhu said: Bo Yi was not without desire—his pride in purity led him to starve to death. Zhan Ji was not without emotion—his pride in chastity led him to a childless lineage. Such errors of purity and chastity—goodness taken to this extreme! These are words that slander the worthy and betray reality. Yet to praise or blame, one must direct judgment toward the loftiest.

Lu said: Such excess in pursuing reputation to this end is not how one embodies truth, perfects the Dao, forgets fame, and realizes reality.

*Zhenghe*: People cannot be without desire—being human, how can they lack emotion? Thus, desire and human emotion exist. Bo Yi’s pride in purity was not without desire; Zhan Ji’s pride in chastity was not without emotion. Yet they starved to death, left their lineage barren—this is not what is meant by "not allowing likes and dislikes to internally harm the body, always following spontaneity without forcing life."

Fan said: Bo Yi’s starvation and Zhan Ji’s childlessness were not free from pride—this merely critiques the traces of their conduct to correct the flaw of seeking fame. Those who read this text must grasp the meaning beyond the words.

Yang Zhu said: Yuan Xian was impoverished in Lu; Zigong prospered in Wei. Poverty harmed his life; prosperity burdened his body. If poverty is not permissible, nor is prosperity—where then lies the permissible? It lies in delighting in life, in easing the body. Thus, those who delight in life do not suffer poverty—they suffice in what sustains them, never lacking. Those who ease the body do not seek prosperity—they do not labor the mind to amass wealth.

Lu said: Clinging to poverty without striving harms life; amassing wealth for survival burdens the body. Only those who embody the Dao neither seek wealth to ease the body nor cling to poverty to afflict life. They delight in the Dao, preserve truth, and respond to things without obstruction.

*Zhenghe*: Those who honor life, though rich and noble, do not harm the body with excess; though poor and lowly, do not burden form with profit. Yuan Xian’s poverty harmed his life because he burdened his form with profit; Zigong’s prosperity burdened his body because he harmed himself with excess. Sorrowing over the body, injuring life with grief and distress—this is not delighting in life. Thus, those who truly delight in life do not suffer poverty. Wearing out the body, falling ill, amassing wealth without using it—this is not easing the body. Thus, those who truly ease the body do not seek prosperity.

Fan said: Yuan Si sat alone in his walled hut—his poverty was evident. Zigong traded wealth between Qi and Lu—his prosperity was evident. These two, one harmed life, the other burdened the body—I do not see how either is permissible.

Yang Zhu said: An ancient saying goes: The living cherish one another; the dead abandon one another. This saying is perfect. The way of cherishing is not merely emotion—it is diligence that brings ease, hunger that brings fullness, cold that brings warmth, poverty that brings success. The way of abandonment is not without sorrow—it is not burying pearls and jade, not clothing in patterned silks, not offering sacrifices, not preparing funerary objects.

Lu said: Knowing the way of cherishing and abandonment as perfect, yet none can attain it—why? Cherishing lies in sustaining and aiding life; abandonment lies in not burdening form. This is the utmost appropriateness. If in life one cannot sustain and secure it, yet in death vainly buries treasures to dazzle with fame, inviting bandits to inflict further harm—this is to lose what is fitting.

*Zhenghe*: In all under heaven, only reality and sincerity matter. Diligence that brings ease, hunger that brings fullness, cold that brings warmth, poverty that brings success—this is the reality of cherishing.

Not interring pearls and jade, not clothing in patterned silks, not offering sacrifices, not preparing funerary objects—this is the sincerity of abandonment.

Fan said: Those who cherish in life seem to delight in life; those who abandon in death seem to detest death. The ways of life and death differ, and one cannot truly regard them as one. Did Yangzi speak of this with some intent?

Yan Pingzhong asked Guan Yiwu about nurturing life. Guan Yiwu said: Let it be unrestrained—do not obstruct or confine it. Yan Pingzhong said: What does that entail? Yiwu said: Indulge the ears in what they desire to hear, the eyes in what they desire to see, the nose in what it desires to smell, the mouth in what it desires to speak, the body in what it desires to rest in, and the mind in what it desires to do.

Guan Zhong’s achievements resound in the world; as minister to Qi, he brought hegemony, acting through power and strategy. The way of effortless action was not his concern, nor did circumstances permit such words. Earlier, he urged Duke Huan to journey to the northern lands at life’s end—these were likely all allegories.

What the ears desire to hear is sound, yet if denied, it is called *obstructing hearing* (阏聪). What the eyes desire to see is beauty, yet if denied, it is called *obstructing sight* (阏明). What the nose desires to smell is fragrance, yet if denied, it is called *obstructing breath* (阏颤). What the mouth desires to speak is right and wrong, yet if denied, it is called *obstructing wisdom* (阏智). What the body desires is comfort, yet if denied, it is called *obstructing ease* (阏适). What the mind desires is freedom, yet if denied, it is called *obstructing nature* (阏性). All these obstructions are the tyranny of the ruler. *Tyranny* (废) means great

Liezi records this as an allegory meant to remedy prevailing ills. When Zichan served as minister of Zheng, he monopolized the state’s governance. After three years, the virtuous submitted to his transformative influence, while the wicked feared his prohibitions—Zheng thus achieved order, and the feudal lords stood in awe of it.

Yet Zichan had an elder brother named Gongsun Chao and a younger brother named Gongsun Mu. Chao was addicted to wine, Mu to sensual pleasures. In Chao’s chambers, a thousand *zhong* of wine were amassed, yeast piled like hills; from a hundred paces outside the gate, the reek of fermented mash assailed the nostrils. When he was lost in drink, he knew nothing of the world’s peace or peril, the regrets and resentments of human affairs, the presence or absence of kin within his household, the closeness or estrangement of his nine clans, or the sorrows and joys of life and death. Even if water and fire, blades and weapons clashed before him, he remained oblivious.

Mu’s rear courtyard contained dozens of adjoining chambers, each filled with young women of tender age and alluring beauty—*wǒ* (婑, pronounced *wūguǒ*) and *duò* (媠, pronounced *núzuò*). When he was engrossed in sensual indulgence, he shunned close kin, severed friendships, and fled to the inner quarters, sating himself day and night. He emerged but once every three months, and even then, his desires remained unfulfilled. If a maiden of exquisite beauty appeared in the village, he would bribe and summon her, employ matchmakers to entice her, and relent only when he had secured her.

Zichan grieved over this day and night. Secretly, he sought Deng Xi’s counsel, saying: “I have heard that governing the self extends to the household, and governing the household extends to the state—this means proceeding from the near to the far. I have brought order to the state, yet my own household is in chaos. Is my Way perverse? What method can save these two brothers? I beg you to instruct them.”

Deng Xi replied: “I have long been perplexed by this but dared not speak first. Why not admonish them in due time, illustrating the weight of life and fate, and enticing them with the dignity of ritual and righteousness?”

Lu commented: “To illustrate life and fate and entice with ritual and righteousness is to curb their avaricious and dissolute impulses, tempting them with the allure of high rank and eminence—these are the worldly ends to which all aspire.”

Zichan heeded Deng Xi’s advice. Seizing an opportune moment, he visited his brothers and addressed them: “What elevates humans above beasts is wisdom and foresight. What wisdom and foresight guide is ritual and righteousness. When ritual and righteousness are perfected, fame and rank follow. If you act on impulse, indulging in desires, your lives will be imperiled. Heed my words, and by evening, you will enjoy emoluments.”

Chao and Mu replied: “We have long known this and long deliberated upon it. Yet affairs are multifarious, and we have simply chosen what we prefer. Why must we await your words to recognize it? Life is hard to attain but death comes easily. With a life so difficult to obtain, awaiting a death so easily reached—how can one not ponder this deeply? To exalt ritual and righteousness merely to impress others, or to feign a nature contrary to one’s true self for the sake of reputation—we deem this worse than death.

How profound are these words! As for those who painstakingly conform to convention, violate their nature to comply with external things, forfeit the fleeting joys of the present, and harbor lifelong sorrow—though their limbs remain intact, they are in truth as good as dead. We wish to exhaust life’s pleasures and plumb the depths of joy in our prime, fearing only that our bellies will burst from unrestrained drinking or our strength fail before we sate our passions. We have no leisure to fret over the tarnishing of our reputation or the peril to our lives.

Moreover, do you presume to dazzle others with your governance of the state, seeking to sway our hearts with honeyed words and delight our minds with honors and emoluments? Is this not contemptible and pitiable? We wish to part ways with you—*part ways* meaning to debate. Those skilled in governing externals may not bring order to things, yet their persons suffer toil; those skilled in governing the internal may not plunge things into chaos, yet their natures enjoy ease. Your method of governing externals may be temporarily enforced in one state, but it does not accord with human hearts. Our method of governing the internal can be extended to the world, and the Way of ruler and minister will cease.

We have long wished to expound this art to you, yet you instead seek to instruct us with that other art?” Zichan was left speechless, unable to respond.

Lu said: “Those who martyr themselves to passion and indulgence wield deceptive words and perverse arguments sufficient to silence the mouths of sages and disrupt the laws of the world. Thus, Jie and Zhou’s cunning was enough to adorn their wrongs, and Shaowao’s eloquence enough to delude the masses.”

Though they may not prevail in their own time, they stir the age with their advocacy. Thus, Confucius yielded to the arguments of Robber Zhi, and Zichan was confounded by the words of Chaomu—such setbacks are no cause for deep regret. Yet the deluded believe that Liezi acquiesced to indulge his passions, and Zhang Zhan annotated his text to elucidate its principles—these are but the vulgar predilections of the common crowd, how could they be the refined arts of the Dao’s lineage?

One day, he recounted this to Deng Xi, who replied: “You have dwelt with a true person and yet do not recognize him—who would call you wise? The governance of Zheng was merely fortuitous, not your achievement. Without knowing a true person, one cannot govern a state; those who govern states do so by chance alone.” This entire discourse is too blunt and unyielding, lacking the tone and spirit of a noble person. Yet its intent is to free those bound by self-imposed constraints, hence its occasional excesses.

Lu said: “When rewards are bestowed according to ability and appointments made by virtue, the worthy advance day by day while the unworthy retreat. To employ only by talent is to ensure the Way of the good flows unimpeded; to dismiss the unworthy is to leave the petty without grounds for resentment. If the worthy and unworthy each settle into their proper place and fulfill their aspirations, then the governance of Zheng would be fitting. Those two men—Chaomu and his like—indulge in wine and sensual pleasures, neglecting ritual and righteousness, neither relying on the influence of fathers and elder brothers to seize the moment nor restraining their hearts’ desires with remorse: these are truly *true persons*. Yet you seek to correct their conduct, mold their hearts, and seize emoluments and rank for private gain—this is but a state accidentally governed by principle, not your utmost impartiality. How can this be called wisdom? Such ‘true persons’ are not true sages but truly talentless men.”

*Zhenghe* commentary: Those who toil the body and startle the heart are enslaved by external forces; those who release the heart and free the body draw near to spontaneity. External forces breed delusion, while spontaneity preserves one’s authenticity. Chaomu, steeped in wine and sensuality, acted without regard for the disgrace of his reputation or the peril to his life—he was one who released the heart and freed the body, unburdened by constraints. Zichan, proud of his governance through ritual, righteousness, laws, and standards, forced his nature to conform to the allure of honor and emolument, ever fearful his person might go ungoverned—he was one who toiled the body and startled the heart, bound by constraints. The unburdened suffice to perfect their death; the constrained cannot delight in their life. Thus, those who labor the body and exhaust the life are deluded, while those who follow their passions and indulge their hearts are true.

Hence, Chaomu deemed his governance *internal* and Zichan’s *external*, saying: “Those skilled in governing the external may not bring order to things yet wear themselves out; those skilled in governing the internal may not plunge things into chaos yet enjoy ease in their nature.” Who but a true person could grasp this?

Fan said: “To govern a state with cunning is to plunder it; to govern a state without cunning is to bless it. Zichan was like the mother of the masses—able to feed them but not to teach them. Even the sage condemned the use of imperial carriages for crossing rivers; thus, in governing a state, he still fell short. Hence, he dwelt with a true person and did not recognize him.”

The true persons of old did not delight in life nor loathe death. They came serenely, they departed serenely. The pains of anguish, the ease of contentment—these did not register in their bodies. The fears of alarm, the joys of elation—these did not register in their hearts. When had they ever wearied the heart or labored the body to hazard their authenticity?

*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Expounded in Four Commentaries*, Scroll Seventeen, concludes.
*The True Classic of Perfect Virtue, Expounded in Four Commentaries*, Scroll Eighteen.

Wei’s Duanmu Shu lived in the age of Zigong. Relying on his ancestral wealth, his family amassed ten thousand gold. He did not attend to worldly affairs but gave free rein to his desires. Whatever the people of his time wished to do, whatever men’s hearts longed to enjoy—he did it all, he indulged in it all. His walls and halls, terraces and pavilions, gardens and parks, ponds and pools, food and drink, carriages and robes, music and concubines—all rivaled the lords of Qi and Chu.

Whatever his nature craved, his ears wished to hear, his eyes wished to see, his mouth wished to taste—even if it came from distant lands or remote regions, from places beyond Qi’s borders—he would inevitably procure it, as if it were but a thing within his garden walls. When he traveled, though mountains and rivers barred the way, though the roads were long and winding, he would inevitably go, as if it were but a step across the room. Guests filled his courtyard daily by the hundreds; his kitchens never ceased their smoke and fire; his halls and corridors never stilled their music.

From what remained after his own sustenance, he first distributed to his clansmen; from what remained after the clansmen, he next distributed to his village; from what remained after the village, he then distributed throughout the state. At sixty years of age, his vitality waning, he abandoned his household affairs and dispersed all his storehouses—his treasures, jewels, carriages, robes, concubines, and attendants. Within a year, he had exhausted it all, leaving no wealth for his descendants. When he fell ill, he had no medicines or stones in reserve; when he died, he had no funds for burial.

Those who understood the principle knew that the ten thousand things are impermanent, that wealth and goods are but temporary gatherings. Their accumulation was not his achievement, yet he fulfilled all proper sustenance. Their dispersal was not his gift, but rather revealed that things do not endure in one place. Such a man—could he be moved by fame or reputation, bound by ritual and law?

The people of the state who received his bounty pooled their resources to bury him and returned his wealth to his descendants. Qin Gu and Qu Li heard of this and said, “Duanmu Shu was a madman—he shamed his ancestors.” Duangan Sheng heard of it and said, “Duanmu was a man of understanding—his virtue surpassed his ancestors.” What he did, what he pursued, shocked the common mind, yet it was truly in accord with principle. The gentlemen of Wei, who clung to ritual and teaching, were fundamentally unable to grasp such a man’s heart.

**Zhenghe’s Commentary:**
To grasp the essence of life is to let it flow without obstruction; to know the fixity of one’s allotted share is to accumulate yet be able to disperse. Whatever one desires to do, one does without restraint; whatever the heart longs to enjoy, one enjoys without limit. To indulge the heart’s desires without hindrance—if this is not to be far from the true nature of life, what else could suffice? To scatter wealth among village and kin, to cast aside hoarded stores, to accumulate yet be able to disperse—if this is not to know the fixity of one’s allotted share, what else could suffice? To exhaust the joys of one’s years without heed for cares after death—only one who has attained understanding can fully comprehend this. Thus, to have no resources for burial is acceptable; for the people of the state to pool their means and bury him is also acceptable. Qin Gu and Qu Li, measuring his conduct by conventional virtue, deemed it a disgrace to his ancestors; Duangan Sheng, perceiving his heart through the virtue of understanding, saw that his power surpassed his ancestors’. To seek it externally is why the common mind is shocked; to seek it internally is why true principle affirms it. The gentlemen of Wei, clinging to ritual and teaching, were bound within the confines of form and appearance—how could they comprehend such a mind, and thus they were fundamentally unable to grasp this man’s heart?

**Fan’s Commentary:**
One who embodies the Dao regards things as transient lodgings, knowing that wealth and possessions are but temporary gatherings. To claim them as one’s own is delusion. Thus, one does not confine worldly profit to one’s private share. In this, Duanmu Shu may be said to have understood this truth.

Meng Sunyang asked Master Yang: “Suppose there were a man who prized life and cherished his body, seeking not to die—would that be acceptable?”
He replied: “In principle, there is no not-dying.”
“Then seeking long life—would that be acceptable?”
He replied: “In principle, there is no long life. Life is not preserved by prizing it, nor is the body made substantial by cherishing it. Moreover, what is the purpose of long life? Even if one were granted long life, it would not be what one truly wished. The five passions of love and hate were the same in ancient times as now; the safety and peril of the four limbs were the same in ancient times as now; the joys and sorrows of worldly affairs were the same in ancient times as now; the shifts between order and chaos were the same in ancient times as now. Having heard of these, having seen them, having experienced them, one finds even a hundred years wearisome in their excess—how much more the suffering of long life? The course of a single life is thus: now liking, now disliking; now at ease, now in peril, like an endless cycle. If one takes this as joy, then repeated experiences bring no renewed delight; if one takes this as suffering, then personal afflictions cannot be endured again. Thus, the longer one lives, the more sorrows accumulate.”

Meng Sunyang said: “If so, then swift death is preferable to long life. One could then walk upon blades or enter boiling water to achieve one’s aim.”
Master Yang replied: “Not so. Once born, one should let go and follow the course, pursuing one’s desires while awaiting death. One should simply give free rein to one’s nature and await the end. When death comes, one should let go and follow the course, pursuing one’s destined path to its final dissolution. Since control does not lie with oneself, there is nothing to cling to or regret. To let go of all, to follow all—why then hasten or delay between them?”

**Zhenghe’s Commentary:**
That there is life must mean there is death; that there is a beginning must mean there is an end—this is the natural principle. To prize the body and cherish life, seeking not to die—how could this be the understanding of one who has grasped principle? For where there is life, there is a return to non-life, so life is not preserved by prizing it; where there is form, there is a return to formlessness, so the body is not made substantial by cherishing it. To seek long life in this way is only to deepen one’s delusion. The passions of love and hate stir within; the safety and peril of the body press from without; worldly affairs of joy and sorrow burden the heart; the shifts between order and chaos move one’s conduct. From ancient times to the present, what one has heard, seen, and experienced can be foreseen and known in advance. Thus, a hundred years of life bring lifelong cares and not a single morning’s joy. One soon wearies of its length and suffers its duration—how then could one seek long life? This is why Meng Sunyang, upon hearing Master Yang’s words, wished for swift death. Yet while seeking long life is not the understanding of one who has grasped principle, desiring swift death is also not the penetration of the Dao—both are in error. Only by being born and then letting go, following one’s desires while awaiting death, can one avoid the affliction of harming life. When death comes, letting go and following one’s destined path to its final dissolution, one avoids the affliction of hating death. To live when one can live, to die when one can die, with life and death bringing no change to oneself—this is what it means to have attained understanding.

**Fan’s Commentary:**
One who prizes life does not harm himself, yet is enslaved by things—it seems as though he could preserve life, yet life is not preserved by prizing it. One who cherishes the body does not harm himself, yet is constrained by things—it seems as though he could make it substantial, yet the body is not made substantial by cherishing it. Though one may desire long life and not die, how can this be achieved? Moreover, the five passions of love and hate, the safety and peril of the four limbs, the joys and sorrows of worldly affairs, the shifts between order and chaos—all turn like a ring without end. Having lived long, having seen much, even a hundred years seem wearisome in their excess. The long-lived grow dull, and the longer one’s sorrows, the more unbearable not dying becomes. How far removed is this from the form one inhabits! Thus, not to covet long life is for good reason. Those who fail to grasp this deem swift death preferable to long life, walking upon blades or entering boiling water to achieve their aim. Yet they do not understand: once born, one should let go and follow the course, giving free rein to one’s nature without resistance. This is not to delight in life. When death comes, one should let go and follow the course, yielding to what is fitting without longing. This is not to hate death. To let go of all, to follow all, to dwell in accord with the times and rest in what is fitting, to fulfill what one has received from Heaven—why then hasten or delay between them?

Yang Zhu said: "Bo Chengzi Gao did not use even a single hair to benefit others, abandoning his state to plow in reclusion. Great Yu did not use even his own body for self-benefit, leaving one limb withered. The ancients would not yield even a single hair to profit the world, nor would they take the whole world to serve a single body. If each person refrained from harming even a hair, and each person refrained from benefiting the world, the world would be well-ordered."

*Commentary (Zhenghe):* "Not using even a single hair to benefit others is self-regard. Not using even one’s own body for self-benefit is regard for others. One who regards others must not lose oneself; one who regards oneself must not lose others. To harm even a single hair to benefit the world—there are things one does not yield. To take the whole world to serve a single body—there are things one does not do. When the distinction between self and others is each satisfied and halted, then one’s regard for others is too little, and one’s self-regard is too much—this is certainly insufficient to govern the world. Yet Yang Zhu’s teachings uniquely reside in this: he is a scholar of one corner."

*Commentary (Fan):* "Bo Cheng abandoned his state to plow in reclusion—this is self-regard. Great Yu passed by his door without entering—this is regard for others. Though the traces of their conduct differ, their hearts of saving the world are one. The ancients would not give even the smallest thing to others if it were not righteous, not in accord with the Way—how much less a single hair? They would not take even the smallest thing from others if it were not righteous, not in accord with the Way—how much less the whole world? Yang Zhu’s conduct, in its excess of self-regard, refuses to pluck even a single hair to benefit the world—Mencius indeed likened him to beasts. Yet Liezi found something to take from him. At that time, the customs of the world were steeped in deceit and cunning, and simplicity and honesty had scattered. Even the learned scholars and great officials of the age did not know the Way of valuing oneself and devaluing things. Thus, they cast aside the threads of ritual and propriety, seized and snatched amid profit and harm, pursued gain without shame, and perished without resentment, gradually sinking into irredeemable ruin. Therefore, what this chapter records, taking from Yang Zhu, was likely intended to correct the world’s ills.

Qinzi asked Yang Zhu: 'Would you remove a single hair from your body to save the world?' He doubted that Yang Zhu valued the body too much, so he posed this question. Yang Zhu said: 'The world is certainly not saved by a single hair.' He suspected Qinzi did not grasp his meaning, so he answered thus. Qinzi said: 'Suppose it could save it—would you do it?' Yang Zhu did not respond.

Qinzi went out and spoke to Meng Sunyang. Meng Sunyang said: 'You do not understand the Master’s heart. Allow me to explain. If someone could gain ten thousand gold by harming your flesh, would you do it?' He said: 'I would.' Meng Sunyang said: 'If someone could gain a state by severing one of your limbs, would you do it?' Qinzi was silent for a while. Meng Sunyang said: 'A single hair is smaller than flesh, and flesh is smaller than a limb—consider this carefully. Yet hairs accumulate to form flesh, and flesh accumulates to form limbs. A single hair is indeed one ten-thousandth of the body—how can you treat it lightly?' Qinzi said: 'I cannot answer you. But if you asked Lao Dan and Guan Yin, your words would be correct—their teachings value the body and devalue things. If you asked Great Yu and Mo Di, my words would be correct—their teachings forget the self to save others.'

Meng Sunyang then turned and spoke of other matters with his disciples.

*Commentary (Zhenghe):* "The teachings of Laozi and Guan Yin value the body and devalue things. The teachings of Great Yu and Mo Di forget the self to save others. Yet one who regards oneself does not lose others, and one who regards others does not lose oneself. Yang Zhu studied the Way of Laozi and Guan Yin but did not reach it, so he would not pluck a single hair to benefit the world. Mo Di studied the Way of Great Yu but did not reach it, so he wore himself from head to heel to benefit the world. Yet neither grasped the fullness of the Way. Meng Sunyang saw something in Yang Zhu’s Way, and Qin Guli saw something in Mo Di’s Way—thus each affirmed what they affirmed, but neither was comprehensive."

*Commentary (Fan):* "Zihua Zi said to Duke Zhao Xi: 'Suppose the world inscribed a pledge before you: if you seize it with your left hand, your right hand will be disabled; if you seize it with your right hand, your left hand will be disabled. Yet whoever seizes it will gain the world—would you seize it?' Duke Zhao Xi said: 'I would not.' For his two arms were weightier than the world. Then how could one harm flesh for ten thousand gold or sever a limb for a state? Yang Zhu’s conduct exceeds self-regard. Those who record such sayings do so only to remedy the ills of the time. If measured by the Way of the sages, they are not without bias.'

Thus, Qinzi said to Meng Sunyang: 'If you asked Lao Dan and Guan Yin, your words would be correct—for their teachings devalue things and value the self. If you asked Great Yu and Mo Di, my words would be correct—for their teachings forget the self to save others.'

Yang Zhu said: 'The world’s praise gathers upon Shun, Yu, the Zhou, and Confucius; the world’s blame gathers upon Jie and Zhou. Yet Shun plowed at Heyang and made pottery at Leize, his four limbs never resting, his mouth and belly never enjoying fine fare. His parents did not love him, his brothers and sisters did not cherish him. At thirty, he married without informing them. When he received Yao’s abdication, he was already old, his wisdom already in decline. Shang Jun was unworthy, so he abdicated to Yu, living in sorrow until his death.'"

These were the bitterest afflictions endured by heaven and man. Gun governed the waters and lands, yet his achievements were never completed, and he was banished to Feather Mountain. Yu inherited his task and avenged his cause, but was consumed by the labor of reclaiming the wilds—his son went unraised, and passing his own door, he did not enter. His body became emaciated, his hands and feet calloused. When he received Shun’s abdication, he dwelt in a humble palace, yet adorned his robes and crowns with splendor, living in sorrow until his death. These were the sorrows and hardships endured by heaven and man.

When King Wu passed, King Cheng was young and weak, and the Duke of Zhou assumed the Son of Heaven’s governance. The Duke of Shao was displeased, and the four states spread slander. Exiled to the east for three years, he executed his brother and banished his kin, barely preserving his own life, living in anxiety until his death. These were the perils and fears endured by heaven and man.

Confucius illuminated the Dao of emperors and kings, responding to the summons of timely rulers. He was driven to fell a tree in Song, to erase his traces in Wei, impoverished in Shang and Zhou, besieged in Chen and Cai, humiliated by the Ji family, and insulted by Yang Hu, living in distress until his death. These were the harried tribulations endured by the people of heaven.

All four of these sages lived without a single day of joy, yet in death, they gained a name for ten thousand generations. But a name is not something real that can be grasped. Though praised, they knew it not; though rewarded, they were unaware—no different from a clod of earth or a block of wood. Observing their forms and deeds, the traces of their cares and dangers are manifest. Searching within their hearts, there was none whose mind was not gripped and disturbed.

To clarify the words of the utmost principle, one must present the extremes of beauty and ugliness in opposing pairs.

Jie relied on the resources of generations, occupying the honored southern throne. His wisdom was sufficient to resist all beneath him, his might sufficient to shake the realm. He indulged his ears and eyes in whatever pleased them, exhausted his thoughts and desires in whatever he fancied, living in bliss until his death—this was the wanton dissipation of the people of heaven.

Zhou too relied on the resources of generations, occupying the honored southern throne. His might knew no bounds, his will no opposition. He gave free rein to his passions in the Leaning Palace, indulged his desires through long nights.

They did not torment themselves with rites and righteousness, but lived in bliss until their execution—such were the wanton excesses of the people of heaven.

Those two tyrants enjoyed the pleasures of following every desire in life, yet in death bore the names of folly and brutality. In truth, names have no part in reality: though slandered, they knew it not; though praised, they knew it not—how did they differ from clods of earth or blocks of wood? Having exhausted every extreme of arrogance and luxury, indulged every insatiable nature, though nourished by all within the four seas, they never once found contentment in their hearts. This was but lifelong suffering in disguise.

Those four sages, though the paragons to whom all beauty turned, endured hardship to the end, and all alike met death. Those two tyrants, though the embodiments of all evil, reveled in pleasure to the end, yet likewise met death. In governance and harmony: what sets living things apart is life, what unites them is death—so too with man. Thus, whether sage or fool, wise or wicked, their endowments were surely different, yet in returning to the void, they were not at all distinct. Then what is there to debate between name and reality? What is there to choose between sorrow and joy? This is why those who wander beyond the bounds of the world equalize life and death and forget both their ways.

In Fan Yue’s words: what sets living things apart is life; what unites them is death. Shun’s bitter toil, Yu’s exhausting labors, the Duke of Zhou’s perilous fears, Confucius’s restless haste—those four sages, to whom all the world’s beauty turned, yet lived in sorrow until death, and in death were the same. The wanton dissipation of Jie of Xia, the reckless abandon of Zhou of Shang—those two tyrants, to whom all the world’s evil turned, yet lived in bliss until death, and in death were the same. Thus, the benevolent sage dies, the wicked fool dies—where do we see their difference?

When Yang Zhu met King Hui of Liang, he said that governing the world was as easy as turning one’s palm. The king replied: “You cannot even manage one wife and one concubine, nor tend three acres of garden—how can you claim governing the world is as easy as turning your palm?” Yang Zhu answered: “Have you seen a shepherd? A hundred sheep in a flock, led by a boy of five feet carrying a switch, will go east when he wishes, west when he wishes. But if Yao were to lead a single sheep, with Shun carrying the switch behind him, they could not make it move forward. Moreover, I have heard that fish large enough to swallow a boat do not swim in shallow streams, and swans soaring high do not alight in filthy ponds. Why? Their aims are too far-reaching.”

The grand tones of *huangzhong* and *dalü* cannot accompany frantic dances. Why? Their notes are too sparse. One who would govern the great does not govern the petty; one who would achieve great works does not achieve small ones—this is what is meant. *Zhenghe* states: Governing the household to extend to the state—this speaks of gradual progression in sequence. What applies to the state cannot be applied to the household—this speaks of appropriateness in scale. Thus, herding sheep is a task for a child, while governing the world requires the Dao of Yao and Shun. One who would govern the great does not govern the petty; one who would achieve great works does not achieve small ones. This is the essence of governance, and therein lies the importance of understanding the Dao.

Fan says: A crossbow of a thousand *jun* is not loosed for a mole; a bell of ten thousand *dan* is not struck by a stalk to produce sound. The *kun* fish cannot move its body without the vast sea; the phoenix cannot dry its wings without the towering *wu* tree. One who would govern the great does not govern the petty; one who would achieve great works does not achieve small ones. This is the natural principle.

Yang Zhu says: The affairs of the distant past are extinguished—who records them? The deeds of the Three Sovereigns exist as if they exist not; the deeds of the Five Emperors seem like waking dreams. The deeds of the Three Kings are sometimes hidden, sometimes revealed, yet one in a hundred million is not recognized. Affairs of one’s own time are sometimes heard, sometimes seen, yet one in ten thousand is not recognized. Immediate affairs are sometimes preserved, sometimes discarded, yet one in a thousand is not recognized. From the distant past to the present, the years are truly beyond counting. Yet since Fuxi’s time, over three hundred thousand years have passed—whether wise or foolish, fair or foul, success or failure, right or wrong—all vanish, differing only in the swiftness or slowness of their passing. To be deluded by such swiftness or slowness, to strive endlessly in vain—is this not contemptible? To pride oneself on fleeting praise or blame, to exhaust one’s spirit and body in pursuit of a name that may linger a few hundred years after death—can this moisten dry bones? Where is the joy in life?

*Zhenghe* states: Time does not linger, and traces fade with it. Those who come after view the present as we view the past. Thus, to pursue fleeting praise or blame and regard life as joyous is to fail to grasp the principle. The distant past is remote, its affairs untransmitted, so they seem extinguished, unrecorded. Since the Three Sovereigns, the past is nearer, so their affairs waver between existence and nonexistence. Since the Five Emperors, the past is nearer still, so their affairs seem real yet resemble dreams. To say they exist as if they exist not is to doubt their presence when in truth they are absent; to say they seem like waking dreams is to doubt their reality when in truth they are unreal. From the Three Kings onward, the past is even nearer, so their deeds are sometimes hidden, sometimes revealed. What has passed is already fading into nothingness, while what remains can attest to its existence—thus their remembrance is uncertain. As for affairs of one’s own time, though recent, what has passed is already heard, what remains is seen, yet both hasten toward stillness. Immediate affairs are unfolding now, yet what the eye fixes upon remains, while what it passes over fades—nothing endures. Thus, in discussing time, there is a difference between near and far; in discussing affairs, there is a disparity in quantity. Yet as the years pass, all become equally unrecognizable. Whether wise or foolish, fair or foul, success or failure, right or wrong—all differ in principle, yet in the end, they vanish alike. To spend one’s life in toil, clashing with things, competing for empty fame in the moment, scheming for posthumous glory—this is not the way to honor life.

Fan says: Affairs in the world arise and perish in an instant, wax and wane in turn, rise and fall in succession, flicker into being and vanish—this is an endless cycle of existence and nonexistence. From the earliest times to the present, countless millions of years have passed. Whether wise or foolish, fair or foul, right or wrong, success or failure—though differing in ten thousand ways, all meet the same end. Yet the deluded do not understand this. They begin by entangling themselves in schemes, exhausting their bodies and startling their hearts, inwardly filling their minds with thorns, outwardly burdening themselves with snares, toiling their whole lives without a moment’s peace. If they do not even permit themselves this, how can life be joyful?

Yang Zhu says: Humans resemble the heavens and earth in kind, bearing the nature of the Five Constants. *Resemble*—to be like; *kind*—to share the same *yin* and *yang*; *nature*—endowed with the Five Phases. They are the most spiritually endowed of living beings. Yet humans’ claws and teeth are insufficient for defense, their skin insufficient for protection, their speed insufficient to escape harm or seek advantage. Lacking fur or feathers to shield against cold and heat, they must rely on external things to nourish their nature, employing wisdom rather than strength. Thus, what wisdom values is preserving oneself; what strength disdains is encroaching upon others. Yet the body does not belong to oneself—once born, one cannot but preserve it; things do not belong to oneself—once possessed, one cannot but part with them. The body is the master of life, just as things are the masters of sustenance. Though one preserves life, one cannot possess the body; though one does not part with things, one cannot possess them. To possess things or the body is to wrongly claim private ownership of the world’s body and the world’s things. Only the sage can do this—knowing the body cannot be privately owned, things cannot be possessed. To share the world’s body and the world’s things—only the perfected person can do this. This is what is called the utmost of the utmost. The world’s body is the same as one’s own body; the world’s things are the same as one’s own things—how could this be achieved by anyone but the perfected person? Having realized that selfishness is wrong and knowing that impartiality is right—this is what is called the utmost of the utmost.

*Zhenghe* states: *Resembling the heavens and earth in kind*—referring to their form, round and square, moving and still; *bearing the nature of the Five Constants*—referring to the virtues of benevolence, righteousness, propriety, wisdom, and trust. All things share life, yet only humans are the most spiritually endowed of all living beings.

As humans are the most spiritually endowed of all living beings, their intelligence is thus in surplus; yet with claws and teeth unsharp, lacking fur and feathers for protection, their physical strength is thus insufficient. Intelligence in surplus and strength insufficient, they must therefore rely on external things for sustenance—precisely because their intelligence can control the strength of other beings, making them serve their purposes.

Yet if one relies solely on intelligence while also depending on brute force, one fails to discern the relative worth of self and things. What intelligence values is the preservation of the self, for the self is nobler than external things; what brute force degrades is the encroachment upon things, for things can then enslave the self.

The body is not one’s own possession—how could the sage sacrifice things for the body? It is entrusted by the divine luminosity, and thus, once born, one cannot but preserve it. Things are not one’s own possession—how could the sage pursue things at the expense of the body? Knowing they serve only the ears and eyes, once acquired, one cannot but relinquish them.

What is prized in life is this: the body is fundamentally the master of life. Nurturing the form must first rely on external things, and thus things are also the master of sustenance. Nevertheless, what threatens life is the body itself—even if one preserves life, the body cannot be possessed as one’s own. What corrupts the will is external things—even if one does not relinquish them, things cannot be possessed as one’s own.

To claim things as one’s own externally and the body as one’s own internally is to be blinded by partiality and ignorant of the great principle. Secretly hoarding the world’s body and its things as private possessions—how could this be the impartiality of the Dao?

The sage knows the body is the world’s entrusted form and thus can share the world’s body; knowing how things differ from one another, the sage can share the world’s things. Only the utmost sage can achieve this, and thus it is called the utmost of the utmost.

*Fan* states: Your body is not your own—possessing what cannot be possessed is to wrongfully claim the world’s body as private. External things cannot be certain—insisting on what cannot be certain is to wrongfully claim the world’s things as private.

*Laozi* says: *"Know what should be accommodated, and accommodation becomes impartial."* Only impartiality enables all-embracing inclusivity.

*Zhuangzi* says: *"The great person merges all into impartiality."* Only impartiality enables unification.

To share the world’s body is to treat the self as if it were one’s own, yet the self fundamentally has no self. To share the world’s things is to treat things as if they were one’s own, yet things fundamentally have no things.

Those who advance in this Dao—how could there remain any trace of self-serving knowledge in their hearts? How could they make self-interest their concern? Only the perfected person, free from self, can achieve this. The sage, however, has not yet transcended the human way and thus still falls short in this regard.

Is this not what *Zhuangzi* meant by *"that which has never yet begun to be a thing"*—that which cannot be surpassed?

*Yang Zhu* said: *"The people’s inability to rest arises from four causes: first, for longevity—daring not indulge their desires; second, for reputation—"*

second, for reputation—daring not indulge their conduct; third, for rank—bending their will to seek advancement; fourth, for wealth—hoarding gains and begrudging expenditures. Those bound by these four live in fear: fear of spirits, fear of others, fear of authority, fear of punishment. Such are called the "fugitive people," for they violate their own nature. Their lives hang by a thread, controlled by forces beyond themselves. Only when nothing is tied to the self—when one does not defy fate—why covet longevity? When one does not prize nobility, why covet reputation? When one does not crave power, why covet rank? When one does not lust for riches, why covet wealth? These are called the "obedient people." They follow the principle of life, and nothing in the world can oppose them, for they hold command within. External things cannot control them.

*Zhenghe* states: Those who labor for life seek longevity; those who seek acclaim chase reputation; those who exalt titles pursue rank; those who chase profit covet wealth. With inner restlessness comes outer dread—thus in darkness they fear the reproach of spirits, in daylight they fear the censure of men. Where power is applied, where punishment reaches, there is no end to their fear. Their lives are spent in toil, never a moment’s rest. Is this not why they cannot find respite?

To know one’s allotted portion and refrain from envy is to dwell in stillness and rest. Where could one go and not find leisure? Those called "fugitive people" act against their natural principles; those called "obedient people" follow what is inherently so. Those who violate their nature submit to fate without understanding, so their lives may be taken or spared, and command lies beyond them. Those who follow what is inherently so command all things without submission, so nothing in the world can oppose them, and command lies within.

*Fan* said: Those who seek longevity live in a daze, endlessly anxious about death—what suffering is this? Their form is already far removed from the Way, so they take life as a burden, even fearing the reproach of spirits. Day and night they ponder right and wrong, their form already estranged from the Way, so they take fame as truth, even fearing the judgment of men. If power is not great, yet the boastful grieve over it, then rank is all they have—how can they not fear authority? If wealth is not amassed, yet the greedy fret over it, then wealth is all they have—how can they not fear punishment? Acting thus, they flee from heaven and betray their nature, forgetting what they have received. Life and death are named, but control lies not with them—how can they find their own contentment?

Only those who embody the Dao rest in the fixed portion of nature, follow the unchanging truth, welcome what comes with equanimity, and accept what goes with compliance. Never do they look outward with envy or desire. Thus, they stand apart yet equal to heaven, leave things behind yet abide in solitude, and turn the myriad transformations as they will. This is what the ancients meant by "commanding all things without submission." As the saying goes:

If one neither marries nor enters officialdom, half of human desires are lost; if one neither wears fine clothes nor eats rich food, the Way of ruler and subject ceases. As the Zhou proverb says: "A field laborer can be killed by sitting still." Rising at dawn and returning at dusk, he takes this as the constant of his nature; sipping bean broth and chewing coarse greens, he deems this the height of flavor. His flesh is rough and thick, his sinews and joints taut—should he suddenly be clad in soft furs and fine silks, offered fine grains and meats, fragrant orchids and citrus, his heart would grow restless, his body vexed, and inner heat would breed illness. Thus exhausted, he would be undone. Whatever one is accustomed to cannot be abruptly changed—how much more so for the eyes and the heart?

Thus, what the rustic finds at peace, the rustic finds beautiful, and he deems nothing in the world surpasses it. Once in Song there was a field laborer who wore only tattered hemp to endure the winter. When spring came and he began his eastern toil, he basked in the sun, unaware of the world’s grand halls and secluded chambers, its silk floss and fox-fur robes. Turning to his wife, he said: "The warmth of the sun at my back—none know of this. If I offered it to our lord, he would surely reward me richly." A wealthy man of his village told him: "Long ago, there was one who praised the fine flavor of wild beans, the sweetness of water celery and duckweed seeds to the village magnate—the village’s most honored man. The magnate took and tasted them, only to be stung in the mouth and stricken in the belly. The crowd mocked and reviled him, and the man was deeply shamed. You are of this kind."

When the world is at peace with the dispositions of life and nature, then these four—marriage, office, fine clothes, rich food—may exist or vanish as they will. When the world is not at peace with the dispositions of life and nature, then the foolish and the wise mock one another, and envy arises. The duty between ruler and subject, the rites between husband and wife—these are fate, yet rooted in nature; the noble person does not call them fate. The mouth’s delight in flavor, the limbs’ ease in comfort—these are nature, yet subject to fate; the noble person does not call them nature.

Yangzi cited the words on marriage and office, ruler and subject, and invoked the tale of the field laborer and the village magnate—all to show that when the world is not at peace with the dispositions of life and nature, it comes to this. Yang Zhu said: "Grand houses, fine robes, rich flavors, fair faces—with these four, what more could one seek? Yet those who have them and still seek beyond are of insatiable nature. Such insatiable nature is a canker in the balance of yin and yang. It not only burdens the self but also erodes the upright qi."

When movement transgresses, punishment takes hold. Those who suffer inner punishment are consumed by yin and yang. If insatiable nature is a canker in yin and yang, is it not then the fault of inner punishment?

Fan said: The roc of the southern sea cannot spread its wings in the brambles, while the sparrow’s freedom has space to spare. The turtle of the eastern sea cannot stretch its feet in a roadside ditch, yet the frog leaps and bounds with water to spare. The fixed portion of nature cannot be altered. Thus, one must not harbor ambitions beyond one’s lot. Should one stray beyond the bounds of one’s allotted span and reach extravagance, then desires swell, likes and dislikes grow, and the dispositions of life and nature sicken. The raiders of yin and yang—how can one escape them?

The arguments of this chapter lean toward excess and indulgence, for they aim to remedy prevailing ills. Yet if one fails to grasp the intent to rescue from ruin and takes this as the norm, then the world’s noble persons will endanger their lives and abandon their nature in pursuit of things—how can they find peace in their fixed portion? Thus, the tale of the field laborer follows.

Loyalty is insufficient to secure the ruler’s peace and only endangers the self; duty is insufficient to benefit others and only harms one’s life. The ruler’s peace does not arise from loyalty, and so the name of loyalty perishes; benefiting others does not arise from duty, and so the name of duty vanishes. When ruler and subject are both at peace, when self and others both profit—this was the Way of the ancients.

Loyalty is meant to secure the ruler’s peace, but if loyalty is used lightly at the cost of one’s life, it cannot secure the ruler’s peace and only endangers the self. Duty is meant to benefit others, but if duty brings distress to oneself, it cannot benefit others and only harms one’s life. Thus, loyalty that secures the ruler’s peace desires that ruler and subject both be at peace; duty that benefits others desires that self and others both profit. This was the Way of the ancients.

Master Yu said: "Abandon fame, and you will have no worries." Laozi said: "Fame is the guest of reality." Yet the careless pursue fame without end. Fame cannot be abandoned—can it not be treated as a guest? Now, with fame comes honor and glory; without fame comes lowliness and disgrace. Honor and glory bring ease and joy; lowliness and disgrace bring sorrow and suffering. Sorrow and suffering violate nature; ease and joy accord with it. These are bound to reality. How can fame be abandoned? How can it be treated as a guest? One need only despise those who cling to fame and thus burden reality. Clinging to fame and burdening reality, one will fret over the threat of ruin—how could it be merely a matter of ease and joy, sorrow and suffering?

From within, abandoning fame brings freedom from worry; from without, having fame brings honor and glory. Yet the sage lets things be as they are—why should he trouble his heart? To relinquish merit and fame, returning them to the multitude, is not the way to seek freedom from worry. If one possesses reality, yet when others grant fame, one refuses it—this is not the way to pursue honor and glory. Unbound by either, this is called the Way of following the dispositions of life and nature.

Fan said: Fame cannot be schemed for, for that is contention; it cannot be boasted of, for that is vanity; it cannot be wielded with power, for that is coercion. Thus, the ancients called it a public vessel, not to be taken in excess. Those who die for fame, those who prize fame, those who steal fame—how could they know that the perfected person sees it as shackles and speaks of being free from the burden of fame?

This chapter began with the discourse on fame and necessarily concludes with it, thereby shedding the encumbrance of its words. *The Fourfold Exegesis of the True Scripture of Perfect Virtue and Utmost Emptiness, Scroll Eighteen* ends here.

*The Fourfold Exegesis of the True Scripture of Perfect Virtue and Utmost Emptiness, Scroll Nineteen*
*Compiled by He Guang of Harmonizing Radiance*

**On Correspondences**
Zhang said: Affairs have no fixed direction; fortune and misfortune propel one another. When words are verified by events, they are grasped at the convergence of transformation and adaptation.

Lu said: This chapter strips away the superficial to reveal the fundamental, distinguishing form from spirit to establish matters and manifest the real. By seeking reality through names, one then perceives how following emotions leads one astray from the Dao, and how pursuing desires results in the loss of authenticity. Thus, those who know the Dao do not lose their proper timing, and those who employ their abilities need not flee from harm.

*Zhenghe*: One who speaks well of Heaven must have verification in man. *Heaven’s Auspices* demonstrates the verification of spontaneity; *On Correspondences* aligns human affairs with it. This is the meaning of the book’s chapter titles from beginning to end.

Fan said: The changes of events and things are myriad and disparate. Success and failure follow one another; fortune and misfortune alternate like the succession of generations. When words are verified, how could it be mere coincidence? They accord with the natural correspondences, and that is all. Confucius said: “I wish to be without words.” To be without words is the original mind of the sage. Yet, compelled by necessity, he speaks—his purpose being to clarify the Dao. If all under Heaven attained the Dao, what need would there be for words? Thus, the *Laozi* concludes with “trustworthy words are not beautiful,” summarizing the intent behind its composition. The *Liezi* concludes with *On Correspondences*, thereby erasing the traces of its own authorship.

Master Liezi studied under Master Huqiu Zilin. Master Huqiu Zilin said: “If you understand how to hold back, then we may speak of how to preserve the self.” The *Laozi* says: “Hold back the self, and the self will advance.” Liezi said: “I wish to hear about holding back.” The master replied: “Observe your shadow, and you will understand.” Liezi turned and examined his shadow: when his form bent, the shadow curved; when his form straightened, the shadow aligned. Thus, crookedness and straightness followed the form and did not reside in the shadow; bending and extending depended on the object and not on the self. This is what is meant by “holding back to be first.” No thing can contend with it, and so it is always first. These words seem to be Huzi’s answer, though not explicitly stated. Liezi grasped the meaning of holding back and thus explained it himself. Huzi, taking this as clarification, did not respond further.

Lu said: The shadow arises from the form, and its crookedness or straightness lies in the form. The living form arises from the spirit, and its authenticity or falsity lies in the spirit’s function. If one sees the shadow and discerns the form, or knows the form and illuminates the spirit—without demanding that the shadow rectify the body, or clinging to the body to clarify the Dao—then by observing the branch, one knows the root; by examining the manifest, one perceives the subtle. Only then can one always be first.

*Zhenghe*: The Dao takes softness, weakness, and humility as its outward expression. Thus, it responds to stimuli without ever preceding others. Like a well-sweep, it rises and falls with others. Not contending with things, yet nothing under Heaven can contend with it—this is how “holding back the self, the self advances,” as the principle resides herein. Thus it is said: “Bending and extending depend on the object and not on the self.” This is what is meant by “holding back to be first.”

Fan said: The shadow, as a thing, is present with fire and sunlight, and absent with darkness and night. It seems to depend on conditions, yet in truth depends on nothing. When it goes, I go with it; when it comes, I come with it. When it grows bright, I grow bright with it. Whether crooked or straight, it merely follows the form. Thus, Liezi observed it and grasped the principle of holding back. While others all seek to be first, he alone sought to be last. Daily bearing the world’s filth—this is what is called “holding back.” Not contending with things, yet nothing under Heaven can contend with it, and so it is always first. The *Laozi* says: “The sage holds back the self, and the self advances.” It also says: “Desiring to be first among men, place the self behind them.” The meaning here accords with that.

Guan Yin said to Master Liezi: “Beautiful words produce a beautiful echo; ugly words produce an ugly echo. A tall body casts a long shadow; a short body casts a short shadow. A name is like an echo; the self is like a shadow. The retribution of good and evil is like an echo or shadow—there is no discrepancy in principle. Thus it is said: ‘Be cautious in your words, for they will be known; be cautious in your conduct, for it will be followed.’ This is what is meant by ‘Utter good words, and a thousand *li* away they will respond; act near at hand, and it will be seen afar.’ Therefore, the sage perceives the outward to know the inward, observes the past to know the future—this is the principle by which he knows beforehand. Seeing words go forth, the echo returns; seeing the form depart, the shadow arrives. The principle of retribution is no different from this. Yet beings do not comprehend it, and so it is called ‘knowing beforehand.’”

Lu said: The echo follows the sound; if the sound is good, the echo is beautiful. The name follows the reality; if the reality is good, the name is true. Thus, the name is the echo of the voice, and the self is the shadow of the spirit. The voice goes forth and the echo responds; conduct is practiced and the spirit follows. Therefore, the sage hears the echo and knows the sound, sees the conduct and knows the Dao.

*Zhenghe*: Words are uttered and the echo responds; the form moves and the shadow follows. Beauty and ugliness, length and shortness reside here and not there. Thus, when the noble person is about to speak or act, he must be cautious in solitude. The *Book of Changes* says: “To foreknow the incipient is to be divine, is it not?” Perceiving the outward to know the inward, observing the past to know the future, acting before things arise—is this not being divine in foreknowledge?

Fan said: Words and deeds in interaction with things are like sound to echo, form to shadow. If the sound is beautiful or ugly, the echo responds accordingly; if the form is long or short, the shadow follows suit. Thus, words issue from the self and are applied to the people; conduct arises near at hand and is seen afar. Words and deeds are how the noble person moves Heaven and Earth—how could he not be cautious? He must examine the incipient. The sage is lucid at dawn in perceiving the solitary, radiant in the subtlest and most profound. Perceiving the outward to know the inward, observing the past to know the future.

The greatness of words and deeds begins with deliberation and ends in transformation and change. Thus, words should be free from flaws that could bring blame, and deeds should leave no traces that can be followed. The measure lies within oneself, while verification rests with others. If others love me, I must love them in return; if others hate me, I must hate them in return. The rites and measures reside in oneself, but their testing and proof lie with others. Love and hatred follow accordingly, and things will not betray oneself.

Tang and Wu loved all under Heaven, thus they reigned as kings; Jie and Zhou hated all under Heaven, thus they perished—this is the verification and proof of what is tested. Lu said: The rites and measures lie within oneself, while testing and verification lie with others, like a shadow responding to form or an echo answering sound. The traces of Tang, Wu, Jie, and Zhou can be tested, their measures can be discerned, and the heart of love and hatred cannot but be cautious.

If the principles of testing and measuring are clear yet the Dao is not followed, it is like going out without using the door or walking without following the path. When the principles of testing and measuring are understood yet the Dao is still not practiced, then one may leave without passing through the gate or walk without taking the path. Seeking profit in this way—is it not difficult? To gain profit by violating principle—this has never been.

Lu said: If the matters of testing and measuring are clear yet the Dao is not followed, it is like walking without using doors, gates, or thoroughfares. To seek profit for oneself in the world in this way—is it not difficult? I have observed the virtue of Shennong and the Flame Emperor, examined the books of Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou, and weighed the words of learned scholars and worthies. In all cases of survival or ruin, decline or rise, none have departed from this Dao. From ancient times to the present, none have failed to accord with this verification.

Lu said: Examine their conduct, test their traces, and the words of emperors, kings, and sagely ministers throughout the ages—whether in survival or ruin, rise or decline—are as clear as firewood. Those who govern without following this Dao have never existed.

*Governing in Harmony* states: "Measuring" speaks of what can be measured, "testing" speaks of what can be examined. To know what can be measured in oneself, one must consider what others test—thus it is attained. Therefore, others’ love or hatred toward me arises from my own love or hatred. The rise of emperors and the flourishing of kings—many officials and scholars can clarify this. In testing the Dao of rise and decline, none have departed from this.

Fan said: To take oneself as the measure—its root lies here; to take testing as the decision—its effect lies there. If one loves others, others will love me; if one hates others, others will hate me. The sentiments of love and hatred never fail to originate from oneself. Tang and Wu accumulated virtue and possessed all within the seas; their love was evident, thus their rise was swift. Jie and Zhou were unbenevolent and lost the world; their hatred was evident, thus their fall was sudden. Is this not the verification of what is tested in others and the proof of this Dao? From ancient times to the present, none have departed from this.

Yan Hui said: Those who inquire about the Dao do so for the sake of wealth. To inquire is like to learn. Now that you have obtained a pearl, you are wealthy—why still seek the Dao? The Dao is the root of wealth; the pearl is but its branch. When the root exists, the branch endures; if one clings only to the branch, the root is lost.

Master Liezi said: Jie and Zhou prized profit and slighted the Dao, hence their downfall. They were not without wealth, yet losing the root brought ruin to themselves. How fortunate I am not to have spoken to you earlier! A man without righteousness is but a feeder—righteousness is propriety. To attain the proper principle is to hold what no thing can wrest away. Such a one is no better than a chicken or dog. To vie for food with brute force, where the stronger prevails, is the way of beasts. To seek victory through strength is not the human Dao. To become like chickens, dogs, or beasts yet desire others’ respect is impossible. How could one expect others to honor them? When the Dao is present, self-respect naturally follows. If others do not honor you, peril and disgrace will befall you. To be joyfully esteemed without weariness—this is what it means to be honored. Should one violate this principle, ruin will come.

Lu said: To be free from want is called wealth, not the wealth of seeking profit. To prize profit and slight the Dao was the downfall of Jie and Zhou. Chickens, dogs, and beasts know not benevolence or righteousness; they contend for food by force, heedless of all else. Acting thus invites peril and disgrace, and how can one then expect others’ respect? This is what it means to seek substance through name.

*Zhenghe* states: The Classic says: Though one possesses a large jade disc preceded by a team of four horses, it is not as good as sitting and advancing in this Dao. If one slights the Dao to pursue things, others will not honor them, and peril and disgrace will follow.

Fan said: Balance is fortune; excess is misfortune—no thing is exempt, and wealth is the most extreme case. The rich toil their bodies and labor diligently, amassing wealth they cannot fully use. Their form becomes externalized—how could they know the Dao-followers who speak of contentment? Within the realm of free wandering, moving without name, though ten thousand differences exist, all needs are met, and the wealth of nations is encompassed. Thus, none confer rank upon them, yet they remain naturally esteemed; the world delights in honoring them without weariness. They have never prized profit and slighted the Dao, nor regarded wealth as right.

Liezi learned archery and hit the mark. He hit it effortlessly, not by deliberate aiming. He consulted Master Guan Yin. The master asked: Do you know why you hit the mark? He replied: I do not. Master Guan Yin said: It is not yet time. Though you hit the mark, you do not know why—thus, it is not yet time. He withdrew and practiced. After three years, he returned to report to Master Guan Yin. The master asked again: Do you know why you hit the mark? Liezi said: I know now. Master Guan Yin said: It is time. Hold to this and do not lose it. With a calm mind and upright body, seeking within oneself, one attains the Dao of hitting the mark. Then, one can aim beforehand and shoot without fail. This is not only true of archery but also of governing a state and cultivating oneself.

Thus, the sage does not examine existence or extinction but examines their causes. Though one hits the mark in archery, not knowing why means it is not the true Dao of hitting. Though one’s body endures, not knowing why means it is not the true principle of endurance.
