Therefore, in archery, both the clumsy and the skilled may hit the mark, but their understanding of why differs. Both the worthy and the foolish may endure, but their understanding of why diverges.

Lu said: One who does not know why they hit the mark is not the most excellent. To grasp it with the hand and respond with the heart, to hit the mark as intended—this is attainment. To attain and hold it is called the Dao. To know this Dao is not only for archery but also for governing a state and cultivating oneself. The excellent archer does not value merely hitting the mark but values the reason for certain success. Likewise, one who excels in governing a state or cultivating oneself does not value mere endurance but values the reason for certain endurance. Thus, the worthy and foolish, order and chaos, can be understood through this Dao.

*Zhenghe* states: Can an archer who hits the mark without prior aim be called a good shot? Only by aiming beforehand can one control the outcome, ensuring no duality and acting as one wills. Extending this to self-cultivation and state governance follows the same principle. The sage does not examine existence but examines its cause, does not examine extinction but examines its cause. Existence and extinction are branches; their causes are the root. Examining the cause of existence reveals how to avoid extinction; examining the cause of extinction reveals how to preserve existence.

Fan said: The archers of old aligned their inner will, straightened their outer form, steadied themselves, then released the arrow, never missing the target—this is what it means to aim beforehand and hit. If one reflects within yet does not know the Dao of hitting the mark, how can arrow after arrow follow and shot after shot strike true? Yet this is not only true of archery but also of governing a state and cultivating oneself. Only the sage, deeply attuned to the subtle workings, clearly perceiving the formless, does not examine existence or extinction but examines their causes. Thus, in nurturing life, they are abundant in flexibility; in conducting themselves, they are fitting in ebb and flow; in managing affairs, they know the strategies of success and failure; in confronting enemies, they grasp the power of capture and release. Responding to myriad changes, they are flawless everywhere—no different from aiming beforehand and hitting the mark.

Liezi said: Those abundant in beauty are arrogant; those abundant in strength are aggressive—they cannot be spoken to of the Dao. Beauty and strength are what ordinary people pursue. Thus, even those with graying hair who speak of the Dao err, let alone practice it. Only when beauty and strength wane do they seek to speak of the Dao, but their awakening comes too late. Even speaking of it is beyond their grasp, let alone practicing it. Thus, those who act aggressively find none to instruct them. With none to instruct them, they are isolated and without aid. The arrogant and aggressive, though instructed, do not accept it—they harbor envious hearts, their ears and eyes are self-blocked, and who would assist them?

The worthy entrust others, thus even in old age they do not decline, and when their wisdom is exhausted, they do not fall into chaos. If they do not rely solely on their own wisdom, all things willingly serve them. Therefore, the difficulty in governing a state lies in recognizing the worthy, not in considering oneself worthy. Those who consider themselves worthy are precisely the "isolated and without aid" mentioned above. Recognizing the worthy allows the wise to plan for them, the capable to serve them, and no talent is wasted—thus the state is easily governed.

Lu said: What the common people rely on are beauty and strength. Relying on beauty breeds arrogance and indolence; relying on strength fosters aggression and violence—they cannot be spoken to of the Dao. Those whose beauty and strength have faded are the gray-haired. Even hearing the Dao in old age, they cannot practice it, let alone put it into action. Thus, those who cling to weakness are surely embraced by the Dao, while those who boast of their strength find none willing to instruct them. With none willing to instruct them, how could they have any aid? The worthy entrust others, thus even in their final years their spirit does not wane, and when their wisdom is exhausted, their minds do not fall into chaos. Those who govern by this principle, recognizing the worthy and entrusting them, find worthy talents at their service. Those who consider themselves worthy and lack aid lose the people.

*Zhenghe* states: The Dao takes plain simplicity as its essence and meekness, humility, and deference as its outward form. Thus, those who flaunt their beauty to dominate others yet do not discard their beauty, or those who exalt their strength yet cannot but rely on it, are both unfit to discuss the great methods of the Dao. As the saying goes: "Practice worthiness and abandon the conduct of self-worth—where could one go and not be loved?" Thus, those who do not boast of themselves find others gladly teaching them the good Dao. Therein, those who hear the Dao may be advanced in years yet retain the countenance of a child—this is what is meant by "even in old age they do not decline." Therein, those who understand the Dao may grasp principles yet not let things harm them—this is what is meant by "when their wisdom is exhausted, they do not fall into chaos." This is why the way to govern a state lies in embodying it oneself, humbling oneself before others, and only then casting aside arrogance and self-importance.

Fan said: Only by not boasting can one avoid flaunting beauty to dominate others; only by not being self-important can one avoid using strength to overpower others. Those who embody the Dao cannot advance further. Moreover, in the principles of the world, relying on oneself leads to pettiness, while seeking counsel leads to abundance. Those who govern well humble themselves before the worthy, never lording their worth over others. Thus, the perceptive exhaust their sight and hearing, the wise and capable devote their schemes and efforts. If one can practice worthiness while abandoning the conduct of self-worth, how could there be any state left ungoverned?

A man of Song crafted a mulberry leaf from jade for his lord, taking three years to complete it. Its veins, stems, and branches, its finest details and lustrous sheen, were indistinguishable from real mulberry leaves when mixed among them. This man then lived off his craft in Song. When Master Liezi heard of this, he said: If heaven and earth took three years to produce a single leaf, there would be few things with leaves. Thus, the sage relies on the transformative power of the Dao, not on cleverness and skill. This illustrates that relying on skill and ability is insufficient to provide for all things, but following the Dao in transformation, nothing is left unfulfilled.

Lu said: Carving and polishing to return to simplicity is the way of restoring purity. Thus it is said: The skilled binder needs no glue or lacquer; the skilled locker needs no bolts or keys. Therefore, great eloquence seems inarticulate, and great skill seems clumsy. If it takes three years to craft a single leaf indistinguishable from a real one, is this the skill of one who governs a state and preserves the Dao? Thus, the sage relies on the transformative power of the Dao, like the harmonious qi spreading to generate the myriad things, not on cleverness and skill. If one violates heavenly principles and produces false craft, this is failing to understand the root and branches.

*Zhenghe* states: The Dao carves and shapes the myriad forms yet is not deemed clever. Secretly relying on one’s own wisdom and strength to do so, how could one provide for every single thing? Thus, without carving or polishing, it measures and sustains the myriad things without exhaustion—this is why the sage entrusts the transformative power of the Dao and not cleverness and skill.

Fan said: Great systems are not divided. The carving and shaping of the myriad forms—those that fill the space between heaven and earth, with trunks and fruits, branches and vines—take shape without compass or square, their forms cut and tailored by themselves; they take on color without cinnabar or indigo, their adornments applied by themselves. Though there are ten thousand differences, not one is lacking. How could it be that heaven is assisted by man and thus bears the labor of carving mulberry leaves? The sage is simply heaven and earth, thus taking the Dao as transformation, acting without action so the world aids him—who would toil over cleverness and skill?

Master Liezi was poor, his appearance bearing the look of hunger. A guest mentioned this to Ziyang of Zheng, saying: Lie Yukou is surely a man of the Dao, yet he lives in your state in poverty—could it be that you do not favor scholars? Ziyang of Zheng immediately ordered an official to send him grain. Master Liezi went out to meet the messenger, bowed twice, and declined. The messenger departed.

When Master Liezi returned home, his wife gazed at him and, striking her breast, said: "I have heard that the wives and children of those who possess the Dao all enjoy ease and joy. Yet you bear the look of hunger, and the lord, having learned of it, sent you food. You refused to accept—could this not be fate?" Master Liezi smiled and said to her: "The lord did not know me of his own accord. It was on another’s word that he sent me grain, and when he punishes me, it will likewise be on another’s word. This is why I did not accept." In the end, the people indeed rose in rebellion and killed Ziyang.

Lu said: "To eat another’s grain is to share his concerns. The lord did not know me; he bestowed his gift on the word of another. If he were to punish me, it would also be on the word of another. What I value is the true understanding of one who knows me—a man who has awakened to the Dao. When Ziyang’s rebellion arose and I was not harmed, this was truly what is called ‘not being injured or burdened by external things.’"

*Zhenghe* states: "Those who honor life do not harm their bodies with sustenance." In this, Master Liezi had the principle of foreknowledge.

Fan said: "The exemplary scholars of old would not exchange their integrity even for the rank of the Three Ducal Ministers, nor abandon their resolve even for the emoluments of ten thousand *zhong*. If something could be refused yet was given, they never accepted it with delight. The passage states that the people indeed rose in rebellion and killed Ziyang, further illustrating how the sage’s awareness of the subtle is like this."

The Lu family of the Shi clan had two sons. One loved learning, the other military arts. The one fond of learning offered his skills to the Marquis of Qi, who received him and made him tutor to the noble scions. The one fond of arms went to Chu and presented his methods to the King of Chu, who was pleased and appointed him military commandant. Their salaries enriched the household, and their ranks brought honor to their kin.

The Meng family, neighbors of the Shi clan, also had two sons with the same pursuits, yet they were impoverished. Envying the Shi family’s prosperity, they inquired about the means to advancement. The two sons truthfully told the Mengs. One of the Meng sons went to Qin and offered his skills to the King of Qin. The King of Qin said: "Now the feudal lords contend with force, and their sole concern is arms and provisions. If I were to govern my state with benevolence and righteousness, it would be the path to ruin." He then castrated the man and banished him. The other son went to Wei and presented his methods to the Marquis of Wei. The Marquis of Wei said: "My state is weak and hemmed in by great powers. I serve the great states and soothe the small—this is the way to seek security. If I were to rely on military authority, destruction would be imminent. If I were to let you return intact and you went to another state, the harm to me would be no small matter." He then had the man’s feet cut off and sent him back to Lu.

Upon their return, the Meng father and sons beat their breasts and reproached the Shi family. The Shi patriarch said: "Those who seize the moment prosper; those who miss it perish. Your path was the same as ours, yet your success differed—you missed the moment, not that your conduct was flawed. Moreover, in the world, principles have no constant right, and affairs no constant wrong. What accords with the moment is right; what misses the meeting is wrong. What was once used may now be discarded; what is now discarded may later be used. In this use and disuse, there is no fixed right or wrong. Seizing opportunities and meeting the times, responding to affairs without fixed method—this belongs to wisdom. Even with the arts of the Seven Virtues and ritual propriety, if wisdom does not suit the time, then action will miss the meeting. If wisdom is insufficient, even if one were as learned as Confucius or as skilled as Lü Shang, where could one go and not be in dire straits? The reason your two sons fell into hardship was not due to their learning or skill, but because they did not follow what was timely."

The Meng father and sons relaxed their expressions, free of resentment, and said: "We understand. Say no more."

Lu said: "To study the Way of benevolence and righteousness, to master the arts of strategy—though civil and martial differ, both return to the use of talent and conduct, which must rely on wisdom’s timely application. Wisdom is the function of the Dao; to employ wisdom alone is not the Dao. To strike is to hit the gap; to meet is to suit the time. Responding to change without fixed method—all this is wisdom. Thus, those who suit the time are never at a loss for talent; those who understand the Dao are never lacking in wisdom. If wisdom is insufficient, even if one were as learned as Confucius or as martial as Lü Shang, one would not escape hardship. The Mengs having awakened, they said, ‘Say no more.’"

*Zhenghe* states: "Principles have no constant right—the timely is right. Affairs have no constant wrong—what does not suit the time is wrong. When one’s mandate accords with the time and one is greatly in harmony with the world, what was discarded may be used; when one’s mandate does not accord with the time and one is greatly in dire straits, what was used may be discarded. The noble man knows that hardship has its mandate and that success has its time. Thus, he rests in the time and follows the mandate—how could he rely on petty cunning to seize opportunities and meet affairs?"

Fan said: "Things have no constant suitability; suitability lies in following the time. What is right and what is wrong are not yet fixed. The Mengs’ two sons shared the same path as the Shi sons yet differed in success—was their conduct flawed? This is what is called ‘not encountering the time.’"

Duke Wen of Jin went out to a meeting, intending to attack Wei. Viscount Chu looked up to the sky and laughed. The Duke asked why he laughed. He replied: "I laugh at my neighbor who was escorting his wife to her parents’ home. On the road, he saw a woman picking mulberries and, pleased, spoke with her. Yet when he turned to look at his wife, there was someone beckoning to her as well. I secretly laughed at this." The Duke grasped his meaning and called off the attack.

Returning with his troops, before they had even arrived, there came those who would raid his northern borders. What I have done, others will also do. Yet one seeks to indulge one’s own ambitions, believing things will not give rise to intentions—this is confusion over the feelings of self and other.

Lu said: "To value attainment yet not know that attainment requires something to uphold—this is the common disposition of the vulgar. Thus desires are endless, and the true Dao daily diminishes. What is precious about knowing the Dao is that one inwardly upholds the Dao without loss, outwardly employing things without omission. The people of the world, however, are not so: outwardly they covet sensual pleasures, delighting in other men’s wives, while inwardly they lose the Dao—thus their own wives are beckoned away."

Zhenghe said: "Observing the full and the empty, knowing that division has no constancy—how then could one alone elevate oneself over others in departure or approach? This is why the sage perceives the cicada and magpie entangled yet does not allow things to harm the self."

Fan said: "Those who accompany things are in turn accompanied by things; those who harm others are repaid in kind. Things inherently entangle one another—these two kinds summon each other. This is why the forester of the chestnut grove took me for a target of vengeance, and why the true person of old did not appear at court for three months."

The tradition records that when the King of Wu wished to attack Jing, a youth remonstrated with him—his principle accords with this. The state of Jin suffered from bandits.

There was a man named Xie Yong who could discern thieves by gazing into their eyes, scrutinizing the space between their brows and lashes to uncover their true nature. The Lord of Jin employed him to identify thieves, and among thousands, not one escaped his detection. The Lord of Jin rejoiced greatly and told Zhao Wenzi: "I have found a man who can eradicate all thieves in the state—what need is there for more?" Wenzi replied: "If my lord relies on surveillance to catch thieves, thieves will never be eradicated. Moreover, Xie Yong will surely not meet a natural death." Soon after, the bandits plotted among themselves: "The one who has cornered us is Xie Yong." They then joined forces to assault and kill him. The Lord of Jin, hearing this, was greatly alarmed. He immediately summoned Wenzi and told him: "Just as you predicted, Xie Yong is dead. But what course should we take now?" Wenzi said: "There is a Zhou proverb: 'He who sees the fish in the abyss brings misfortune; he who predicts hidden things invites calamity.' This is why he met his end. If you wish to eliminate thieves, nothing is better than elevating the worthy and entrusting them with office. Let instruction be clear from above, and transformation will flow below. When the people possess a sense of shame, what need is there for thieves?" This is the way to stop thievery. Thereupon, Sui Hui was appointed to govern, and the bandits fled to Qin.

Those who employ cleverness to discern right and wrong provide an escape for the deceitful; those who rely on foresight to expose hidden evils incur the hatred of the wicked. Is the peril of cunning merely empty words?

Lu said: "Instruction is the trace. It is what the multitude follow and practice. Transformation is the Dao. It is what the multitude employ daily and submit to in their hearts. When hearts submit, shame arises; when traces are clear, instruction is accomplished. Elevate the worthy and employ the capable, and thieves will flee."

Someone asked: "Zhuangzi said, 'When the sage is born, great thieves arise.' Yet here it is said that elevating the worthy and employing the capable causes bandits to depart. What does this mean?" The reply: "Those who seek empty fame while losing substance give rise to great thieves; those who attain substance while forsaking fame cause bandits to depart. Thus, elevating the worthy and employing the capable is the pursuit of fame; appointing Sui Hui is the attainment of substance. The principles do not contradict—what doubt can there be?"

Zhenghe said: "Guide them with virtue, and they will possess shame and be corrected." The sage teaches the people and transforms them through the Dao, so that even if rewarded, they would not steal. To regard severity as wisdom is to grasp at the branch and neglect the root. Excessive scrutiny inevitably provokes unfilial hearts in response. Xie Yong’s scrutiny of thieves is why he was killed. Elevate the worthy, and the unbenevolent will distance themselves. Sui Hui’s governance is why the bandits fled to other lands.

Fan said: "When water serves as a mirror, it reflects forms without employing cunning, so that the square, round, crooked, and straight cannot escape. The skillful ruler conceals his sharp instruments, does not display them to others, governs through non-action so the people naturally transform, and remains desireless so the people naturally become simple. Why would he ever rely on schemes, multiply laws, embellish intelligence to astonish the foolish, or depend on keen observation to expect the capture of thieves? Xie Yong did not understand this."

Confucius, returning from Wei to Lu, halted his carriage by the bridge over the Yellow River to observe. There was a waterfall thirty *ren* high, its circular current flowing ninety *li*, where fish and soft-shelled turtles could not swim, and alligators could not dwell. Yet a man was about to ford it. Confucius sent someone along the bank to stop him, saying: "This waterfall is thirty *ren* high, its current flows ninety *li*, where fish and soft-shelled turtles cannot swim, and alligators cannot dwell. Surely it is difficult to cross?" The man, without hesitation, proceeded to wade across and emerged. Confucius asked him: "Is this skill? Or do you possess a method? How were you able to enter and exit?" The man replied: "When I first entered, I began with loyalty and trust; when I exited, I followed with loyalty and trust. Loyalty and trust placed my body amidst the waves, and I dared not act selfishly. This is how I was able to enter and exit." Confucius said to his disciples: "Remember this, my disciples. If even water can be traversed by entrusting oneself to loyalty and trust, how much more so with people?" This passage also appears in the *Huangdi* chapter, though with slight differences, but the meaning is not distinct, so its interpretation is not repeated here.

Lu said: "Loyalty is harmony with things; trust is absence of doubt. Harmonious and without doubt, not selfish toward oneself—this is how one enters and exits. Thus, to be harmonious and without doubt, not selfish toward oneself, is to know the Dao." This passage already appears in the *Huangdi* chapter.

Zhenghe said: "The Dao of utmost sincerity is unobstructed in all things. Loyal and without deceit, trustworthy and without doubt—acting with a sincere heart can move things. It can stir heaven and earth, move ghosts and spirits, traverse the six directions without resistance. Thus, one may traverse metal and stone, tread through water and fire."

Fan said: "He who swims at Lüliang must follow the principles of life and destiny; he who crosses the river bridge must embody the Dao of loyalty and trust. Their essence is one."

Bai Gong asked Confucius: "Can one speak with you in subtle words?" Confucius did not respond. Bai Gong was the grandson of King Ping of Chu and the son of Crown Prince Jian. His father Jian, slandered by Fei Wuji, fled to Zheng, where the people of Zheng killed him. Sheng wished to have Prime Minister Zixi and Marshal Ziqu attack Zheng, but though they agreed, they had not yet acted. When Jin attacked Zheng, Zixi and Ziqu prepared to rescue Zheng. Sheng grew angry and said: "The people of Zheng are here—our enemies are not far!" He intended to kill Zixi and Ziqu, hence his question to Confucius. Confucius, knowing this, did not respond.

Lu said: "Subtle words are secret words, which others cannot know. Bai Gong was the grandson of King Ping of Chu and the son of Crown Prince Jian. Jian fled to Zheng, and Bai Gong sought to cause chaos, which is why Confucius did not respond."

Bai Gong asked: "If one casts a stone into water, what then?"
Confucius replied: "A skilled diver of Wu could retrieve it."
The stone sinks when cast into water—this illustrates how subtle words escape notice, and so Confucius answered that only a skilled diver could recover it, making clear that nothing can remain hidden.

Bai Gong asked: "If one pours water into water, what then?"
Confucius replied: "When the waters of the Zi and Meng rivers mingle, Yi Ya could taste and distinguish them."
Again, even a master of flavors could discern them.

Lu said: "Casting a stone into water is like traces that cannot be seen; pouring water into water is like blending that cannot be concealed. Flavors may be distinct, yet the waters of the Zi and Meng cannot truly merge. Only through spiritual resonance with principle can one grasp such things."

Bai Gong asked: "Is it then impossible to speak in subtle words with others?"
Confucius replied: "Why should it be impossible? Only those who do not understand the meaning of words would think so."
The meaning of words is what conveys their intent; when the intent of speech is revealed, then the subtleties of language are manifest. When embodied in action, nothing remains hidden. Those who understand the meaning of words do not rely on words themselves—for when words are used to speak, subtlety and concealment vanish.

Lu said: "When emotions arise, events become evident; when flavors differ, they can be tasted. Only the boundless spirit, the one who understands the meaning of words, achieves spiritual communion. Those who fight over fish get wet; those who chase beasts run—not because they delight in it, but because the situation compels them. Thus, the highest words transcend words, and principle becomes self-evident; transformation proceeds of itself. The highest action is non-action, principle fulfills itself, and things follow naturally. What shallow minds contend over is but the trivial. When the root remains while the branches are pursued, clarity comes only after affairs are exposed—and few escape unscathed."

Lu said: "Fish dwell in water, and those who contend for them get wet; beasts flee across the wilds, and those who pursue them must run—not by choice, but by the force of circumstance. Therefore, the highest words lie beyond speech, and the highest action requires no action. Shallow wisdom chases the trivial and constantly loses the Way. Where the Dao advances, all things find their proper place."

Bai Gong, unable to restrain himself, later died in the bathhouse. Not understanding the meaning of words, he incited rebellion and thus met with disaster.

Lu said: "Acting in rage without principle, dying to satisfy his impulses—this is how the foolishly ignorant disrupt order."

*Zhenghe* notes: "When a stone is cast into water, it takes form; if one form were cast into another form that had never taken form, not even a skilled diver could retrieve it. When the waters of the Zi and Meng mingle, they acquire flavor; if one flavor were mingled with another flavor that had never manifested, not even Yi Ya could taste and distinguish them. Subtle words are indeed hidden yet not fully revealed, for words, once spoken, exist. Only when the Way is perceived in an instant, without need for sound, does one understand the meaning of words without relying on words themselves. Contending over fish or chasing beasts—these are trivial pursuits. Thus, the highest words must transcend words to be truly profound; the highest action must transcend action to be truly accomplished. How could Bai Gong be worthy of such discourse?"

Fan said: "When a stone is cast into water, a skilled diver can retrieve it; when water is poured into water, a master of flavors can taste the difference. Once something touches the realm of things, there is no escaping detection. Yet those who do not understand the meaning of words—how can one speak with them? Those who do understand the meaning of words need only remain silent, and their intent is already conveyed. To engage in subtle speech, one must be one who understands the meaning of words. Moreover, in the principles of the world, those who fight over fish get wet, and those who chase beasts must run—not because they inherently delight in it, but because the situation compels them. Why is this? Things are moved by stimuli, all arising from the mind. When the mind is fixated, thoughts shift with each passing notion. If speech is careless, even the smallest matter may leak and lead to harm. Thus, the highest words transcend words, appearing as though unspoken; the highest action transcends action, achieving non-action that accomplishes all. What shallow minds contend over is but the trivial. Bai Gong’s contention led to his destruction—this is what it means."

Viscount Xiang of Zhao sent Xin Zhi Muzi to attack the Di. Muzi was Xin Zhi Gou, a household retainer of Viscount Xiang. The Di were the people of Xieyu. He defeated them and captured Zuoren and Zhongren. Zuoren and Zhongren were two towns in Xieyu. He sent a messenger to report the victory. The messenger, a courier, came to announce the news.

Lu said: "He came in haste to report the triumph."

Viscount Xiang was eating when his expression turned troubled. His attendants said: "In a single morning, two cities have fallen—this is cause for joy. Why does my lord appear troubled?"
Viscount Xiang replied: "The greatness of the Yangtze and Yellow River lasts no more than three days—referring to the ebb and flow of tides. Fierce winds and torrential rains do not last the morning; the sun at its zenith does not linger. What flourishes must inevitably decline."

Now the virtue and conduct of the Zhao clan have found no ground for accumulation. Without accumulated virtue yet possessing weighty merit—this cannot but inspire caution and fear. In a single morning, two cities have fallen; if ruin does not overtake them, what will? To never forget the prospect of ruin is the way to avoid ruin.

Lu said: "They have not amassed virtue or piled up good deeds, yet by sheer force they have taken two cities. When things reach their zenith, they must decline—what awaits them but destruction? Thus the wise do not rejoice in greed, for they know the peril of fleeting gain. Only when one understands the fear that comes with reckless success can one begin to accumulate true virtue."

Confucius, hearing of this, said: "The Zhao clan shall prosper. For it is anxiety that brings prosperity, and deep vigilance that sustains it. Joy, on the other hand, is the path to ruin, for it leads to arrogance and boasting. Victory is not the difficult part; maintaining it is. A worthy ruler preserves victory through this principle, and so his blessings extend to later generations. The states of Qi, Chu, Wu, and Yue all once triumphed, yet in the end they perished—failing to grasp how to hold their victories. Only a ruler aligned with the Dao can truly sustain victory. Those who conquer their enemies may rival great states, yet if they cannot maintain their triumph, peril and ruin will befall them."

Lu said: "Boasting of merit and flaunting ability bring ruin; anxiety over gain and caution in strength bring prosperity. The worthy ensure blessings for their descendants through this, while those who follow the Dao extend their influence to all living beings—this is what it means to hold victory. To hold victory is to maintain this vigilance and prudence, overcoming the strong and overbearing. Only those who walk the Dao can practice this. Confucius possessed such strength that he could lift the crossbar of a city gate, yet he refused to be known for his power."

*Strength* here means force; *lift* means to raise. Confucius had the power to raise a gate’s crossbar, yet his reputation was not built on force—because he did not rely on it.

Mozi devised defensive strategies, and even Gongshu Ban was subdued, yet he refused to be recognized for his military skill. Gongshu Ban excelled in crafting siege weapons, but Mozi’s defensive designs overcame him, earning Ban’s admiration. Yet Mozi did not claim expertise in warfare—because he did not take pride in his ability.

Thus, those who truly hold victory turn strength into weakness. For weakness is the mother of attack.

Lu said: "Confucius had the strength to lift a gate’s crossbar, and Mozi had the skill to overcome enemies. Yet neither sought fame through force or strategy, but through virtue and the Dao—this is the art of holding victory, of turning strength into weakness. Those who perfect their skills will inevitably be enslaved by others; those who love victory will inevitably encounter stronger foes. Only through virtue, benevolence, and righteousness can one command things and inspire transformation."

*Governance and Harmony* states: To be full yet dwell in emptiness, to be complete yet dwell in deficiency—this is the Way of holding victory. To be firm yet guard with softness, to be strong yet guard with weakness—this is the Way of constant victory.

The greatness of the Yangtze and Yellow River is diminished by time. The gathering of wind and rain must eventually cease. The sun at its zenith must eventually decline. If even heaven and earth cannot endure forever, how much more so for man? Only those who begin with diligent care end in ease and joy—this is why anxiety brings prosperity. Those who indulge in pleasure and grow idle invite disaster—this is why joy brings ruin. Understanding this ensures blessings for later generations—this is the Way of holding victory.

To possess strength enough to overpower the many yet bear no record of martial prowess, to command armies capable of defeating enemies yet leave no fearsome reputation, to dwell in softness and humility yet overcome the hard and strong—none can surpass this. This is the Way of constant victory. Yet the Way of constant victory is precisely what it means to hold victory.

Fan said: "Winning battles is easy; preserving victory is hard. Thus only a ruler aligned with the Dao can hold victory."

*True Classic of Perfect Virtue, with Four Commentaries, Scroll Nineteen* concludes.
*True Classic of Perfect Virtue, with Four Commentaries, Scroll Twenty* begins.

**Matching Omens**

There was a man of Song devoted to practicing benevolence and righteousness, unwavering for three generations. One day, his family’s black ox unexpectedly bore a white calf. He asked Confucius about it, who said: “This is an auspicious omen—offer it in sacrifice to the Supreme Lord.”

A year later, his father suddenly went blind, and the ox again bore a white calf. The father once more instructed his son to consult Confucius. The son said: “Last time we asked, you lost your sight. Why ask again?” The father replied: “The words of a sage first seem contrary but later align. The matter is not yet resolved—ask again for now.” The son returned to question Confucius, who again said: “It is auspicious.” He instructed them to perform the sacrifice.

The son returned and relayed the message. His father said: “Follow Confucius’ words.” A year later, the son also went blind without cause. Later, when Chu attacked Song and besieged its capital, the people resorted to exchanging children to eat and breaking bones for fuel. All able-bodied men climbed the walls to fight, and more than half died. But this man and his father, both afflicted with blindness, were spared. When the siege lifted, their sight returned. This is what is meant by misfortune and fortune leaning upon each other.

Lu said: “The benevolent love others; the righteous aid all things. To practice this for three generations without ceasing is to accumulate goodness profoundly. If one possesses the talent, it invites emolument; if not, it still brings fortune—this is where blessings concentrate. Auspicious omens are the signs of goodness, ensuring preservation of life and attainment of benefit. Is the reward for accumulated virtue mere empty words?”

Zhenghe said: “Brightness arises from obscurity; order emerges from formlessness. Misfortune leans upon fortune, and fortune harbors misfortune. Who knows their limits? Thus, the principle of mutual dependence has no constancy. Only those of deep virtue enjoy enduring fortune, for blessings take root while misfortune does not come. This is why the man of Song, through practicing benevolence and righteousness, could turn misfortune into fortune.”

Fan said: “An omen in things foretelling fortune is called auspicious; an omen foretelling misfortune is also called auspicious. Is not what we call ‘auspicious’ merely the forerunner of good? Yet when a pig’s omen appears, the father deemed it inauspicious; what shamans and priests call inauspicious, spirits and sages regard as greatly auspicious. For sorrow and joy gather at the same gate, good and ill share the same domain, and the mutual dependence of misfortune and fortune remains unknowable—only the sage understands its workings.”

There was a man of Song called Lanzi, who, whenever people did not recognize his origins, was simply called *Lan*. He sought an audience with Duke Yuan of Song to display his skill: he bound two rods twice his height to his legs, running swiftly while juggling seven swords, five of which remained aloft at once. The Duke was astonished and immediately bestowed gold and silk.

Another Lanzi, skilled in acrobatics like today’s tumblers, heard of this and also sought an audience with the Duke. Enraged, the Duke said: “Previously, a man with extraordinary skill sought my favor—he was called Xianqiao. His skill was of no use, but I happened to be in high spirits, so I rewarded him with gold and silk. He must have heard of this and come again, hoping for my reward.” He had the man seized and nearly executed, releasing him only after a month.

Lu said: “One who accumulates benevolence and righteousness to uphold the Dao may preserve fortune; one who relies on strength or skill for luck will not enjoy lasting emolument. Liezi presents these two cases to illustrate the benefits of virtuous conduct.”

Zhenghe said: “Principles have no fixed right, affairs no fixed wrong—they depend on the times one encounters. Yet clinging to skill without understanding the Dao is especially not what can be called a constant Way. The noble person, knowing that allotments are not fixed, is thus careful in advancing or withdrawing.”

Fan said: “Those who meet the times prosper; those who miss them perish. If one indulges a heart bent on seeking advantage while ignorant of timely action, few escape harm.”

Duke Mu of Qin said to Bo Le: “You are advanced in years. Is there anyone among your kin who can seek horses?” (Bo Le was skilled in judging horses.) Bo Le replied: “A good horse can be discerned by its form and bone structure. But the finest horse in the world appears as if vanished, as if lost—it cannot be judged by form, bone, or coat. Such a horse seems to leave no tracks, its speed unmatched. My sons are all mediocre; they can be taught to recognize good horses but not the world’s finest.”

“I have a companion who gathers firewood with me—his name is Jiu Fanggao. In judging horses, he is not inferior to me. I ask to present him to you.” (Jiu Fanggao carried ropes and firewood, a menial task.)

Lu said: "One who carries ropes and firewood is a menial laborer. 'Kin' refers to those of the same clan among one's descendants."

Duke Mu received him and sent him to seek horses. After three months, he returned and reported: "I have found one—it is in Shaqiu." The Duke asked: "What kind of horse is it?" He replied: "A yellow mare." The Duke sent men to fetch it, but it was a black stallion. Displeased, Duke Mu summoned Bo Le and said: "It is ruined! The man you sent to seek horses—Jiu Fanggao—cannot even distinguish color or sex. How could he know horses?"

Bo Le sighed deeply and said: "Has it truly come to this? This is why he surpasses me ten thousandfold without measure." (This means his skill in judging horses was so extraordinary that he exceeded him by an incalculable degree.)

Lu said: "In judging horses, Fanggao discerned their spirit, not their form. Form is what ordinary people distinguish." Bo Le marveled that he forgot form to grasp the spirit, his focus reaching such singular depth that he deemed himself immeasurably inferior to Fanggao. Thus, Duke Mu saw failure where Bo Le saw ability.

What Fanggao observed was the *heavenly mechanism*—the inner essence that governs the movement of hooves and limbs, grasped in the heart yet not outwardly manifest. He perceived the essence while forgetting the coarse, attended to the inner while disregarding the outer. (The "essence" refers to the heavenly mechanism; the "coarse" refers to sex and coat color.) He saw only what was to be seen—the heavenly mechanism alone.

He does not see what is not to be seen—what is not to be seen is sex and coat color. He gazes upon what is to be gazed upon, attending only to what ought to be seen, never forgetting what he sees while disregarding what he ought not. What ought not to be seen is not worth his attention.

In Fanggao’s judgment of horses, there was something more valuable than the horse itself. His art was not merely about judging horses; what his spirit grasped must have been something far nobler than mere equine appraisal—such was its marvel. When the horse arrived, it proved to be a steed of the world.

Lu said: "Form and substance are the manifest aspects of the myriad things, while spirit and qi are the subtle, formless essence. That which moves the formed is itself formless; that which employs the formless gives shape to things. We use it all day without perceiving its work, operate it all year without feeling its labor. To know and nurture it is to master the Dao.

Fanggao saw what he saw through spirit, for he connected with the horse’s spirit and thus valued what transcended the horse. Others do not know what he valued.

*Zhenghe* states: The Dao resides in the formless. It vanishes as if extinguished, eludes sight as if lost; it cannot be grasped, leaves no dust nor track, and pursuit cannot reach it. Mediocre scholars and lesser talents—how could they comprehend this? What can be spoken of is the coarse aspect of things; what can be grasped in thought is their essence. To attain the essence while forgetting the coarse is beyond the reach of words. To know the outer while remaining ignorant of the inner, or to dwell within while forgetting the outer—this is knowledge that transcends knowing.

He sees only what is to be seen and not what is not; this is why his seeing is unique. He gazes only upon what ought to be gazed upon and not upon what ought not; this is why his gaze is divine. Since what he seeks does not lie within form and bones, what he attains is not confined to the superficialities of form, name, or reputation—this is the way of advancing toward the sage’s Dao.

The fine horse is an analogy for talent; the steed of the world is an analogy for the sage’s Dao.

Fan said: A yellow mare and a black stallion—how vastly different they are! Yet in Fanggao’s judgment of horses, he grasped the essence and forgot the coarse, attended to the inner while disregarding the outer, penetrated the marvel of the heavenly mechanism, and remained oblivious to color, sex, or outward traits. This is why Bo Le sighed in awe, deeming himself immeasurably inferior to Fanggao.

King Zhuang of Chu asked Zhan He: "How should one govern the state?"
Zhan He, a recluse, replied: "Your servant is enlightened in cultivating the self but not in governing the state."
The king said: "I have been entrusted with the ancestral temples and the altars of soil and grain; I wish to learn how to preserve them."
Zhan He replied: "I have never heard of a well-ordered self leading to a disordered state, nor of a disordered self leading to a well-ordered state. Thus, the root lies in the self; I dare not respond with mere branches."
The king said: "Well spoken."

Lu said: To diminish things for the sake of enriching life is the common inclination of petty men; to diminish life for the sake of benefiting things is the deceptive conduct of those who crave fame. To secure the altars of soil and grain, one must place the self last; to cultivate the self well is to ensure the state governs itself. The ruler is the master of the state; the spirit is the master of the body. Governing the state depends on pacifying the ruler; cultivating the self depends on pacifying the spirit. When the spirit is at peace, the Dao is exalted; when the Dao is exalted, the state is well-ordered. The spirit is the root of the self; the Dao is the function of the spirit. Thus, I dared not respond with mere branches.

*Zhenghe* states: The root of the state lies in the self. Therefore, those who illuminate virtue throughout the world, wishing to govern their state, first cultivate themselves—this is what is meant by "ordering the root so the branches follow." The ancients used the truth of the Dao to cultivate the self, and its remnants were applied to the state. How could a well-ordered self lead to a disordered state?

Fan said: The root of the state lies in the family; the root of the family lies in the self. What else does one who governs well require but to return to the root?

Zhan He’s fishing relied on using the weak to overcome the strong. By employing the light to attain the heavy, one may speak of governing the state. If one is truly capable of this, then the world may be moved within a single grasp. From this perspective, applying such a mind to other matters would surely render what was once neglected as effortless.

The elder of Huqiu said to Sunshu Ao, a grand minister of Chu: "There are three resentments a man may incur—do you know them?" (Huqiu was the name of a town; *zhangren* refers to an elder.) Sunshu Ao replied: "What are they?" The elder answered: "Those of high rank are envied by others; those in great office are resented by their lord; those with generous emoluments are pursued by grievances." Sunshu Ao said: "As my rank rises higher, my aspirations grow humbler; as my office grows greater, my heart grows smaller; as my emoluments grow richer, my generosity grows wider. By this, may I avoid the three resentments?"

Lu commented: "A heart that grows humbler with higher rank is the function of the Dao; generosity that grows wider with greater wealth is the function of virtue. One who employs the Dao to humble himself incurs no resentment; one who employs virtue to extend his bounty earns the lord’s favor. From whence could the three resentments arise?"

*Zhenghe* states: The noble man does not seek to elevate others excessively. As his rank grows higher, his heart grows humbler—this is how he avoids the envy of others and suffers no loss. As his office grows greater, his heart grows smaller—this is how he avoids the lord’s suspicion and suffers no loss. As his emoluments grow richer, his generosity grows wider—this is how he avoids the pursuit of grievances and suffers no loss. Such abundance is called "holding the rear while occupying the front."

Fan said: Sunshu Ao thrice served as prime minister without vanity, and thrice resigned without distress. With such a disposition, he was bound to avoid resentment wherever he went.

When Sunshu Ao fell ill and neared death, he instructed his son: "The king has repeatedly offered me a fief, but I have not accepted it. After my death, the king will surely enfeoff you. You must not accept fertile land. Between Chu and Yue lies a place called Qinqiu—this land is barren, and its name is most inauspicious. The people of Chu revere ghosts, while the people of Yue believe in omens." (Lu notes: The character *ji* is pronounced *jiyi* or *jixi*. The *Huainan Tradition* states: "The people of Wu revere ghosts; the people of Yue believe in *ji* [portents].") "Only this place may be held in perpetuity." After Sunshu Ao’s death, the king indeed offered his son fertile land. The son declined and requested Qinqiu instead, and it was granted to him.

To this day, it has not been lost. The Han minister Xiao He likewise said: "Let not your descendants be overpowered by powerful families"—this is of the same principle.

Lu notes: What men contend for, the strong will surely seize; what yields little profit, men will not vie for. That which is not contested may be long enjoyed, while that which is fiercely disputed cannot be eternally preserved. Men know only to contend for immediate gain, not that lasting possession brings deeper profit. Thus, desire is the ground of contention, while preserving the Way is the path to enduring prosperity. One skilled in the Way extends this principle to all things—wherever he goes, how can it not be the Way?

*Zhenghe* states: When all under heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness arises. Only he who dwells where the multitude shun and does not contend approaches the Way and may endure.

Fan notes: The ancients who attained the Way dwelt where others recoiled, not allowing personal preferences to burden their hearts. The enfeoffment of Qinqiu was how Sunshu Ao admonished his son.

Niu Que was a great Confucian of Shangdang. Traveling to Handan, he encountered bandits in the reeds of Ou Sha, who took all his clothing and carriage. Niu walked away unhurried, gazing back with a joyful countenance, free of distress or regret. The bandits pursued him and asked the reason. He replied: "A noble man does not harm what sustains him for the sake of what sustains him." The bandits exclaimed: "Ah! How worthy!"

Then they conferred among themselves, saying: "With his worthiness, if he goes to see the Lord of Zhao, he will surely expose us—this will surely bring us to ruin. Better to kill him." So they pursued and slew him together.

When the people of Yan heard of this, they gathered their clans and warned one another: "If you encounter bandits, do not be like Niu Que of Shangdang." All took the lesson to heart. Soon after, Niu’s younger brother traveled to Qin. Arriving at the palace gates, he too encountered bandits. Remembering his brother’s warning, he struggled fiercely against them. When this proved futile, he pursued them with humble pleas for his belongings. The bandits grew enraged: "We spared your life generously, yet you hound us without end—your tracks will soon be exposed. Once a bandit, where is your humanity?" They killed him on the spot, slaying four or five of his companions as well.

Niu Que brought misfortune upon himself by refusing to show regret, while the people of Yan, though pretending to cherish life, met with disaster. The unpredictability of safety and peril is of this kind.

Lu observes: He who knows the times and responds to principle acts without confusion when events arise, seizes opportunity when it comes, moves in accord with truth, and aligns with change. But to display one’s name and leave traces without regard for timing is to court failure at every turn. Niu Que did not know the times, and his brother overreached—much like the two sons of the Meng clan, who diverged from the paths of Wen and Wu. Those who force reputation beyond measure never escape such consequences.

*Zhenghe* states: Life cannot be preserved by valuing it, nor the body enriched by cherishing it—this is what befell Niu Que. The wealthy harm themselves with indulgence, the poor burden their bodies with profit—this is what befell the people of Yan. Thus, those who treat life lightly are not exempt from calamity, while those who cling to it remain entangled. This is why the sage regards the body as a great affliction.

Fan remarks: Niu Que invited misfortune by refusing regret, while the people of Yan met harm through forceful resistance. The workings of fortune and misfortune cannot be foreseen—such is the way of things.

Yu Shi was a wealthy man of Liang. His household overflowed with abundance, his wealth and silk beyond measure, his treasures beyond estimation. He would ascend a high tower overlooking the main road, where he set out music and wine and played *bo* on the upper floor. As knights-errant passed by, the gamblers on the tower would shoot dice—when the bright *qiong* piece landed true, they would reverse two *yu* pieces and burst into laughter. (The bright *qiong* was a die with five white faces; rolling five whites won the game, reversing two *yu* pieces secured victory, hence their laughter.) Just then, a kite swooped down and dropped a rotting rat onto the board. The knights-errant conferred: "Yu Shi has reveled in wealth and pleasure for too long, yet he constantly scorns others. Though we have not provoked him, he insults us with this rotting rat. If we do not avenge this, we cannot establish our reputation in the world." (Reputation for bravery.) They vowed in unison: "Let us join forces and lead our followers to destroy his household and all its equals." All agreed.

On the appointed night, they gathered their forces and weapons to attack Yu Shi’s estate, utterly annihilating his family. The ruin wrought by arrogance and extravagance follows no single path. Yu Shi harbored no intent to humiliate others, yet his household was destroyed—this too stemmed from his failure to cultivate humility and restraint in better times.

Lu observes: The previous chapter spoke of how the study of benevolence and righteousness brought blessings to the Three Dynasties, while this chapter shows how arrogance and extravagance invite disaster in a single day. When conduct lacks steadfastness, flying calamities strike. Fortune and misfortune have no fixed gate—they are summoned by man alone. These paired examples serve as profound warnings.

*Zhenghe* states: Fortune and misfortune come as man summons them. Yet who knows the reasons for Heaven’s displeasure?

Fan remarks: The Way rejects extravagance and excess. Extravagance corrupts virtue, excess strains one’s nature—how can this be the Way? Yu Shi reveled in wealth and pleasure for too long, indulging his scornful heart and abandoning humility and reverence. Thus, his ruin came swiftly, standing ready to befall him.

In the east there was a man named Yuan Jingmu. While traveling, he starved by the roadside. A bandit from Hufu named Qiu saw him and offered a gourd of food to feed him. After Yuan Jingmu ate three mouthfuls, his vision cleared, and he asked: "Who are you?" The man replied: "I am Qiu of Hufu." Yuan Jingmu exclaimed: "Ah! Are you not a bandit? Why feed me? I will not eat your food on principle." He pressed his hands to the ground and retched, but nothing came up. Gagging repeatedly, he collapsed and died.

The man from Hufu was indeed a bandit, but the food was not stolen. To condemn the food as stolen merely because the giver was a bandit and refuse to eat it—this is to confuse name and reality.

Lu observes: To seek reputation at the cost of reality, to violate the Way and forfeit life—does this not describe Yuan Jingmu? The man of the Way acts otherwise: he transforms the bandit’s heart, perfecting his humanity. By embodying the Way, others bathe in his influence. The noble man rescues the imperiled, preserving both himself and the giver. To retch is to lose both, and to gag repeatedly—what pretense of foolishness is this?

*Zhenghe* states: The excesses of the worthy make the Way difficult to practice. This is the narrowness of Boyi, which the noble man does not follow.

Fan remarks: A haughty offer is spurned even by beggars; food given with contempt is refused even by the starving. How much more so when the giver is called a bandit? Yet there are those who turn others away beyond the city gates—when their exchange is guided by the Way and their gifts by ritual, even Confucius would accept them. Would he refuse food merely because the giver was a bandit?

Zhu Lishu served Duke Ao of Ju but considered himself unappreciated and lived by the sea. In summer, he ate water caltrop and water chestnut; in winter, acorns and chestnuts. When Duke Ao faced danger, Zhu Lishu bid farewell to his friend and went to die for him. His friend said: "You left because you believed yourself unappreciated. Now you go to die for him—does this not erase the distinction between appreciation and lack of it?" Zhu Lishu replied: "Not so. I left because I believed myself unappreciated. Now, in dying, I prove that I was indeed unappreciated. I will die to shame those rulers of later times who fail to recognize their ministers."

Those who recognize their worth will die for it, while those who do not will not—this is the conduct of one who follows the straight Way. Zhu Lishu may be called one who resented injustice to the point of forgetting himself.

Lu said: "He never truly knew you, yet you died to prove his ignorance—how could this be the conduct of one who walks the Way? Such foolish attachment to reputation abounds, far removed from those who preserve life and treasure the Dao."

Zhenghe said: "The noble person may sacrifice their life to perfect benevolence, for benevolence cannot be abandoned; or lay down their life to uphold righteousness, for righteousness cannot be refused. Yet resentment, lacking any proper outlet, serves only to destroy the body. Those who cannot restrain their anger or curb their desires, who deliberately defy convention to squander their lives—such have never heard the great Way of the noble person."

Fan said: "Death may weigh heavier than Mount Tai or lighter than a goose feather—it depends entirely on how one meets it. Dying is not difficult; how one dies is. When Prince Jiu faced peril, Shao Hu died while Guan Zhong did not. The ancients never failed to criticize the prince yet praised Guan Zhong—how much more so in cases of mere unrecognized worth? To resent injustice to the point of forgetting oneself is not what the noble person values."

Yang Zhu said: "Benefits bestowed will truly reach others; resentments borne will bring harm in return. Benefits do not travel alone, nor do resentments spread universally—this is the natural order. What arises within will find response without: this is *qing* (emotion)." *Qing* here should read *emotion*. What emotion stirs reaches without regard for distance or depth. Thus the worthy are cautious in what they express. Goodness made manifest brings auspicious response; evil accumulated invites calamity.

Lu said: "This *qing* is as if one summons it oneself. The coming of fortune or misfortune is like shadow and echo—thus the worthy are cautious in what they express."

Nowadays, those who admire the Dao all cast off the teachings of names and rites, lightly abandoning ritual and law. They indulge their emotions and follow their own inclinations, believing this to be mastery of life; they treat duty as shackles, dwellings as mere garments, and scorn karmic retribution while neglecting human affairs. Thus Ji Kang’s followers perished without time to reflect, while Xi Kang’s generation hated the world as an enemy yet never grasped the true principle.

Zhenghe said: "Words issue from oneself and affect others; conduct begins near yet is seen afar. Words may invite disaster, actions may bring disgrace—the noble person cannot but be cautious. The saying that what arises within finds response without refers to the coming of fortune or misfortune, honor or disgrace, which are summoned solely by oneself."

Fan said: "Fortune and misfortune have no gate—they are summoned only by oneself. The coming of honor or disgrace reflects one’s virtue. What goes forth from you returns to you—can one not be cautious?"

Yangzi’s neighbor lost a sheep. Having already led his own kinsmen in pursuit, he then asked Yangzi’s servant to join the chase. Yangzi said: "Hmm, why so many in pursuit of one lost sheep?" The neighbor replied: "There are many branching paths." When they returned, Yangzi asked: "Did you recover the sheep?" The neighbor said: "It is lost." Yangzi asked: "How was it lost?" The neighbor said: "Among the branching paths, there were further branches. I knew not where to go, so I turned back."

Yangzi’s countenance fell into sorrow, and for a long while he did not speak. For the rest of the day, he did not smile. His disciples found this strange and asked: "The sheep is a lowly beast, and moreover not your own. Why has this diminished your words and smiles?" Yangzi did not answer, and his disciples could not fathom his intent.

Disciple Meng Sunyang went out and told this to Xin Duzi. Another day, Xin Duzi came with Meng Sunyang and asked: "Once there were three brothers who traveled between Qi and Lu. They studied under the same master and returned home having advanced in the Way of benevolence and righteousness. Their father said..."

The father asked: "What is this Way of benevolence and righteousness like?"

The eldest son said: "Benevolence and righteousness make me cherish my body first, then my reputation. I dare not harm even my hair or skin."

The second son said: "Benevolence and righteousness make me sacrifice my body to achieve renown. I do not seek life at the cost of benevolence, but embrace death to fulfill it."

The youngest son said: "Benevolence and righteousness allow me to preserve both body and reputation. Being enlightened and wise, I safeguard myself."

These three paths diverge yet all stem from Confucianism. Which is right, and which is wrong?

Yangzi said: "There was a man who lived by the river. Skilled in water, bold in swimming, he ferried passengers for a living, supporting his hundred-member household. Those who came with provisions to learn from him formed a crowd, yet nearly half drowned. They came to learn swimming, not drowning, yet the outcomes were so divergent. Which is right, and which is wrong?"

Xin Duzi left in silence. Meng Sunyang reproached him: "Why did you ask such a roundabout question, and why did the Master answer so obliquely? I am even more confused."

Xin Duzi said: "The great Way loses the sheep through many branching paths; scholars lose their lives through many methods. Though their fundamental learning is not different, nor their root distinct, their ends diverge so widely. Only by returning to unity can one avoid loss and gain. You have long studied at the Master’s gate, practiced the Master’s Way, yet fail to grasp his circumstances—how lamentable!

Lu said: "The sheep was lost by straying from the spirit; guarding the spirit without fail is the Way. Once the sheep is lost and one rushes along branching paths, it cannot be recovered. Simply guard the spirit, and there will be no loss or gain, no dependence."

Zhenghe said: "Since the Daoist arts were divided by the world, the Hundred Schools have gone forth and not returned. Thus, the people of the world each follow their own methods, fragmented and scattered, no longer seeing the great wholeness of the ancients. This is why many paths lose the sheep, and many methods lose lives. 'Benevolence and righteousness make me cherish my body first, then my reputation'—this is like the departure of Viscount Wei. 'They make me sacrifice my body to achieve renown'—this is like the death of Bi Gan. 'They allow me to preserve both body and reputation'—this is like the wisdom of Viscount Ji. Yet these three benevolent ones all returned to the same Way. If the people of the world, though taking different roads, could return to the same destination, there would be no gain or loss."

Fan said: "The Way does not desire complexity; complexity leads to multiplicity, and multiplicity to disorder. Those who embody the Way unify it with singular focus—how could multiplicity be valued? Converging different roads into one return, encompassing a hundred considerations into a single principle—this is near to the Way."

Yang Zhu’s younger brother, Bu, went out wearing white clothes. When it rained, he removed his white garments and returned in black. His dog, not recognizing him, barked as it ran to meet him. Yang Bu grew angry and was about to strike it. Yang Zhu said: "Do not strike it. You would do the same. If your dog had gone out white and returned black, how could you not find it strange?"

This passage clarifies that when one’s own form changes, external things cannot comprehend it, hence the arising of right and wrong. To seek nothing within oneself and blame others exclusively is like Yang Bu, who changed his clothes and was surprised by his dog’s barking.

Lu said: "Guard truth and return to unity, and even seagulls can be tamed. Lose the Way and alter constancy, and even a household dog will grow fearful."

Zhenghe said: "The conduct of the noble person is inwardly guarded and outwardly unchanging. Some know white but uphold black; some value black but esteem white—ordinary people surely do not understand. If one loses the true essence of their nature and outwardly changes between white and black, how can one prevent others from finding it strange?"

Fan said: "Things change without constancy, right and wrong vary. To know that it is so but not why it is so is delusion. Distinguishing true and false by force, names and realities shifting by morning and evening, praise and blame confusing again and again—is this not bewildering?"

Yang Zhu said: "Acting with goodness not for the sake of reputation, yet reputation follows; reputation does not seek profit, yet profit comes; profit does not invite conflict, yet conflict arrives. Thus, the noble person must be cautious in doing good. In wisdom, others contend with you; in strength, others compete with you—this is the natural order. There has never been one who dwells amid profit and reputation yet avoids calamity."

As the saying goes: "In doing good, do not seek nearness to fame." Is this not true?

Lu said: "Goodness that seeks reputation is what people inevitably contend over. Thus, 'in doing good, do not seek nearness to fame' means not contending with others for profit. Doing what others cannot do without boasting—this is being cautious in doing good."

Zhenghe said: "Goodness does not seek reputation, yet reputation comes of itself; reputation does not seek profit, yet profit comes of itself. Wealth and goods gather, and those who see them contend—thus their flow generates disaster. The sage sees the outcome to know the beginning, observes the past to know the future. Therefore, in the issuance of words and actions, one must be cautious in solitude."

*Zhuangzi* says: "In doing good, do not seek nearness to fame."

Fan said: "Goodness is what people desire. Once there is desire, some may pursue fame without rest, some may chase profit without satisfaction, risking their very nature and life to contend for it. They will resort to plunder, deceit, and every kind of falsehood. Thus, Bo Yi starved at the foot of Mount Shouyang, while Robber Zhi raged atop Dongling. Is this not bewildering?"

Formerly, there was a man who claimed to know the Way to immortality. The Lord of Yan sent someone to learn it, but before success, the teacher died. The Lord of Yan was furious and was about to punish the envoy. A favored minister advised: "What people fear most is nothing so urgent as death; what they value most is nothing beyond life. He himself lost his life—how could he enable the lord to avoid death?" Thus, the envoy was not punished.

There was a man of Qi who also wished to learn this Way. Hearing that the teacher had died, he struck his chest in regret. Fuzi heard of this and laughed: "He wished to learn the Way to immortality, yet when the teacher died, he still regretted it—this is not knowing why one studies."

Huzi said: Fuzi’s words are wrong. Generally, there are those who possess a technique but cannot practice it, and there are those who can practice it but do not possess the technique. There was a man of Wei skilled in calculation who, on the verge of death, explained its principles to his son. The son memorized his father’s words but could not put them into practice. Another man asked him, and he recounted what his father had said. The questioner applied the words and practiced the technique, matching his father’s skill without discrepancy. If this is so, how can it be said that the dead cannot transmit living techniques? Some things can be spoken of but not practiced, while others can be practiced but not spoken of—such are the differences in talent and nature.

Lu said: There are those who claim to possess techniques but merely speak empty words to argue principles, never having practiced or verified them themselves. Thus, people doubt their words, believing them to be untrue. Therefore, this passage speaks of those who know, those who know but cannot practice, and those who practice but do not know. Thus, knowing without practicing, practicing without knowing, or neither practicing nor knowing—though all may attain understanding—are not without distinction. How much more worthy is one who hears and practices, gaining enlightenment through understanding, than one who does not know the words?

Zhenghe said: Little Liang possessed the talent of a sage but not the Way of a sage; Lady Ou possessed the Way of a sage but not the talent of a sage. Some have the talent, others the Way—this is why none fully attain sagehood. Those who expound but cannot practice are said to have the Way; those who can practice but lack the technique are said to have talent. If the Way of the sage is taught to one with the talent of a sage, they may yet become a sage. Who says the dead cannot transmit living techniques?

Fan said: This speaks of how people’s talents and natures differ. The people of Handan presented doves to Viscount Jian on New Year’s Day, and the viscount was greatly pleased, rewarding them generously. A guest asked the reason, and Viscount Jian replied: “On New Year’s Day, I release living creatures to show my benevolence.” The guest said: “The people know you wish to release them, so they compete to capture them, and many die. If you wish to preserve life, it would be better to forbid the people from capturing them. To capture and then release them—your kindness does not compensate for the harm.” Viscount Jian said: “So it is.”

Lu said: What people consider good are all the branches of affairs. If one addresses the root, which most cannot comprehend, the effort is doubled compared to tending the branches—such is the case here. Thus, petty kindness is the thief of great kindness. The traces of name-based teachings are the branches; the workings of the Great Dao are the root. Most people see the small but do not recognize the great. Therefore, I briefly cite the release of doves to illustrate this grand principle.

Zhenghe said: Heaven and earth, in their forms, colors, wisdom, and power, allow the myriad things to fulfill themselves. The sage’s virtue of cherishing life is likewise all-encompassing. Tian of Qi held a sacrifice in his courtyard, with a thousand retainers in attendance. During the feast, someone presented fish and wild geese. Tian gazed at them and sighed: “Heaven is bountiful to the people. It grows the five grains and gives birth to fish and birds for their use.” The retainers echoed his words in unison. Bao’s son, twelve years old, was present and stepped forward, saying: “It is not as you say. Heaven and earth, the myriad things, and we are born together as the same kind. There is no nobility or baseness among kinds. Though all are living kinds, they esteem themselves and despise others. They merely dominate one another by size, wisdom, and power, devouring each other in turn—not born for one another’s sake. People take what is edible and eat it—did Heaven originally create them for human use? Moreover, mosquitoes and gnats bite skin, and tigers and wolves eat flesh—did Heaven originally create mosquitoes and gnats to feed on humans, or tigers and wolves to feed on flesh?”

Lu said: Those that eat flesh devour one another, extinguishing Heaven’s principle—how could this be Heaven’s intent? Bao’s words strike the proper principle. There was once a vulgar scholar who said that Fuxi invented nets and traps, and Suiren cooked meat to eat—yet those two emperors were sages. How far are sages from tigers that eat flesh? The sutras of the Buddha, though not from the sages of China, restrain people for their benefit. The Buddha is a sage of the six penetrations, restraining consciousness for the teaching, universally benefiting sentient beings. Now, the book of Liezi again clarifies this meaning—thus, the teachings of the Great Dao have never differed.

Zhenghe said: Heaven and earth are born with me; the myriad things and I are one. Thus, the similar and dissimilar are all of the same kind. Yet from the descent of forms and names, the large dominate the small, the near and far take from one another, the high and low vie with each other, and the ebb and flow of wisdom and power are all as they naturally are. Therefore, the sage’s Way is to let the myriad things be as they are and not act.

Fan said: People cherish all things. How can releasing creatures to show kindness truly be so? Heaven gives birth to all things. How can it be said that it creates things for the people’s use? In Qi, there was a poor man who constantly begged in the city. The city’s people, troubled by his frequent appeals, gave him nothing. He then went to the stables of the Tian family, working as an assistant to the horse doctor to obtain food. Someone in the capital mocked him: “Working for a horse doctor for food—is this not shameful?” The beggar replied: “Of all shames in the world, none surpasses begging. If begging is not shameful, how is working for a horse doctor shameful?” He did not consider working for a horse doctor a disgrace. This passage speaks of things reaching their extreme in one place—once distinctions are set, there is no longer any sense of shame or honor. How much more so for those who naturally equalize gain and loss?

Lu said: There are scholars who break their limbs or lick sores to gain advancement—this too is seeking clothing and food. Serving at the gate of a lowly physician is also seeking clothing and food. Those who gain much profit consider it honorable; those who gain little consider it shameful. Who among people truly knows the reality of honor and shame?

"In the *Zhenghe* era: Viewed through the Dao, things have no nobility or baseness; viewed through custom, nobility and baseness do not reside in things. Once this is understood, then the world’s shames are not enough to shame one’s person. This is what those who embody the Dao value.

There was a man of Song who, while traveling on the road, found a contract someone had lost and discarded. He took it home and hid it, secretly counting its teeth-like notches. The carved marks resembled teeth. He told his neighbor: ‘My wealth is imminent.’ Those who grasp at empty names to seek substance are like this man clutching a lost contract to seek riches."

Lu said: "The common people, deluded by empty names and losing sight of true principle, are all like one who picks up a lost water contract, calculates the number of its notches, and awaits wealth. It is also like feeling no shame at begging in the marketplace yet feeling shame at serving others. How is this different from a runaway slave who abandons his master to serve another, performing the same labor yet believing himself no longer bound to anyone? Following deluded emotions and losing sight of true meaning—all such cases are of this kind."

"In the *Zhenghe* era: What the world values as the Dao is books. Though the Dao is transmitted through books, it is not something books can truly capture. Those who rely on empty words to seek the Dao are far from the Dao indeed. Clutching a lost contract to await wealth—this illustrates the point."

Fan said: "Those who focus on inner observation understand the limits of sufficiency, so serving a horse doctor brings no shame. Those who crave external admiration indulge boundless desires, so they clutch lost contracts hoping for wealth.

A man had a withered parasol tree. His neighbor’s elder said withered parasol trees were inauspicious, so the man immediately cut it down. Though the elder’s words were fair, they were misplaced. The neighbor’s elder then asked to take it as firewood. This treads on dubious ground. The man became displeased, saying: ‘The neighbor’s elder only wanted firewood and taught me to cut it down.’ In matters of doubt, things cannot be trusted. To have such a treacherous neighbor—how can this be? "

Lu said: "Advising him to cut the tree was fair; asking for it as firewood was reasonable. Yet suspicion arose from the request after the advice. Thus it is said: ‘What people fear, one cannot but fear. Do not think it harmless—its calamity will grow.’ This is the meaning."

"In the *Zhenghe* era: In the realm of suspicion, every encounter breeds doubt, which is why the Dao cannot be practiced."

Fan said: "There was a rich man of Song whose wall collapsed in the rain. His son said: ‘If it is not repaired, thieves will come.’ The neighbor’s elder said the same. That evening, the family indeed lost much of their wealth. They trusted their son but suspected the neighbor’s elder. Thus, treading on dubious ground and misplacing words—how can one prevent others from doubting?

A man lost his axe and suspected his neighbor’s son. His gait seemed like an axe-thief; his expression, like an axe-thief; his speech, like an axe-thief; his movements and manner—nothing was not like an axe-thief. Later, he dug in his grain field and found the axe. The next time he saw his neighbor’s son, his movements and manner no longer resembled an axe-thief. When the mind is biased and deluded, it shifts with one’s thoughts. In extreme cases, white and black become the same color, square and round share the same form—can external things truly alter them? Thus the saying: ‘The myriad affairs, tangled and confused, all arise from the mind.’"

Lu said: "Many disasters arise from resemblance and doubt. Even a falling kite or a rat can cause suspicion. Only those who accumulate virtue, guard the Dao, and harbor no private attachments can avoid calamity. Thus, losing an axe and suspecting a neighbor—these are one and the same."

"In the *Zhenghe* era: Harboring a suspicious mind leads to obstruction everywhere. This is why the Dao remains obscure."

Fan said: "The myriad things, tangled and confused, all arise from the mind. When the mind is biased, it shifts with thoughts. Moreover, when something probes the clarity within, it sinks and drowns one’s true nature. Becoming infatuated with external goods, things transform. This is even more severe than suspecting an axe-thief.

Bai Gongsheng’s thoughts were in chaos. *Lü* here means ‘to consider’—he was plotting rebellion. After court, he stood still, inverted his staff, and its tip pierced his chin. Blood flowed to the ground, yet he did not know it. A man of Zheng heard of this and said: ‘If he could forget his chin, what would he not forget?’ When the mind is fixated, one may stumble over a tree stump or bump one’s head against a post without realizing it."

"In the *Zhenghe* era: Where the mind is fixed, the body is forgotten. Once, a man of Qi coveted gold. At dawn, he dressed and went to the market. Arriving at a gold-seller’s stall, he seized the gold and left. The authorities arrested him and asked: ‘With people all around, why did you seize their gold?’ He replied: ‘When I took the gold, I saw no one—only the gold.’ The chaos of craving distorts the human heart to such an extent. Thus the ancients said: ‘One who examines the tip of an autumn hair cannot see the form of Mount Tai; one who tunes the harmony of the five notes cannot hear the sound of thunder.’ When the mind is swiftly attached to external things, even great forms and sounds are missed. How much more so for one whose heart abides by principle, restrains emotions, gathers thoughts, and remains still and settled—can the movements of the myriad things disturb them?"

Lu said: "Zhang Zhan said: ‘The chaos of craving distorts the human heart to such an extent.’ Thus it is said: ‘One who examines the tip of an autumn hair cannot see the form of Mount Tai; one who listens to the harmony of the five notes cannot hear the sound of thunder.’ When the mind is fixed, the body is forgotten—all such cases are like this. This passage speaks of the great harm in indulging desires: the loss of one’s person and the destruction of one’s nature."

"Now, one seeks to enrich the body with that which destroys it. If one could be without this body, what use would gold have? To speak of being without the body is not to annihilate it, but merely to refrain from indulgence.

*Commentary (Zhenghe edition):* To perceive gain and forget form, to see profit and forget truth—this is how the worldly lose themselves to things. This chapter concludes with the tale of seizing gold, echoing the *Tianrui*’s account of thievery.

Fan said: When Duke Bai contemplated rebellion, he took up a thornwood staff with a spike to pierce his skull; when the man of Qi seized gold, he did so in the marketplace amid the gentry. The mind becomes so fixated that the body is forgotten—how can one not be cautious of such tendencies?"

*The Twenty-First Scroll of the Four Commentaries on the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue concludes.*
